Refuting the Necessity of Divine Will for Creation
The author rejects attributing will to Allah and refutes the conditional argument for the world's temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that attributing will to Allah is rationally invalid and how he refutes the conditional argument that the world's origination requires intention.

Core Idea
Divine will cannot be rationally attributed; the world's origination does not require an act of intention.

This lesson presents a two-part argument. First, the author concludes from prior reasoning that the term 'will' carries no meaningful content that can be attributed to Allah. Second, he considers an objection: if the world is originated, it must be by intention. He refutes this by showing the impossibility of a preponderance without a preponderant, thereby defending the original position that the world is not originated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the full English translation. Notice the logical flow from the conclusion about will to the conditional argument and its refutation.
2
Understand the conclusion about will
The author states that nothing remains from the term 'will' that can be truly attributed to Allah. Reflect on why this is a significant claim in Islamic theology.
The argument builds on prior reasoning not included here.
3
Analyze the conditional argument
Identify the conditional: 'If the world is originated, then it must be by intention.' Then follow the explanation that shows this leads to an impossibility (preponderance without a preponderant). Understand how this refutes the conditional.
The key concept is 'preponderant' (al-murajjih) – what tips the scales.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معقول . فيثبت بهذا : أنه لم يبق عند العقل من لفظ الإرادة ، معنى يمكن إثباته في حق الله تعالى . فكان القول به باطلاً . ثم قالوا : لو كان العالم حادثاً ، لوجب أن يقال : إنما حدث بقصد الفاعل وبإرادته ، والتالي محال . فالمقدم مثله . بيان الشرطية : هو أن العالم لما كان مستمر العدم من الأزل إلى ذلك الوقت المعين وذات الفاعل كانت موجودة من الأزل إلى ذلك الوقت المعين ، مع أنه في الأزل ما كان لتلك الذات أثر في خروج العالم من العدم إلى الوجود ، فلو حصل العالم في ذلك الوقت من غير أن يكون لذلك الفاعل قصد واختيار ، في إيقاعه . لكان وقوع العالم في ذلك الوقت دون ما قبله وما بعده : رجحاناً لأحد ظرفي الممكن على الآخر من غير مرجح . وإنه محال
Thus it is established that nothing remains from the expression 'will' that can be attributed to Allah in truth, so the statement of it is false. Then they said: If the world were originated, it would have to be said that it occurred by the intention and will of the agent, but the consequent is impossible, so the antecedent is likewise. Explanation of the conditional: that since the world was continuously non-existent from eternity until that specific time, and the essence of the agent existed from eternity until that specific time, and yet in eternity that essence had no effect in bringing the world from non-existence to existence, so if the world came into existence at that time without that agent having intention and choice in its occurrence, then the occurrence of the world at that time rather than before or after would be a preponderance of one of the two states of the possible over the other without a preponderant, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage presents two moves. First, the author concludes from previous reasoning that the concept of will (irada) cannot be meaningfully attributed to Allah, so any claim of such an attribute is false. Second, the author considers an objection: opponents might argue that since the world is originated, it must be due to an agent's intention and will. The author refutes this by showing the conditional's consequent is impossible. The explanation: the world was non-existent for eternity, and the agent existed, but in eternity no effect occurred. If the world came to be at a specific time without intention, that would be a preference for that time without a preponderant, which is rationally impossible. Thus the argument against the world's origination holds.

Key Terms
الإرادة (irada)
القصد (qasd)
الاختيار (ikhtiyar)
المرجح (preponderant)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The reader might think the author denies God's will entirely, but the argument is about a specific meaning of will that is impossible to attribute.
Correction: The author is not denying that Allah has will in a transcendent sense; rather, he argues that the ordinary meaning of will (involving intention and choice) cannot be rationally established for Allah.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main argument against attributing will (irada) to Allah?
A) Will is linguistically ambiguous.
B) No meaning of will can be rationally established for Allah.
C) Will is an eternal attribute.
D) Will requires a temporal intention.

Key Takeaway
The author argues that 'will' cannot be rationally attributed to Allah and that the world's origination does not require an act of intention, refuting the conditional argument by demonstrating the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the rational analysis of 'will' shows that no meaning can be attributed to Allah in the way you usually think, how does that affect your personal understanding of God's actions in your life? Are you willing to accept that your conception of divine will might be inadequate, and what does that demand from you in terms of humility and trust?
?
The opponents in this passage used a logical argument to try to prove that the world cannot be originated. Have you ever encountered a rational argument that seemed to challenge a core Islamic belief? How did you respond? Did you seek deeper understanding or simply reject it? What would it take for you to seriously engage with such arguments without losing faith?
?
The author concludes that attributing will to Allah is rationally unjustified. Yet you likely speak of Allah's will often. How can you reconcile your speech with this rational limitation? What concrete change in your daily conversation about God does this lesson require from you starting today?

Proof that Intention Cannot Be Eternal or Originated
Sanusi presents a proof that intention cannot be eternal or originated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why intention (qasd) cannot be eternal or originated, and why the existence of intention in the specific act of creation is rationally impossible.

Core Idea
Intention cannot exist because it would be either eternal or originated, both absurd.

We now examine a proof that intention cannot be the cause of creation at a specific time. The author argues that any such intention would have to be either eternal or temporal, but both entail absurdities. This lesson presents that argument and sets the stage for distinguishing eternal will from temporal intention.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the first proof, which sets up the dichotomy.
2
Understand the two possibilities
The author states that if intention existed, it would be either eternal (qadim) or originated (hadith). Identify these two categories.
3
See why both are false
Consider why an eternal intention is impossible (God's attributes are eternal, but intention is a relationship to a temporal act) and why an originated intention is impossible (God's will is eternal, so a temporal intention would imply change).
The passage does not elaborate, but the commentary clarifies.
4
Conclude with the author
The conclusion is that intention does not exist as a separate entity; rather, eternal will suffices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيان أنه يمتنع أن يكون حدوث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين ، بسبب القصد والاختيار : وجوه : الحجة الأولى : إن ذلك القصد لو حصل ، لكان إما أن يكون قديماً أو حادثاً . والقسمان باطلان ، فالقول بوجود القصد : باطل . إنما قلنا أنه يمتنع ، لأن الإرادة القديمة ، لا معنى لها إلا أنه تعالى يريد في الأزل إحداث العالم في وقت معين لا يزال . وهذه الإرادة ليست هي نفس القصد إلى إيقاع الفعل في ذلك الوقت المعين، عند حضور ذلك الوقت المعين والدليل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن من عزم على أن يفعل بكرة الغد : فعلاً معيناً ، ثم جلس في بيت مظلم لا يميز فيه بين النهار وبين الليل ، واستمر على ذلك الجلوس إلى أن دخل بكرة الغد ، مع أنه لا يعلم أن ذلك الوقت المعين قد حضر ، فإنه بمجرد تلك الإرادة الأولى لا يصير قاصداً لإيقاع ذلك الفعل في ذلك الوقت. ولو كان العزم على أن يفعل الفعل الفلاني في الوقت المستقبل، هو نفس القصد إلى إيقاع ذلك الفعل عند حضور ذلك الوقت ، لوجب في هذه الصورة التي ذكرناها : أن قاصداً إلى إيقاع ذلك الفعل ، عند حضور ذلك الوقت ، سواء علم أن ذلك الوقت حضر ، أو لم يعلم ذلك . ولما لم يكن الأمر كذلك ، علمنا : أن العزم على الفعل الفلاني في الوقت المعين ، من الأوقات المستقبلية : ليس هو نفس القصد إلى إيقاع ذلك الفعل في ذلك الوقت بل الحق أنه إذا عزم على أن يفعل الفعل الفلاني ، في وقت معين من الأوقات المستقبلة ، ثم استمر ذلك العزم الأول إلى أن حضر ذلك الوقت ، وعلم ذلك العازم ، حضور ذلك الوقت. فإن بقاء العزم الأول مع العلم بحضور ذلك الوقت : يوجبان حدوث قصد متعلق ، بإيقاع ذلك الفعل . فيثبت : أن العزم المتقدم، لا يكفي في إيقاع الفعل في الوقت الذي سيحضر بل لا بد من حدوث قصد متعلق بإيقاع ذلك الفعل
And an explanation that it is impossible for the world to come into existence at that specific time due to intention and choice: aspects. The first proof: if that intention existed, it would either be eternal or originated; both divisions are false, so the statement that intention exists is false. We only said that it is impossible because eternal will has no meaning except that He, exalted, wills from eternity to bring the world into existence at a specific time without cessation. And this will is not itself the intention to perform the act at that specific time, at the presence of that specific time. And the proof of that has aspects. The first: if a person resolves to do a specific action tomorrow, then sits in a dark room where he cannot distinguish between day and night, and continues sitting until tomorrow enters, while he does not know that that specific time has come, then by that first will alone he does not become an intender of performing that action at that time. And if resolving to do a specific action in the future time were itself the intention to perform that action at the presence of that time, then it would follow in this picture we mentioned that he would be an intender to perform that action at the presence of that time, whether he knew that time had come or did not know it. But since it is not so, we know that resolving to do a specific action at a specific time among future times is not itself the intention to perform that action at that time. Rather, the truth is that if he resolves to do a specific action at a specific future time, then that first resolve continues until that time arrives, and the resolute person knows the arrival of that time, then the persistence of the first resolve with knowledge of the arrival of that time causes the occurrence of an intention related to performing that action. Thus it is established that prior resolve is not sufficient for performing the action at the time that will come; rather, there must be an occurrence of an intention related to performing that action.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage begins by stating that it is impossible for the world's origination at that specific time to be due to intention and choice. The first proof: if intention existed, it would be either eternal or originated; both are impossible, so intention is false. The author then explains that eternal will only means that God eternally wills to create the world at a specific time, but that eternal will is not itself the intention to perform the act at that time. Evidence for this: the example of a man who resolves to do an action tomorrow but sits in a dark room unaware of time; his prior resolve alone does not make him intend the action at the time. If prior resolve were the same as intention, then he would intend it even without knowing the time. Since he doesn't, they are distinct. The correct view: when the time comes and he knows it, the persistence of the prior resolve together with knowledge generates a new intention specifically related to performing the action. Hence prior resolve is insufficient; a new intention must occur.

Key Terms
القصد (qasd)
قديم (eternal)
حادث (originated)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the proof, if intention existed, what must it be?
A) Either essential or accidental
B) Either eternal or originated
C) Either necessary or possible
D) Either prior or posterior

Key Takeaway
Intention cannot be eternal or originated, so it does not exist as a separate entity distinct from eternal will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where you cannot see God's 'intention' behind events, do you truly trust that His eternal will is enough, or do you secretly demand a clear purpose before you submit? What would it take for you to surrender that demand today?
?
If intention is not an attribute of God, then what does it mean for your own intentions? Are you building your life on the assumption that your intentions are real and effective, while God's are not? How does this truth reshape your reliance on your own planning?
?
This passage proves that God does not form new intentions. How does this challenge your habit of asking 'Why did God do this to me?' Can you stop seeking explanations for events and instead accept that His eternal will is beyond your understanding, but never beyond His wisdom?

Distinction Between Eternal Will and Temporal Intention
The author distinguishes eternal will from temporal intention using a human analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between God's eternal will and temporal intention, and why eternal will alone is not sufficient to produce an act at a specific time.

Core Idea
Eternal will is not the same as temporal intention; prior resolve requires knowledge to become intention.

Having shown that intention cannot be eternal or originated, the author now clarifies that God's eternal will is not an intention to act at that moment. Through a human analogy, he demonstrates that prior resolve and knowledge of time together generate a new intention. This lesson explores that distinction and its implications for understanding divine action.

Guided Path
1
Read the distinction passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the claim that eternal will is not itself intention at the time of action.
2
Understand the example
Consider the man who resolves to act tomorrow but sits in a dark room unaware of time. He does not intend the action at the moment it becomes time because he lacks knowledge.
This illustrates that prior resolve is not intention.
3
Analyze the logical argument
If prior resolve were intention, then the man would intend even without knowledge. Since he does not, resolve and intention are distinct.
The author uses a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Grasp the correct view
The correct view: when resolve persists and knowledge of time arrives, a new intention is generated. Thus prior resolve alone is insufficient; a new intention must occur.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيان أنه يمتنع أن يكون حدوث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين ، بسبب القصد والاختيار : وجوه : الحجة الأولى : إن ذلك القصد لو حصل ، لكان إما أن يكون قديماً أو حادثاً . والقسمان باطلان ، فالقول بوجود القصد : باطل . إنما قلنا أنه يمتنع ، لأن الإرادة القديمة ، لا معنى لها إلا أنه تعالى يريد في الأزل إحداث العالم في وقت معين لا يزال . وهذه الإرادة ليست هي نفس القصد إلى إيقاع الفعل في ذلك الوقت المعين، عند حضور ذلك الوقت المعين والدليل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن من عزم على أن يفعل بكرة الغد : فعلاً معيناً ، ثم جلس في بيت مظلم لا يميز فيه بين النهار وبين الليل ، واستمر على ذلك الجلوس إلى أن دخل بكرة الغد ، مع أنه لا يعلم أن ذلك الوقت المعين قد حضر ، فإنه بمجرد تلك الإرادة الأولى لا يصير قاصداً لإيقاع ذلك الفعل في ذلك الوقت. ولو كان العزم على أن يفعل الفعل الفلاني في الوقت المستقبل، هو نفس القصد إلى إيقاع ذلك الفعل عند حضور ذلك الوقت ، لوجب في هذه الصورة التي ذكرناها : أن قاصداً إلى إيقاع ذلك الفعل ، عند حضور ذلك الوقت ، سواء علم أن ذلك الوقت حضر ، أو لم يعلم ذلك . ولما لم يكن الأمر كذلك ، علمنا : أن العزم على الفعل الفلاني في الوقت المعين ، من الأوقات المستقبلية : ليس هو نفس القصد إلى إيقاع ذلك الفعل في ذلك الوقت بل الحق أنه إذا عزم على أن يفعل الفعل الفلاني ، في وقت معين من الأوقات المستقبلة ، ثم استمر ذلك العزم الأول إلى أن حضر ذلك الوقت ، وعلم ذلك العازم ، حضور ذلك الوقت. فإن بقاء العزم الأول مع العلم بحضور ذلك الوقت : يوجبان حدوث قصد متعلق ، بإيقاع ذلك الفعل . فيثبت : أن العزم المتقدم، لا يكفي في إيقاع الفعل في الوقت الذي سيحضر بل لا بد من حدوث قصد متعلق بإيقاع ذلك الفعل
And an explanation that it is impossible for the world to come into existence at that specific time due to intention and choice: aspects. The first proof: if that intention existed, it would either be eternal or originated; both divisions are false, so the statement that intention exists is false. We only said that it is impossible because eternal will has no meaning except that He, exalted, wills from eternity to bring the world into existence at a specific time without cessation. And this will is not itself the intention to perform the act at that specific time, at the presence of that specific time. And the proof of that has aspects. The first: if a person resolves to do a specific action tomorrow, then sits in a dark room where he cannot distinguish between day and night, and continues sitting until tomorrow enters, while he does not know that that specific time has come, then by that first will alone he does not become an intender of performing that action at that time. And if resolving to do a specific action in the future time were itself the intention to perform that action at the presence of that time, then it would follow in this picture we mentioned that he would be an intender to perform that action at the presence of that time, whether he knew that time had come or did not know it. But since it is not so, we know that resolving to do a specific action at a specific time among future times is not itself the intention to perform that action at that time. Rather, the truth is that if he resolves to do a specific action at a specific future time, then that first resolve continues until that time arrives, and the resolute person knows the arrival of that time, then the persistence of the first resolve with knowledge of the arrival of that time causes the occurrence of an intention related to performing that action. Thus it is established that prior resolve is not sufficient for performing the action at the time that will come; rather, there must be an occurrence of an intention related to performing that action.

Commentary
This passage begins by stating that it is impossible for the world's origination at that specific time to be due to intention and choice. The first proof: if intention existed, it would be either eternal or originated; both are impossible, so intention is false. The author then explains that eternal will only means that God eternally wills to create the world at a specific time, but that eternal will is not itself the intention to perform the act at that time. Key Terms
الإرادة القديمة (eternal will)
القصد (qasd)
العزم (azm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's eternal will is equivalent to a temporal intention, making God's will in time.
Correction: Eternal will is a prior resolve, not an intention at the time of action. The intention arises only with knowledge, and God's knowledge is eternal, so no temporal intention is needed.

Checkpoint
In the example of the man in the dark room, why does his prior resolve not make him intend the action at the time?
A) He forgets his resolve.
B) He changes his mind.
C) He does not know that the time has come.
D) He is sitting in a dark room.

Key Takeaway
Eternal will and temporal intention are distinct; prior resolve requires knowledge to become intention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a time you made a firm resolution to do something good, like pray or help someone, but when the moment came you were distracted or unaware. Did that prior resolve make you truly intend at that moment? What does this distinction teach you about the need to be spiritually present and aware when the time for action arrives? How can you cultivate that awareness starting today?
?
God's will is eternal and unchanging, yet it brought about a world in time. If you truly internalized that God's eternal will is distinct from a mere wish or intention, how would it change the way you trust His decree? Would you still worry about the future, knowing that His resolve is backed by perfect eternal knowledge? This week, try to consciously affirm that God's will for you is both firm and precisely timed—and see if your anxiety lessens.
?
The person in the dark room could not intend because he lacked knowledge. Are there areas of your life where you are acting on autopilot—based on old resolves—without truly knowing the present moment or God's current call? What would it look like to pause, seek knowledge of where you are right now, and renew your intention before God? Commit today to examine one habit and ask: Is this done with a living intention or just a routine?

Divine Attributes

Distinct Essences of Resolve and Intention
Sanusi argues from contradictory consequences to distinct essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why prior resolve (azm) and immediate intention (qasd) are considered different in essence based on their contradictory consequences.

Core Idea
Resolve and intention differ in essence because their consequences are mutually exclusive.

Sanusi now presents a second proof that prior resolve (azm) and immediate intention (qasd) are not merely different in degree but in essence. He argues by comparing their necessary implications in time. The passage shows that each has a distinct mahiyya (essence) by the fact that what they necessitate are incompatible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice how the author states the claim and then provides evidence.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the core argument is complete.
2
Identify the two essences and their consequences
Note that 'azm' (resolve) concerns future action and necessitates leaving it in the present, while 'qasd' (intention) concerns immediate action and necessitates doing it now and leaving it for later.
3
Understand the logical connection
Ask yourself: why does difference in consequences prove difference in essences? The answer is that if two things have contradictory effects, they cannot be identical in reality.
This is a common argument in kalam: differences in effects indicate differences in causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن العزم على الإيجاد في الزمان المستقبل : له ماهية . والقصد إلى إيجاد الفعل في الحال : له ماهية أخرى. إحدى الماهيتين لا تساوي الأخرى بدليل أن كل واحدة من هاتين الماهيتين ، لا تقوم مقام الأخرى . فإن العزم على الإيجاد في المستقبل ، يوجب تركه في الحال . وفعله في المستقبل . والقصد إلى الفعل في الحال ، يوجب الفعل في الحال ، والترك في المستقبل فهذان النوعان من الإرادة ولوازمها متنافية . واختلاف اللوازم ، يدل على اختلاف ماهيات الملزومات . وإذا ثبت هذا ، كان القول بأن العزم على الإيجاد في المستقبل ، يصير غير القصد إلى الإيجاد في الحال : معناه : أن
Second: prior resolve to bring about existence in the future has one essence, and intention to bring about existence in the present has another essence. One of the two essences is not equivalent to the other, as evidenced by the fact that each of these two essences does not take the place of the other. For prior resolve to bring about existence in the future necessitates leaving it in the present and doing it in the future. Intention to do the act in the present necessitates doing it in the present and leaving it in the future. So these two types of will and their consequences are mutually exclusive. Difference of consequences indicates difference of essences. And when this is established, the statement that prior resolve to bring about existence in the future becomes identical to intention to bring about existence in the present means that...
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof that prior resolve (azm) and intention (qasd) are distinct. The author states that each has a distinct essence (mahiyya). Evidence: they have different and contradictory consequences. Azm leads to leaving the act now and doing it later; qasd leads to doing it now and leaving it later. Since consequences differ, the essences must differ. The author then concludes that saying azm becomes qasd is meaningless. The text ends mid-sentence, but the argument is clear: they are fundamentally different.

Key Terms
العزم (azm)
القصد (qasd)
الماهية (essence)
اللوازم (consequences)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Resolve and intention are merely degrees of the same thing (e.g., stronger vs weaker will).
Correction: The author shows they are different in essence because they have contradictory logical consequences, not just different intensities.

Checkpoint
What evidence does the author use to show that prior resolve (azm) and immediate intention (qasd) have different essences?
A) They are mentioned separately in scripture.
B) Their consequences are contradictory.
C) One is eternal and the other temporal.
D) They depend on different agents.

Key Takeaway
Prior resolve (azm) and immediate intention (qasd) are different in essence because they necessitate opposite temporal outcomes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you aware of a gap between your resolve to obey Allah in the future and your intention to obey Him now? What concrete step can you take today to close that gap?
?
When you examine your own intentions, do you find that you sometimes confuse a future resolve with a present intention, using the former as an excuse to procrastinate? How will you change this after understanding that they are opposite in essence?

The Impossibility of a Temporal Divine Will
Argument that the will cannot be temporal, with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the divine will cannot be temporal and how the objection from daily events is answered within this argument.

Core Idea
A temporal will would require an infinite regress of particularizers, making it impossible.

This passage presents a logical proof that the divine will must be eternal. It begins by showing that a temporal will would need a cause to specify its time, leading to an infinite regress. The argument then addresses an objection based on everyday temporal events, clarifying that such events do not refute the core reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow. Notice the logical structure from premise to conclusion.
The passage uses technical terms like 'ikhtisas' (particularization) and 'tasalsul' (infinite regress).
2
Identify the main argument
Trace the argument: if the will were temporal, its occurrence at a specific time would need an external cause (another will) to specify it, leading to an infinite chain—an impossibility.
This argument is a standard kalam proof for the eternity of the divine will.
3
Understand the objection
The objection claims that daily temporal events (like the creation of the world) contradict the argument. Consider why someone might think this.
4
See how the answer reframes the issue
The answer does not deny daily events but refers to the 'first method' for a full resolution. Recognize that the objection is deflected, not answered in detail here.
The 'first method' likely refers to an earlier proof about the eternity of the world or a different line of reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مختصاً حقيقة مخصوصة، تصير هي بعينها عين حقيقة أخرى، تخالفها في الماهية والنوعية. وذلك محال. فهذا لبيان أن القول بقدوم تلك الإرادة: قول باطل. وإنما قلنا: إنه يمتنع كونها حادثة، وذلك لأنها لما كانت حادثة، كان حدوثها بوقت معين مع جواز حصول الحدوث قبله أو بعده. فوجب أن لا يحصل ذلك الاختصاص إلا لإرادة أخرى. والكلام فيها كما في الأول؛ فيلزم التسلسل، وهو محال. فيثبت: أنه لو حصلت تلك الإرادة لكانت إما قديمة أو حادثة، وثبت فساد القسمين، فلزم الحكم بامتناع حصول الإرادة. لا يقال هذا معارض بالحوادث اليومية. لأنا نقول: المعارضة بالحوادث اليومية واردة على كل هذه الوجوه والجواب الممكن: ما ذكرناه في الطريقة الأولى والله أعلم.
It is particularized to a specific reality, becoming identical to another reality that differs from it in essence and type. That is impossible. So this is to show that the claim that that will is temporal is a false claim. And we say: it is impossible for it to be temporal, because if it were temporal, its occurrence would be at a specific time, while it is possible for it to occur before or after. So that particularization cannot occur except through another will. And the discussion about it is as in the first; so an infinite regress follows, which is impossible. So it is established: if that will occurred, it would be either eternal or temporal, and the corruption of both divisions is established, so it is necessary to judge the impossibility of the occurrence of the will. It is not said: this is countered by daily events. For we say: the objection by daily events applies to all these aspects, and the possible answer is what we mentioned in the first method, and God knows best.

Commentary
This passage presents an argument that the divine will cannot be temporal (haditha), followed by an objection and answer. The argument begins by stating that if the will were temporal, its occurrence at a specific time rather than before or after would require a particularization (ikhtisas). That particularization would need another will to cause it, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible. Therefore, the will cannot be temporal; if it existed, it would have to be either eternal or temporal, but both are shown to be impossible. The objection claims that this argument is refuted by the occurrence of daily temporal events (al-hawadith al-yawmiyya). The answer is that the objection applies to all these aspects, and the possible response is that already given in the first method (al-tariqa al-ula), with reliance on God's knowledge. The passage thus defends the impossibility of a temporal divine will.

The Second Proof: The Eternal Will Must Cease
Second proof that the eternal will must cease.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that the divine will cannot be eternal, based on the necessary cessation of its attachment to a specific time.

Core Idea
An eternal will cannot cease; but this proof shows it must, so it cannot be eternal.

This lesson examines the second proof against the eternity of the divine will. The argument focuses on the attachment of the will to a specific time and shows that this attachment must be essential, yet it ceases after the event, forcing the will itself to cease. We will trace the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two possibilities for the attachment
The proof begins by presenting a dichotomy: the attachment (ta'alluq) of the eternal will to the creation of the world at a specific time is either a necessary concomitant of the will's essence or not.
This sets up the logical structure of the proof.
2
See why the second possibility is impossible
If the attachment is not a necessary concomitant, it would require an external cause, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is rationally impossible. Therefore, the attachment must be essential.
This eliminates the alternative.
3
Grasp why the attachment must cease after the world's creation
After the world comes into existence at that specific time, the attachment to bringing it into existence at that same time becomes impossible. Two reasons are given: (1) you cannot bring into existence what already exists, and (2) intending an act in the past is impossible.
Thus the attachment cannot persist.
4
Recognize that the will itself must cease
Since the attachment is a necessary concomitant of the will's essence, when the attachment ceases, the will itself must cease. Therefore, the will is not eternal but temporal.
This is the core conclusion.
5
Conclude that the claim of an eternal will is false
Whatever can cease to exist cannot be eternal. Therefore, the claim that God's will is eternal is false.
This proof is considered strong by the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية: أن نقول: تعلق الإرادة القديمة بإيقاع العالم في ذلك الوقت المخصوص المعين، إما أن يكون من لوازم ماهية تلك الإرادة وإما أن لا يكون. والثاني باطل، وإلا لافتقرت تلك الإرادة في حصول ذلك التعلق المخصوص، إلى سبب آخر، ولزم التسلسل. ولما بطل هذا القسم، بقي الحق هو الأول. فنقول: إذا دخل العالم في الوجود في ذلك الوقت المعين، فبعد انقضاء ذلك الوقت المعين وبعد دخول العالم في الوجود يمتنع أن تبقى الإرادة متعلقة بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين. ويدل عليه وجهان: الأول: إنه بعد دخوله في الوجود، يمتنع إدخاله في الوجود. لأن تكوين الكائن وإيجاده محال. والثاني: إن ذلك الوقت لما انقضى ومضى، فبعد انقضائه لو بقيت الإرادة متعلقة بإحداث العالم، في ذلك الوقت: كان ذلك قصداً، إلى إيقاع الفعل في الزمان الماضي، وإنه محال. فيثبت: أن العالم بعد دخوله في الوجود، يمتنع بقاء الإرادة الأزلية متعلقة بإدخال عين ذلك العالم في عين ذلك الوقت، في الوجود، فوجب القول بزوال ذلك التعلق وبطلانه. لكنا بينا: أن ذلك التعلق المخصوص من لوازم ماهية تلك الإرادة. وزوال اللازم يدل على زوال الملزوم. فيثبت: أن تلك الإرادة واجبة العدم، بعد دخول ذلك المراد في الوجود، وكل ما صح عدمه، امتنع قدمه. فهذا دليل قوي على أن القول بأن إرادة الله تعالى قديمة قول باطل.
The second proof: we say: The attachment of the eternal will to the occurrence of the world at that specific designated time is either a necessary concomitant of the essence of that will or not. The second is false, otherwise that will would need another cause for that specific attachment, and an infinite regress would follow. When this division is invalidated, the truth remains the first. We say: When the world comes into existence at that designated time, after the passing of that designated time and after the world's entry into existence, it is impossible for the will to remain attached to bringing the world into existence at that designated time. Two points indicate this: First: After its entry into existence, bringing it into existence again is impossible, because the formation of the existent and its bringing into existence is impossible. Second: When that time has passed and elapsed, after its passing if the will remained attached to bringing the world into existence at that time, that would be an intention to perform the act in the past time, and that is impossible. So it is established: After the world's entry into existence, it is impossible for the eternal will to remain attached to bringing that same world into existence at that same time; so it is necessary to say that that attachment ceases and is invalid. But we have shown that that specific attachment is a necessary concomitant of the essence of that will. The cessation of the concomitant indicates the cessation of the thing conjoined. So it is established: That will is necessarily non-existent after the entry of that object of will into existence, and everything that can possibly cease to exist is impossible to be eternal. So this is a strong proof that the claim that the will of God Almighty is eternal is a false claim.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof (al-hujja al-thaniyya) that the divine will cannot be eternal (qadima). The proof proceeds by examining the attachment (ta'alluq) of the eternal will to the creation of the world at a specific time. The attachment is either a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the will's essence or not. If it is not a necessary concomitant, then it would require an external cause, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible. Therefore, the attachment must be essential (lazim al-mahiyya). The argument then focuses on what happens after the world comes into existence at that specific time. After that time passes, it is impossible for the will to remain attached to creating the world at that same time, because: (1) bringing into existence something already existing is impossible; (2) intending an act in the past is impossible. Therefore, the attachment must cease. But since the attachment is a necessary concomitant of the will's essence, its cessation implies the cessation of the will itself. Thus, the will would become non-existent after the world's creation, meaning it is temporal (haditha) and not eternal. Therefore, claiming that God's will is eternal is false. This proof leverages the principle that whatever can cease to exist cannot be eternal (qadim).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes the attachment is accidental.
Correction: The author first demonstrates that the attachment is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the will's essence, not accidental, by eliminating the alternative that would lead to infinite regress.

Checkpoint

C) Because God changes His mind.
D) Because the will is temporal by nature.

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that the divine will cannot be eternal because its essential attachment to a specific time must cease after that time, implying the will itself ceases.

The Impossibility of a Temporal Will and the Third Proof
Brief argument against temporal will and third proof against eternal will with intention dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two arguments from kalam regarding the divine will: why it cannot be temporal, and why an eternal will with specific intention leads to either divine imperfection or an irrational preference.

Core Idea
The divine will cannot be temporal, and an eternal will with intention must avoid both deficiency and arbitrary preference.

In this passage, the author presents two concise arguments about the nature of the divine will. First, he argues that the will cannot be temporal (haditha) because that would require it to inhere in God's essence or elsewhere, both impossible. Then, he offers a third proof against the will being eternal, focusing on the intention behind creation. The argument leads to a dilemma: if God's intention is for His own benefit, He would be deficient; if not, then there is no basis for preference. The text cuts off during the response to an objection, but the core reasoning is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the two arguments presented. Note the concise style and the strong logical structure.
The second argument is incomplete, but the main thrust is clear.
2

Identify the three options for where a temporal will could occur: in God’s essence, in another essence, or in no locus. Understand why each is considered invalid by well-known kalam proofs.
This argument relies on standard refutations of accidents in God's essence.
3
Analyze the third proof’s dilemma
Break down the dilemma: if God’s intention to create is for His own benefit, He would be deficient and needing completion; if not, there is no reason to prefer creation over non-creation, leading to irrationality.
The proof targets the idea that an eternal will with specific intention must have a motive.
4
Consider the objection and note the cut-off
Read the objection that the deficiency only follows if the benefit returns to God, not to others. The author begins a response but it is cut off. Reflect on how you might complete the argument.
The incomplete response leaves room for further study.

Understand that these arguments protect God’s perfection and self-sufficiency. The divine will must be eternal and free from any deficiency or arbitrary preference.
This connects to the broader theme of divine attributes in kalam.

وأما أنه يمتنع كونها حادثة، فلأنها لو كانت حادثة، لكانت إما أن تحدث في ذات الله تعالى، أو في ذات أخرى، أو لا في محل. والكل باطل بالوجوه المشهورة، فكان القول به: باطلا.

And as for the impossibility of it being temporal, then because if it were temporal, it would either occur in the essence of God Almighty, or in another essence, or in no locus. All are invalid by the well-known considerations, so holding that view is false.

The third proof: Every person who intends to bring something into existence, either that bringing into existence is more preferable in his opinion and belief than leaving it, or not. If the first, then it would follow that he is deficient in himself, completing himself through another, and that with respect to the Necessary Existence in Himself is impossible. If the second, then the intention to bring into existence is not preferred over the intention to leave, because the intention to bring into existence is a preference, and the occurrence of preference at the time of non-occurrence of preference is impossible. If they say: His being deficient in Himself, completing Himself through another, follows only if He preferred bringing into existence over leaving it for a benefit returning to Him. But if He preferred it for a benefit returning to another, the mentioned impossibility does not follow. We say: Conveying good and benefit to another, and not conveying it to him, if they are equal with respect to

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct arguments. First, a brief argument that the divine will cannot be temporal (haditha): if it were temporal, it would have to occur either in God's essence, in another essence, or in no locus. All these options are considered invalid based on well-known arguments (al-wujuh al-mashhura), so the claim is false. This argument is concise and relies on standard kalam refutations of accidents in God's essence. Second, the third proof (al-hujja al-thalitha) argues against the will being eternal (qadima) by considering the intention (qasd) behind creation. If God intends to create something, either creating is preferable to Him or not. If it is preferable, then He is deficient (naqis) and seeks completion through another, which is impossible for the Necessary Existence. If it is not preferable, then there is no ground for preferring creation over non-creation, and preference without a cause is impossible. An objection is raised: the deficiency only follows if the preference is for God's own benefit; if it is for the benefit of others, no deficiency arises. The author begins to respond by noting that conveying good to others and not conveying it, if they are equal in God's view, but the text cuts off before completing the response. The proof aims to show that an eternal will with a specific intention leads to either imperfection or irrationality.

?

The Second Proof: The Divine Will Cannot Be Eternal
Al-Razi proves divine will is not eternal by showing its attachment ceases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Fakhr al-Din al-Razi argues that the divine will cannot be eternal because its attachment to a specific time ceases after that time passes, and the cessation of the concomitant implies the cessation of the essence.

Core Idea
The divine will cannot be eternal because its object is temporal.

In this passage, al-Razi presents the second proof against the eternity of the divine will. He argues that the will's attachment to a specific moment of creation ceases once that moment has passed. Since that attachment is a necessary concomitant of the will's essence, its cessation forces the will itself to cease, proving it cannot be eternal. This lesson walks through that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of al-Razi's reasoning.
Notice the precise logical steps.
2
Identify the key concepts
Identify the terms 'lāzim' (concomitant) and 'malzūm' (conjoined) and understand their relationship.
This relationship is crucial to the proof.
3
Follow the logical chain
Trace the reasoning: time passes, attachment ceases, concomitant ceases, therefore the will ceases; hence not eternal.
Write down each step in order.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why al-Razi calls this a 'strong proof' and how it refutes the claim that the divine will is eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : إن ذلك الوقت لما انقضى ومضى ، فبعد انقضائه لو بقيت الإرادة متعلقة بإحداث العالم ، في ذلك الوقت : كان ذلك قصداً ، إلى إيقاع الفعل في الزمان الماضي ، وإنه محال . فيثبت : أن العالم بعد دخوله في الوجود ، يمتنع بقاء الإرادة الأزلية متعلقة بإدخال عين ذلك العالم في عين ذلك الوقت ، في الوجود، فوجب القول بزوال ذلك التعلق وبطلانه . لكنا بينا : أن ذلك التعلق المخصوص من لوازم ماهية تلك الإرادة . وزوال اللازم يدل على زوال الملزوم. فيثبت : أن تلك الإرادة واجبة العدم ، بعد دخول ذلك المراد في الوجود ، وكل ما صح عدمه ، امتنع قدمه . فهذا دليل قوي على أن القول بأن إرادة الله تعالى قديمة قول باطل . وأما أنه يمتنع كونها حادثة ، فلأنها لو كانت حادثة ، لكانت إما أن تحدث في ذات الله تعالى ، أو في ذات أخرى ، أو لا في محل . والكل باطل بالوجوه المشهورة ، فكان القول به : باطلا.
And the second [argument]: When that time has passed and gone, after its passing if the will remained attached to bringing the world into existence in that time, that would be an intention to place the act in past time, and that is impossible. Thus it is established that after the world enters existence, it is impossible for the eternal will to remain attached to bringing that same world into existence at that same time. So it is necessary to say that that attachment ceases and is nullified. But we have shown that that particular attachment is a necessary concomitant of the quiddity of that will. And the cessation of the concomitant indicates the cessation of the thing conjoined. Thus it is established that that will is necessarily non-existent after the entrance of that willed thing into existence, and everything whose non-existence is sound is impossible to be eternal. So this is a strong proof that the statement that the will of God the Exalted is eternal is a false statement. As for the impossibility of its being temporal, that is because if it were temporal, then it would either occur in the essence of God the Exalted, or in another essence, or not in a locus. All of this is false by well-known arguments, so holding it is false.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument against the eternity of the divine will, and also shows that it cannot be temporal. The argument proceeds as follows: First, consider a specific time for creation. After that time passes, if the eternal will remained attached to creating the world at that time, it would be an intention to make the act occur in the past, which is impossible. Therefore, the attachment must cease. But the author has already shown that this attachment is a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of the quiddity of that will. Since the concomitant ceases, the thing conjoined (malzūm) also ceases. Thus the will itself must cease to exist after the creation, proving that it is not eternal. Furthermore, if the will were temporal (ḥāditha), it would have to originate either in God's essence, or in another essence, or without a locus—all of which are impossible by well-known arguments. Hence the will cannot be temporal either. The conclusion is that the divine will is neither eternal nor temporal, refuting those who ascribe an eternal or temporal will to God.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The divine will is an eternal attribute that never ceases.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that the will's attachment to a temporal object forces the will itself to cease, so it cannot be eternal.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's second argument, why must the divine will itself cease to exist after the world is created?
A) Because God's will is temporal and must pass away.
B) Because the attachment of the will to creation is a necessary concomitant, and its cessation implies the will's cessation.
C) Because the world's existence makes the will unnecessary.
D) Because God cannot will something that already exists.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's second proof shows that the divine will cannot be eternal because its necessary concomitant (attachment to a temporal event) ceases, forcing the will itself to cease.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will is not eternal but ceases after its object exists, what does that mean for your own will? Do you hold onto past desires that should have ceased after their object was fulfilled or lost? How does this lesson challenge you to let go of what is no longer relevant?
?
Al-Razi's proof relies on the precision of necessary concomitants. Consider a deeply held belief or habit you assume is permanent. Is it truly necessary, or is it only attached to a temporal situation? Are you willing to examine whether it should cease when its object changes? Make a commitment this week to identify one 'attachment' you wrongly treat as eternal and begin releasing it.
?
The divine will is relational—its existence depends on its object. Your own will often claims independence, but in reality it is shaped by passing circumstances. Will you today admit that your will is not absolute, but contingent on the One whose will is unceasing? What would change if you stopped asserting your will as eternal and aligned it with the ever-living will of God?

The Second Proof: The Divine Will Cannot Be Temporal
Al-Razi proves divine will is not temporal by eliminating possible loci.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi argues that the divine will cannot be temporal (ḥādith) based on the impossibility of an accident inhering without a locus or in God's essence.

Core Idea
The divine will cannot be temporal because a temporal accident would need an impossible locus.

After establishing that the divine will is not eternal, al-Razi also refutes the possibility that it could be temporal (originating in time). He appeals to the well-known arguments that a temporal attribute must inhere in a substance, but it cannot inhere in God's essence (as that would imply change), nor in another essence (as that would make it a creation), nor without a locus (as accidents require a substrate). This lesson examines that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part
Read the Arabic and translation for the temporal argument. Note its brevity.
Al-Razi relies on standard kalām arguments.
2
Understand the three options
Al-Razi enumerates three possible loci for a temporal will: in God's essence, in another essence, or no locus.
These are the only logical possibilities.
3
Recall the well-known refutations
Each option is deemed impossible by standard kalām arguments. Think about why each is problematic.
If needed, consult a commentary for the well-known arguments.
4
Conclude the impossibility
Since all options are false, the will cannot be temporal.
This completes the proof that the will is neither eternal nor temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : إن ذلك الوقت لما انقضى ومضى ، فبعد انقضائه لو بقيت الإرادة متعلقة بإحداث العالم ، في ذلك الوقت : كان ذلك قصداً ، إلى إيقاع الفعل في الزمان الماضي ، وإنه محال . فيثبت : أن العالم بعد دخوله في الوجود ، يمتنع بقاء الإرادة الأزلية متعلقة بإدخال عين ذلك العالم في عين ذلك الوقت ، في الوجود، فوجب القول بزوال ذلك التعلق وبطلانه . لكنا بينا : أن ذلك التعلق المخصوص من لوازم ماهية تلك الإرادة . وزوال اللازم يدل على زوال الملزوم. فيثبت : أن تلك الإرادة واجبة العدم ، بعد دخول ذلك المراد في الوجود ، وكل ما صح عدمه ، امتنع قدمه . فهذا دليل قوي على أن القول بأن إرادة الله تعالى قديمة قول باطل . وأما أنه يمتنع كونها حادثة ، فلأنها لو كانت حادثة ، لكانت إما أن تحدث في ذات الله تعالى ، أو في ذات أخرى ، أو لا في محل . والكل باطل بالوجوه المشهورة ، فكان القول به : باطلا.
And the second [argument]: When that time has passed and gone, after its passing if the will remained attached to bringing the world into existence in that time, that would be an intention to place the act in past time, and that is impossible. Thus it is established that after the world enters existence, it is impossible for the eternal will to remain attached to bringing that same world into existence at that same time. So it is necessary to say that that attachment ceases and is nullified. But we have shown that that particular attachment is a necessary concomitant of the quiddity of that will. And the cessation of the concomitant indicates the cessation of the thing conjoined. Thus it is established that that will is necessarily non-existent after the entrance of that willed thing into existence, and everything whose non-existence is sound is impossible to be eternal. So this is a strong proof that the statement that the will of God the Exalted is eternal is a false statement. As for the impossibility of its being temporal, that is because if it were temporal, then it would either occur in the essence of God the Exalted, or in another essence, or not in a locus. All of this is false by well-known arguments, so holding it is false.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument against the eternity of the divine will, and also shows that it cannot be temporal. The argument proceeds as follows: First, consider a specific time for creation. After that time passes, if the eternal will remained attached to creating the world at that time, it would be an intention to make the act occur in the past, which is impossible. Therefore, the attachment must cease. But the author has already shown that this attachment is a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of the quiddity of that will. Since the concomitant ceases, the thing conjoined (malzūm) also ceases. Thus the will itself must cease to exist after the creation, proving that it is not eternal. Furthermore, if the will were temporal (ḥāditha), it would have to originate either in God's essence, or in another essence, or without a locus—all of which are impossible by well-known arguments. Hence the will cannot be temporal either. The conclusion is that the divine will is neither eternal nor temporal, refuting those who ascribe an eternal or temporal will to God.

ذات
محل

Correction: Al-Razi argues that a temporal attribute would require an impossible locus, so the will cannot be temporal.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, why is it impossible for the divine will to be temporal?
A) Because a temporal will would have to inhere in God's essence, which is impossible.
B) Because a temporal will could not be attributed to God.
C) Because all three possible loci for a temporal accident are impossible.
D) Because the will is eternal by definition.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's brief argument shows that the divine will cannot be temporal because a temporal accident cannot inhere in God, nor in another essence, nor without a locus.

The Third Argument: Ground for Preference
Al-Razi argues that any ground for preference implies deficiency or indeterminacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument against a temporal will: the ground for preference (tarjīḥ) dilemma and how it refutes a temporal divine will.

Core Idea
A ground for preference implies either deficiency in God or no preference at all.

Al-Razi presents a powerful logical argument that any reason for God to create the world at a particular time would either make Him dependent on something else or leave the choice arbitrary. This passage explores the dilemma of the ground for preference (muwajjih) in divine action. By examining both horns of the dilemma, Al-Razi shows that a temporal will is rationally impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation to get an overview. Notice the structure: a dilemma, then an objection, then a rebuttal.
The passage is dense; take your time to see the logical flow.
2
Identify the two options of the dilemma
The argument begins by dividing the case: either the act of creation is preferable to God or it is not. Recognise these as the two horns.
This is a classic kalam argument form.
3
Understand the consequence of each option
If creation is preferable, then God is deficient and perfects Himself by another, which is impossible for the Necessary Existence. If it is not preferable, then there is no preference, so intention cannot arise.
Focus on why each leads to impossibility.
4
Examine the objection and rebuttal
An objector says God may prefer creation for the benefit of others, not Himself. Al-Razi responds: if benefit and non-benefit are equal, the same problem arises; if one is more fitting, then again deficiency.
Notice how the rebuttal closes the loophole.
5
Conclude the argument
Summarise why the argument refutes a temporal will: a ground for preference is either impossible (deficiency) or non-existent (no preference), so a temporal will cannot be grounded.
This connects to broader discussions of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إن كل من قصد إلى إحداث شيء ، فإما أن يكون ذلك الإحداث به أولى في ظنه واعتقاده من تركه ، وإما أن لا يكون . فإن كان الأول لزم كونه ناقصاً بذاته، مستكملاً بغيره، وذلك في حق واجب الوجود لذاته محال ، وإن كان الثاني ، فحينئذ لا يترجح قصد الإحداث على قصد الترك ، وذلك لأن قصد الإحداث ترجيح ، وحصول الرجحان حال حصول عدم الرجحان : محال . فإن قالوا : كونه ناقصاً لذاته ، مستكملاً بغيره : إنما يلزم لو رجح الإحداث على تركه ، لنفع يعود إليه . أما إذا رجحه لنفع يعود إلى الغير، لم يلزم المحال المذكور . قلنا : إيصال الخير والنفع إلى الغير، وعدم إيصاله إليه إن استويا بالنسبة إليه ، عاد المحال المذكور . وإن كان أحدهما أولى به ، فقد عاد ما ذكرنا . كونه ناقصاً بذاته ، مستكملا بغيره . وهو محال .
The third argument: Every person who intends to bring about something, either that bringing about is preferable to him in his opinion and belief than leaving it, or it is not. If it is the first, then it follows that he is deficient in himself, perfecting himself by another, and that is impossible regarding the Necessary Existence in Himself. If it is the second, then the intention to bring about is not preferable to the intention to leave, because the intention to bring about is a preference, and the occurrence of preference in a state of having no preference is impossible. If they say: His being deficient in himself, perfecting himself by another, only follows if he prefers bringing about over leaving for a benefit returning to him. But if he prefers it for a benefit returning to another, the mentioned impossibility does not follow. We say: Delivering good and benefit to another, and not delivering it to him, if they are equal in relation to him, then the mentioned impossibility returns. And if one of them is more fitting for him, then what we mentioned returns: his being deficient in himself, perfecting himself by another. And that is impossible.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against a temporal divine will. The argument examines the ground for preference (tarjīḥ). If God intends to create, either the act of creation is preferable to Him than not creating, or it is not. If it is preferable, then God must be deficient in Himself and in need of another to perfect Him, which is impossible for the Necessary Existence. If it is not preferable, then there is no preference for creation over non-creation, so the intention to create cannot occur, because intention is a preference and preference cannot arise from a state of no preference. The author then addresses an objection: perhaps God prefers creation not for His own benefit but for the benefit of the creature. The answer is that if delivering benefit and not delivering it are equal in relation to God, then the same impossibility returns (no preference). If one is more fitting for God, then again God would be deficient and perfecting Himself by another. Thus the argument refutes both possibilities and concludes that a temporal will is impossible.

Key Terms
قصد
إحداث
ترجيح
ناقص بذاته
مستكمل بغيره
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God can will something for the benefit of others without any implication of deficiency or dependence.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that even if the benefit is for others, either delivering and not delivering are equal (leading to no preference) or one is more fitting (leading to deficiency). Thus the objection fails.

Checkpoint
In the third argument, what is the consequence if God's creation is preferable to Him?
A) He would be perfecting others
B) He would be deficient and perfecting Himself by another
C) He would have no reason to create
D) He would be acting out of necessity

Key Takeaway
The third argument against a temporal will shows that any ground for preference either implies God is deficient or leads to no preference, thus a temporal will is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi argues that God cannot act for any benefit to Himself. When you worship, do you ever assume that your acts of obedience somehow benefit God or fill a need in Him? How does this passage expose that assumption as a form of deficiency in your understanding of God?
?
If God's will is not based on any preference that arises from His own need, then why should you turn to Him in supplication? Is your prayer based on a belief that God 'prefers' to answer you for His own sake, or for yours? How does this argument purify your intention in du'a?
?
The argument shows that a ground for preference implies deficiency. In your own life, are there reasons you give for why you obey God that actually suggest you see Him as needing your obedience? What would it mean for you to serve God purely out of recognizing His perfection, not out of any perceived benefit to Him?

The Fourth Argument: Specified Intention and Infinite Regress
Al-Razi divides the intention to create into specified and unspecified time, then refutes the specified case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi refutes the idea of a temporally specified divine intention by showing it leads to an infinite regress or an eternal world.

Core Idea
A specified time for creation either requires an eternal world or an infinite chain of times.

Al-Razi presents the fourth argument against a temporal divine will. He divides the intention to create into two types: specified to a time or not. In this passage, he refutes the first type, showing it leads to absurd consequences. The argument is rigorous in exposing the logical problems of positing a specific moment for divine action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then the English translation. Notice the two divisions introduced at the start.
The passage is incomplete; it cuts off at the beginning of the second division.
2
Identify the first division and its subcases
The first division is a specific time. Al-Razi splits this into two: the time was present in eternity, or it was not. Understand each subcase.
If the time was present in eternity, the world would be eternal. If not, the time itself is temporal.
3
Follow the regress argument
If the time is temporal, it needs a creator, and the same division applies to that creator's intention, leading to an infinite chain of times or an absolute intention.
The argument shows that either the times occur together (impossible) or successively (requiring infinite past events).
4
Connect to the broader debate
Understand that this argument targets those who claim God's will is temporal and specific. It forces them into a dilemma: either the world is eternal, or there is an infinite regress.
The second division (non-specific intention) is not fully discussed here, but the argument implies it would lead to perpetual existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : لو قصد إلى إحداث العالم، لكان إما أن يكون ذلك القصد مختصاً بوقت معين ، أو غير مختص بوقت معين . أما الأول : فكما إذا قصد إلى إحداث العالم في الوقت الفلاني وأما الثاني : فكما إذا قصد إلى إحداث العالم ، ولم يقصد إلى تخصيص إحداثه بوقت معين . وأما القسم الأول : فهو باطل . لأن ذلك الوقت المعين إما أن يقال : إنه كان حاضراً في الأزل ، أو ما كان حاضراً في الأزل ، فإن كان ذلك الوقت حاضراً في الأزل مع أن إله العالم قصد إحداث العالم في هذا الوقت ، فحينئذ يلزم حدوث العالم في الأزل . وعلى هذا التقدير ، يلزم كون العالم سرمدياً . وأما إن كان ذلك الوقت غير حاضر في الأزل ، كان ذلك الوقت حادثاً ، ولا بد له من محدث . وهو الله سبحانه . وحينئذ يعود التقسيم الأول وهو أنه إما أن يقال : إنه تعالى قصد إحداث ذلك الوقت في وقت خاص ، أو قصد إحداثه مطلقاً . فإن كان الأول ، افتقر ذلك الوقت إلى وقت آخر ، والكلام فيه كما في الأول ، ويلزم التسلسل . فإما أن تحصل تلك الأوقات الغير المتناهية معاً ، وهو محال بالبديهية. أو على التعاقب ، وهو يوجب القول بحدوث حوادث لا أول لها . وهو المطلوب . وهذا كله إذا قلنا : إنه تعالى قصد إحداث العالم في وقت معين . وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : إنه تعالى قصد إحداث العالم من غير أن يخصص إحداثه بوقت معين ، بل قصد على وجه يكون موجوداً دائماً أبداً . فهذا
The fourth argument: If He intended to bring about the world, then either that intention is specific to a particular time, or not specific to a particular time. As for the first: such as if He intended to bring about the world at a certain time. As for the second: such as if He intended to bring about the world and did not intend to specify its bringing about to a particular time. As for the first division: it is false. Because that particular time is either said to have been present in eternity, or not present in eternity. If that time was present in eternity while the God of the world intended to bring about the world at this time, then it follows that the world occurs in eternity. And on this assumption, it follows that the world is eternal. But if that time was not present in eternity, then that time is temporal, and it must have a temporal originator, and He is God the Glorified. Then the first division returns, namely that it is either said that He the Exalted intended to bring about that time at a specific time, or He intended to bring it about absolutely. If it is the first, then that time depends on another time, and the discussion about it is as in the first, and an infinite regress follows. Then either those infinite times occur together, which is self-evidently impossible, or they occur successively, which necessitates the statement that there are temporal events with no first. And that is what is sought. All this if we say that He the Exalted intended to bring about the world at a specific time. As for the second division: that it is said that He the Exalted intended to bring about the world without specifying its bringing about to a particular time, rather He intended it in such a way that it would exist perpetually forever. So this...
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument against a temporal divine will. The author divides the intention to create into two types: either it is specific to a particular time, or it is not specific. He first refutes the specific case. The specific time either existed in eternity or it did not. If it existed in eternity, then the world would occur in eternity, making the world eternal—which contradicts the assumption of temporal creation. If it did not exist in eternity, then that time itself is temporal and requires a creator, which is God. Then the same division recurs: either God intended that time at a specific time (leading to another time and thus an infinite regress) or He intended it absolutely. If the latter, then we have an infinite series of times that either exist all together (impossible) or successively (implying an infinite past, which the argument seems to accept as the desired conclusion for the opponent). The author then states that this entire discussion is for the case of a specific time. Then he starts to discuss the second division (non-specific intention) but the passage cuts off. The argument is incomplete in this window. The first full paragraph establishes that a specific intention leads to an infinite regress or an eternal world, both problematic for the opponent who posits a temporal will.

Correction: Al-Razi shows that a specified time leads either to the world being eternal or to an infinite regress of temporal specifications, both of which are problematic for a temporal creation view.

The Second Proof: Eternal Dominion and Perfection
Presents the second proof that God must have eternal dominion and action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second rational proof for God's eternal dominion and why a transition from deficiency to perfection is impossible for God.

Core Idea
God's perfection requires eternal action and dominion; change from deficiency is impossible.

In this passage, the author presents a second proof to establish that God must have always possessed effective disposal and dominion. The argument is based on the principle that God is eternally perfect and cannot move from deficiency to perfection. Let's work through the proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the logical flow and key terms.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the conditional structure.
2
Identify the premise and conditional
The premise states that one with effective disposal and great dominion is better than one without. The conditional asks: what if God lacked these in eternity?
This sets up the argument for God's perfection.
3
Understand the impossibility of transition
The conclusion says that if God lacked dominion and later acquired it, He would move from deficiency to perfection, which is impossible for an eternally perfect being.
This relies on the principle that God cannot change for the better.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Therefore, God must have always possessed these attributes. This proof establishes God's eternal perfection in action and dominion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إن من كان له التصرف النافذ ، والملك العظيم ، والرفع والخفض ، والإبرام والنقض ، أفضل ممن لا يكون له شيء من ذلك . فلو قلنا : إنه تعالى ما كان له في الأزل : ملك ولا ملكوت ولا تصرف ، ثم صار في لا يزال كذلك ، لزم أن يقال : إنه انتقل من النقص إلى الكمال ، وذلك : محال .
The second proof: that one who has effective disposal, great dominion, elevation and lowering, binding and loosening, is better than one who has none of that. So if we say that He, exalted, had in eternity no dominion, no sovereignty, and no disposal, then later became such, it would be necessary to say that He moved from deficiency to perfection, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage provides the second proof that God must be eternally perfect in action and dominion. It argues: a being with effective disposal (taṣarruf nāfidh), great dominion (mulk ʿaẓīm), ability to elevate and lower, and to bind and loose, is superior to one lacking these. To claim that God had no dominion, sovereignty (malakūt), or disposal in pre-eternity (al-azal) and later acquired them would imply that He passed from deficiency (naqṣ) to perfection (kamāl). Such transition is impossible for God, who is eternally perfect. Therefore, God must have always possessed these attributes. This proof relies on the principle that God's perfection precludes any change from deficiency to perfection.

Key Terms
التصرف النافذ (al-taṣarruf al-nāfidh)
الملك (al-mulk)
الملكوت (al-malakūt)
النقص (al-naqṣ)
الكمال (al-kamāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's eternal disposal means creation is eternal.
Correction: The proof focuses on God's capacity and perfection, not on the actual existence of creation. It establishes that God always had the power to act, not that creation is eternal.

Checkpoint
What would it imply if God did not have dominion and disposal in eternity?
A) That He was weak.
B) That He moved from deficiency to perfection, which is impossible.
C) That creation is eternal.
D) That He is not all-powerful.

Key Takeaway
God must have always possessed effective disposal and great dominion because a transition from deficiency to perfection is impossible for an eternally perfect being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever doubted God's complete control over your life, as if His disposal is limited? How does the certainty that He has always had perfect dominion change how you view your current difficulties?
?
If God cannot change from deficiency to perfection, what does that mean for your own expectations of His response to your prayers? Do you sometimes act as if God might 'improve' or become more involved?
?
This proof says that transition from deficiency to perfection is impossible for God. Does your worship reflect a reliance on an eternally perfect God, or do you secretly think He will become more pleased with you if you do more? How can you align your heart with His unchanging perfection?
