Refutation of the Second Argument on Divine Action
The author refutes the second argument by showing impossibility and ability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the argument that God's not creating in eternity implies deficiency.

Core Idea
Divine deficiency only follows from inability, not from the impossibility of an action.

This passage addresses a key objection: If God did not create in eternity, does that imply a deficiency? The author clarifies that impossibility itself does not entail deficiency, and then argues that origination in eternity is inherently impossible. He further shows that the argument is refuted by the existence of temporal events.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Note the main claim: God's not creating in eternity is not a deficiency.
2
Identify the first point: impossibility vs deficiency
The author states that if an action is impossible in itself, then not doing it does not entail deficiency. Find this distinction.
3
Understand why origination in eternity is impossible
Origination requires being preceded by non-existence, but eternity precludes being preceded. This makes the combination impossible.
This is the core logical argument.
4
Note the additional argument about ability
The author adds that deficiency would only follow if God lacked ability. But He was always able to create, so no deficiency.
5
See the refutation by daily events
The author points out that the occurrence of specific individuals and daily events refutes the argument, because they are created in time.
This shows the argument is self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يجوز ، لو كان الإحسان ممكناً في نفسه . أما إذا كان ممتنعاً في نفسه محالاً في ذاته ، لم يلزم منه النقص والإيجاد في الأزل : محال . لأنه عبارة عن الإخراج من العدم إلى الوجود ، وذلك لا ينقرر إلا فيها يكون مسبوقاً بالعدم . والأزل ينافي المسبوقية ، فكان الجمع بينهما محالاً . وهذا الكلام بعينه ، هو الجواب عن الحجة الثانية وأيضاً : فالنقص إنما يلزم لو لم يكن قادراً على تحصيل الملك والملكوت ، وهو تعالى في الأزل كان قادراً على تحصيله ، فلم يلزم من عدم الملك والملكوت نقصان . وأيضاً : فكل واحدة من هاتين الحجتين منقوض بحدوث الأشخاص المعينة ، وحدوث الحوادث اليومية وأجابوا عن السؤال الأول : بأن هذا الكلام إنما يتم إذا قلنا مسمّى الأزل مانع من الإيجاد والتكوين . وقد بينا بالدلائل القاطعة : أن ذلك باطل . على أنا نقول : إن هذا المانع كان زائلاً قبل أن خلق الله العالم بتقدير مائة سنة ، فكان الإلزام عائداً إليه وأما السؤال الثاني : فباطل أيضاً. لأن الأزل عندهم مانع من صحة الفعل ، وعلى هذا التقدير لم يكن الله متمكناً من تحصيل الملك والملكوت في الأزل ولا معنى للفقير الضعيف إلا ذلك . وهذا أيضاً يوجب العيب والضعف من وجه آخر ، وهو أن الامتناع من الإحسان في حق من كان قادراً على الإحسان ، ولا يضره ذلك الإحسان بوجه من الوجوه ، أقوى في العيب والنقصان منه في حق من لم يقدر عليه . فإذا قلتم : إله العالم كان قادراً على الإحسان ، وما كان ذلك الإحسان سبباً للضرر في حقه البتة ، فحينئذ يكون الإمتناع منه مذموماً في غاية النهاية . ويمكن تقرير هذا الكلام بوجه آخر ، فيقال : إنه تعالى إما أن يقال : إنه ما كان قادراً على الإيجاد في الأزل ، أو يقال : إنه كان قادراً عليه ، إلا أنه لو فعله ، لصار ذلك سبباً لعود نوع من أنواع الضرر إليه ، أو يقال : كان قادراً على الإيجاد في الأزل ، وكان منزها عن عود الضرر إليه . أما القسم الأول : فإنه يقتضي انتقال الخالق من العجز إلى القدرة وأما القسم الثاني : فإنه يقتضي انتقال الخالق من الضرر إلى الراحة . وأما القسم الثالث : فإنه يقتضي البخل والنقص . لأنه لما كان قادراً على هذا الإحسان، وما كان له فيه ضرر البتة ، فيكون الإمتناع عنه محض البخل والنقص وكل ذلك على الإله الحكيم محال . وأما السؤال الثالث : فجوابه : أن عند القوم : حدوث كل حادث متأخر ، مشروط بدخول الحادث المتقدم في الوجود ، بناء على القاعدة التي ذكرناها في سبب حدوث الحوادث اليومية . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، لم يلزم من عدم إيجاد هذا الشخص المعين قبل أن وجد : عيب ولا نقص
It is permissible, if beneficence were possible in itself. But if it is impossible in itself and inconceivable in its essence, then deficiency does not follow from it. And origination in eternity is impossible because it is the bringing out from non-existence into existence, and that is only established when preceded by non-existence. And eternity precludes being preceded, so combining them is impossible. This very statement is the answer to the second argument. Also: deficiency only follows if He were not able to acquire sovereignty and dominion, while He, exalted, was able in eternity to acquire it, so from the absence of sovereignty and dominion no deficiency follows. Also: each one of these two arguments is refuted by the occurrence of specific individuals and the occurrence of daily events. And they answered the first question: that this statement is only valid if we say that the meaning of eternity prevents origination and formation. And we have demonstrated with decisive proofs that that is false. Rather we say: this impediment was removed before God created the world by an estimation of a hundred years, so the objection returns against him. As for the second question: it is also false, because eternity according to them prevents the validity of action, and on this estimation God was not capable of acquiring sovereignty and dominion in eternity, and there is no meaning for the poor weak one except that. And this also entails defect and weakness from another aspect, namely that refraining from beneficence in the case of one who is capable of beneficence and that beneficence does not harm him in any way is stronger in defect and deficiency than in the case of one who is not capable of it. So when you say: the God of the world was capable of beneficence, and that beneficence was not a cause of harm to Him at all, then refraining from it is blameworthy to the utmost degree. And this statement can be presented in another way: it is said that He, exalted, either it is said that He was not capable of origination in eternity, or it is said that He was capable of it but if He did it, it would become a cause for a type of harm to return to Him, or it is said that He was capable of origination in eternity and was free from harm returning to Him. As for the first division: it entails the Creator's transition from incapability to capability. As for the second division: it entails the Creator's transition from harm to ease. As for the third division: it entails stinginess and deficiency, because when He was capable of this beneficence and there was no harm to Him in it at all, then refraining from it is pure stinginess and deficiency. And all of that is impossible for the wise God. As for the third question: its answer is that according to the people, the occurrence of every later event is conditioned by the entry of the earlier event into existence, based on the principle we mentioned concerning the cause of the occurrence of daily events. And if the matter is so, then from the non-creation of this specific individual before he existed, no defect or deficiency follows.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a second argument (possibly from opponents) that God's not creating in eternity implies deficiency. The author begins by clarifying that impossibility itself does not entail deficiency. He then argues that origination in eternity is impossible because it requires being preceded by non-existence, which eternity precludes. He then adds that deficiency only follows if God lacked ability, but He was able. He further objects that the argument is refuted by the occurrence of specific individuals and daily events. Then he answers three specific objections. The first objection claimed that eternity prevents action; the author responds that this impediment was removed before creation. The second objection claimed that impossibility of action in eternity implies weakness; the author argues that refraining when capable is worse. He presents three divisions: incapability, harm, or stinginess, and shows each is impossible for God. The third objection concerns the timing of events; the author responds that events are conditioned by previous events, so not creating earlier does not imply deficiency.

Key Terms

النقص
الإيجاد
الأزل
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's not creating in eternity implies He is deficient.
Correction: The passage shows that deficiency only follows if God lacked ability, but He is eternally able. Moreover, origination in eternity is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is origination in eternity impossible?
A) Because God lacks the power to create in eternity.
B) Because origination requires being preceded by non-existence, which eternity precludes.
C) Because God chooses not to create due to deficiency.
D) Because the world could not exist without a temporal beginning.

Key Takeaway
God's not creating in eternity does not imply deficiency because it is either impossible or God is fully able to create.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fail to do something that is genuinely impossible for you, do you consider yourself deficient? How does this lesson challenge the way you measure your own worth by what you can achieve?
?
If you ever felt that God's delay or non-action in your life was a sign of His inability, what does this passage reveal about that assumption? Are you demanding from Him something that is actually impossible?
?
The passage shows that God's power is not diminished by not doing the impossible. How does this reshape your trust in His wisdom when He does not act according to your expectations? Will you now accept that His refraining is not weakness but consistency with His nature?

Answers to Three Objections on Divine Action in Eternity
The author answers three objections about divine action in eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three objections regarding God's action in eternity and their refutations.

Core Idea
Refraining from beneficence when capable is more blameworthy than inability.

After refuting the second argument, the author addresses three specific objections. The first concerns the meaning of eternity as a barrier, the second argues that impossibility implies weakness, and the third deals with the timing of events. Each is answered decisively.

Guided Path
1
Read the answers to the three objections
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice that there are three distinct objections: first, second, and third.
2
Understand the first objection and its answer
The objection says eternity prevents action. The author responds that this impediment was removed before creation. Identify this.
3
Analyse the second objection and its detailed refutation
The objection claims impossibility implies weakness. The author refutes by showing that refraining when capable is worse. He gives three divisions: incapability, harm, or stinginess. Note each division and why it is impossible for God.
This is the most detailed part.
4
Examine the third objection and its answer
The objection concerns the timing of events. The author responds that events are conditioned by previous events, so not creating earlier does not imply defect.

Three Cases of Divine Abstention from Benevolence
The author divides the reason for divine abstention into three cases and refutes each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible scenarios for why God might abstain from benevolence and why each is impossible for the Wise God.

Core Idea
God's abstention from benevolence implies deficiency unless harm is involved.

This passage refutes the notion that God might legitimately withhold benevolence. The author presents three possible scenarios and demonstrates that each leads to an impossibility for God. Understanding this argument strengthens one's conviction in God's perfect generosity and wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the structure of the argument.
The passage uses a conditional structure and three divisions.
2
Identify the three divisions
Notice how the author divides the reasons for abstention into three cases: inability, ability with harm, and ability without harm. Identify the conclusion for each.
The third division leads to the charge of stinginess.
3
Understand the answer to the third question
The passage ends with an answer to a potential objection: why didn't God create a particular person earlier? Reflect on the principle invoked.
The answer relies on the Ash'ari principle of causal ordering.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإحسان ، وما كان ذلك الإحسان سبباً للضرر في حقه البتة ، فحينئذ يكون الإمتناع منه مذموماً في غاية النهاية . ويمكن تقرير هذا الكلام بوجه آخر ، فيقال : إنه تعالى إما أن يقال : إنه ما كان قادراً على الإيجاد في الأزل ، أو يقال : إنه كان قادراً عليه ، إلا أنه لو فعله ، لصار ذلك سبباً لعود نوع من أنواع الضرر إليه ، أو يقال : كان قادراً على الإيجاد في الأزل ، وكان منزها عن عود الضرر إليه . أما القسم الأول : فإنه يقتضي انتقال الخالق من العجز إلى القدرة . وأما القسم الثاني : فإنه يقتضي انتقال الخالق من الضرر إلى الراحة . وأما القسم الثالث : فإنه يقتضي البخل والنقص . لأنه لما كان قادراً على هذا الإحسان، وما كان له فيه ضرر البتة ، فيكون الإمتناع عنه محض البخل والنقص وكل ذلك على الإله الحكيم محال . وأما السؤال الثالث : فجوابه : أن عند القوم : حدوث كل حادث متأخر ، مشروط بدخول الحادث المتقدم في الوجود ، بناء على القاعدة التي ذكرناها في سبب حدوث الحوادث اليومية . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، لم يلزم من عدم إيجاد هذا الشخص المعين قبل أن وجد : عيب ولا نقص
Benevolence, and when that benevolence is not a cause of harm to Him at all, then abstaining from it is blameworthy in the utmost degree. And this statement can be presented in another way: it is said: He, exalted, either it is said that He was not able to bring into existence in pre-eternity, or it is said that He was able to do it, but if He did it, it would become a cause of some kind of harm returning to Him, or it is said that He was able to bring into existence in pre-eternity and was free from harm returning to Him. As for the first division: it entails the Creator moving from inability to ability. As for the second division: it entails the Creator moving from harm to comfort. As for the third division: it entails stinginess and deficiency. Because when He was able to do this benevolence, and there was no harm to Him in it at all, then abstaining from it is pure stinginess and deficiency. And all of that is impossible for the Wise God. And as for the third question: its answer is that according to the people, the coming-to-be of every later event is conditioned by the entry of the earlier event into existence, based on the principle we mentioned concerning the cause of the daily events. And when the matter is like this, then not bringing into existence this particular person before he came to be does not entail defect or deficiency.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the idea that God might refrain from performing acts of benevolence. The author first establishes that if an act of benevolence causes no harm to the agent, then refraining from it is blameworthy. He then reformulates this into three divisions concerning God's power and potential harm. (1) If God was unable to create in pre-eternity, that would imply a change from inability to ability, which is impossible for God. (2) If He was able but creating would cause harm to Him, then refraining would imply a change from harm to comfort, also impossible. (3) If He was able and free from harm, then refraining is pure stinginess and deficiency, which is impossible for the Wise God. The author then addresses a third question: why didn't God create a particular person earlier? The answer is that, according to the Ash'ari principle, later events require the prior existence of earlier events; thus, not creating earlier does not imply defect.

Key Terms
الإحسان (benevolence)
الضرر (harm)
البخل (stinginess)
النقص (deficiency)
الإله الحكيم (the Wise God)
الحدوث (coming-to-be)
الحادث المتأخر (later event)
الحادث المتقدم (earlier event)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's refraining from creation is simply a matter of will and does not imply any deficiency.
Correction: The passage argues that if God is able and no harm results, abstention is pure stinginess and deficiency, which is impossible for the Wise God.

Checkpoint
According to the third division in this passage, what does God's abstention from benevolence when He is able and free from harm entail?
A) A change from inability to ability
B) A change from harm to comfort
C) Stinginess and deficiency
D) Wisdom

Key Takeaway
God's perfect wisdom and generosity require that He always acts benevolently when no harm befalls Him; abstention otherwise would imply deficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Consider a good deed you have been putting off. What prior condition are you waiting for? Could it be that your delay is not wisdom but an excuse? How does God's perfect wisdom challenge your hesitation?
?
If God never withholds good without reason, what excuse do you have for your own stinginess? Will you let this truth change how you respond to those in need starting today?

Razi's Second Argument: Need Precedes Benefit
Razi's second argument against divine acts being for creature benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's logical argument that any supposed beneficence to creatures must be preceded by the creation of need, which is itself a harm, thus negating the claim of beneficence.

Core Idea
Giving a benefit requires a prior need — creating that need is not beneficent.

Razi presents a sharp logical critique of the claim that God's acts are motivated by beneficence to creatures. He argues that any benefit presupposes a need in the recipient, and creating that need is itself a harm. This argument challenges the very concept of divine beneficence as ordinarily understood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the structure of Razi's reasoning become clear.
2
Identify the two key premises
Find where Razi states that benefit requires desire/need, and where he says any supposed beneficence is preceded by the creation of need (which is harm).
Note the Arabic terms: ذوات (essence), إحسان (beneficence), ضرر (harm).
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand how the benefit is cancelled by the prior harm, so the act is not truly beneficence. Ask yourself: does this argument hold?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في إبطال هذا القسم: أن الذات ما لم تكن مشتهية للشيء ومحتاجاً إليه، لم يكن إيصال ذلك الشيء إليه انتفاعاً له، ولا إحساناً إليه. فكل ما يفرض إحساناً إليه، فإنه قد تقدمه خلق الحاجة فيه، وذلك ضرر. وحينئذ يتقابل قدر المنفعة الحاضرة بقدر المضرة السابقة، وذلك يخرجه عن كونه إحساناً. وقد تقدم في الكتاب الثالث تقرير هذا الكلام على سبيل الاستقصاء.
The second aspect in nullifying this division: that the essence (al-dhāt), as long as it is not desiring of a thing and in need of it, the conveyance of that thing to it is neither a benefit (intifāʿ) for it nor a beneficence (iḥsān) to it. So everything that is supposed to be a beneficence to it, then the creation of need in it has preceded it, and that is harm (ḍarar). And then the amount of present benefit is counterbalanced by the amount of previous harm, and that removes it from being a beneficence. And the confirmation of this statement has already preceded in the third book in detail.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (al-wajh al-thānī) against the claim that God's acts are motivated by beneficence to creatures. The argument is a logical analysis: if a being has no inherent desire or need for something, then giving it that thing cannot be considered a benefit or an act of kindness. The author reasons that any supposed beneficence must be preceded by the creation of a need in the recipient, which itself is a harm. Thus, the benefit is cancelled out by the prior harm, so the act cannot truly be called beneficence. The author notes that this point has already been thoroughly established in the third book of the work.

Key Terms
ذات
إحسان
انتفاع
ضرر
منفعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any good act is automatically beneficence.
Correction: But this argument shows that creating a need first negates the beneficence, because the need itself is a harm that offsets any supposed benefit.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, what must precede a supposed act of beneficence?
A) The recipient's merit
B) The creation of need in the recipient
C) The recipient's gratitude
D) The recipient's existence

Key Takeaway
The creation of need is itself a harm, so any supposed divine beneficence that requires prior need cannot be considered genuine beneficence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself expecting that God must give you good things because you are His servant? This argument says that any need in you was created by Him—and that very need is a harm. How does that shake your sense of entitlement? Will you repent from expecting ease and instead thank Him even for the trials of desire and need?
?
If you truly believe that God's acts are not for your benefit, then why do you still prioritize your own comfort in worship? What would it mean to pray, fast, or give charity not because you hope for reward but purely because He is worthy? Are you willing to examine your heart right now and ask: am I worshipping God, or am I worshipping the benefits I hope to get from Him?

The Regress Argument Against Creation for Beneficence
Razi argues against creation for beneficence via motive regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing a temporal motive to Allah leads to an infinite regress and undermines the claim that creation is for beneficence.

Core Idea
A motive that arises in time creates an endless chain of reasons.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a powerful logical argument against the idea that Allah created the world for the sake of beneficence (al-ihsan). By examining the nature of the motive (al-dāʿī), he shows that whether the motive is eternal or temporal, it leads to absurdity. The argument is concise but profound, exposing a key weakness in the theological claim that creation aims at benefit. This lesson will guide you through Razi's reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
Razi sets up a disjunction: the motive for creation exists from eternity or it does not. Read the passage to see how he frames this.
This is the starting point of the argument.
2
Analyze the first possibility (eternal motive)
If the motive existed in eternity, then creation would have to occur before the motive, which is impossible. Identify why this is contradictory.
The absurdity arises from mixing eternal and temporal.
3
Analyze the second possibility (temporal motive)
If the motive is temporally originated, we must explain why it originated at that specific time, leading to an infinite regress of motives. See how Razi likens it to the world's origination.
The regress is the key problem.
4
Note the dismissal of counterarguments
Razi mentions that some answers appeal to a specific benefit, but he says they have already been refuted. Understand that this passage is part of a larger discussion.
This shows Razi's confidence in his argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث: في بيان أنه لا يجوز أن يقال: إنه تعالى خلق الخلق للإحسان إلى المخلوقات. وذلك لأن هذا الداعي إما أن يقال: إنه كان حاصلاً في الأزل، أو ما كان حاصلاً. فإن كان الأول، لزم حصول الإيجاد قبل حصوله، وهو محال. والثاني يوجب كون تلك الداعية متجددة. والكلام في اختصاص حدوث تلك الداعية بذلك الوقت، كاختصاص حدوث العالم بذلك الوقت، ولزم الدور إلى ما لا نهاية له. وأما الأجوبة المذكورة عن هذا الكلام، وهو أنه تعالى إنما خصص إحداث العالم بذلك الوقت، لاشتمال ذلك الوقت على مصلحة معينة، فالكلام في إفساده قد سبق على الاستقصاء.
The third aspect: in clarifying that it is not permissible to say that God, exalted, created creation for beneficence to creatures. That is because this motive (al-dāʿī) must either be said to have been present in eternity (al-azal), or not present. If the first, then the occurrence of bringing into existence before its occurrence would be necessary, which is impossible. The second necessitates that motive to be temporally originated (mutajaddid). And the discussion about the specification of the temporal origination of that motive to that time is like the specification of the temporal origination of the world to that time, and an infinite regress (al-dawr ilā mā lā nihāya lahu) would be necessary. As for the mentioned answers to this statement, which is that God, exalted, only specified the bringing about of the world to that time because that time included a specific benefit, the discussion about refuting it has already preceded in detail.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against the idea that God creates for beneficence. The argument focuses on the motive (dāʿī) for creation. If the motive existed in eternity, then creation would have to occur before the motive (since the motive would be eternal but creation is temporal), which is absurd. If the motive originated in time, then we must explain why it originated at that particular time, which leads to an infinite regress of motives. After presenting this argument, the author mentions that some have responded by saying that God specified the time of creation because that time contained a specific benefit. However, he dismisses these responses by noting that their refutation has already been given in detail earlier.

Key Terms
داعي
أزل
متجدد
دور
مصلحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a temporal motive is unproblematic.
Correction: But it leads to an infinite regress of why that specific time was chosen.

Checkpoint
What does the third argument conclude about the motive for creation if it is said to be temporally originated?
A) It is impossible
B) It leads to an infinite regress
C) It is acceptable
D) It proves beneficence

Key Takeaway
Razi's regress argument shows that the motive for creation cannot be beneficence without leading to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that attributing a motive of beneficence to God leads to absurdity. Think about why you believe God created you. Have you ever, even secretly, thought that God created you because He needed to bring good into the world? How does this passage challenge that assumption? What does it mean for your relationship with your Creator if His actions are not driven by need or benefit?
?
The regress argument shows that every temporal reason requires a prior reason, leading to an infinite chain. In your own life, do you ever try to justify your actions to yourself by appealing to temporary motives that shift over time? Can you identify a time when you realized that a motive you had was actually leading to an endless cycle of justifications? How can this argument help you cut through that and seek a more ultimate purpose in your deeds?
?
Razi uses logic to show that attributing any need or benefit to God is ultimately impossible. But you still act as if your actions matter and have value. How can you maintain a sense of urgency and moral seriousness in your life while fully accepting that God is completely self-sufficient and not in need of your worship or obedience? What would it look like to act solely out of love and gratitude, with no expectation of return?

Fourth Argument: Equality of Benefit Delivery Refutes Beneficence
Razi argues that if delivering benefit is equal to withholding it, beneficence cannot be a motive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of Razi's fourth argument against beneficence as a divine motive, and how it refutes the claim that God creates for the sake of benefiting others.

Core Idea
Beneficence cannot be God's motive because it either makes no difference or implies divine need.

Razi presents a sharp disjunction to dismantle the idea that God creates for beneficence. The argument forces a choice: either delivering benefit is equal to withholding it, or not. Both options lead to conclusions that undermine beneficence as a motive. Let's examine the logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the clear disjunctive structure (إما...أو).
The argument is concise; read carefully.
2
Identify the two options
The disjunction offers two possibilities: either delivering benefit and not delivering it are equal (على السوية) for the agent, or they are not equal. Mark these two branches.
3
Understand the first consequence
If they are equal, then there is no preference for beneficence over withholding. Therefore, beneficence cannot be a motive (داعي) for creation. Ask yourself why equality removes motivation.
A motive requires a preference; equality eliminates preference.
4
Understand the second consequence
If they are not equal, then the agent must be perfecting himself (مستكملاً) through the act of creation, implying need or imperfection. Razi says this has already been refuted. Consider why this is problematic for God.
The second horn is already invalidated in prior arguments.
5
See the overall conclusion
Both horns lead to unacceptable conclusions: either beneficence is no motive, or it implies divine imperfection. Thus, beneficence cannot be the reason for creation. Reflect on the strength of this dialectical argument.
The argument refutes the opponent regardless of which horn they choose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع: وهو أن إيصال النفع إلى الغير وعدم إيصاله إليه، إما أن يكون بالنسبة إلى الفاعل على السوية، أو لا يكون الأمر كذلك. فإن كان الأول، امتنع أن يكون غرض الإحسان داعياً للفاعل إلى الإيجاد. وإن كان الثاني، لزم أن يكون الفاعل مستكملاً بذلك الإيجاد. وقد أبطلناه.
The fourth aspect: that the conveyance of benefit to another and the non-conveyance of it to him, either are equal (ʿalā al-sawiyya) with respect to the agent, or the matter is not so. If the first, then it is impossible for the purpose of beneficence to be a motive (dāʿī) for the agent to bring into existence. And if the second, then it would be necessary for the agent to be perfecting himself (mustakmilan) by that bringing into existence. And we have invalidated that.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument. It is a disjunction: either delivering benefit to another and not delivering it are equal for the agent, or they are not. If they are equal, then beneficence cannot be the motive for creation because there is no preference. If they are not equal, then the agent must be perfecting himself through the act of creation, which implies a need or imperfection in the agent, a position the author says has already been refuted. Thus, in either case, beneficence cannot be the motive.

Key Terms
إيصال النفع
سوية
داعي
مستكمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that beneficence always motivates action, especially for a generous being like God.
Correction: This argument shows that if delivering benefit is equal to withholding it, there is no motivation to choose one over the other. If it is not equal, then the agent acts for self-perfection, which is incompatible with God's self-sufficiency.

Checkpoint
What does the fourth argument conclude if delivering benefit and not delivering it are equal for the agent?
A) The agent would be perfecting himself
B) Beneficence cannot be the motive
C) The agent must create
D) The agent is imperfect

Key Takeaway
Razi's fourth argument shows that beneficence cannot be a motive for creation because either it provides no preference or it implies divine imperfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you have ever thought that God created you because He loves you and wants to benefit you, how does Razi's argument challenge that assumption? Do you hold that belief? If so, what does that say about your view of God's perfection?
?
Consider a good deed you do for someone else. Is your motive truly selfless, or do you gain something from it? How does this passage force you to examine your own motives? Commit to one action this week that you will do purely for God's sake, with no expectation of benefit to yourself.
?
Razi says that if benefit and non-benefit are equal for God, then beneficence cannot be a motive. Does this make you uncomfortable? Does it feel like God is distant? How can you reconcile God's goodness with this argument? Write a journal entry tonight addressing your feelings.

Fifth Argument: Creation Entails Need and Loss of Superior Independence
Razi contrasts independence from vs. by a thing, concluding creation forfeits the better.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi argues that creation necessitates need, and that independence from need is superior to independence by means of something, thus creation cannot be beneficence.

Core Idea
Independence from something is an attribute of Allah; independence by something is a human limitation.

This lesson presents the fifth of Razi’s arguments against the claim that creation is an act of beneficence. He draws a sharp contrast between two states: independence from a thing (istighnāʾ ʿan al-shayʾ) and independence by a thing (istighnāʾ bi-l-shayʾ). By comparing these to the attributes of God and creatures, he concludes that existence necessarily brings about a lesser state than non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi uses a very structured argument with clear steps.
2
Identify the two types of independence
Find where Razi contrasts 'independence from the thing' and 'independence by the thing'. Understand that the former is the state when no need exists, the latter when need is satisfied by an external object.
3
Examine the proof that one is better
The proof uses attributes: independence from things is an attribute of God, while independence by things is an attribute of creatures. Since God's attributes are more perfect, independence from is better.
This is a key theological move: linking metaphysical states to divine attributes.
4
Follow the conclusion
Trace how the argument concludes that existence (creation) results in the inferior state (independence by things), while non-existence yields the superior state. Therefore, creation forfeits the better and cannot be beneficence.
5
Reflect on the implication for beneficence
Ask yourself: if creation causes loss of a more perfect state, how can it be considered a gift? This challenges the idea that existence is inherently good.
This connects to the broader debate on the purpose of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس: هو أن الإيجاد يستلزم حصول الحاجة إلى المأكول والمشروب وغيرهما. فإذا أعطى الخالق تعالى المحتاج إليه، فقد حصل الاستغناء بالشيء. أما إذا لم يوجد، فقد حصل الاستغناء عن الشيء. لكن الاستغناء عن الشيء أفضل من الاستغناء بالشيء. وبدليل: أن الاستغناء عن الشيء من صفات الله تعالى والاستغناء بالشيء من صفات العباد، وما كان صفة الله تعالى فهو أكمل مما كان صفة للعباد. وإذا ثبت هذا، فالحاصل عند العدم هو الاستغناء عن الشيء. والحاصل عند الوجود هو الاستغناء بالشيء. وقد ثبت: أن الأول أفضل من الثاني. فيلزم: أن يقال: الإيجاد يوجب تفويت الأكمل ويوجب حصول الأخس والأرذل، وإذا كان كذلك، امتنع كون الإيجاد إحساناً إلى المخلوق.
The fifth aspect: that bringing into existence necessitates the occurrence of need for food, drink, and other things. So when the Creator, exalted, gives the needy what he needs, then independence by the thing (al-istighnāʾ bi-l-shayʾ) has occurred. But if it is not brought into existence, then independence from the thing (al-istighnāʾ ʿan al-shayʾ) has occurred. However, independence from a thing is better than independence by a thing. And by evidence: that independence from a thing is among the attributes of God, exalted, and independence by a thing is among the attributes of servants, and whatever is an attribute of God, exalted, is more perfect than what is an attribute of servants. And when this is established, what is obtained in the state of non-existence is independence from the thing. And what is obtained in the state of existence is independence by the thing. And it has been established that the first is better than the second. So it follows that it must be said: bringing into existence necessitates forfeiting the more perfect and necessitates obtaining the baser and more vile, and if that is so, it is impossible for bringing into existence to be beneficence to the creature.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument in six structured points. The author first states that creation entails the need for sustenance. Then he contrasts two states: when the Creator provides what is needed, the creature gains independence by means of that thing (istighnāʾ bi-shayʾ); but if the creature is not created, it has independence from that thing (istighnāʾ ʿan shayʾ). The argument then asserts that independence from something is superior to independence by it, using the proof that independence from things is an attribute of God while independence by things is an attribute of creatures, and God's attributes are more perfect. Consequently, non-existence yields the better state and existence yields the lesser. Therefore, creation results in making the creature lose the more perfect state and obtain an inferior one, so it cannot be an act of beneficence.

Key Terms
إيجاد
حاجة
استغناء عن الشيء
استغناء بالشيء
صفات الله
أكمل
أخس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that providing for needs is an act of beneficence, but this argument shows that the need itself is a deficiency, and independence from need is superior.
Correction: The argument demonstrates that creation introduces a state of dependence that is inherently inferior to the state of no need, so creation cannot be purely beneficial.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth argument, what is obtained in the state of non-existence?
A) Independence by the thing
B) Independence from the thing
C) Need for the thing
D) Beneficence from God

Key Takeaway
Creation necessarily brings about a state of need and dependence, which is inferior to the state of independence from all needs, so creation cannot be an act of beneficence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your life, what are the things you rely on to feel independent—your job, relationships, skills? This passage says that kind of independence is inferior. How does it feel to realize that your existence itself is a state of need? Will you let that humble you or drive you to seek true independence from everything but Allah?
?
Can you admit that you often act as if your needs are a blessing, because they allow you to receive and be grateful? But Razi says the need itself is a deficiency. Do you have the courage to wish for non-existence if it meant no need? Or do you cling to existence even as it reveals your dependence?
?
This argument undermines the idea that creation is a good deed to the creature. If you truly believed that, would it change how you view your own existence? Would you still thank Allah for creating you, or would you question His wisdom? Be honest with yourself: are you willing to trust that there is a deeper purpose beyond this logical puzzle?

God's Foreknowledge and the Claim of Beneficence
The author presents a logical problem: God's foreknowledge vs. the claim of beneficence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's eternal knowledge of suffering contradicts the claim that He created humans for their own good.

Core Idea
If God knew most would suffer, 'creation for good' becomes irrational.

The passage begins by stating a stark reality: the majority of people—disbelievers and sinners—will experience great punishment. Since God knew this from eternity, how can we rationally claim that He created them for beneficence? This lesson examines the logical problem that the author presents.

Guided Path
1
Read the first paragraph
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the rhetorical question at the end.
This paragraph is a complete unit presenting the core problem.
2
Identify the key facts
The author asserts two facts: the majority suffer punishment, and God knew this eternally. How do these relate?
3
Understand the contradiction
The author asks: how can it be said that God created for good if He knew most would end in misery? Grasp why this is a logical tension.
This sets up the problem that the rest of the lesson addresses.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why foreknowledge matters. If someone knows an action will cause harm, can we say they intended good?
This prepares you for the objection and rebuttal in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالأكثرون وهم الكفار والفساق من أهل الملة يقعون في العذاب العظيم. وإله العالم كان عالماً في الأزل بكل هذه الأحوال، فكيف يعقل أن يقال: إنه تعالى خلقهم للإحسان، مع أنه كان يعلم أن الأكثرين منهم، يكونون في الدنيا في الألم والفقر، والعمى. وفي الآخرة في الدرك الأسفل من النار؟

فإن قالوا: إنه تعالى مكن الكل من وجدان تلك السعادات العظيمة، وأما حصول الحرمان، فإنما كان لأجل أنهم قصروا في حق أنفسهم قلنا: لما كان إله العالم عالماً يقيناً: أنهم لا يستفيدون من الوجود والتكلف، إلا البلاء العظيم، والشقاء التام. امتنع أن يقال: إنه تعالى أراد بهم الخير والنفع، والصلاح.
The majority, and they are the disbelievers and the sinners from the people of the religious community, fall into great punishment. And the God of the world was eternally knowing all these states. So how can it be rationally said that He, exalted, created them for beneficence, while He knew that the majority of them would be in this world in pain, poverty, and blindness, and in the hereafter in the lowest depth of the Fire?

If they say: He, exalted, enabled all to attain those great felicities, and as for the occurrence of deprivation, it was only because they fell short in their own right. We say: Since the God of the world knew with certainty that they would not benefit from existence and exertion except for great affliction and complete misery, it is impossible to say that He, exalted, intended good, benefit, and rectitude for them.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental objection against the claim that God created creatures for their own benefit. The author begins by pointing out that the majority of people—disbelievers and sinners—will experience severe punishment. God knew this from eternity. So how can it be said that He created them for beneficence? The objection is reinforced by the fact that God knew most would suffer in this world and the next. Then the author anticipates a response: the Mu'tazila might argue that God enabled everyone to attain happiness, and deprivation is due to their own failure. The author replies that since God knew with certainty that they would only get great affliction from existence and effort, it is impossible that He intended good for them. The structure is: problem, objection, response.

Key Terms
الأكثرون
الإحسان
العلم الأزلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's foreknowledge does not affect His intention; He can still intend good even if He knows evil will occur.
Correction: The author argues that foreknowledge of overwhelming suffering makes the claim of intending good logically untenable, because intention is tied to expected outcomes.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it irrational to claim that God created humans for beneficence?
A) Because God knows everything.
B) Because most people are disbelievers and sinners who will suffer.
C) Because God has no purpose.
D) Because people can choose.

Key Takeaway
God's eternal knowledge that most creatures will suffer makes the claim of creation for beneficence logically problematic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the passage again: God knew from eternity that most people would suffer greatly. Does this ever make you doubt His goodness? When you face pain or see others suffering, do you question whether your creation was truly for your benefit? This week, when hardship comes, consciously affirm that God's purposes are beyond your understanding, and ask Him to increase your trust in His wisdom.
?
If God created you knowing every sin you would commit and every trial you would face, does that change how you view your own existence? Do you live as if you were created for a purpose greater than your own comfort? Right now, silently recommit to seeking God's pleasure, regardless of your circumstances.
?
The passage presents a logical problem: how can creation be for beneficence if most suffer? But you are not 'most'—you are a Muslim who has been guided. Does this realization fill you with gratitude or complacency? How does your knowledge of God's foreknowledge affect your daily efforts to avoid being among those who fall into great punishment?

Responding to the Objection: Intending Good Despite Foreknowledge
The author presents an objection and rebuts it using God's certain knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that God enabled all to choose happiness fails to resolve the contradiction.

Core Idea
Certain knowledge of misery negates the intention of good.

The author now considers a potential objection: perhaps God enabled everyone to attain happiness, and deprivation is due to their own failure. In reply, the author argues that since God knew with certainty that they would only suffer, He could not have intended good for them. This lesson examines that rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the second paragraph
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the structure: 'If they say... We say...'
This is a classic objection-answer format.
2
Identify the objection
The opponents claim that God enabled everyone and deprivation is self-caused. Understand this argument.
3
Follow the rebuttal
The author counters: since God knew they would only get affliction, He cannot have intended good. Trace this logic.
The key is the certainty of the outcome.
4
Appreciate the force
Notice that even if humans are responsible, God's foreknowledge of the result makes the intention of good impossible.
This refutes the idea that free will absolves God's intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالأكثرون وهم الكفار والفساق من أهل الملة يقعون في العذاب العظيم. وإله العالم كان عالماً في الأزل بكل هذه الأحوال، فكيف يعقل أن يقال: إنه تعالى خلقهم للإحسان، مع أنه كان يعلم أن الأكثرين منهم، يكونون في الدنيا في الألم والفقر، والعمى. وفي الآخرة في الدرك الأسفل من النار؟

فإن قالوا: إنه تعالى مكن الكل من وجدان تلك السعادات العظيمة، وأما حصول الحرمان، فإنما كان لأجل أنهم قصروا في حق أنفسهم قلنا: لما كان إله العالم عالماً يقيناً: أنهم لا يستفيدون من الوجود والتكلف، إلا البلاء العظيم، والشقاء التام. امتنع أن يقال: إنه تعالى أراد بهم الخير والنفع، والصلاح.
The majority, and they are the disbelievers and the sinners from the people of the religious community, fall into great punishment. And the God of the world was eternally knowing all these states. So how can it be rationally said that He, exalted, created them for beneficence, while He knew that the majority of them would be in this world in pain, poverty, and blindness, and in the hereafter in the lowest depth of the Fire?

If they say: He, exalted, enabled all to attain those great felicities, and as for the occurrence of deprivation, it was only because they fell short in their own right. We say: Since the God of the world knew with certainty that they would not benefit from existence and exertion except for great affliction and complete misery, it is impossible to say that He, exalted, intended good, benefit, and rectitude for them.

This passage presents a fundamental objection against the claim that God created creatures for their own benefit. The author begins by pointing out that the majority of people—disbelievers and sinners—will experience severe punishment. God knew this from eternity. So how can it be said that He created them for beneficence? The objection is reinforced by the fact that God knew most would suffer in this world and the next. Then the author anticipates a response: the Mu'tazila might argue that God enabled everyone to attain happiness, and deprivation is due to their own failure. The author replies that since God knew with certainty that they would only get great affliction from existence and effort, it is impossible that He intended good for them. The structure is: problem, objection, response.

Correction: The author argues that certain knowledge of a purely harmful outcome contradicts the claim of intending good, regardless of human responsibility.

God's certain knowledge that creatures will suffer renders the claim of intending good invalid, even if humans are responsible for their choices.

First Proof: Creatures Cannot Act Against God's Knowledge
First proof: impossibility of acting against divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that creatures cannot act contrary to what God knows, which shows that God did not create for their benefit.

Core Idea
What God knows must happen; creatures cannot disprove His knowledge.

The author begins with a simple yet powerful argument: if creatures could act against God's knowledge, they could make His knowledge ignorance, which is absurd. This proof directly counters the Mu'tazilite claim that God creates for our benefit, because His foreknowledge of our actions limits our ability to do otherwise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic sentence and its English translation carefully. Notice the conditional structure: 'if they could act contrary to God's knowledge, then...'
This is a short but dense argument.
2
Identify the reductio ad absurdum
The author shows that the opposite assumption leads to an impossibility: making God's knowledge ignorance. This proves the original statement.
This is a standard kalam technique.
3
Connect to the overall claim
This proof supports the impossibility of God creating for our benefit because if we could act against His knowledge, He could not have intended a specific benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويدل عليه وجوه

الأول: إنهم لا يقدرون على خلاف معلوم الله، وإلا لزم أن يقال: إنهم قادرون على أن يجعلوا علم الله جهلاً.

الثاني: إنا نعلم من بنى رباطاً في مفازة عظيمة، وعلم يقيناً أنه بنى ذلك الرباط، صار ذلك الرباط مأوى اللصوص والقتالين وقطاع الطريق. ثم إن الذي بنى ذلك الرباط مع جزمه بأن الأمر كذلك، يقول: كان غرضي منه: دفع الشر، وأن يصير ذلك الموضع مجمعاً للأولياء والأتقياء، ولأقوام يسعون في أمن الطرقات، وإزالة الآفات، فإن العقلاء يقولون، لما كنت عالماً بأن بناءه يوجب مزيد الآفات، فإنك في دعواك: أنك ما أردت به إلا الخير: كذاب مزور. وهذا الباب فيه إطناب، وقد سبق ذكره في الكلام على الحسن والقبح مع المعتزلة.

والوجه الثالث: في دفع هذا السؤال: أن القدرة الصالحة للكفران، كانت متعينة للكفر، فحينئذ يكون خلق تلك القدرة في الكافر، إلجاء له إلى الكفر، وإن كانت صالحة للإيمان والكفر معاً، فرجحان أحد الجانبين على الآخر، إن لم يتوقف على المرجح، لزم حصول الرجحان لا المرجح، وهو محال. وإن توقف على المرجح، فذلك المرجح إن كان من العبد، عاد التقسيم فيه. ولزم التسلسل. وإن كان من الله، فحينئذ يكون مجموع القدرة مع ذلك المرجح موجباً للكفر، فيعود ما ذكرنا من أن الله تعالى ألجأ الكافر، إلى الكفر. وذلك يبطل قولهم: أنه تعالى ما أراد به إلا الخير والصلاح. فيثبت بهذه الوجوه: أنه يمتنع أن يقال: إنه تعالى خلق الخلق لغرض نفسه، أو يقال: إنه خلقهم لغرض يعود إليهم.
There are proofs for this:

The first: They are not able to act contrary to what God knows, otherwise it would be necessary to say that they are able to make God's knowledge ignorance.

The second: We know that someone who builds a fortress in a vast desert, and knows with certainty that he built that fortress, [and] that fortress becomes a haven for thieves, killers, and highway robbers. Then the one who built that fortress, while being certain that the matter is so, says: 'My purpose from it was to repel evil, and that that place become a gathering place for the saints and the pious, and for people who strive for the safety of roads and the removal of harms.' Then the rational people say: Since you were certain that its construction would cause increased harms, you are, in your claim that you only intended good by it, a liar and a forger. And this topic is lengthy, and it has already been mentioned in the discussion on good and evil with the Mu'tazila.

The third proof, in repelling this question: That the power which is suitable for disbelief was determined for disbelief; then the creation of that power in the disbeliever is compulsion to disbelief. And if it is suitable for both belief and disbelief together, then the preponderance of one side over the other, if it does not depend on a preponderating factor, then the preponderance would occur without a preponderating factor, which is impossible. And if it depends on a preponderating factor, then that preponderating factor: if it is from the slave, the same division applies to it, and infinite regress follows. And if it is from God, then the sum of the power together with that preponderating factor causes disbelief, so it returns to what we mentioned: that God, exalted, compelled the disbeliever to disbelief. And that invalidates their statement that He, exalted, only intended good and rectitude for them. So it is established by these proofs that it is impossible to say that He, exalted, created creatures for His own purpose, or that He created them for a purpose that returns to them.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage provides three proofs to support the impossibility of God creating creatures for their own benefit. The first proof: creatures cannot act contrary to God's knowledge; otherwise they could make God's knowledge ignorance. The second proof uses an analogy: a person who builds a fortress in a wasteland, knowing it will become a haven for criminals, cannot honestly claim his purpose was good. Similarly, God's foreknowledge of evil outcomes contradicts the claim that He intended good. The third proof is more technical: it concerns the nature of the power (qudra) given to the disbeliever. If the power is specifically for disbelief, then its creation compels disbelief. If it is for both, then a preponderating factor (murajjih) is needed; if that factor comes from the slave, there is infinite regress; if from God, then God compels disbelief. Thus God cannot have intended good. The conclusion: it is impossible that God created for His own purpose or for a purpose returning to creatures.

Key Terms
القدرة
علم الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Creatures can freely choose to act in any way, independent of God's knowledge.
Correction: This proof shows that creatures cannot act contrary to God's knowledge; otherwise, they could make His knowledge ignorance, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
What does the first proof conclude?
A) Creatures can act contrary to God's knowledge.
B) If creatures could act against God's knowledge, they could make His knowledge ignorance.
C) God created creatures for their benefit.
D) The Mu'tazilites are correct.

Key Takeaway
Creatures cannot act contrary to God's perfect and immutable knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God knows everything that will happen, how does that affect the way you plan your life? Are there areas where you act as if you can surprise God? What would need to change in your daily decisions to live consistently with this truth?
?
When you sin, do you ever think 'God didn't know I would do that'? Reflect on how this argument strips away that excuse. How does it change your sense of accountability?
?
Since God's knowledge is perfect and unchangeable, your entire future is known to Him. Does that fill you with fear or hope? Why? What does that reveal about your trust in His plan?

Second Proof: The Fortress Analogy
Second proof: analogical argument against good intentions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the analogical argument that foreknowledge of evil outcomes contradicts any claim of intending good.

Core Idea
If you build a fortress knowing it will become a den of thieves, you cannot claim you built it for good.

The author uses a vivid analogy: a person who builds a fortress in a desert, knowing it will become a haven for criminals, cannot honestly claim his purpose was good. Similarly, God's foreknowledge of the evil resulting from creation contradicts the claim that He created for our benefit. This proof appeals to rational intuition.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy
Read the passage describing the builder and the fortress that becomes a refuge for criminals.
Notice the certainty (علم يقيناً) of the builder.
2
Understand the contradiction
The builder claims his purpose was good despite knowing the outcome. Rational people call him a liar. This illustrates that foreknowledge of evil negates the claim of good intention.
This applies to God: He knows the future evils, so He cannot be said to intend good.
3
Connect to the Mu'tazila context
The author mentions this proof was previously discussed in the context of good and evil with the Mu'tazila, who argued that God must do what is best for creation.

Third Proof: Power and Preponderating Factor
Third proof: analysis of power and preponderater.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the intricate argument about the nature of created power (qudra) and how it leads to either compulsion or infinite regress, refuting the claim that God creates for our benefit.

Core Idea
Either power compels disbelief or a preponderater leads to the same conclusion.

The third proof is the most technical. It examines the power (qudra) given to the disbeliever. If the power is specifically for disbelief, its creation compels the disbeliever. If it is suitable for both belief and disbelief, a preponderating factor (murajjih) is needed. This factor either comes from the slave (leading to infinite regress) or from God (compulsion). In both cases, God cannot be said to have intended good for the creature.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first branch
If the power (qudra) is determined for disbelief, then creating it compels the disbeliever to disbelieve. That contradicts the claim that God intended good.
This is a simple case.
2
Understand the second branch
If the power is neutral (suitable for both), then a preponderater is needed to tip the balance. If the preponderater is from the slave, infinite regress follows because the same question repeats for each power. If from God, then God compels disbelief again.
This refutes the Mu'tazilite assertion of free will.
3
Draw the conclusion
Both branches lead to the impossibility of God intending good for His creatures. Thus, the claim that God created for our benefit is false.
The concluding sentence summarizes all proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الثاني: إنا نعلم من بنى رباطاً في مفازة عظيمة، وعلم يقيناً أنه بنى ذلك الرباط، صار ذلك الرباط مأوى اللصوص والقتالين وقطاع الطريق. ثم إن الذي بنى ذلك الرباط مع جزمه بأن الأمر كذلك، يقول: كان غرضي منه: دفع الشر، وأن يصير ذلك الموضع مجمعاً للأولياء والأتقياء، ولأقوام يسعون في أمن الطرقات، وإزالة الآفات، فإن العقلاء يقولون، لما كنت عالماً بأن بناءه يوجب مزيد الآفات، فإنك في دعواك: أنك ما أردت به إلا الخير: كذاب مزور. وهذا الباب فيه إطناب، وقد سبق ذكره في الكلام على الحسن والقبح مع المعتزلة.

There are proofs for this:

The first: They are not able to act contrary to what God knows, otherwise it would be necessary to say that they are able to make God's knowledge ignorance.

The second: We know that someone who builds a fortress in a vast desert, and knows with certainty that he built that fortress, [and] that fortress becomes a haven for thieves, killers, and highway robbers. Then the one who built that fortress, while being certain that the matter is so, says: 'My purpose from it was to repel evil, and that that place become a gathering place for the saints and the pious, and for people who strive for the safety of roads and the removal of harms.' Then the rational people say: Since you were certain that its construction would cause increased harms, you are, in your claim that you only intended good by it, a liar and a forger. And this topic is lengthy, and it has already been mentioned in the discussion on good and evil with the Mu'tazila.

The third proof, in repelling this question: That the power which is suitable for disbelief was determined for disbelief; then the creation of that power in the disbeliever is compulsion to disbelief. And if it is suitable for both belief and disbelief together, then the preponderance of one side over the other, if it does not depend on a preponderating factor, then the preponderance would occur without a preponderating factor, which is impossible. And if it depends on a preponderating factor, then that preponderating factor: if it is from the slave, the same division applies to it, and infinite regress follows. And if it is from God, then the sum of the power together with that preponderating factor causes disbelief, so it returns to what we mentioned: that God, exalted, compelled the disbeliever to disbelief. And that invalidates their statement that He, exalted, only intended good and rectitude for them. So it is established by these proofs that it is impossible to say that He, exalted, created creatures for His own purpose, or that He created them for a purpose that returns to them.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage provides three proofs to support the impossibility of God creating creatures for their own benefit. The first proof: creatures cannot act contrary to God's knowledge; otherwise they could make God's knowledge ignorance. The second proof uses an analogy: a person who builds a fortress in a wasteland, knowing it will become a haven for criminals, cannot honestly claim his purpose was good. Similarly, God's foreknowledge of evil outcomes contradicts the claim that He intended good. The third proof is more technical: it concerns the nature of the power (qudra) given to the disbeliever. If the power is specifically for disbelief, then its creation compels disbelief. If it is for both, then a preponderating factor (murajjih) is needed; if that factor comes from the slave, there is infinite regress; if from God, then God compels disbelief. Thus God cannot have intended good. The conclusion: it is impossible that God created for His own purpose or for a purpose returning to creatures.

Key Terms
القدرة
المرجح
التسلسل
الإلجاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The slave has complete free will to choose belief or disbelief without any preponderating factor.
Correction: The author argues that without a preponderater, preponderance is impossible; if the preponderater is from the slave, infinite regress occurs; if from God, it is compulsion. Thus, the slave's power alone is insufficient for free choice.

Checkpoint
What happens if the preponderating factor comes from the slave?
A) It leads to compulsion from God.
B) It leads to infinite regress.
C) It leads to belief.
D) It leads to no preponderance.

Key Takeaway
The analysis of power and preponderater shows that any attempt to ascribe good intention to God for creation ends in either compulsion or infinite regress, thus proving the impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that God must act in a way that you consider good? How does this proof challenge that assumption, and what does it demand from you in terms of submission?
?
If God's actions are not for your benefit, how does that affect your trust in Him? Are you willing to accept that His will may not align with your understanding of good, even when it seems harsh?
?
Reflect on a time when you tried to justify your own actions by claiming good intentions. How does this proof about God's lack of purpose in creation expose the danger of imposing purposes on God—and in turn, how should you view your own accountability before Him?

Refuting Creation Without Purpose
Author refutes the idea that God created without any purpose or wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing purposelessness to God's creation contradicts His attributes of wisdom and mercy, and leads to absurd consequences that undermine divine justice and revelation.

Core Idea
A purposeless creation would make God a fool, not the Wise Merciful.

The author now tackles the claim that God created without any purpose. He presents two concise but powerful arguments: first, purposelessness is foolishness, unbecoming of God; second, if allowed, it would make any act permissible, destroying the very basis of religion and morality. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two proofs
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the author enumerates two distinct reasons (لوجوه: الأول, الثاني) to refute purposeless creation.
2
Understand the first proof: why is purposelessness foolishness?
Examine how the author equates an act without wisdom (حكمة) with foolishness (سفاهة). Ask yourself why foolishness is incompatible with God being الحكيم الرحيم (the Wise, the Merciful).
This proof relies on the inherent perfection of God's attributes.
3
Understand the second proof: what absurd consequences follow?
Trace the argument: if purposelessness were possible, then nothing would be evil from God. Explore the four consequences mentioned: punishing prophets, rewarding atheists, breaking trust in promises and threats, and blurring obedience and disobedience.
These consequences directly contradict divine justice and revelation.
4
Reflect on the connection between divine wisdom and moral accountability
Consider how the author shows that denying purpose in creation leads to the collapse of moral and religious distinctions. This highlights why understanding God's wisdom is central to Islamic theology.
The passage ends by stating God is the originator, implying the argument continues to affirm purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثالث، وهو أن يقال: إنه تعالى خلقهم لا لغرض وحكمة أصلا. فنقول: هذا أيضاً: باطل لوجوه:

الأول: إن الفعل الخالي عن الحكمة: سفاهة، وهي غير لائقة بالحكيم الرحيم.

والثاني: إنه لم يمتنع ذلك، فحينئذ لا يقبح من الله تعالى شيء أصلاً فوجب أن يحسن منه أن يعاقب الأنبياء والرسل بأعظم أنواع العقاب، وأن يحسن منه إدخال الدهرية في أعلى درجات الجنة، وأن لا يحصل الوثوق بوعده ووعيده، وأن لا تتميز طاعته عن معصيته. وكل ذلك يبطل الحكمة الإلهية. فيثبت: أنه تعالى لو كان محدثاً للعالم، بعد أن لم يكن
As for the third division, which is that one says: He, exalted, created them without any purpose or wisdom. We say: This is also false for reasons:

The first: An act devoid of wisdom is foolishness, and it is unbecoming of the Wise, the Merciful.

The second: If that is not impossible, then nothing would be evil from God, exalted, at all. Then it would be permissible for Him to punish the prophets and messengers with the greatest types of punishment, and it would be permissible for Him to admit the atheists to the highest ranks of Paradise, and there would be no trust in His promise and threat, and His obedience would not be distinguished from His disobedience. And all of that invalidates divine wisdom. So it is established that He, exalted, if He were the originator of the world after it was not...
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage refutes the third possible position: that God created without any purpose or wisdom. The author gives two proofs. First, an act devoid of wisdom is foolishness, which is beneath the Wise and Merciful. Second, if such were permissible, then no act of God would be evil, leading to absurdities: He could punish the prophets and reward atheists, promises and threats become unreliable, and obedience and disobedience become indistinguishable. This would undermine divine wisdom. The passage ends by stating that God is the originator of the world, but the argument is cut off; presumably the author goes on to prove that God created with wisdom.

Misconception: God's actions are beyond any judgment, so He could create without purpose.
Correction: Attributing purposelessness to God would make His actions foolish and would destroy the basis for distinguishing good from evil.

Checkpoint

A) People would be free from religious obligations.
B) Punishing prophets and rewarding atheists would be permissible.
C) God would be ignorant of human actions.
D) Religion would become easier to follow.

Key Takeaway
God's creation is necessarily purposeful; otherwise His wisdom and mercy would be negated, and moral accountability would collapse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Think about your own heart: do you truly believe that God's commands and prohibitions are backed by real consequences? Or do you sometimes act as if obedience and disobedience don't matter? How can you strengthen your conviction that every action has a purpose and will be accounted for?
?

What is one change you need to make in your daily life to live as if God's wisdom is behind everything?

Razi's Third Refutation: Power and Coercion
Razi refutes the claim that God's power coerces disbelievers by considering two cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's logical argument showing that God's power over disbelief cannot be considered non-coercive without leading to contradictions in the Mu'tazilite position.

Core Idea
Either power determines action or requires a preponderating factor—both lead to God coercing disbelief.

Razi presents the third and final aspect of his refutation against the claim that God's power over disbelief forces the disbeliever into disbelief. He examines two possibilities: either the power is determined for disbelief, or it is balanced. Both lead to the conclusion that if God creates the power and the preponderating factor, He is the ultimate cause of disbelief, invalidating the claim that He only intends good.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possibilities
Read the passage and note the two cases Razi considers regarding the power (qudra) for disbelief: either it is determined (muta'ayyina) for disbelief, or it is balanced (saliha li'l-kufr wa'l-iman ma'an).
2
Trace the consequence of each case
For the first case, the creation of determined power is coercion (ilja'). For the second case, preponderance (rujhan) requires a preponderating factor (murajjih). Follow the subcases.
Pay attention to the logical necessity of a preponderating factor.
3
Analyze the preponderating factor subcases
If the preponderating factor is from the servant, infinite regress (tasalsul) follows. If from God, then God coerces the disbeliever. Both invalidate the Mu'tazilite claim.
4
Understand the final conclusion
Razi concludes that it is impossible to say God created for any purpose—whether for Himself or for the creatures. This disproves the original objection.
This is a strong conclusion against teleological explanations of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثالث : في دفع [ هذا ] السؤال : أن القدرة الصالحة للكفران ، كانت متعينة للكفر ، فحينئذ يكون خلق تلك القدرة في الكافر ، إلجاء له إلى الكفر ، وإن كانت صالحة للإيمان والكفر معاً ، فرجحان أحد الجانبين على الآخر ، إن لم يتوقف على المرجح ، لزم حصول الرجحان لا المرجح ، وهو محال . وإن توقف على المرجح ، فذلك المرجح إن كان من العبد ، عاد التقسيم فيه . ولزم التسلسل . وإن كان من الله ، فحينئذ يكون مجموع القدرة مع ذلك المرجح موجباً للكفر ، فيعود ما ذكرنا من أن الله تعالى ألجأ الكافر ، إلى الكفر . وذلك يبطل قولهم : أنه تعالى ما أراد به إلا الخير والصلاح . فيثبت بهذه الوجوه : أنه يمتنع أن يقال : إنه تعالى خلق الخلق لغرض نفسه ، أو يقال : إنه خلقهم لغرض يعود إليهم.
And the third aspect: in repelling this question: that the power (qudra) fit for disbelief was determined for disbelief, then at that time the creation of that power in the disbeliever would be coercion (ilja') of him to disbelief. And if it were fit for both belief and disbelief equally, then the preponderance (rujhan) of one side over the other, if it does not depend on a preponderating factor (murajjih), would necessitate the occurrence of preponderance without a preponderating factor, which is impossible. And if it depends on a preponderating factor, then that preponderating factor, if it were from the servant, the division (taqsim) would recur in it, and infinite regress (tasalsul) would follow. And if it were from God, then the sum of the power together with that preponderating factor would necessitate disbelief, and what we mentioned returns: that God, exalted, coerced the disbeliever to disbelief. And that invalidates their saying that He, exalted, only desired good and righteousness for them. So it is established by these aspects: that it is impossible to say that He, exalted, created creation for His own purpose, or to say that He created them for a purpose returning to them.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is the third aspect of a refutation against a claim about divine power and human action. The question is: if God creates in the disbeliever a power that is capable of disbelief, does that force the disbeliever into disbelief? Razi considers two possibilities. First, if the power is determined (muta'ayyina) for disbelief, then creating it in the disbeliever is coercion (ilja'). Second, if the power is balanced, capable of both belief and disbelief, then one side must preponderate. If preponderance occurs without a preponderating factor, that is impossible. If it requires a preponderating factor, that factor is either from the servant (leading to infinite regress) or from God (making God the coercer). Either way, the claim that God only intends good for them is invalidated. The conclusion: it is impossible that God created for a purpose of His own or for a purpose returning to the creatures.

Key Terms
القدرة (qudra)
الكفر (kufr)
الإيمان (iman)
المرجح (murajjih)
التسلسل (tasalsul)
الإلجاء (ilja')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's creation of the power for disbelief necessarily forces the disbeliever into disbelief, making God the direct author of sin without considering the role of the agent's choice and preponderating factors.
Correction: Razi shows that the very structure of divine power and preponderance leads to God being the ultimate cause of disbelief, regardless of whether the power is determined or balanced, contradicting the claim that God only intends good.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what happens if the power for disbelief is balanced and the preponderating factor is from God?
A) The servant is free and God is not responsible.
B) God coerces the disbeliever to disbelief.
C) The preponderating factor is unnecessary.
D) Infinite regress is avoided.

Key Takeaway
Razi's argument demonstrates that if God creates the power for disbelief and the preponderating factor, He is the ultimate cause of disbelief, thereby falsifying any claim that He only intends good for His creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you instinctively attribute it to your own weakness, or do you find yourself blaming God's decree? How does Razi's argument challenge both extremes and force you to examine the true source of your actions?

?

What does it now mean for you to live in submission to a God whose actions are beyond purpose as you understand it?
