Refuting Purposeless Creation
Razi refutes the claim that God created without purpose by showing it leads to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that claiming God created without any purpose leads to absurdities that invalidate divine wisdom.

Core Idea
A purposeless act from God is not just unreasonable—it unravels all religious trust.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi tackles the radical claim that God created the world without any purpose or wisdom. He argues that such a notion is not only unfitting for God’s attributes of wisdom and mercy, but also leads to absurd consequences that destroy the very foundation of religion. Let’s follow Razi’s logical dismantling of this position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi presents two reasons and then a concluding logical structure.
2
Identify the two reasons
Find the first reason (action without wisdom is foolishness) and the second reason (leading to absurdities). Note how each is introduced.
The second reason is longer and lists several unacceptable consequences.
3
Understand the absurd consequences
List the consequences Razi mentions: punishing prophets, rewarding disbelievers, losing trust in promise and threat, blurring obedience and disobedience. Ask yourself why these are absurd.
These are not just hypothetical; they undermine the entire moral and religious order.
4
Grasp the final syllogism
Razi ends with a conditional: if God originated the world, it was either for a purpose or not; both are false, so the claim of origination is false. Understand how this ties back to the refutation.
This shows the broader context: the argument against purposelessness is part of a larger debate about temporal creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثالث ، وهو أن يقال : إنه تعالى خلقهم لا لغرض وحكمة أصلا . فنقول : هذا أيضاً : باطل لوجوه : الأول : إن الفعل الخالي عن الحكمة : سفاهة ، وهي غير لائقة بالحكيم الرحيم . والثاني : إنه لم يمتنع ذلك ، فحينئذ لا يقبح من الله تعالى شيء أصلاً فوجب أن يحسن منه أن يعاقب الأنبياء والرسل [ بأعظم ] أنواع العقاب ، وأن يحسن منه إدخال الدهرية في أعلى درجات الجنة ، وأن لا يحصل الوثوق بوعده ووعيده ، وأن لا تتميز طاعته عن معصيته . وكل ذلك يبطل [ الحكمة ] الإلهية . فيثبت : أنه تعالى لو كان محدثاً للعالم ، بعد أن لم يكن محدثاً لكان إما أن يكون ذلك الإحداث لحكمة ومصلحة ، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك . وثبت فساد القسمين ، فوجب فساد القول بكونه محدثاً للعالم بعد أن لم يكن.
As for the third division (al-qism al-thalith), which is to say that He, exalted, created them with no purpose (gharad) and wisdom (hikma) at all. We say: this also is false for reasons: First: action devoid of wisdom is foolishness (safaha), and it is not fitting for the Wise (al-hakim), the Merciful (al-rahim). Second: If that is not impossible, then at that time nothing would be evil from God at all, so it would have to be good for Him to punish the prophets and messengers with the greatest types of punishment, and good for Him to enter the materialists (dahriyya) into the highest ranks of Paradise, and that trust in His promise (wa'd) and threat (wa'id) would not be obtained, and that His obedience would not be distinguished from His disobedience. And all that invalidates divine wisdom (al-hikma al-ilahiyya). So it is established: that if He, exalted, were an originator (muhdith) of the world after not having been an originator, then either that origination (ihadath) would be for a wisdom and benefit (maslaha), or it would not be so. And the falsity of both divisions is established, so the statement that He is an originator of the world after not having been must be false.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents the third division of the argument: the claim that God created without any purpose or wisdom. Razi gives two reasons for its falsehood. First, action without wisdom is foolishness (safaha), which is unbecoming of God, who is Wise and Merciful. Second, if such action were not impossible, then nothing would be evil from God at all, leading to absurdities: it would be good for God to punish prophets and reward atheists (dahriyya), trust in His promises and threats would be lost, and obedience would not be distinguished from disobedience. These consequences undermine divine wisdom. Razi then concludes that if God were to originate the world after not having done so, the origination would either be for a wisdom and benefit or not. Both possibilities are proven false, so the belief that God is an originator of the world in time is false.

Key Terms
القسم الثالث (al-qism al-thalith)
الغرض (gharad)
الحكمة (hikma)
السفاهة (safaha)
الحكيم (al-hakim)
الرحيم (al-rahim)
الدهرية (dahriyya)
الوعد (wa'd)
الوعيد (wa'id)
الإحداث (ihadath)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That an action without purpose is always permissible for God; it does not lead to any negative consequences.
Correction: Razi argues that such an action would necessarily be foolishness and would lead to absurdities that contradict God's wisdom and justice, thereby invalidating the entire religious framework.

Checkpoint

B) Action without wisdom is foolishness, unbecoming of God.
C) It would make God's will conditional.
D) It would necessitate a preponderating factor.

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that denying purpose in creation leads to logical absurdities that undermine God’s wisdom and the entire religious framework.

?

Extending the Dichotomy to Divine Will
Razi applies the same dichotomy to divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi applies the same dichotomy (wisdom-based or not) to the attribute of divine will, concluding that affirming will leads to the same contradictions.

Core Idea
Divine will is not exempt from the problem of purpose.

Razi now turns the same logical knife on the attribute of will (irada). If God's act of creating the world were accompanied by a will, that will itself would have to be either guided by wisdom and benefit or not. Since both options have been shown to be impossible (as argued in the previous lesson), the very claim that God has a will to create the world must be rejected. This short but powerful passage completes the critique of anthropomorphic reasoning about God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic once, then the English translation. Notice that this is a single conditional argument: 'if … then either … or … ; both false, so the premise is false.'
The phrase 'والله أعلم' is a closing formula, not part of the argument.
2
Identify the two branches of the dichotomy
The argument splits into two possibilities: the will is either based on wisdom/benefit or not. Identify exactly how these two options are presented.
These options mirror the earlier dichotomy about the creation itself.
3
Understand why both branches are false
The passage does not repeat the earlier refutation but relies on it. Recall that if the will is based on wisdom, then the creation would have a purpose (impossible for the First Agent); if not based on wisdom, the act would be foolish (impossible for God).
This links back to the previous lesson's reasoning.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since both possibilities are false, the original assumption (that God has a will to originate the world) must be false. Razi concludes that affirming will is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يحتجون بهذه الحجة على أنه لا يجوز أن يكون مريداً لإحداث العالم : أنه لو كان مريداً لكانت تلك الإرادة ، إما أن تكون تابعة لحكمة ومصلحة ، وإما أن لا تكون كذلك . والقسمان بـاطـلان ، فالقول بثبوت الإرادة : باطل [ والله أعلم ].
And they may use this proof (hujja) to prove that it is not permissible that He be willing (muridan) to originate the world: that if He were willing, then that will (irada) would either be dependent on a wisdom (hikma) and benefit (maslaha), or not be so. And both divisions are false, so the statement affirming will is false. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This short passage applies the same reasoning used earlier (the dichotomy of purpose or no purpose) to the attribute of divine will (irada). The argument states that if God had a will to originate the world, that will would either be based on wisdom and benefit or not. Both possibilities have already been shown to be false (in the previous lesson: if with wisdom, then the world would have a purpose; if without wisdom, then it would be foolishness). Therefore, the claim that God has a will to originate the world is false. The phrase 'And God knows best' (والله أعلم) is a concluding formula.

Key Terms
الإرادة (irada)
الحكمة (hikma)
المصلحة (maslaha)
مريد (murid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God's will is an eternal attribute that is independent of any consideration of wisdom or benefit, thereby avoiding the earlier dilemma about purpose.
Correction: Razi shows that any act of will, including God's, must either be for a reason or not. Both options lead to untenable conclusions for the divine nature. Hence, attributing a will to originate the world is not permissible.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's argument in this passage, if God's will to originate the world is not based on wisdom and benefit, what follows?
A) The will is necessary and eternal.
B) The will is false, just like the case where it is based on wisdom.
C) The will is unknowable.
D) The will is purely voluntary.

Key Takeaway
Divine will cannot be affirmed without falling into the same dilemma of purpose that disproves any act of creation with a specific intent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that your own will is free from needing a purpose, simply because it is yours? This lesson exposes that any will attached to an action must either serve a purpose or be arbitrary. In which area of your life have you been acting as if your will alone justifies your choices, without grounding them in wisdom? 

Will you now examine one decision you are making today and ask yourself: 'Is my will here based on genuine benefit and wisdom, or am I just following whim?'
?
Razi’s argument strips away the comfort of thinking that God’s will is exempt from scrutiny. But if you are honest, do you not sometimes treat your own will the same way—as if it needs no justification? What would it mean for you to surrender the idea that your desires are self-justifying and instead hold them accountable to the same standard of wisdom and benefit? 

Are you ready to let go of one arbitrary habit this week and replace it with a purposeful action?
?
This lesson challenges the very notion that will can exist without purpose. Think about the last time you did something you later regretted. Was it because you acted on a will that was not based on wisdom or benefit? The argument here suggests that such a will is not worthy of God—and it is not worthy of you as a steward of your own life. 

Will you commit to asking yourself before every significant action: 'Is my will here dependent on hikma and maslaha, or is it empty?'

The Fifth Proof: Pre-Eternal Motive Necessitates Pre-Eternal Creation
Fifth proof: a pre-eternal motive would necessitate the world's pre-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth proof from motive (dā'iyah) that argues for the world's pre-eternity by showing that any originated motive leads to regress, and any pre-eternal motive necessitates creation from eternity.

Core Idea
A pre-eternal motive leaves no room for a temporal beginning.

This lesson presents the fifth proof from the Matalib al-Aliyah, which uses the concept of divine motive to argue that the world must be eternal. The proof systematically examines whether the Creator's motive for creation is eternal or originated, and shows that only an eternal motive without obstacles is coherent, leading to the necessity of creation from pre-eternity. Follow the logical branches carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. The proof starts by assuming the world is originated and then deduces a contradiction. Notice the initial conditional: 'If the world were originated, then the Creator must have a motive.'
The proof is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Trace the first branch: motive originated
Follow the case where the motive is originated. The argument says this leads to an infinite regress because the same question applies to that motive. Understand why this is problematic.
Infinite regress is rejected in kalam.
3
Trace the second branch: motive eternal and conditioned
If the motive is eternal but conditioned, examine whether the condition is eternal or originated. If the condition is originated, circularity (dawr) arises. If the condition is eternal, move to the obstacle analysis.
Circularity is impossible in causal chains.
4
Examine the obstacle argument
When the motive is eternal and unconditioned, consider whether an obstacle existed in pre-eternity. If an obstacle existed, it must be either necessary (impossible to remove) or possible (requiring an effecter, leading to an infinite regress that ends in a necessary necessitator, which also cannot be removed). Therefore, no obstacle could have existed.
This eliminates all possible hindrances.
5
Conclude: pre-eternal motive without obstacles necessitates creation from eternity
Since the motive was present from eternity and no obstacles existed, the act of creation was necessary from pre-eternity. This contradicts the assumption that the world is originated, so the world must be eternal.
This proof is a cornerstone of the argument for pre-eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة: لو كان العالم حادثاً وجب أن يكون للخالق داع إلى إيجاده وتكوينه. فنقول: تلك الداعية، إما أن تكون قديمة، أو حادثة. فإن كانت حادثة، عاد البحث في كيفية حدوثها. وإن كانت قديمة، فإما أن تكون مشروطة بشرط، أو لا تكون. فإن كانت مشروطة بشرط، فذلك الشرط، إما أن يكون حادثاً أو لا يكون. فإن كان ذلك الشرط حادثاً، لزم الدور. لأنه لا تتم الداعية التامة المؤثرة في الإحداث إلا [؟] عند حدوث ذلك الشرط، وأن لا يحدث ذلك الشرط إلا إذا تمت تلك الداعية المؤثرة، وذلك يوجب الدور. وأما إن كان ذلك الشرط قديماً، أو قلنا: إن تلك الداعية القديمة غير مشروطة بشرط: فالكلام في هذين القسمين واحد، وذلك لأنه إما أن يقال: حصل في الأزل مانع، أو لم يحصل. والأول باطل. لأن ذلك المانع إن كان واجباً لذاته، امتنع زواله، فوجب أن لا يزول ذلك الامتناع. وإن كان ممكناً لذاته، افتقر إلى مؤثر. ويعود التقسيم فيه، ولا ينقطع إلا عند الانتهاء إلى موجب واجب لذاته: ويلزم من امتناع زوال ذلك الموجب، امتناع زوال ذلك المانع. وقد بان بطلانه. فيثبت: أن الداعية القديمة إلى الإيجاد حاصلة في الأزل، وأن الموانع بأسرها كانت زائلة. ومتى كان الأمر كذلك، كان الفعل واجب الحصول.
The fifth proof: If the world were originated, then the Creator must have a motive (dā'ī) to bring it into existence and form it. We say: that motive (dā'iyah) is either eternal (qadīm) or originated (ḥādith). If it is originated, the inquiry returns to how it occurs. And if it is eternal, then either it is conditioned by a condition or not. If it is conditioned by a condition, that condition is either originated or not. If that condition is originated, circularity (dawr) follows, because the complete effective motive for bringing into existence is not [؟] except at the occurrence of that condition, and that condition does not occur except when that effective motive is complete, and that necessitates circularity. As for if that condition is eternal, or we say: that eternal motive is not conditioned by a condition: the discussion in these two divisions is the same, because either it is said: an obstacle (māniʿ) existed in pre-eternity, or it did not. The first is false, because if that obstacle is necessary (wājib) in itself, its removal is impossible, so that impossibility must not cease. And if it is possible (mumkin) in itself, it needs an effecter (muʾaththir). The division applies to it, and it does not end except by arriving at a necessitator (mūjib) necessary in itself. And from the impossibility of the removal of that necessitator follows the impossibility of the removal of that obstacle. Its falsehood has become evident. So it is established that the eternal motive for bringing into existence is present in pre-eternity, and that all obstacles were absent. And when the matter is such, the act is necessary of occurrence.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents a kalam proof by contradiction against the view that the world is temporally originated. The author begins by stating the proof: if the world is originated, the Creator must have a motive (dā'ī) for bringing it into existence. The argument then divides the motive into two possibilities: eternal or originated. If originated, we face an infinite regress (the same question applies to that motive). If eternal, it is either conditioned by a condition or not. If conditioned, the condition must be either eternal or originated. If the condition is originated, a circularity (dawr) arises: the motive cannot be effective until the condition occurs, and the condition cannot occur until the motive is effective. This is impossible. If the condition is eternal or the motive is unconditioned, we consider whether there was an obstacle (māniʿ) in pre-eternity or not. If there was an obstacle, that obstacle is either necessary in itself (impossible to remove) or possible (requiring an effecter, leading to an infinite chain that terminates only in a necessary necessitator). The impossibility of removing the necessary necessitator implies the impossibility of removing the obstacle, which is false because the world exists. Therefore, no obstacle existed. Hence, the eternal motive was present from pre-eternity with no obstacles, making the act (creation) necessary from eternity. This contradicts the premise that the world is temporally originated. Thus, the assumption of origination is false; the world must be eternal.

Key Terms
داعية (dā'iyah)
دور (dawr)
مانع (māniʿ)
موجب (mūjib)
قديم (qadīm)
حادث (ḥādith)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think a conditional pre-eternal motive allows temporal creation, but the argument shows any condition leads to circularity or necessity from eternity.
Correction: The proof demonstrates that if the motive is eternal and conditioned by an originated condition, circularity arises; if the condition is eternal or absent, the act becomes necessary from eternity, eliminating any temporal origination.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the fifth proof?
A) The world must be originated because the motive is originated.
B) The world must be eternal because a pre-eternal motive necessitates creation from eternity.
C) The Creator has no motive for creation.
D) The motive is conditional and thus allows temporal creation.

Key Takeaway
A pre-eternal motive with no obstacles necessitates creation from eternity, contradicting the view that the world is originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof uses logic to argue that the world must be eternal. Does your faith in God's creation rest on a specific belief about time beginning? What would it mean for your relationship with God if the world has always existed?
?
If God's motive for creation is eternal and necessary, is your own act of worship truly free? How does the idea that God's will necessitates creation challenge or deepen your understanding of divine love and generosity?
?
The argument eliminates all obstacles to creation in pre-eternity. Are there obstacles in your own life—fears, doubts, or sins—that you have allowed to keep you from responding to God's call? What would it take for you to remove them today?

Refuting Equality Between Creation and Non-Creation
Three arguments refuting equality of creation and non-creation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the idea that creation and non-creation are equal in all respects is rationally impossible.

Core Idea
Equality between act and omission leads to absurdity: it makes the act arbitrary and the agent foolish.

The sixth proof from al-Maṭālib al-ʿĀliya addresses the possible relations between God creating the world and not creating it. The first possibility considered is that both are equal in every way. At first glance, this might seem to allow God freedom, but the proof shows it leads to contradictions that cannot be attributed to the Wise Creator. Let's examine the three arguments that refute this division.

Guided Path
1
Understand the four divisions
The proof begins by listing four possible cases. Notice that the first case is absolute equality. This is the target of the first refutation.
This step sets the context.
2
Examine the three sub-arguments
Read the three reasons given against equality. Each one shows a different impossibility: preponderance without a preponderator, preponderance at time of equality, and futility of the act.
These are three independent proofs.
3
See the consensus of rational people
The passage concludes that rational people agree that God must be above such an attribute. This shows the theological stakes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة: كونه تعالى محدثاً للعالم، وكونه غير محدث له. إما أن يتساويا من كل الوجوه، أو يكون الإحداث أفضل مطلقاً، أو يكون الترك أفضل مطلقاً، أو يقال: الفعل أفضل في بعض الأوقات، والترك أفضل في البعض. أما القسم الأول: وهو أن يقال: استوى الطرفان واعتدل الجانبان من غير رجحان: فهذا باطل لوجوه: الأول: إنه يقتضي رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر، لا لمرجح. وإن جاز ذلك، فلم لا يجوز رجحان وجود العالم على عدمه لا لمرجح؟ والثاني: وهو أن الإيجاد ترجيح الجانب الوجود، وحصول الترجيح حال حصول الإستواء: محال. والثالث: إن الذي يكون فعله وتركه، متساويين في كل الجهات، كان فعله عبثاً، وكان فاعله سفيهاً، ولا يكون حكيماً، ولا يكون فعله إحساناً. وأجمع العقلاء على أنه يجب تنزيه إله العالم عن مثل هذه الصفة. وأما القسم الثاني: وهو أن يقال: الفعل أفضل من الترك. فنقول: فعلى هذا التقدير، يلزم أن يقال: إنه تعالى كان تاركاً للجانب الأفضل مدة غير متناهية، ثم انتقل من الأفضل إلى الأخس، وذلك باطل.
The sixth proof: His being, exalted, a creator (muḥdith) of the world, and His not creating it. Either they are equal in all respects, or creation (al-iḥdāth) is absolutely better, or non-creation is absolutely better, or it is said: the act is better at some times, and non-creation is better at others. As for the first division: which is that it is said: the two sides are equal and the two aspects are balanced without preponderance (tarjīḥ): this is false for reasons. First: it necessitates the preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible over the other without a preponderator (murjīḥ). And if that is permissible, then why is it not permissible for the preponderance of the existence of the world over its non-existence without a preponderator? Second: bringing into existence is a preponderance of the side of existence, and the occurrence of preponderance at the time of equality is impossible. Third: one whose act and its omission are equal in all respects, his act would be futile (ʿabath), and its agent would be foolish (safīh), and he would not be wise (ḥakīm), nor would his act be beneficence (iḥsān). The rational people agree that it is necessary to declare the God of the world above such an attribute. As for the second division: which is that it is said: the act is better than omission. We say: on this assumption, it would be necessary to say that He, exalted, was omitting the better side for an infinite duration, then moved from the better to the inferior, and that is false.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof, which classifies the possible relations between the Creator's action of creating the world and its opposite (non-creation). The author enumerates four possibilities: (1) they are equal in all respects, (2) creation is absolutely better, (3) non-creation is absolutely better, (4) creation is better at some times and non-creation at others. The passage then refutes the first two divisions. For the first division (equality), the author gives three sub-arguments: first, equality would imply preponderance without a preponderator, which is impossible—if allowed, then one could also allow the world's existence over non-existence without a cause. Second, bringing into existence is itself a preponderance, and it cannot occur at the moment of equality. Third, if act and omission are equal, the act is futile (ʿabath) and the agent foolish, which is incompatible with divine wisdom. The second division (creation always better) is refuted by noting that it would require God to have abandoned the better for an infinite past and then switched to the inferior, which is absurd. The third and fourth divisions are not addressed in this lesson but would likely be refuted by similar reasoning. The proof aims to show that temporal creation is impossible because it leads to one of these false implications.

Key Terms
إحداث
ترجيح
مرجح
عبث
حكيم
سفيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think equality means God is free to choose, but this is wrong.
Correction: Equality makes the choice arbitrary and the act futile, which is impossible for God.

Checkpoint
According to the first division refutation, what does equality imply?
A) Preponderance without a preponderator.
B) The act is wise and beneficial.
C) God is all-powerful.
D) The world must be eternal.

Key Takeaway
The equality of creation and non-creation is impossible because it requires arbitrary preponderance, simultaneous preponderance and equality, and results in futile action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult choice and both options seem equally valid, what do you rely on to decide? Could you be acting without a real preponderator? How does this passage challenge you to examine the reasons behind your own decisions?
?
Think of a time you did something that felt pointless or futile. Did you rationalize it to yourself? In light of this lesson, how does the attribute of divine wisdom confront the excuses you make for wasted actions?
?
If God’s actions can never be futile, then what about the painful events in your life that seem meaningless? Are you willing to trust that there is a wisdom you cannot see, even when your heart screams ‘why’? What would change in your attitude today if you truly believed that?

Refuting the Claim that Creation is Always Better
Refutation of the second division: creation always better leads to infinite delay

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the assumption that creation is always better than non-creation is rationally impossible, because it would imply God abandoned the better for an infinite duration.

Core Idea
If creation were always better, God's delay in creating would be a choice for the inferior over the superior.

The sixth proof continues by considering the second possibility: that creation is absolutely better than non-creation. This might seem to support divine wisdom, but it leads to a different absurdity: an infinite past during which God refrained from the better act. Let's see why this is false.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second division
The proof states that if creation is always better, then God was omitting the better side for an infinite duration before creating. This is the core of the refutation.
Notice the assumption of temporal creation.
2
Grasp the absurdity
The proof argues that moving from the better to the inferior is not fit for God. This refutes the idea that creation is always better.
This argument relies on the principle that God always chooses the best.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The sixth proof: His being, exalted, a creator (muḥdith) of the world, and His not creating it. We say: on this assumption, it would be necessary to say that He, exalted, was omitting the better side for an infinite duration, then moved from the better to the inferior, and that is false.

Refuting Absolute Refraining
Al-Razi refutes the third division of the claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's refutation of the claim that refraining is absolutely better than acting.

Core Idea
Absolute refraining would force God from noble to base — impossible.

The third division of the argument claims refraining is always better. Al-Razi shows that this would imply God moved from a more noble state to a base one, which is absurd for the perfect Creator. Let's examine his concise refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the third division passage
Read the Arabic and translation of al-Razi's refutation of the absolute claim. Notice how he derives an impossible consequence.
2
Identify the logical implication
Al-Razi says that if refraining is always better, then God's act of creation would be moving from the more noble (refraining) to the base (acting). Understand why this is false for God.
God cannot transition from a better state to a worse one.
3
Consider the underlying principle
Reflect on why al-Razi considers this implication false — it conflicts with God's perfection and wisdom.
This connects to divine attributes of wisdom and immutability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثالث: وهو أن يقال: الترك أفضل من الفعل مطلقاً، فنقول: يلزم أن يقال: إن خالق العالم قد انتقل من الأشرف إلى الأخس، وذلك أيضاً باطل.
وأما القسم الرابع: وهو أن يقال: كان الترك أفضل في بعض الأوقات، ثم صار الفعل أفضل من الترك في سائر الأوقات، فنقول: هذا أيضاً باطل. لأن هذا التبديل، إن وقع لذاته، فليجز تبدل العدم بالوجود لذاته، وهو باطل. وإن وقع بجعل جاعل، فحينئذ يعود التقسيم الأول: وهو أن الخالق إنما خصص أولوية الترك بالأوقات السالفة، وأولوية الفعل بالأوقات الحاضرة. لأن الأولى بالأوقات السالفة، عدم تلك الأولوية. والأولى بهذه الأوقات الحاضرة، أولوية الفعل، وحينئذ يعود التقسيم الأول فيه. وذلك يوجب الذهاب إلى ما لا نهاية له. والله أعلم.
As for the third division: that it is said that refraining is absolutely better than acting, we say: it necessarily follows that it would be said that the Creator of the world has moved from the more noble to the base, and that too is false.
As for the fourth division: that it is said that refraining was better at some times, then acting became better than refraining at other times, we say: this too is false. Because this change, if it occurs in His essence, then let the non-existence change into existence in His essence be permissible, and that is false. And if it occurs by the making of a maker, then the first division returns: that the Creator only specified the preference of refraining for past times and the preference of acting for present times. Because what is fitting for past times is the absence of that preference, and what is fitting for these present times is the preference of acting, and then the first division returns in it. And that necessitates going into infinite regress. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents al-Razi’s refutation of two specific claims within the broader argument that refraining (al-tark) is better than acting (al-fi‘l). The passage is a classification and refutation of two divisions of that claim. First, he addresses the third division: the absolute claim that refraining is always better. He refutes it by showing that it implies God moved from a more noble state (ashraf) to a base one (akhass), which is absurd for the perfect Creator. Second, he addresses the fourth division: the claim that refraining was better at certain times and acting became better later. He refutes this by analyzing the change: either it occurs in God’s essence—which would necessitate that non-existence can change into existence in His essence, a clear falsehood—or it occurs through an external agent, which leads to an infinite regress because any temporal change would again require specifying reasons, and so on. Al-Razi concludes that such arguments lead to infinite regress (ma la nihaya lahu), which is invalid. The phrase 'Allahu a‘lam' marks the end of the argument.

Key Terms
الترك
الفعل
الأشرف
الأخس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God can choose any action without implications.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that claiming refraining is absolutely better forces an absurd consequence about God's state.

Checkpoint
What does al-Razi say necessarily follows from the claim that refraining is absolutely better?
A) That God is all-powerful.
B) That God moved from the more noble to the base.
C) That acting is always better.
D) That refraining is sometimes better.

Key Takeaway
Absolute refraining is impossible for God because it would imply a transition from a superior to an inferior state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you willing to act for God even when it seems 'less noble' than remaining still, or does al-Razi's argument expose a hidden pride in your inaction?
?
When you look at your life, where have you assumed that God's refraining (e.g., delaying an answer) is 'better' than His acting, and how does this lesson demand you trust His perfection instead?
?
Is there a situation right now where you are choosing to refrain out of comfort, and how can the impossibility of God moving from noble to base inspire you to move from passive safety to active obedience?

Refuting Temporal Change in Refraining
Al-Razi refutes the fourth division of the claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's refutation of the claim that refraining was better at some times and acting better at others.

Core Idea
Temporal change in divine preference leads to infinite regress.

The fourth division of the argument claims that refraining was better at some times and acting became better later. Al-Razi examines two possibilities for this change: in God's essence or by an external maker. Both lead to absurdities. Let's trace his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth division passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice al-Razi's two-pronged refutation.
2
Analyze the first option
If the change occurs in God's essence, it would allow non-existence to change into existence within Him—an impossibility.
This attacks the idea of change in God.
3
Analyze the second option
If the change is by an external maker, the same problem of specifying preference for times recurs, leading to infinite regress.
Infinite regress is invalid in kalam arguments about God's actions.
4
Understand the conclusion
Neither option is acceptable, so the claim is false. Reflect on why infinite regress is a decisive refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثالث: وهو أن يقال: الترك أفضل من الفعل مطلقاً، فنقول: يلزم أن يقال: إن خالق العالم قد انتقل من الأشرف إلى الأخس، وذلك أيضاً باطل.
وأما القسم الرابع: وهو أن يقال: كان الترك أفضل في بعض الأوقات، ثم صار الفعل أفضل من الترك في سائر الأوقات، فنقول: هذا أيضاً باطل. لأن هذا التبديل، إن وقع لذاته، فليجز تبدل العدم بالوجود لذاته، وهو باطل. وإن وقع بجعل جاعل، فحينئذ يعود التقسيم الأول: وهو أن الخالق إنما خصص أولوية الترك بالأوقات السالفة، وأولوية الفعل بالأوقات الحاضرة. لأن الأولى بالأوقات السالفة، عدم تلك الأولوية. والأولى بهذه الأوقات الحاضرة، أولوية الفعل، وحينئذ يعود التقسيم الأول فيه. وذلك يوجب الذهاب إلى ما لا نهاية له. والله أعلم.
As for the third division: that it is said that refraining is absolutely better than acting, we say: it necessarily follows that it would be said that the Creator of the world has moved from the more noble to the base, and that too is false.
As for the fourth division: that it is said that refraining was better at some times, then acting became better than refraining at other times, we say: this too is false. Because this change, if it occurs in His essence, then let the non-existence change into existence in His essence be permissible, and that is false. And if it occurs by the making of a maker, then the first division returns: that the Creator only specified the preference of refraining for past times and the preference of acting for present times. Because what is fitting for past times is the absence of that preference, and what is fitting for these present times is the preference of acting, and then the first division returns in it. And that necessitates going into infinite regress. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents al-Razi’s refutation of two specific claims within the broader argument that refraining (al-tark) is better than acting (al-fi‘l). The passage is a classification and refutation of two divisions of that claim. First, he addresses the third division: the absolute claim that refraining is always better. He refutes it by showing that it implies God moved from a more noble state (ashraf) to a base one (akhass), which is absurd for the perfect Creator. Second, he addresses the fourth division: the claim that refraining was better at certain times and acting became better later. He refutes this by analyzing the change: either it occurs in God’s essence—which would necessitate that non-existence can change into existence in His essence, a clear falsehood—or it occurs through an external agent, which leads to an infinite regress because any temporal change would again require specifying reasons, and so on. Al-Razi concludes that such arguments lead to infinite regress (ma la nihaya lahu), which is invalid. The phrase 'Allahu a‘lam' marks the end of the argument.

First Proof: Impossibility of Knowledge of Particulars
First proof that God cannot know particulars, with two reasons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that the world cannot be created because it would require God's knowledge to change, which is impossible for God.

Core Idea
If the world were created, God's knowledge would change—impossible.

This passage presents an argument from the impossibility of God knowing particulars. The proof argues that if the world were contingent (created), then God would need to intend its creation, requiring knowledge of its non-existence and then existence—implying change. Since change is impossible for God, the world must be eternal. Let's examine the argument and its two key reasons.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the English translation. Notice the logical flow from premise to conclusion.
The argument uses a conditional: if world is contingent, then God knows particulars. That is then shown impossible.
2
Identify the two reasons
The text gives two reasons why God knowing particulars is impossible. Find each one and understand how they relate to change and corporeality.
The first reason deals with knowledge remaining or changing; the second with the need for a body.
3
Reflect on the argument's purpose
This proof is used to deny that the world is created. Ask yourself: why does the author think this refutes the world's contingency? How does it support the eternity of the world?
The argument is part of a larger debate about divine attributes and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي وجوه : في الدلائل المستنبطة من صفة العالم الحجة الأولى : لو كان العالم حادثاً ، لما حدث إلا إذا قصد الفاعل إلى تكوينه . وهذا القصد يمتنع حصوله ، إلا إذا كان عالماً بأنه كان معدوماً ، ثم إنه يحاول (۱) أن يجعله موجوداً . وإذا كان كذلك ، فقد كان عند عدمه عالماً ، بأنه معدوم ، وعند وجوده يصير عالماً بأنه موجود . فثبت أنه لو كان العالم ، لوجب كونه عالماً بالجزئيات. وإنما قلنا : إن ذلك : محال . لوجوه : الأول : إنه تعالى لما علم أن العالم معدوم. فعندما يصير موجوداً ، إن بقي علمه بأنه معدوم ، فهو جهل. وهو على الله محال . [ وإن لم يبق ، فهو تغير . وهو على الله محال (۲) ] . والثاني : [ إن ](٣) العالم بالجزئيات ، لا بد وأن يكون جسماً أو جسمانياً . وهذا في حق الله [ تعالى ](4) محال ، فيمتنع كونه عالماً بالجزئيات
And they are aspects: In the evidences derived from the attribute of the world. The first proof: If the world were contingent, it would only come to be if the agent intended its formation. And this intention is impossible to obtain unless He was knowing that it was non-existent, then He attempts (1) to make it existent. And if that is the case, then when it was non-existent He was knowing that it was non-existent, and when it becomes existent He becomes knowing that it is existent. So it is established that if the world were [contingent], it would be necessary that He be knowing of particulars. And we say: that is impossible. For reasons: The first: He, exalted, when He knew that the world is non-existent, then when it becomes existent, if His knowledge that it is non-existent remains, then it is ignorance. And that is impossible for God. [And if it does not remain, then it is change. And that is impossible for God (2).] And the second: [Indeed] (3) the knower of particulars must be a body or corporeal. And this is impossible with respect to God [exalted] (4), so it is impossible that He be knowing of particulars.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof in a series of arguments derived from the attribute of the world. It argues that if the world were created (hadith), then its creation would require the agent (God) to intend its formation. Intention, in turn, requires that the agent know that the world is non-existent and then will its existence. This would mean that God’s knowledge changes from knowing the world as non-existent to knowing it as existent—a change in His knowledge. The text then states that such a consequence is impossible, and gives two reasons. First, if after the world exists God’s knowledge that it was non-existent remains, that is ignorance (because the world is now existent). If that knowledge does not remain, it is change. Both ignorance and change are impossible for God. Second, a knower of particular changing things must be a body or be corporeal, because knowledge of particulars is conditioned by the presence of the object. But God is not a body, so He cannot know particulars. Therefore, the premise that the world is created leads to an impossible conclusion, so the premise must be false. This is a classic kalam argument for the eternity of the world.

Key Terms
العالم
حادث
القصد
علم بالجزئيات
تغير
جسم
جسماني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies God's omniscience—that God does not know everything.
Correction: The argument does not deny God's omniscience; it refutes the claim that the world is created by showing that if it were, God's knowledge would have to change, which is impossible. The point is to prove the world's eternity, not to limit God's knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the first reason given in the proof, what would it mean if God's knowledge that the world is non-existent remained after the world came into existence?
A) Change in God
B) Ignorance (جهل)
C) A new attribute
D) Perfection of knowledge

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that God knowing particulars would require change, which is impossible, so the world cannot be created.

The Core Argument: If the World is Originated, God Must Know Particulars
Razi presents the core conditional argument that origination necessitates knowledge of particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's initial argument that the origination of the world necessitates that the agent has knowledge of particulars.

Core Idea
Origination requires intention, which implies knowledge of the object's states.

Razi begins his first argument by establishing a conditional: if the world is originated, then its agent must have intended it. That intention presupposes knowledge of the world's non-existence and existence. Thus, if the world exists, God must have knowledge of particulars. This sets the stage for the objections that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation to understand the logical flow of the argument.
Notice the conditional structure: if...then.
2
Identify the key conditional
Identify the conditional: 'If the world is originated, then the agent intended it.' Reflect on why intention requires knowledge.
Intention cannot occur without knowledge of the object's state.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that the conclusion is that God must be knowing of particulars. This is the premise that the objections will attack.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي وجوه : في الدلائل المستنبطة من صفة العالم
الحجة الأولى : لو كان العالم حادثاً ، لما حدث إلا إذا قصد الفاعل إلى تكوينه . وهذا القصد يمتنع حصوله ، إلا إذا كان عالماً بأنه كان معدوماً ، ثم إنه يحاول أن يجعله موجوداً . وإذا كان كذلك ، فقد كان عند عدمه عالماً ، بأنه معدوم ، وعند وجوده يصير عالماً بأنه موجود . فثبت أنه لو كان العالم ، لوجب كونه عالماً بالجزئيات. وإنما قلنا : إن ذلك : محال . لوجوه : الأول : إنه تعالى لما علم أن العالم معدوم. فعندما يصير موجوداً ، إن بقي علمه بأنه معدوم ، فهو جهل. وهو على الله محال . وإن لم يبق ، فهو تغير . وهو على الله محال . والثاني : إن العالم بالجزئيات ، لا بد وأن يكون جسماً أو جسمانياً . وهذا في حق الله تعالى محال ، فيمتنع كونه عالماً بالجزئيات الثالث : إن العلم بأن الشيء الفلاني موجود ، وبأن الشيء الفلاني معدوم : تبع للمعلوم. فلو كان واجب الاتصاف بالعلم بالجزئيات وقد ثبت : أن العلم بالجزئيات متوقف على وقوع تلك الجزئيات ، على تلك الوجوه المخصوصة . فحينئذ ذاته مما يمتنع وجودها إلا عند حصول تلك العلوم . ثم إن تلك العلوم متوقفة التحقق على حصول تلك المعلومات الخارجية ، والموقوف على الموقوف على الشيء موقوف على الشيء فيلزم أن تكون ذاته موقوفة على الغير، والموقوف على الغير : ممكن لذاته ، فيلزم أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته . وهو محال . واعلم أن هذه الوجوه الثلاثة في نفي علمه تعالى بالجزئيات قد سبق ذكرها بالاستقصاء مع الأجوبة الوافية
These are facets: regarding the proofs derived from the attribute of the world. The first proof: If the world were originated, it would only occur if the agent intended its formation. This intention cannot occur unless He knows that it was non-existent, then He attempts to make it existent. If this is so, then at the time of its non-existence He knew that it was non-existent, and at the time of its existence He becomes knowing that it is existent. So it is established that if the world [existed], He must be knowing of particulars. But we say: that is impossible. For reasons: The first: When He, exalted, knew that the world is non-existent, then when it becomes existent, if His knowledge that it is non-existent remains, that is ignorance, and that is impossible for God. And if it does not remain, that is change, and that is impossible for God. The second: The knower of particulars must necessarily be a body or corporeal. That is impossible with respect to God, exalted, so His being knowing of particulars is impossible. The third: Knowledge that such-and-such thing is existent and that such-and-such thing is non-existent is dependent on the known. If He is necessarily described with knowledge of particulars, and it has been established that knowledge of particulars depends on the occurrence of those particulars in those specific ways, then His essence cannot exist except when those knowledges occur. Then those knowledges depend on the occurrence of those external knowns, and what depends on what depends on something depends on that something. So it follows that His essence depends on another, and what depends on another is possible in itself, so it would follow that the Necessary Existence in itself is possible, which is impossible. And know that these three reasons for negating His knowledge of particulars have been mentioned previously in detail with sufficient answers.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument from the attributes of the world. It begins by stating that if the world is originated, its occurrence requires an agent’s intention. That intention presupposes that the agent knows the world was non-existent and then wishes to bring it into existence. Consequently, if the world exists, God must have knowledge of particulars (i.e., of the world’s states). Razi then asserts that this is impossible, and he gives three reasons. 

First reason: If God knew the world was non-existent, then when it becomes existent, either His previous knowledge remains or it does not. If it remains, that would be ignorance (knowing a non-existent as existent), which is impossible for God. If it does not remain, that implies change in God, also impossible.

Second reason: The knower of particulars must be a body or a corporeal entity, because grasping changing particulars requires material receptivity. Since God is neither, He cannot know particulars.

Third reason: Knowledge of particulars depends on the actual occurrence of those particulars in specific ways. If God necessarily knows all particulars, then His essence would depend on the existence of external objects—since His knowledge depends on them, and His essence depends on that knowledge. This would make God’s essence dependent on others, implying contingency, which contradicts His necessity.

Finally, Razi notes that these three arguments have been presented elsewhere with complete answers, hinting that they are not his final position but part of a dialectical exposition.

علم
جزئيات
حادث

Checkpoint

A) Power
B) Intention
C) Knowledge of particulars
D) Both intention and knowledge

Key Takeaway
The origination of the world implies that its Creator must have knowledge of the world's specific states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If you truly believe that God knows every detail of your life—your past failures, present struggles, and future decisions—how does that change the way you approach repentance and planning? Are you living as if He is unaware of your struggles, or are you owning up to the fact that He already knows?
?
The argument states that intention requires knowledge. In your own life, how often do you act with intention without truly knowing the situation? What does this teach you about the perfection of God's actions compared to your own impulsive decisions?
?
Razi presents this as a necessary implication, yet later shows it can be refuted. How comfortable are you with holding tension between logical arguments and theological mysteries? Is your faith dependent on intellectual certainty, or can you trust in God's wisdom even when human reasoning reaches its limits?

Two Objections: Change and Corporeality in Divine Knowledge
Razi presents the first two reasons against God's knowledge of particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first two reasons why God's knowledge of particulars is considered impossible: it would entail either ignorance and change in God, or that God is corporeal.

Core Idea
Knowledge of changing particulars would require change in God or make God corporeal.

Razi presents two objections to the conclusion that God knows particulars. First, if God knew the world was non-existent, then after creation that knowledge would either remain (causing ignorance) or change (causing change in God). Both are impossible. Second, knowing particulars requires being corporeal, which is impossible for God.

Guided Path
1
Read the two objections
Read the Arabic and translation for the first and second objections.
2
Analyze the first objection
Understand the dilemma: if God's knowledge that the world was non-existent remains after creation, that is ignorance; if it does not remain, that is change. Both are impossible for God.
This objection targets the idea of God having changing knowledge.
3
Analyze the second objection
Understand why knowledge of particulars is associated with corporeality: because particulars are material and changing, requiring a receptive substrate.
God is not a body or corporeal.
4
Reflect on impossibility
Consider why both options are considered impossible for God based on classical Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

These are facets: regarding the proofs derived from the attribute of the world. The first proof: If the world were originated, it would only occur if the agent intended its formation. This intention cannot occur unless He knows that it was non-existent, then He attempts to make it existent. If this is so, then at the time of its non-existence He knew that it was non-existent, and at the time of its existence He becomes knowing that it is existent. So it is established that if the world [existed], He must be knowing of particulars. But we say: that is impossible. For reasons: The first: When He, exalted, knew that the world is non-existent, then when it becomes existent, if His knowledge that it is non-existent remains, that is ignorance, and that is impossible for God. And if it does not remain, that is change, and that is impossible for God. The second: The knower of particulars must necessarily be a body or corporeal. That is impossible with respect to God, exalted, so His being knowing of particulars is impossible. The third: Knowledge that such-and-such thing is existent and that such-and-such thing is non-existent is dependent on the known. If He is necessarily described with knowledge of particulars, and it has been established that knowledge of particulars depends on the occurrence of those particulars in those specific ways, then His essence cannot exist except when those knowledges occur. Then those knowledges depend on the occurrence of those external knowns, and what depends on what depends on something depends on that something. So it follows that His essence depends on another, and what depends on another is possible in itself, so it would follow that the Necessary Existence in itself is possible, which is impossible. And know that these three reasons for negating His knowledge of particulars have been mentioned previously in detail with sufficient answers.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument from the attributes of the world. It begins by stating that if the world is originated, its occurrence requires an agent’s intention. That intention presupposes that the agent knows the world was non-existent and then wishes to bring it into existence. Consequently, if the world exists, God must have knowledge of particulars (i.e., of the world’s states). Razi then asserts that this is impossible, and he gives three reasons. 

First reason: If God knew the world was non-existent, then when it becomes existent, either His previous knowledge remains or it does not. If it remains, that would be ignorance (knowing a non-existent as existent), which is impossible for God. If it does not remain, that implies change in God, also impossible.

Second reason: The knower of particulars must be a body or a corporeal entity, because grasping changing particulars requires material receptivity. Since God is neither, He cannot know particulars.

Third reason: Knowledge of particulars depends on the actual occurrence of those particulars in specific ways. If God necessarily knows all particulars, then His essence would depend on the existence of external objects—since His knowledge depends on them, and His essence depends on that knowledge. This would make God’s essence dependent on others, implying contingency, which contradicts His necessity.

Finally, Razi notes that these three arguments have been presented elsewhere with complete answers, hinting that they are not his final position but part of a dialectical exposition.

جهل
تغير
جسماني

A) God would be ignorant
B) God would have changed
C) God would be corporeal
D) God's essence would depend on others

Key Takeaway
The first two objections claim that knowledge of particulars would force God into either ignorance/change or corporeality.

?
If God's knowledge is perfect and unchanging, then He already knows every sin you will commit and every repentance you will make. How does this reality affect the way you approach Him in prayer and repentance today? Will you let it lead you to deeper humility and gratitude, or to neglect?

The Third Objection: Dependency and Contingency
Razi presents the third objection based on dependency and the final note.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third objection: that knowledge of particulars would make God's essence dependent on external objects, implying contingency, which contradicts His necessary existence.

Core Idea
Knowledge of particulars depends on the objects, making God's essence contingent.

The third objection is more complex: it argues that knowledge of particulars depends on the actual occurrence of those particulars. If God necessarily knows all particulars, then His knowledge depends on external events, and since His essence is inseparable from His knowledge, His essence would depend on external things. That would make God possible in essence, contradicting His necessary existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the third objection
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the logical chain.
This objection is longer and more intricate.
2
Follow the dependency chain
Trace the argument: knowledge depends on known objects → God's knowledge depends on particulars → God's essence depends on His knowledge → essence depends on external objects → contingency.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that God would be contingent, contradicting His necessity. Then note that Razi says these objections have been answered elsewhere.
This indicates the dialectical nature of the argument.

These are facets: regarding the proofs derived from the attribute of the world. The first proof: If the world were originated, it would only occur if the agent intended its formation. This intention cannot occur unless He knows that it was non-existent, then He attempts to make it existent. Second Argument: A Dilemma on God's Knowledge of Particulars
Razi presents a dilemma: whether God knows particulars or not, both lead to problems.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses a dilemma to challenge the view of voluntary creation by showing that whether God knows particulars or not, both options lead to problems.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of particulars forces a choice between two impossible outcomes.

Razi's second argument uses a dilemma to challenge the Ash'ari view that God creates voluntarily. He sets up two possibilities: either God knows particulars or He does not. Both lead to conclusions that seem to conflict with the idea of a voluntary, temporal creation. This lesson unpacks the first horn and introduces the second.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the dilemma's structure. Note the two alternatives and the reasoning for the first horn.
The text cuts off at the beginning of the second horn.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Find the two alternatives: God does not know particulars, or God knows particulars. Both are presented as possibilities, and each is supposed to lead to a problem.
3
Examine the first horn
Focus on the argument for the first horn: if God does not know particulars, He cannot intend the creation of the world, because intention requires knowledge of the object's non-existence. Without that knowledge, intention is impossible.
This implies the world cannot be created through will, so it must be eternal.
4
Consider the second horn (as far as given)
Notice how the second horn is introduced: 'if the truth is that He is knowing of particulars, then we say: this requires...' The text cuts off, but the implication is that it also leads to a contradiction.
The full argument would continue on the next page.
5
Reflect on the argument's purpose
Remember that Razi is presenting this as a dialectical argument, not necessarily his own position. He will later refute it. This is a common method in kalam to test opposing views.
Do not mistake this as Razi's final belief.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية من الوجوه المبنية على العلم : أن نقول : إما أن يكون الحق هو أنه سبحانه غير عالم بالجزئيات. وإما أن يكون هو أنه سبحانه عالم بالجزئيات . وعلى التقديرين فإنه يجب كونه موجباً بالذات . ثم يلزم من دوامه : دوام المعلومات . أما إذا كان الحق هو القسم الأول ، وهو أنه غير عالم بالجزئيات . فعل هذا التقدير ، يمتنع كونه قاصداً إيجاد العالم . لأن هذا القصد إنما يمكن حصوله ، لو كان عالماً بأنه معدوم ، ثم إنه يريد أن يجعله موجوداً . فعلى تقدير ان لا يكون عالماً بالجزئيات ، امتنع حصول هذا العلم ، فامتنع حصول القصد المذكور ، فامتنع منه أن يقصد إلى إيجاده وإحداثه وتكوينه . وأما إذا كان الحق هو القسم الثاني ، وهو أنه تعالى عالم بالجزئيات . فنقول : فهذا يقتضي
The second proof from the facets based on knowledge: We say: Either the truth is that He, glorified, is not knowing of particulars, or that He, glorified, is knowing of particulars. In both cases, He must be an agent by essence. Then from His perpetuity follows the perpetuity of the knowns. If the truth is the first division, that He is not knowing of particulars, then on this supposition, it is impossible for Him to intend the world's existence, because this intention can only occur if He knows that it is non-existent and then He wants to make it existent. So on the supposition that He is not knowing of particulars, this knowledge is impossible, so the mentioned intention is impossible, so He cannot intend to bring it into existence and originate it and create it. And if the truth is the second division, that He, exalted, is knowing of particulars, then we say: this requires...
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument, which is a dilemma based on knowledge. Razi poses two alternatives: either God does not know particulars, or He does. In both cases, He is an agent by essence (موجب بالذات), meaning His action is necessary, not voluntary. From His perpetuity follows the perpetuity of the known objects—if He knows something, it must always exist.

Razi then examines the first horn: if God does not know particulars, then He cannot intend the creation of the world. Intention presupposes knowledge that the object does not exist and then a desire to bring it into existence. Without such knowledge, intention is impossible, so the world could not be created through divine will. This horn suggests that the world must be eternal.

The second horn (which is cut off) presumably argues that if God does know particulars, then His knowledge depends on the occurrence of those particulars, making His essence dependent on created things, which is impossible. The argument is incomplete here; the full argument would continue to show contradiction.

Correction: This is a dialectical argument; Razi will later refute it. It is presented to test the opposing view, not as a final conclusion.

The Third Argument Against Divine Knowledge of Particulars
The third proof that God's knowledge of particulars would entail dependence and impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the third argument against God's knowledge of particulars and why it entails impossibility.

Core Idea
If God’s knowledge depends on external objects, then God would be contingent.

Sanusi presents the third argument against God's knowledge of particulars. The argument is a chain of dependencies: if God's knowledge of particulars depends on the existence of those particulars, then God's essence becomes dependent on external things. This would contradict God's necessary existence. Let's follow the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Try to grasp the overall flow of the argument.
Notice that it begins with a general principle: knowledge follows the known.
2
Identify the logical premises
Identify the key premises: (1) Knowledge follows the known. (2) God necessarily knows particulars. (3) Knowledge of particulars depends on the occurrence of those particulars. (4) Dependence on another implies contingency.
These premises form the backbone of the argument.
3
Trace the chain of dependence
Follow how the argument links the premises: God's knowledge depends on particulars → particulars depend on external objects → God's essence depends on external objects → God would be contingent → contradiction with necessary existence.
The conclusion is that God's knowledge of particulars is impossible.
4
Consider the author's note
Note that Sanusi remarks that these three arguments have been exhaustively presented and answered elsewhere. This indicates that the argument is not final; it is part of a dialectic.
This prepares you for responses that will be discussed later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن العلم بأن الشيء الفلاني موجود ، وبأن الشيء الفلاني معدوم : تبع للمعلوم. فلو كان واجب الاتصاف بالعلم بالجزئيات [ وقد ثبت : أن العلم بالجزئيات ] متوقف على وقوع تلك الجزئيات ، على تلك الوجوه المخصوصة . فحينئذ ذاته مما يمتنع وجودها إلا عند حصول تلك العلوم . ثم إن تلك العلوم متوقفة التحقق على حصول تلك المعلومات الخارجية ، والموقوف على الموقوف على الشيء [ موقوف على الشيء ] فيلزم أن تكون ذاته موقوفة على الغير، والموقوف على الغير : ممكن لذاته ، فيلزم أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته . وهو محال . واعلم أن هذه الوجوه الثلاثة في نفي علمه تعالى بالجزئيات قد سبق ذكرها بالاستقصاء مع الأجوبة الوافية
The third: Knowledge that a certain thing exists and that a certain thing does not exist follows the known. So if He is necessarily described by knowledge of particulars, and it has been established that knowledge of particulars is dependent on the occurrence of those particulars in their specific modes, then at that time His essence would be impossible of existence except when those knowledges occur. Then those knowledges are dependent for their realization on the occurrence of those external known things, and that which is dependent on that which is dependent on a thing is dependent on the thing. So it follows that His essence would be dependent on another, and that which is dependent on another is possible in itself, so it follows that the Necessary of Existence in Himself [would be possible] – and that is absurd. And know that these three arguments regarding the negation of His knowledge of particulars have been previously mentioned exhaustively with sufficient answers.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a proof (the third of a set) against God's knowledge of particulars. The argument runs: knowledge of existence or non-existence follows the object of knowledge. If God is necessarily described by knowledge of particulars, and such knowledge depends on the occurrence of those particulars in their specific modes, then God's essence cannot exist except when those knowledges exist. But those knowledges themselves depend on external objects. So God's essence becomes dependent on external things. Whatever is dependent on another is possible in itself, which would imply that the Necessary Existence is contingent — an impossibility. The author then notes that these three arguments have been fully presented and answered elsewhere.

العلم بالجزئيات

موقوف على الغير
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about determinism or predestination.
Correction: The argument is not about determinism but about ontological dependence: God's knowledge would require external objects to exist, making God dependent on them.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The third argument shows that if God's knowledge of particulars depends on external objects, then God would be contingent, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The argument forces a choice: either God knows particulars without dependence, or He is not God. If God knows you intimately yet His essence remains unchanged, does that make His knowledge more terrifying or more comforting to you? Answer honestly before Him.

The First Horn: Without Knowledge of Particulars, Intention Is Impossible
First horn: no knowledge of particulars blocks intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the premise that God does not know particulars leads to the conclusion that He cannot intend to create the world, thus making Him a necessitating cause.

Core Idea
Without knowledge of particulars, God cannot intend creation.

The second proof in the text presents a dilemma regarding God's knowledge of particulars. The first horn considers the possibility that God does not know particulars. This lesson explores why, on that assumption, it becomes impossible for God to intend the creation of the world, leading to the conclusion that He must be a necessitating cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the first horn passage
Read the Arabic passage for the first horn (up to the point where it concludes impossibility of intention). Then read the translation.
Focus on the logical steps: assumption → impossibility of intention → necessitating cause.
2
Understand the conditional link
The argument says that to intend creation, God must know that the world is non-existent and then will it to become existent. If He lacks knowledge of particulars, that knowledge is impossible, so intention is impossible.
Notice the chain: no knowledge of particulars → no knowledge of world's non-existence → no intention to create.
3
Connect to the overall thesis
Without intention, God cannot act as a volitional agent; He must be a necessitating cause. This is one of two paths to the conclusion that the world is eternal.
The argument does not prove that God actually lacks knowledge; it only shows the consequence if that were true.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية من الوجوه المبنية على العلم : أن نقول : إما أن يكون الحق هو أنه سبحانه غير عالم بالجزئيات، وإما أن يكون هو أنه سبحانه عالم بالجزئيات . وعلى التقديرين فإنه يجب كونه موجباً بالذات . ثم يلزم من دوامه : دوام المعلومات . أما إذا كان الحق هو القسم الأول ، وهو أنه غير عالم بالجزئيات . فعل هذا التقدير ، يمتنع كونه قاصداً إيجاد العالم . لأن هذا القصد إنما يمكن حصوله ، لو كان عالماً بأنه معدوم ، ثم إنه يريد أن يجعله موجوداً . فعلى تقدير ان لا يكون عالماً بالجزئيات ، امتنع حصول هذا العلم ، فامتنع حصول القصد المذكور ، فامتنع منه أن يقصد إلى إيجاده وإحداثه وتكوينه . وأما إذا كان الحق هو القسم الثاني ، وهو أنه تعالى عالم بالجزئيات . فنقول : فهذا يقتضي كونه تعالى موجباً بالذات. وذلك يوجب دوام العالم بدوامه . وإنما قلنا : إن كونه تعالى عالماً بالجزئيات يقتضي كونه موجباً بالذات، لأنه لما كان عالماً بالجزئيات كان عالماً بأن الشيء الفلاني يقع في الوقت الفلاني على الصفة الفلانية ، وأن الشيء الفلاني لا يقع البتة ، وما علم الله وقوعه ، كان واجب الوقوع ، لأن عدم وقوعه ، يستلزم أن ينقلب علم الله جهلاً ، وهو محال . ومستلزم المحال : محال . فعدم وقوعه : محال ، فوقوعه : واجب . وبهذا الدليل أيضاً : ما علم الله عدم وقوعه : كان ممتنع الوقوع أيضاً . والشيء الذي يكون واجب الوقوع أو يكون ممتنع الوقوع ، لم يكن القادر متمكناً من فعله وتركه . وذلك يقدح في كونه تعالى قادراً ، ويقتضي كونه موجباً بالذات . وإذا ثبت أن كونه عالماً بالجزئيات ، يقتضي كونه تعالى موجباً بالذات ، وثبت أنه متى كان موجباً بالذات : لزم من دوام الموجب ، دوام الأثر . وذلك هو المطلوب
The second proof from the arguments based on knowledge: We say: Either the truth is that He, exalted is He, is not knowledgeable of particulars, or the truth is that He, exalted is He, is knowledgeable of particulars. On both suppositions, He must be a necessitating cause in essence. Then from His perpetuity follows the perpetuity of the known things. As for if the truth is the first division, i.e., that He is not knowledgeable of particulars, then on this supposition, it is impossible for Him to intend to bring the world into existence. For this intention can only occur if He knows that it is non-existent and then He wills to make it exist. So on the supposition that He is not knowledgeable of particulars, this knowledge is impossible, so the mentioned intention is impossible, so it is impossible for Him to intend its origination, creation, and formation. And as for if the truth is the second division, i.e., that He, exalted is He, is knowledgeable of particulars, we say: This entails that He, exalted is He, is a necessitating cause in essence. And that necessitates the perpetuity of the world along with His perpetuity. We only say that His being knowledgeable of particulars entails His being a necessitating cause in essence because since He is knowledgeable of particulars, He knows that a certain thing occurs at a certain time in a certain mode, and that a certain thing never occurs. And what God knows to occur is necessary of occurrence, because its non-occurrence would necessitate that God's knowledge turns into ignorance, which is impossible. And what necessitates the impossible is impossible. So its non-occurrence is impossible, thus its occurrence is necessary. And by this same proof also: what God knows not to occur is also impossible of occurrence. And a thing that is necessary of occurrence or impossible of occurrence cannot be that the powerful [agent] is able to do it or leave it. That impugns His being powerful, and necessitates His being a necessitating cause in essence. And if it is established that His being knowledgeable of particulars entails His being a necessitating cause in essence, and it is established that whenever He is a necessitating cause in essence, from the perpetuity of the cause follows the perpetuity of the effect. That is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a proof (the second argument based on knowledge) for God's necessitating causality and the eternity of the world. The author presents a dilemma: either God knows particulars or He does not. In the first case (no knowledge of particulars), He cannot intend to create the world because intention requires knowledge of the object's non-existence. In the second case (knowledge of particulars), His knowledge makes the occurrence of what He knows necessary; otherwise His knowledge would turn into ignorance, an impossibility. This necessity is then extended: what He knows to occur is necessary, what He knows not to occur is impossible. Such necessity removes God's power of choice (making Him a mere necessitating cause). The argument concludes that since God is knowledgeable of particulars, He must be a necessitating cause, and as a permanent cause, His effect (the world) must also be permanent, i.e., eternal.

موجب بالذات
قاصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument proves that God does not know particulars.
Correction: The argument only shows what would follow if God did not know particulars; it does not assert that He actually lacks such knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the direct reason why God cannot intend to create the world if He does not know particulars?
A) Because He lacks the power to create.
B) Because intention requires knowledge that the world is non-existent, which He cannot have without knowledge of particulars.
C) Because He is already a necessitating cause.
D) Because the world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The first horn shows that if God lacks knowledge of particulars, He cannot intend creation, which forces Him into being a necessitating cause, implying the world's eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God does not know your smallest actions and intentions, how would that change your worship? Yet the Quran declares He knows even the whisper. Does your daily life reflect that awareness, or do you live as if God does not see? Commit to one specific moment today where you will act with the certainty that He knows.
?
The philosophers argued that if God does not know particulars, He cannot intend anything. But you know He knows and intends. Then why do you sometimes doubt His wisdom in the events of your life? When was the last time you truly submitted to His intention even when it contradicted your own? Repent for the times you questioned His decree.
?
God's knowledge of your existence is the basis of His intention for you. If He intended you to be, then every moment of your life has purpose. Do you live as if your existence is accidental, or as if you were intentionally created for a mission? What would change if you fully internalized that you are not a random occurrence?

The Second Horn: Knowledge of Particulars Necessitates Determinism and Eternity
Second horn: knowledge of particulars necessitates occurrence and removes choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how God's knowledge of particulars leads to the necessity of events, removing His power of choice and making Him a necessitating cause, which in turn implies the world's eternity.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of particulars makes events necessary, stripping Him of free choice.

The second horn of the dilemma starts from the assumption that God knows all particulars. This lesson explores how such knowledge entails that what God knows to occur must occur necessarily, and what He knows not to occur is impossible. This necessity undermines divine power as free choice and renders God a necessitating cause, leading to the perpetuity of the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the second horn passage
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'وأما إذا كان الحق هو القسم الثاني' to the end, along with the translation.
Pay attention to the logical progression: knowledge of particulars → necessity of known events → denial of power → necessitating cause → perpetuity.
2
Analyze the necessity argument
The passage argues that if God knows an event will occur at a certain time, its non-occurrence would change His knowledge into ignorance, which is impossible. Therefore, the event must occur necessarily.
This is a modal argument: impossibility of reversal of knowledge leads to necessity of the known.
3
Connect to necessitating cause
Necessity removes the possibility of doing otherwise, thus God cannot be a powerful agent with free choice; He is a necessitating cause. And as a permanent cause, the effect (the world) must also be permanent.
This directly leads to the eternity of the world, the desired conclusion.

?
