Alternative Formulation: Necessity from Another Cause
Alternative argument tracing necessity to a cause other than knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the necessity of the world's occurrence is traced to a cause other than God's knowledge, establishing God as a necessitating cause.

Core Idea
The world's necessity comes not from knowledge but from God's essence or attribute.

This passage presents an alternative formulation of the argument from contingency. It begins with the world's possibility in itself, then uses the fact that God knows its occurrence to infer its necessity. However, since knowledge does not change the known, the necessity must originate from another cause, either God's essence or another attribute. This refines our understanding of divine causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the logical flow: possibility, knowledge, necessity, and the conclusion about the cause.
The passage builds on a previous argument but offers a new angle.
2
Identify the key logical steps
Break down the argument: 1) The world is possible in itself. 2) It is known to occur, so it must be necessary. 3) Knowledge does not change the essence, so the necessity cannot come from knowledge. 4) Therefore, it comes from another cause (God's essence or attribute).
This is a deductive chain, not an enumeration.
3
Reflect on the theological implications
Ask yourself: Why does this argument matter? It shows that God's knowledge is not the direct cause of existence; rather, His essence or another attribute (like power or will) is the necessitating cause.
This refines how we understand God's attributes in relation to creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا الكلام تقرير آخر ، وهو أن يقال : العالم ممكن الوجود لذاته . وقد بينا : أنه من حيث إنه معلوم الوقوع ، وجب أن يكون واجب الوقوع . والعلم لا يقلب ماهية المعلوم ولا يغيره. فهذا الوجوب ما جاء البتة من العلم ، بل جاء من سبب آخر . فهذا الوجوب يدل على أن سبباً آخر ، اقتضى صيرورة العالم ، واجب الوقوع . لأنه لما دل كونه معلوم الوقوع ، على كونه واجب الوقوع ، وثبت : أن المؤثر في هذا الوجود، ليس هو صفة العلم : وجب أن يكون المؤثر فيه : صفة أخرى . فيثبت : أنه تعالى موجب لوجود العالم ، إما لعين ذاته المخصوصة ، أو لصفة أخرى من صفاته. وعلى هذين التقديرين ، فالتقريب معلوم فيثبت : أنه تعالى إما أن يكون عالماً بالجزئيات
And for this statement there is another formulation, which is to say: The world is possible of existence in itself. And we have shown that insofar as it is known to occur, it must be necessary of occurrence. And knowledge does not change the essence of the known nor alter it. So this necessity came not at all from knowledge, but came from another cause. So this necessity indicates that another cause necessitated the world's becoming necessary of occurrence. For since its being known to occur indicates its being necessary of occurrence, and it is established that the effecting agent in this existence is not the attribute of knowledge, then the effecting agent in it must be another attribute. So it is established that He, exalted is He, is a necessitating cause for the existence of the world, either by His specific essence itself or by another of His attributes. And on both suppositions, the reasoning is known, and it is established that He, exalted is He, is either knowledgeable of particulars...
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is an alternative formulation of the previous proof. It starts from the world's contingency (possible in itself). It then uses the fact that the world is known to occur (by God) to conclude that its occurrence is necessary. However, knowledge does not change the essence of the known thing; therefore the necessity does not come from knowledge itself but from some other cause. This cause must be either God's essence or another attribute. Either way, it establishes that God is a necessitating cause. The passage ends by linking back to the dilemma about God's knowledge of particulars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How can you shift your reliance from your own knowledge to trust in God's causative power?

10 min
God's knowledge of particulars implies necessitarianism
Razi argues that God's knowledge of particulars necessitates His being an essential agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that God's knowledge of particulars leads to necessitarianism and the eternity of the world.

Core Idea
If God knows everything, nothing can be contingent—He becomes a necessary cause.

This passage presents a classical philosophical argument that challenges the traditional Islamic view of divine power. Razi contends that if God has complete knowledge of particulars, then whatever He knows to occur must necessarily occur, and whatever He knows not to occur must necessarily not occur. From this, he concludes that God is an essential necessitator (mujib bi al-dhat), which in turn implies the co-eternity of the world with God. The argument proceeds step by step, building a logical chain that you will unpack in this lesson.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Let the argument unfold in its own terms before analyzing it.
2
Identify the main claim and conclusion
What is Razi trying to prove? Identify the conclusion (that God is an essential necessitator and the world is eternal) and the key premise (God's knowledge of particulars).
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow the steps: from knowledge of occurrence to necessary occurrence, from knowledge of non-occurrence to impossibility, then to the negation of power and necessitarianism.
Notice the use of impossibility arguments (entailment of ignorance).
4
Consider the theological implications
Reflect on how this argument undermines divine power (qudra) as commonly understood in Islamic theology. Why does Razi think this is a problem?
This argument was controversial and refuted by later theologians.
5
Examine the common misunderstanding
Many think this argument only attacks God's power, but its ultimate aim is to prove the world's eternity. Discuss how the two are connected in Razi's reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كونه تعالى موجباً بالذات. وذلك يوجب دوام العالم بدوامه . وإنما قلنا : إن كونه تعالى عالماً بالجزئيات يقتضي كونه موجباً بالذات، لأنه لما كان عالماً بالجزئيات كان عالماً بأن الشيء الفلاني يقع في الوقت الفلاني على الصفة الفلانية ، وأن الشيء الفلاني لا يقع البتة ، وما علم الله وقوعه ، كان واجب الوقوع ، لأن عدم وقوعه ، يستلزم أن ينقلب علم الله جهلاً ، وهو محال . ومستلزم المحال : محال . فعدم وقوعه : محال ، فوقوعه : واجب . وبهذا الدليل أيضاً : ما علم الله عدم وقوعه : كان ممتنع الوقوع أيضاً . والشيء الذي يكون واجب الوقوع أو يكون ممتنع الوقوع ، لم يكن القادر متمكناً من فعله وتركه . وذلك يقدح في كونه تعالى قادراً ، ويقتضي كونه موجباً بالذات . وإذا ثبت أن كونه عالماً بالجزئيات ، يقتضي كونه تعالى موجباً بالذات ، وثبت أنه متى كان موجباً بالذات : لزم من دوام الموجب ، دوام الأثر . وذلك هو المطلوب
That He, exalted, is an essential necessitator. And that necessitates the eternity of the world with His eternity. We only say: that He, exalted, is knowing of particulars necessitates that He is an essential necessitator, because since He is knowing of particulars, He knows that such-and-such a thing occurs at such-and-such time in such-and-such manner, and that such-and-such a thing does not occur at all. And what God knows to occur is necessarily occurring, because its non-occurrence would entail that God's knowledge turns into ignorance, which is impossible. And that which entails the impossible is impossible. So its non-occurrence is impossible, therefore its occurrence is necessary. And by this same proof: what God knows not to occur is also impossible of occurrence. And a thing that is necessarily occurring or impossible of occurrence, the capable agent is not able to do it or leave it. And that impugns His being powerful, and necessitates that He is an essential necessitator. And when it is established that His being knowing of particulars necessitates His being an essential necessitator, and it is established that whenever He is an essential necessitator, the perpetuity of the effect follows from the perpetuity of the cause. That is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage presents a proof against divine power (qudra) from God's knowledge of particulars, a classical Ash'arite argument refuted by Razi. The structure: (1) God's knowledge of particulars implies necessary occurrence of what He knows; (2) impossible occurrence of what He knows not; (3) thus nothing is possible for Him, negating power; (4) thus He is an essential necessitator (mujib bi al-dhat), leading to the eternity of the world. The argument proceeds step by step: if God knows X will occur, X must occur, otherwise His knowledge would be ignorance (impossible). Therefore X is necessary. Similarly, what He knows not to occur is impossible. Hence nothing is contingent for God, so He cannot be truly powerful (qadir), but rather is a necessary cause. If He is an essential necessitator, then the world, as His effect, must be co-eternal with Him.

Key Terms
موجب بالذات
عالم بالجزئيات
واجب الوقوع
ممتنع الوقوع
قادر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument only aims to refute God's power.
Correction: The argument's ultimate goal is to prove the eternity of the world, using the denial of power as a stepping stone.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what follows from God's knowledge that a thing will occur?
A) The thing is possible
B) The thing is necessarily occurring
C) The thing is impossible
D) God's power is confirmed

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of particulars forces a deterministic view, undermining divine power and implying the world's eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi's argument strips God of the ability to do otherwise, making Him a necessary cause rather than a free chooser. Does your heart accept a God who cannot choose differently? How does this affect your trust in His mercy and justice, if He is forced to act? And what will you do with this discomfort—will you search for a counter-argument or reconcile it with your faith?
?
If everything in your life—every choice you make, every event that befalls you—is necessitated by God's eternal knowledge, then your own sense of free will is an illusion. Does that conclusion terrify you or liberate you? And if it terrifies you, what specific belief about yourself or about God are you unwilling to surrender?
?
Razi's logic leads to the eternity of the world, contradicting the common Muslim belief in creation from nothing. Suppose you accept his reasoning as sound—would you give up the doctrine of creatio ex nihilo? Or would you reject his premise about knowledge? Which sacrifice is harder for you to make, and why?

Razi's Third Argument: Eternal Knowledge Requires Eternal Objects
Razi argues for eternal existence of known essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that God's eternal knowledge of essences necessitates their pre-eternal existence outside of God.

Core Idea
Distinct knowledge demands distinct objects; eternal knowledge demands eternal objects.

This passage presents one of the most famous philosophical arguments for the pre-eternity of the world: the argument from God's knowledge. Fakhr al-Din al-Razi builds on the premise that God knows all essences from eternity. He then argues that for such knowledge to be real, the known objects must have a distinct reality of their own, and cannot be mere non-existence or properties of God Himself. This leads to the conclusion that the essences (and thus the world) have existed eternally alongside God.

Guided Path
1
Understand the starting premise
Razi begins with the agreed fact that God knew all essences from eternity. Consider why this is an acceptable starting point for his argument.
The premise is accepted by both theologians and philosophers.
2
Follow the distinction argument
Notice how Razi argues that knowledge requires distinction, and pure non-existence cannot have distinction. Therefore essences must exist.
This step establishes that the essences are real, not mere mental concepts.
3
Examine the location argument
Razi shows that these essences cannot exist in God's essence (otherwise opposites and corporeality), so they must exist externally. Finally, since knowledge is eternal, the objects must be eternal.
The two reasons against internal existence are opposites and corporeality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة من الوجوه المبنية على العلم : أن نقول : هذه الماهيات كانت معلومة الله في الأزل بالاتفاق ، وكل ما كان معلوماً ، فإنه لا بد وأن يكون ذلك المعلوم : متميزاً عما عداه بخاصيته المخصوصة، وماهيته النوعية، ومتى حصل هذا الامتياز بين المايهات، وجب كونها ثابتة . لأن النفي المحض ، والعدم الصرف، لا يحصل فيه الامتياز والاختلاف . فيثبت : أن هذه الماهيات ، كانت موجودة في الأزل. فنقول : إنها إما أن تكون موجودة في ذات العالم، أو كانت موجودة وجوداً مبايناً عن ذات من كان عالماً بها والأول باطل . لأن العلم بالسواد والبياض والاستقامة والاستدارة، لو كان موقوفاً على حصول هذه الماهيات ، في ذات العالم بها : لزم أن يكون ذلك العالم أسود ، أبيض مستقيماً ، ومستديراً ، لأنه لا معنى للأسود والأبيض ، إلا الذي حصل فيه السواد والبياض ، إلا أن ذلك : محال . لوجهين : الأول : إنه يوجب اجتماع الضدين في المحل الواحد . وهو محال . الثاني : إن من كان أسود ، أبيض ، مستقيماً ، مستديراً : كان جسماً ، وذلك في حق واجب الوجود لذاته محال . فيثبت : أن هذه الماهيات موجودة خارجة عن ذات الله تعالى. ولما ثبت أن العلم بهذه الماهيات يتوقف على وجود هذه المعلومات ، وثبت أن العلم بها حاصل في الأزل : وجب القطع بحصول هذه المعلومات في الأزل . وهو مطلوب
The third argument from the proofs based on knowledge: that we say: These essences were known to God from eternity by agreement. And everything that is known must be that the known is distinguished from what is other than it by its specific property and specific quiddity. And when this distinction among essences occurs, their being established becomes necessary. Because pure negation and sheer non-existence do not admit distinction and difference. So it is established that these essences existed from eternity. Then we say: They either exist in the essence of the knower, or they exist in an existence separate from the essence of the one who knows them. The first is false. Because knowledge of blackness, whiteness, straightness, and roundness, if it were dependent on the occurrence of these essences in the essence of the knower of them, it would necessitate that that knower be black, white, straight, and round, because there is no meaning of 'black' and 'white' except that in which blackness and whiteness occur. But that is impossible for two reasons: First, it necessitates the conjunction of opposites in one locus, which is impossible. Second, one who is black, white, straight, and round would be a body, and that is impossible in the case of the Necessary Existent in essence. So it is established that these essences exist outside the essence of God, exalted. And since it is established that knowledge of these essences depends on the existence of these known things, and it is established that knowledge of them is obtained from eternity, it must be definitively concluded that these known things exist from eternity. That is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument based on knowledge. It starts with the agreed premise that essences (mahiyyat) were known to God from eternity. For a thing to be known, it must be distinct from others by its specific property and quiddity. Pure non-existence (al-'adam al-sirf) cannot have distinction. Therefore these essences must have existed from eternity. The question is: where? They cannot exist in God's essence because that would make God black, white, etc., impossible for two reasons: (1) opposites would coexist in Him; (2) He would be a body. Thus they exist externally. Since knowledge of them depends on their existence, and eternal knowledge implies eternal existence, the essences must be eternal. This proves the pre-eternity of the world's constituents.

ماهيات
معلوم في الأزل
امتياز
نفي محض
عدم صرف
واجب الوجود لذاته

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge does not require the existence of the known object; He can know non-existents.
Correction: Razi argues that distinct knowledge requires a distinct positive reality; pure non-existence cannot be known with distinction because it has no properties.

Checkpoint
Why do the essences known to God from eternity have to exist externally?
A) Because pure non-existence can have distinction
B) Because they cannot exist in God's essence due to impossibility of opposites and corporeality
C) Because knowledge does not depend on existence
D) Because God's essence contains all colors

Key Takeaway
God's eternal knowledge of distinct essences proves their pre-eternal existence outside of God.

?
This argument concludes that the essences are eternal and exist outside God. Does that diminish God's absolute sovereignty in your mind? How do you reconcile this with the Islamic belief in God's complete independence and creation ex nihilo? What do you truly believe about God's freedom in creation?

The Third Argument: Pre-Eternal Essences External to God
Razi presents the third argument for the pre-eternity of essences external to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi argues that pre-eternal essences must be distinct from God and exist externally to His essence, based on the nature of knowledge and the requirement of distinction.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of essences forces their real existence outside His essence.

Al-Razi presents a sophisticated argument: since God knew the essences in pre-eternity, they must be truly distinct from Him. The argument proceeds by examining where those essences could exist—either in God's essence or outside it. This lesson unpacks the reasoning step by step, showing why the internal option is impossible and concluding that the essences are pre-eternal and external to God.

Guided Path
1
Understand the starting premise
Begin with the agreed fact that God knew the essences in pre-eternity. This is the foundation of the argument.
2
Recognize that knowledge requires distinction
Anything known must be distinguished from other things by its specific property and essence. Pure nothingness cannot be distinguished.
3
Explore the two possible locations for essences
If the essences are real, they must exist either in God's essence or externally. Consider both options.
4
See why internal existence is absurd
If essences were in God, then blackness would make Him black, etc.—leading to opposites coexisting and making God a body. Both are impossible.
5
Conclude that essences exist externally and are pre-eternal
Since internal existence is impossible, essences must be external. And since God's knowledge of them is pre-eternal, they must have existed pre-eternally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة من الوجوه المبنية على العلم : أن نقول : هذه الماهيات كانت معلومة الله في الأزل بالاتفاق ، وكل ما كان معلوماً ، فإنه لا بد وأن يكون ذلك المعلوم : متميزاً عما عداه بخاصيته المخصوصة، وماهيته النوعية، ومتى حصل هذا الامتياز بين المايهات، وجب كونها ثابتة . لأن النفي المحض ، والعدم الصرف، لا يحصل فيه الامتياز والاختلاف . فيثبت : أن هذه الماهيات ، كانت موجودة في الأزل. فنقول : إنها إما أن تكون موجودة في ذات العالم، أو كانت موجودة وجوداً مبايناً عن ذات من كان عالماً بها والأول باطل . لأن العلم بالسواد والبياض والاستقامة والاستدارة، لو كان موقوفاً على حصول هذه الماهيات ، في ذات العالم بها : لزم أن يكون ذلك العالم أسود ، أبيض مستقيماً ، ومستديراً ، لأنه لا معنى للأسود والأبيض ، إلا الذي حصل فيه السواد والبياض ، إلا أن ذلك : محال . لوجهين : الأول : إنه يوجب اجتماع الضدين في المحل الواحد . وهو محال . الثاني : إن من كان أسود ، أبيض ، مستقيماً ، مستديراً : كان جسماً ، وذلك في حق واجب الوجود لذاته محال . فيثبت : أن هذه الماهيات موجودة خارجة عن ذات الله تعالى. ولما ثبت أن العلم بهذه الماهيات يتوقف على وجود هذه المعلومات ، وثبت أن العلم بها حاصل في الأزل : وجب القطع بحصول هذه المعلومات في الأزل . وهو مطلوب
The third proof from the aspects based on knowledge: that we say: These essences were known to God in pre-eternity by agreement. And everything that is known, it is necessary that that known thing be distinguished from what is other than it by its specific property and its specific essence. And when this distinction occurs among the essences, their being established is necessary. Because pure negation and absolute non-existence do not admit distinction and difference. So it is established that these essences were existent in pre-eternity. Then we say: They are either existent in the essence of the knower, or they exist with an existence separate from the essence of the one who knows them. The first is false. Because knowledge of blackness and whiteness and straightness and roundness, if it were dependent on the occurrence of these essences in the essence of the knower, it would necessitate that that knower be black, white, straight, and round. Because there is no meaning of 'black' except that in which blackness occurs, and 'white' except that in which whiteness occurs. However, that is impossible for two reasons: First, it necessitates the conjunction of two opposites in one locus, which is impossible. Second, whoever is black, white, straight, round would be a body, and that is impossible in the case of the Necessary Existent in His essence. So it is established that these essences exist outside the essence of God the Exalted. And since it is established that knowledge of these essences depends on the existence of these known things, and it is established that knowledge of them is obtained in pre-eternity, it is necessary to definitively conclude that these known things were obtained in pre-eternity. And that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a proof presented by al-Razi as the third argument based on divine knowledge. The argument proceeds step by step: (1) It is agreed that God knew the essences (al-mahiyyat) in pre-eternity. (2) Anything known must be distinguished from others by its specific property and specific essence; otherwise it would be pure negation and absolute non-existence, which admits no distinction. Therefore these essences must be established (thābitah) in pre-eternity—i.e., they have some reality. (3) Now this existence must be either in the essence of the knower (God) or separate from Him. The first option is refuted: if the essence of blackness were in God's essence, then God would be black, etc. That leads to two impossibilities: (a) conjunction of opposites (black and white together) in one locus, and (b) God would be a body, which is impossible for the Necessary Existent. Therefore the essences must exist externally, outside God's essence. (4) Since God's knowledge of them in pre-eternity depends on their existence, and His knowledge is certain, their existence in pre-eternity is also certain. This proves that the essences are pre-eternal and distinct from God.

الماهيات
معلوم
متميز
الامتياز
النفي المحض
العدم الصرف
واجب الوجود

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge does not require any reality in the objects of knowledge.
Correction: Al-Razi's argument shows that for God's pre-eternal knowledge, the essences must be really distinct and have a mode of existence, because knowledge involves distinction, which is impossible in pure nothingness.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Razi reject the possibility that the pre-eternal essences exist in God's own essence?
A) Because God's essence cannot contain multiplicity.
B) Because it would require God to be black, white, straight, and round, leading to absurdities.
C) Because the essences are too many to be contained.
D) Because God has no attributes.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's third argument establishes that essences are pre-eternal and distinct from God because they are known and must be distinguished, and that distinction cannot be in God's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's eternal knowledge of every detail of your life implies that your essence is eternally distinct and real to Him, how does that affect your sense of being known—does it comfort you or make you uneasy? What will you do today to live in light of being fully known by God?
?
Al-Razi argues that God cannot contain opposites like black and white within His essence. Have you ever described God in human terms without realizing it—perhaps attributing to Him changing emotions or contradictory intentions? What step can you take to purify your understanding of God's perfection?
?
The essences are pre-eternal because God's knowledge of them is pre-eternal. If God's knowledge of your actions is eternal, then your deeds are eternally known to Him. How does this reality challenge you to align your choices with His eternal knowledge? What specific habit will you change starting today?

Objection: Does Knowledge Require the Known to Exist and Be Distinct?
Objection that knowledge does not require existence or distinctness of the known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three-part objection that knowledge does not require the known to exist or be distinct, and how al-Razi begins to respond.

Core Idea
Knowledge of impossibilities shows the known need not exist.

In this passage, an objector challenges a key premise: that knowledge depends on the existence and distinctness of the known object. Using clever examples—like our knowledge that a partner of God is impossible, or our vague knowledge of a foster sister—the objection pushes back against strict realism. al-Razi's response begins by granting God's pre-eternal knowledge and questioning the timing of distinction, setting up a deeper discussion on divine omniscience.

Guided Path
1
Read the full Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once through without pausing. Let the rhythm of the objection sink in. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
The passage is cut off mid-sentence; the response continues on the next page.
2
Identify the three objections
Look for the three distinct arguments the objector makes. The first uses the example of the impossible partner of God, the second uses the example of a foster sister, and the third questions the timing of distinction.
Each objection is introduced by a key phrase: 'فإن قيل', 'ثم نقول', and 'ونقول'.
3
Analyze the first objection
Focus on how the objector argues that knowledge of an impossible thing (partner of God) does not require that thing to exist. Notice the contradiction they derive if existence were required.
This challenges the common-sense view that the object of knowledge must be real.
4
See how al-Razi begins his response
Observe that al-Razi starts by conceding that God knows things in pre-eternity. He then questions the necessity of simultaneous distinctness, suggesting future distinction suffices for eternal knowledge.
The response is incomplete here; it will be continued in the next lesson.

فإن قيل : لا نسلم أن العلم بالشيء ، يتوقف على كون ذلك المعلوم موجوداً . والدليل عليه : أنا إذا علمنا أن شريك الإله : ممتنع . فهذا الحكم لا يمكن حصوله إلا بعد تصور ، أن شريك الإله ما هو ؟ فلو كان حصول هذا التصور ، موقوفاً على حصول المتصور ، لزم أن يكون شريك الإله موجوداً . وعلى هذا التقدير، يلزم من الحكم على شريك الإله بكونه ممتنعاً : الحكم عليه بكونه واجب الحصول . وذلك متناقض . ثم نقول : لا نزاع أن الله تعالى كان عالماً بحقائق الأشياء في الأزل . لكن لم قلتم : إن العلم بالشيء ، يستدعي كون المعلوم متميزاً عن غيره ، في علم ذلك العالم ؟ ألا ترى أنا إذا علمنا : أن لنا في هذا البلد أختاً من الرضاع ، مع أنا لا نعلمها بعينها . فههنا العلم بهذا المعلوم : حاصل . مع أن هذا المعلوم ليس متميزاً عن غيره في علم ذلك العالم . فيثبت : العلم بالشيء ، لا يقتضي كون المعلوم متميزا عن غيره ، في علم ذلك العالم . ونقول : سلمنا أنه لا بد وأن يكون المعلوم متميزاً عن غيره ، في علم العالم فلم قلتم : إن ذلك الإمتياز يجب حصوله حال حصول العلم؟ ولم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه يكفي في حصول العلم في الحال ، حصول ذلك التميز ، إما في الحال ، وإما في المستقبل ؟ وبيانه : أنه تعالي عالم بحقائق الجواهر والأعراض في الأزل ثم إن الجواهر والأعراض، وإن لم تكن حاصلة في الأزل ولا متميزاً بعضها عن بعض في الأزل ، إلا أنه مما ستوجد في لا يزال . ثم إنها إذا صارت موجودة وحاصلة ، كان بعضها متميزاً عن البعض . فلم قلتم : إن هذا القدر من الامتياز ، لا يكفي في حصول العلم الأزلي ، بكون هذه
If it is said: We do not grant that knowledge of a thing depends on that known thing being existent. The evidence for that: if we know that the partner of God is impossible, this judgment cannot be obtained except after conceiving what the partner of God is. So if the occurrence of this conception were dependent on the occurrence of the conceived, it would necessitate that the partner of God be existent. And on this assumption, judging the partner of God to be impossible would necessitate judging it to be necessary of attainment, which is contradictory. Then we say: There is no dispute that God the Exalted was knowing of the realities of things in pre-eternity. But why do you say that knowledge of a thing requires that the known be distinct from others in the knowledge of that knower? Do you not see that if we know that we have a sister by fosterage in this country, while we do not know her specifically—here knowledge of this known is obtained, while this known is not distinct from others in the knowledge of that knower. So it is established that knowledge of a thing does not require that the known be distinct from others in the knowledge of that knower. And we say: We grant that it is necessary that the known be distinct from others in the knowledge of the knower. But why do you say that this distinction must occur at the time of obtaining knowledge? And why is it not permissible to say that it suffices for obtaining knowledge in the present that this distinction occurs either in the present or in the future? Its explanation: He the Exalted is knowing of the realities of substances and accidents in pre-eternity. Then, even if substances and accidents are not obtained in pre-eternity nor distinct from each other in pre-eternity, yet they are among what will come to exist in the future. Then when they become existent and obtained, some of them become distinct from others. So why do you say that this amount of distinction is not sufficient for obtaining eternal knowledge of these...
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage contains an objection (al-iʿtirad) and the beginning of al-Razi's response. The objector raises three points: (1) Knowledge does not require the existence of the known. Example: we know that a partner of God (sharīk al-ilāh) is impossible. This knowledge requires a concept of that partner. If the concept required the existence of the partner, then the partner would have to exist, leading to contradiction because we judge it impossible yet it would be necessary. (2) Even if existence were granted, knowledge does not require that the known be distinct from others in the knowledge of the knower. Example: knowing we have a foster-sister without knowing her identity. (3) Even if distinction is required, it need not be simultaneous with knowledge; future distinction suffices. God knows substances and accidents in pre-eternity even though they only become distinct later. The response begins by conceding that God knows realities in pre-eternity and then addresses each point. The text cuts off mid-response, but we see the author arguing that future distinction is sufficient for eternal knowledge.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that for knowledge to be valid, the object known must exist at the time of knowing.
Correction: The objector shows that we can have knowledge of impossible things (like a partner of God) through concepts, so existence is not always required.

Checkpoint
What does the first objection claim about knowledge of an impossible thing like a partner of God?
A) It proves the partner exists.
B) It shows knowledge does not require the known to exist.
C) It shows God cannot know impossibilities.
D) It shows knowledge requires the known to be distinguished.

Key Takeaway
The objection challenges the necessity of existence and distinctness for knowledge, but al-Razi begins to show how future distinctness suffices for eternal divine knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that Allah knows everything, including every choice you will ever make. If His knowledge does not require those choices to exist yet, does that make you feel more accountable for your future actions, or does it make you feel like they are already determined? How does this passage challenge your understanding of divine foreknowledge and your own responsibility?
?
Consider the example of knowing a foster sister without knowing her identity. Are there aspects of Allah's attributes that you claim to know in a general sense (e.g., His mercy, His justice) without having a distinct, concrete conception of them? ?
The objection uses the impossibility of a partner of God to argue that conception does not require existence. Have you ever let your mind wander to conceptions of Allah that are not true to His nature—such as imagining Him in human form or attributing to Him limitations? How does this passage warn you about the danger of conceiving things that are impossible for Allah, and what will you do to guard your thoughts?

Objection to Knowledge Requiring Existence and the Milk-Sister Response
Objection and response about knowledge not requiring existence or distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that knowledge does not require the existence of the known, and the response using the example of an unknown milk-sister to demonstrate that knowledge can occur without the known being distinguished.

Core Idea
Knowledge does not depend on the existence or distinction of its object.

This lesson presents a classic kalam objection and response regarding the relationship between knowledge and its object. The objector argues that knowing impossible things like the partner of God shows that knowledge does not require existence. The responder counters with the example of knowing one has a milk-sister without knowing her identity, demonstrating that knowledge can exist without the known being distinguished.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage (beginning with 'فإن قيل') which presents the objector's argument. Notice the reasoning: knowledge of an impossible thing (partner of God) does not depend on that thing existing, because then the impossible would have to exist.
2
Understand the argument from impossibility
The objector uses the example of knowing that the partner of God is impossible. He argues that this judgment requires a conception of what a partner of God is. If that conception depended on the existence of the partner, then the partner would have to exist, which is contradictory. This is supposed to prove that knowledge does not require existence.
3
Read the response and the milk-sister example
Now read the response ('ثم نقول'). The responder concedes God's eternal knowledge but questions the requirement that the known be distinguished. He provides the example of knowing that one has a milk-sister in a city without knowing her specifically. This shows that knowledge can be present without the object being distinguished from other things.
4
Reflect on how the example refutes the objection
Consider how the milk-sister example addresses the objector's point. The objector assumed that knowledge requires existence or distinction. The responder shows that general knowledge (e.g., that there is a milk-sister) does not require the specific known to be distinguished. This undermines the objector's requirement.
5
Connect to the broader issue of God's knowledge
Think about how this discussion relates to God's eternal knowledge. The passage deals with whether God's knowledge of things in eternity requires those things to be existent or distinguished. The responder is arguing that it does not, using the milk-sister analogy to illustrate that knowledge can precede distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لا نسلم أن العلم بالشيء يتوقف على كون ذلك المعلوم موجوداً. والدليل عليه : أنا إذا علمنا أن شريك الإله ممتنع. فهذا الحكم لا يمكن حصوله إلا بعد تصور أن شريك الإله ما هو؟ فلو كان حصول هذا التصور موقوفاً على حصول المتصور، لزم أن يكون شريك الإله موجوداً. وعلى هذا التقدير، يلزم من الحكم على شريك الإله بكونه ممتنعاً الحكم عليه بكونه واجب الحصول. وذلك متناقض. ثم نقول : لا نزاع أن الله تعالى كان عالماً بحقائق الأشياء في الأزل. لكن لم قلتم : إن العلم بالشيء يستدعي كون المعلوم متميزاً عن غيره في علم ذلك العالم؟ ألا ترى أنا إذا علمنا أن لنا في هذا البلد أختاً من الرضاع مع أنا لا نعلمها بعينها، فههنا العلم بهذا المعلوم حاصل مع أن هذا المعلوم ليس متميزاً عن غيره في علم ذلك العالم. فيثبت : العلم بالشيء لا يقتضي كون المعلوم متميزاً عن غيره في علم ذلك العالم.
If it is said: We do not accept that knowledge of something depends on that known thing being existent. The evidence for it is: when we know that the partner of God is impossible, this judgment cannot be obtained except after a conception of what the partner of God is. So if this conception were dependent on the existence of the conceived thing, then the partner of God would have to exist. On this assumption, judging the partner of God to be impossible would entail judging it to be necessary of existence. That is contradictory. Then we say: There is no dispute that God Almighty knew the realities of things in eternity. But why do you say that knowledge of something requires that the known be distinguished from others in the knowledge of that knower? Do you not see that when we know that we have a milk-sister in this city while we do not know her specifically, here knowledge of this known is obtained while this known is not distinguished from others in the knowledge of that knower. So it is established: knowledge of something does not require the known to be distinguished from others in the knowledge of that knower.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage presents an initial objection and the first part of the response. The objector argues that knowledge does not depend on the existence of the known, using the example of knowing that the partner of God is impossible. The responder then argues that there is no dispute about God's eternal knowledge, but questions the requirement that the known be distinguished in the knower's knowledge, providing the example of knowing one has a milk-sister without knowing her identity, showing that knowledge can occur without distinction.

Key Terms
علم
معلوم
وجود
تميز
شريك الإله
تصور
أخت الرضاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the responder is denying that God's knowledge requires any distinction among objects, but the example only shows that distinction is not necessary for general knowledge, not that God's knowledge is general.
Correction: The responder does not deny that God's knowledge is detailed; rather, he refutes the claim that knowledge requires the object to be distinguished in the knower's mind at the moment of knowledge, using the milk-sister as an example of general knowledge.

Checkpoint
What example does the responder use to show that knowledge can occur without the known being distinguished?
A) Knowing the partner of God is impossible.
B) Knowing one has a milk-sister without knowing her identity.
C) Knowing the realities of substances and accidents.
D) Knowing that contradictories cannot coexist.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of something does not require that the known be existent or distinguished from others in the knower's knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to know that God exists. But have you ever truly examined whether your knowledge of Him depends on your own existence or on His? If God ceased to exist (impossible), would your knowledge vanish? This passage forces you to question the foundations of your belief. Do you really know God as He is, or only as a concept in your mind? Commit to reflecting on the difference between conceptual knowledge and existential encounter.
?
You have knowledge of many things you have never seen: the Prophet ﷺ, Paradise, Hellfire. But is your knowledge of them merely intellectual, or does it shape your actions? The milk-sister example shows knowledge without distinction—do you treat your knowledge of the unseen as a vague notion or as a reality that demands distinction in your life? This week, pick one unseen reality and act as if you knew it with certainty, as if it were distinguished before you.
?
The objector argues that knowledge does not require existence, using the impossible partner of God. But you, in your daily life, often assume that what you know must exist or be real. How many times have you judged something as 'impossible' based on your limited knowledge, only to realize later it was possible? This lesson challenges you to humble your epistemic certainty. Write down one belief you hold that might be based on an assumption of existence or distinction, and ask yourself: is my knowledge of it truly independent of these conditions?

Distinction for Knowledge May Be Deferred to the Future
Response: distinction for knowledge may be future.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the distinction required for knowledge need not be simultaneous and how this argument defends God's eternal knowledge of future contingent things.

Core Idea
Future distinction suffices for present knowledge.

This passage presents a key response in the debate about God's eternal knowledge. The opponent argues that for God to know something, it must be distinguished from others at the moment of knowledge. Our responder grants the need for distinction but insists it need not be simultaneous; future distinction is enough. This opens a path to reconcile eternal knowledge with future contingents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Let the argument's flow register. Then read the translation and note the concessive opening 'sallamnā' (we grant).
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the central claim
The responder asks why distinction must be simultaneous with knowledge. Find the sentence that proposes an alternative: it can be future.
Look for 'wa-lam qultum inna dhālika al-imtiyāz yajibu husūluhu hāla husūli al-ʿilm'.
3
Analyze the example
The example of substances and accidents shows that God knows them in eternity even though they do not yet exist. Their future existence provides the needed distinction.
Notice how this solves the problem of God knowing future contingents.
4
Grasp the broader implication
This argument defends that God's eternal knowledge is compatible with changes in created things. It does not deny the need for distinction, only relaxes its timing.
Contrast with the opponent's insistence on simultaneity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقول : سلمنا أنه لا بد وأن يكون المعلوم متميزاً عن غيره في علم العالم، فلم قلتم إن ذلك الإمتياز يجب حصوله حال حصول العلم؟ ولم لا يجوز أن يقال إنه يكفي في حصول العلم في الحال حصول ذلك التميز إما في الحال وإما في المستقبل؟ وبيانه: أنه تعالى عالم بحقائق الجواهر والأعراض في الأزل، ثم إن الجواهر والأعراض وإن لم تكن حاصلة في الأزل ولا متميزاً بعضها عن بعض في الأزل إلا أنها مما ستوجد في لا يزال. ثم إنها إذا صارت موجودة وحاصلة كان بعضها متميزاً عن البعض. فلم قلتم إن هذا القدر من الامتياز لا يكفي في حصول العلم الأزلي بكون هذه الماهيات متباينة متميزة؟
And we say: We grant that the known must be distinguished from others in the knowledge of the knower. But why do you say that this distinction must occur at the time of knowledge? Why is it not permissible to say that for knowledge to occur in the present, it suffices that the distinction obtains either now or in the future? Explanation: He Exalted is knower of the realities of substances and accidents in eternity. Then substances and accidents, even if they are not present in eternity nor distinguished from each other in eternity, yet they are something that will exist in the future. Then when they become existent and present, some become distinguished from others. So why do you say that this amount of distinction is not sufficient for eternal knowledge of these essences being differentiated and distinct?
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is the second response by the responder, after granting that distinction is necessary. The responder argues that the distinction does not have to be present at the same time as knowledge; it could be future. He uses the example of God's knowledge of substances and accidents: God knows them in eternity, but they do not exist yet. Their distinction occurs when they become existent in the future. This future distinction suffices for eternal knowledge.

Key Terms
إمتياز
حال
جواهر
أعراض
أزل
ماهيات
متباينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Distinction must be simultaneous with knowledge.
Correction: The responder shows that future distinction suffices for present knowledge, as in the case of God's eternal knowledge of future things.

Checkpoint
According to this response, when can the distinction required for eternal knowledge occur?
A) Only at the time of knowledge.
B) In the future.
C) Simultaneously with knowledge.
D) Not at all.

Key Takeaway
The necessary distinction for knowledge can be deferred to the future existence of the known objects, preserving the possibility of eternal knowledge of future contingents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge of your future deeds suffices even though He does not see them now, how does that affect your view of your choices today? Will you live with the weight of that knowledge or ignore it?
?
The opponent demanded immediate distinction for knowledge. Do you, in your personal accountability, demand visible results from your faith now, or can you trust in God's knowledge of what is still hidden in your future?
?
What act of obedience or repentance have you been postponing because you think the 'right time' hasn't come? If future distinction suffices for God's eternal knowledge, does that excuse your delay? When will you act?

Established Does Not Entail Existent: The Case of Non-Existent as Established Thing
Argument that 'established' does not require existence, citing non-existents as established essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in Islamic theology, being 'established' (thabit) does not necessarily imply existence, as non-existent essences can be considered established in their own right.

Core Idea
Established does not mean existent — non-existent essences are still real in their nature.

This passage presents a key argument in the debate about divine attributes and the status of non-existents. The author grants that knowledge requires something to be established, but challenges the assumption that establishment entails existence. He cites a school of thought that non-existents can be established as essences apart from existence, raising the question of what evidence disproves this view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Pay attention to the flow: a concession followed by a challenge.
2
Identify the logical structure
Notice the two parts: granting that knowledge requires the object to be established, and then questioning why every established thing must be existent.
3
Understand the key claim
The group mentioned holds that a non-existent can be an established thing, meaning essences stripped of existence are still determined as essences.
This is not about weak existence but about the reality of quiddities.
4
Reflect on the challenge
The passage ends with a request for evidence against this view. Consider what implications this has for arguments about divine attributes and knowledge.
This challenge shifts the burden of proof to the opponent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : سلمنا أن العلم بالمعلوم يستدعي كون المعلوم ثابتاً. لكن لم قلتم : إن كل ثابت فإنه لا بد وأن يكون موجوداً؟ أما علمتم أن جماعة من العقلاء قالوا : المعدوم مع كونه معدوماً قد يكون شيئاً ثابتاً؟ وعنوا بذلك : أن الماهيات حال عرائها عن الوجود قد تكون متقررة في كونها ماهيات. فما الدليل على فساد هذه المقالة؟
Then we say: We grant that knowledge of the known requires the known to be established. But why do you say that every established thing must be existent? Do you not know that a group of rational people said: The non-existent while being non-existent may be an established thing? They meant by that that essences when stripped of existence may be determined in their being essences. So what is the evidence for the corruption of this statement?
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth response, which grants that the known must be established (thabit) but argues that being established does not require being existent. The responder points to a group of rational people who hold that the non-existent can be established as a thing, referring to the concept that essences (mahiyyat) when stripped of existence are still determined as essences. He then challenges the opponent to provide evidence against this view.

Key Terms
ثابت
موجود
معدوم
شيئ
ماهيات
عراء
تقرر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that 'established' (thabit) is synonymous with 'existent' (mawjud), so that a non-existent cannot be established.
Correction: The passage shows that 'established' refers to the determinate reality of an essence, which can hold even if the essence does not exist externally. Non-existents can be established in the sense of having a fixed quiddity.

Checkpoint
According to the group mentioned, what does it mean for the non-existent to be an established thing?
A) It has some kind of weak existence.
B) Essences when stripped of existence are determined as essences.
C) It is a mental concept only.
D) It is a contradiction in terms.

Key Takeaway
Being 'established' (thabit) does not entail being 'existent' (mawjud); non-existent essences can be established in their quiddity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If even your non-existent deeds have a real essence in God's knowledge, how does that affect the way you think about opportunities you haven't yet taken? Does it make you more deliberate in your intentions? Commit to one intention today that you will treat as 'established' and seek to purify.
?
The philosophers quoted in this lesson say that essences are 'determined in their being essences' even without existence. Does that make you reconsider what you consider 'real' in your life? Are there values or beliefs that are 'established' in your heart but not yet lived out? What is one such belief, and what step will you take to bring it into existence?
?
The opponent assumed that 'established' necessarily means 'existent'. In your own spiritual life, have you ever mistaken a state of being established (like knowing something is true) for actually living it? How can you move from knowing to acting, using this distinction?

Eternal Knowledge Requires Eternal Distinction of Objects
The author rejects the sufficiency of temporal distinction for eternal knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objects of God's eternal knowledge must be eternally distinct, based on the condition-conditioned priority principle.

Core Idea
Eternal knowledge cannot wait for future distinctions; the condition must be prior.

This passage answers a subtle objection: could God's eternal knowledge be about things that only become distinct later? The author calls this idea 'obviously corrupt' and explains why. As you read, pay attention to the logical relationship between condition (sharṭ) and conditioned (mashrūṭ), and how it applies to divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Note the phrase 'ẓāhir al-fasād' – a strong dismissal.
2
Identify the objection and the response
The passage begins with an objection ('Why not...?'). Identify it, then see how the author refutes it with the correspondence principle.
The objection is about future distinction (lā yazāl).
3
Follow the logical chain
Trace the steps: knowledge must match its object → if distinction is known, distinction must exist → existence of distinction is a condition → condition precedes conditioned in rank.
The key move is the application of the condition-conditioned priority.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this matters: God's knowledge is eternal, so all known distinctions must be eternal. This ties into discussions of divine attributes and the relationship between God and creation.
Think about how this argument supports the eternity of God's knowledge of particulars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: لم لا يكفي في حصول العلم الأزلي، حصول الامتياز في المعلومات في لا يزال؟ قلنا: هذا ظاهر الفساد. لأن العلم يجب أن يكون مطابقاً للمعلوم، وإلا لكان جهلاً. وعلى هذا التقدير، فيمتنع كون العالم عالماً بامتياز ذلك المعلوم عن غيره، إلا إذا كان ذلك المعلوم ممتازاً عن غيره. فلما كان كون المعلوم ممتازاً عن غيره شرطاً لتعلق العلم بامتيازه عن غيره، وثبت أن الشرط متقدم على المشروط في الرتبة، امتنع أن يكون حصول الامتياز في المعلومات متأخراً عن تعلق العلم بهذا الامتياز.
He said: 'Why is it not sufficient for the occurrence of eternal knowledge that distinction occurs in the known things in the future (lā yazāl)?' We say: This is obviously corrupt. Because knowledge must correspond to the known, otherwise it would be ignorance. On this basis, it is impossible for the knower to know the distinction of that known from others unless that known is distinct from others. Since the known being distinct from others is a condition for knowledge of its distinction from others, and it is established that the condition is prior in rank to the conditioned, it is impossible that the occurrence of distinction in the known things be posterior to the attachment of knowledge to that distinction.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage answers an objection: why can't eternal knowledge be about objects that become distinct only in the future? The author calls this 'obviously corrupt' (ẓāhir al-fasād). He reiterates the correspondence principle: knowledge must match its object. Thus, for God to know that X is distinct from Y, X must actually be distinct at the time of knowledge. Since God's knowledge is eternal, the distinction must be eternal as well. He uses the logical principle that the condition (sharṭ) is prior in rank (ruthba) to the conditioned (mashrūṭ). Therefore, distinction cannot occur after knowledge of it; it must precede or be simultaneous.

Key Terms
العلم الأزلي (al-ʿilm al-azalī)
لا يزال (lā yazāl)
ظاهر الفساد (ẓāhir al-fasād)
شرط (sharṭ)
مشروط (mashrūṭ)
الرتبة (al-ruthba)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that temporal succession is irrelevant to knowledge, so God's eternal knowledge can be about temporally distinct objects.
Correction: The author insists that for eternal knowledge, the condition (the object's distinction) must be co-eternal with the knowledge, not future.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the sufficiency of future distinction for eternal knowledge?
A) Because future events are uncertain
B) Because the condition must be prior in rank to the conditioned
C) Because God only knows present things
D) Because distinction is not required for knowledge

Key Takeaway
For eternal knowledge, the known must be distinct at the time of knowledge, so distinction must be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's eternal knowledge already distinguishes you from everyone else, why do you still look to others to define your worth? Will you let this truth silence the comparisons that feed your pride or your despair?
?
The argument demands that for God to know a thing, that thing must be fully distinct in His knowledge from eternity. Your worst sin, your secret thought, your future failure—God knows it now as a distinct reality. How does that change what you will do when that sin tempts you today?
?
You often imagine that God's knowledge is passive—merely watching events unfold. But this passage shows that His knowledge sets the very conditions for reality. If your every state is eternally known, what in your life today needs to be brought into alignment with that knowing? Will you act as if God already knows, or as if you can hide?

God's Eternal Knowledge of Future Distinctions: Objection and Answer
Sanusi presents and begins to respond to an objection about God's knowledge of future distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that God's knowledge of future distinctions does not require current distinction, and why this objection is insufficient.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of future states still involves judgments about distinct essences.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a final objection to the problem of God knowing future distinctions. The objectors argue that God does not need to know the current distinction between blackness and whiteness; He only knows that they will be distinct in the future. Sanusi begins his response by pointing out that even this future knowledge is a judgment about a specific essence, which presupposes that the essence is already determinate.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how the objectors frame their claim to avoid the problem of requiring eternal distinction.
The objectors distinguish between knowing present distinction and knowing future distinction.
2
Identify the core claim
What exactly are the objectors saying? They claim that God does not know blackness is distinct from whiteness now, but He knows that blackness, when it exists in the future, will be distinct from whiteness at that future time. This, they say, removes the problem.
3
Examine the author's initial response
The author begins his rebuttal by stating that this does not remove the problem. He points out that knowing 'blackness will exist in the future' is a judgment (ḥukm) about blackness. And a judgment about a specific quiddity requires that quiddity to be determinate. The passage breaks off, but the argument is that even future knowledge presupposes a distinct essence.
The text is incomplete; the full response is on the next page.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: إنه تعالى لا يعلم في الأزل أن السواد متميز عن البياض في الحال، بل يعلم أن السواد إذا وجد في لا يزال، فإنه عند وجوده يكون متميزاً عن البياض عند وجوده، وعلى هذا التقدير فيسقط الإشكال. فنقول: هذا لا يدفع الإشكال، لأن العلم بأن السواد سيوجد في لا يزال: حكم على السواد. والحكم على الماهية المخصوصة، مشروط بكون
For they said: 'He, exalted, does not know in eternity that blackness is distinct from whiteness in the present state, but He knows that blackness, when it exists in the future, will at its existence be distinct from whiteness at its existence. On this assumption, the problem falls away.' We say: This does not remove the problem, because the knowledge that blackness will exist in the future is a judgment about blackness. And the judgment about a particular quiddity is conditional upon being...
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents a final objection and the beginning of its response. The objectors propose that God does not need to know the current distinction of blackness from whiteness; He knows that blackness will be distinct from whiteness in the future when both exist. They claim this avoids the problem of requiring eternal distinction. The author begins his response by stating that this does not solve the problem. He points out that knowing that 'blackness will exist in the future' is itself a judgment (ḥukm) about blackness. And a judgment about a specific essence (māhiyya mukhtaṣṣa) is conditional upon the essence being determinate and distinct. The text breaks off, but the argument is that such knowledge still presupposes that blackness is a distinct reality, even if considered only in the future.

Key Terms
الأزل (al-azal)
السواد (al-sawād)
البياض (al-bayāḍ)
الماهية المخصوصة (al-māhiyya al-mukhtaṣṣa)
حكم (ḥukm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the objectors' claim in this passage?
A) God does not know future events
B) God knows blackness will be distinct from whiteness in the future, not in the present
C) God's knowledge is limited to eternal things
D) Blackness and whiteness are not distinct

Key Takeaway
The objection that God only knows future distinctions does not escape the need for a determinate essence to be judged.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God's knowledge of your future includes a judgment about your very essence, do you feel the weight of being a distinct, known creature before Him—or does it remain an abstract idea? What would change in your daily life if you truly internalized that every judgment about you, past, present, and future, is based on a real, determinate you that God knows intimately?
?
The objectors tried to escape the problem by postponing God's knowledge to the future. Are there truths about yourself or your faith that you push into the future, thinking they will resolve later, rather than facing them now in the light of God's eternal knowledge? How can you stop avoiding and start living in the present reality of being fully known by God?

Divine Attributes
beginner
4 min
The Known Must Be Distinct
The author explains that what is known must be distinct, using an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the object of knowledge must be distinct from other things, and how vague knowledge still involves a distinct aspect.

Core Idea
Every known thing must be distinct from all other things.

The principle that knowledge requires distinction may seem obvious, but it needs careful defense against objections. In this passage, the author addresses a potential counterexample: knowing that you have a foster-sister without knowing her identity. He shows that even in such vague knowledge, the known aspect is distinct. This lesson unpacks his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own reasoning come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author begins by stating the impossibility of knowledge without distinction.
2
Identify the objection and response
The author presents a possible objection: knowing you have a foster-sister in town without knowing her personally. See how he responds by distinguishing the known aspect (existence) from the unknown (identity).
The objection is meant to challenge the claim that all knowledge requires distinction.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that the known part is distinct, and what is not distinct is not known. Ask yourself: does this apply to all cases of knowledge?
This conclusion reinforces the fundamental principle in epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
امتنع كونه عالماً بذلك الشيء. أما إذا علمنا أن لنا أختاً من الرضاع في هذه البلدة، وإن كنا لا نعرفها بعينها، فهذا لا يصلح أن يكون نقضاً على ما ذكرناه وذلك لأن مهنا المعلوم: وجود شخص من الأشخاص الإنسانية، موصوف بكونه أختاً من الرضاع. فهذا القدر، هو المعلوم. وهذا المعلوم متميز عن سائر المعلومات. فأما أن ذلك الشخص من هو؟ فغير معلوم. فظهر: أن القدر الذي هو معلوم، فهو متميز. والذي هو غير متميز، فهو غير معلوم.
It is impossible for Him to be knowing that thing. But if we know that we have a sister from breastfeeding in this town, though we do not know her personally, this does not constitute a refutation of what we mentioned, because the known here is the existence of a human person described as being a sister from breastfeeding. This measure is the known. And this known is distinct from all other known things. As for who that person is, it is not known. So it becomes clear: that which is known is distinct, and that which is not distinct is not known.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection. The author argues that for knowledge to be valid, the object of knowledge must be distinct (mumayyaz) from other objects. He gives an example: we may know that there exists a foster-sister in the town without knowing her identity. The known aspect—the existence of such a person—is distinct. Therefore, the principle stands: what is known is distinct, and what is not distinct is not known.

Key Terms

معلوم
امتياز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vague knowledge (like knowing there is a foster-sister without knowing who) is an example of knowledge without distinction.
Correction: Even in vague knowledge, the known aspect (the existence of such a person) is distinct; the unknown part (identity) is not part of the knowledge.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude from the example of the foster-sister?
A) That we must know the sister's identity for knowledge to be valid.
B) That the known aspect (existence of a sister) is distinct, even if the individual is unknown.
C) That knowledge of a general fact is impossible.
D) That the example refutes the author's own argument.

Key Takeaway
The known aspect of anything must be distinct from all other known aspects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Future Distinction Does Not Suffice for Eternal Knowledge
Objection and refutation regarding eternal knowledge and future distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why future distinction cannot serve as the basis for eternal knowledge, due to the necessary priority of the object's distinction over the knowledge of it.

Core Idea
Knowledge must conform to its object; the object's distinction must precede the knowledge of it.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle objection: if God knows future events as distinct, couldn't that distinction arise in the future rather than from eternity? The refutation hinges on a fundamental principle of knowledge: it must match its object. Let's explore why this principle rules out the objector's proposal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its refutation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to see the structure: objection then answer.
Notice the author's direct response: 'This is clearly invalid.'
2
Identify the key principle
The author first states that knowledge must conform to the known, otherwise it is ignorance. This is the core axiom of the argument.
3
Trace the conditional logic
The author argues that for knowledge of distinction to exist, the object must already be distinct. This makes distinction a condition for knowledge, and the condition must be prior.
The terms 'condition' (sharṭ) and 'conditioned' (mashrūṭ) are crucial.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Thus, if distinction occurs only in the future, it cannot be the basis for eternal knowledge that already knows it as distinct.
This refutes the objection conclusively.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: «لم لا يكفي في حصول العلم الأزلي، حصول الامتياز في المعلومات في لا يزال؟» قلنا: هذا ظاهر الفساد. لأن العلم يجب أن يكون مطابقاً للمعلوم، وإلا لكان جهلاً. وعلى هذا التقدير، فيمتنع كون العالم عالماً بامتياز ذلك المعلوم عن غيره، إلا إذا كان ذلك المعلوم ممتازاً عن غيره. فلما كان كون المعلوم، ممتازاً عن غيره، شرطاً لتعلق العلم بامتيازه عن غيره، وثبت أن الشرط متقدم على المشروط في الرتبة، امتنع أن يكون حصول الامتياز في المعلومات، متأخراً عن تعلق العلم بهذا الامتياز.
His statement: 'Why does not the occurrence of distinction in the objects of knowledge in the future suffice for the occurrence of eternal knowledge?' We say: This is clearly invalid. Because knowledge must conform to the known, otherwise it would be ignorance. And on this assumption, it is impossible for the knower to be knowing the distinction of that known from others unless that known is distinct from others. Since the known's being distinct from others is a condition for the attachment of knowledge to its distinction from others, and it is established that the condition is prior to the conditioned in rank, it is impossible for the occurrence of distinction in the objects of knowledge to be posterior to the attachment of knowledge to this distinction.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector asks why future distinction cannot serve for eternal knowledge. The author refutes this by appealing to the principle that knowledge must conform to its object. For knowledge of distinction to occur, the object must already be distinct. Since the condition (distinction) must precede the conditioned (knowledge of distinction) in rank, future distinction cannot be the basis for eternal knowledge. This establishes a priority of distinction relative to knowledge.

Key Terms
علم أزلي
امتياز
شرط
مشروط
مطابقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think future events can be known as distinct before they exist, because God's knowledge is eternal.
Correction: The author clarifies that the object must be distinct at the time of knowledge; otherwise, knowledge would not conform to its object, and would be ignorance.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that future distinction cannot ground eternal knowledge?
A) Because God's knowledge is limited to the present.
B) Because knowledge must conform to the known, and the known must be distinct at the time of knowledge.
C) Because future events are uncertain.
D) Because the objector's question is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Future distinction cannot ground eternal knowledge because knowledge requires the object to be distinct at the time of knowing, and the condition of distinction must precede the knowledge of it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often claim to ‘know’ things without verifying the conditions—your assumptions, your beliefs about others, your certainty about the future. After reading this lesson, ask yourself: Is your knowledge built on a foundation that actually exists, or are you claiming knowledge of a distinction that has not yet occurred? What one belief about your own future do you need to re-examine today?
?
This lesson proves that even God’s knowledge requires the object to be distinct before the knowledge of its distinction. If the All-Knowing’s knowledge is tied to reality in this way, how much more should your knowledge be tied to real evidence? Where in your life are you content with vague notions instead of clear distinctions? Write down one area where you will pursue clarity today.
?
The condition must precede the conditioned. Is your relationship with Allah based on clear conditions you have fulfilled? Or are you hoping for reward without establishing the prerequisite faith and action? What condition of sincere obedience have you been neglecting, and when will you start fulfilling it?

Refuting Circularity in God's Knowledge of Future Essences
Refutation of a counter-argument about God's knowledge of future distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical circularity that arises when one claims God's knowledge of future distinction is limited to the time of existence, and why prior conception is necessary for any judgment.

Core Idea
Knowledge always requires conception, and conception requires distinction—a circle if distinction only arises at existence.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection to the claim that God knows future essences without eternal distinction. The opponents try to escape by saying God knows them as they will be in the future. The author responds by exposing a logical circularity: to know that something will exist, you must already conceive it, but conception requires distinction, which the opponents say only happens at existence. Thus the objection collapses into an impossible circle.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Opponent's Claim
Read the first part where the opponents propose that God knows future distinction only when it occurs, not eternally. Note their assumption that this removes the problem.
Notice the phrasing: 'He does not know in eternity that blackness is distinct... but He knows that when it exists it becomes distinct.'
2
Identify the Author's Counter
The author immediately rejects this: 'This does not remove the problem.' Track his reasoning step by step.
3
Trace the Circularity Argument
Follow the chain: knowledge of future existence is a judgment (hukm) → judgments require prior conception (tasawwur) → conception of an essence requires its distinction from others → if distinction depends on existence, we get a loop.
Key terms: tasawwur (conception), tasdiq (assent), hukm (judgment), dawr (circularity).
4
Understand the Conclusion
The author concludes that such circularity is impossible (muhal), therefore the objection fails. Reflect on why this refutes the opponent's attempt.
This argument shows that the opponent's escape route is logically incoherent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: إنه تعالى لا يعلم في الأزل: أن السواد متميز عن البياض في الحال [بل يعلم؟] أن السواد إذا وجد في لا يزال، فإنه عند وجوده [يكون] متميزاً عن البياض عند وجوده، وعلى هذا التقدير فيسقط الإشكال. فنقول: هذا لا يدفع الإشكال، لأن العلم بأن السواد سيوجد في لا يزال: حكم على السواد. والحكم على الماهية المخصوصة، مشروط بكون المحكوم عليه: متصوراً. لأن التصديق مسبوق بالتصور لا محالة. أن بنتج الحكم على السواد بأنه سيوجد: مسبوق بتصور هذه الماهية لا يتم إلا بعد العلم بامتياز هذه الماهية عما سواها. فلو كان حصول هذا الامتياز موقوفاً على دخولها في الوجود، لزم الدور. وهو محال.
For if they say: 'He, exalted, does not know in eternity that blackness is distinct from whiteness in the present, but He knows that blackness when it exists in the future, then at its existence it becomes distinct from whiteness at its existence, and on this estimation the problem is removed.' We say: This does not remove the problem, because knowledge that blackness will exist in the future is a judgment upon blackness. And the judgment upon a particular essence is conditional upon the subject of judgment being conceived. Because assent is necessarily preceded by conception. The judgment upon blackness that it will exist is preceded by the conception of this essence, which is not completed except after knowledge of the distinction of this essence from others. So if the occurrence of this distinction were dependent upon its entering into existence, a circularity would result, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a further objection and answer. The opponents propose that God knows in eternity that blackness will be distinct from whiteness when it exists, not that it is distinct now. The author replies that even this involves a vicious circle. Knowing that blackness will exist is a judgment (hukm) on the essence of blackness, which requires prior conception (tasawwur) of that essence. Conception of blackness requires that blackness be distinct from other essences. If that distinction only occurs upon existence, then we need distinction to conceive, but distinction depends on existence, leading to circularity (dawr). This refutes the objection.

Key Terms
دور
تصور
تصديق
حكم
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowing that something will be distinct in the future does not require prior conception of that thing.
Correction: The author argues that any judgment (including knowledge of future existence) requires prior conception, and conception requires distinction, leading to circularity if distinction only arises at existence.

Checkpoint
What logical problem does the author identify in the counter-argument about God knowing future distinction?
A) That God cannot know future events.
B) That it leads to a circular dependency between distinction and existence.
C) That blackness and whiteness are not real essences.
D) That the objection is based on a false premise.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of future distinct essences cannot be limited to when they exist, because that would require prior conception, which presupposes distinction — creating a vicious circle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge of your essence—including your future sins and repentance—is perfect before you ever act, does that deepen your awareness that He sees you completely? How can you live today knowing that your very conception is held in His eternal mind?
?
The author argues that any judgment requires conception, and conception requires distinction. How does this logical necessity affect your trust in God's omniscience? Does it make you realize that He knows you more intimately than you know yourself? What would change if you truly believed that?
?
Imagine that God's knowledge of your future is not conditional on your existence but is timelessly certain. How does that reality confront your illusion of autonomy? Will you let that knowledge drive you to humility and dependence, or will you ignore it? What is one concrete step you can take today to live in light of His complete knowledge?
