Answering the Objection from Daily Events
The author answers the objection that daily events would require pre-eternal existence of temporal things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the proof from quiddities by distinguishing between conceptualization of essences and judgments about future contingents.

Core Idea
Temporal knowledge does not force pre-existence of its objects.

The author has argued that knowledge of quiddities requires their pre-existence. An opponent objects: if so, then our daily knowledge of future events would require those events to exist now. In this passage, the author gives two powerful responses, showing that the objection confuses two different types of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the author's own words set the context before analysis.
2
Identify the two responses
Notice the author presents two separate replies: first denying the existence of temporal knowledge, then conceding it but showing a key distinction.
Look for 'فنقول' and 'وأيضاً'.
3
Understand the key distinction
The author distinguishes between 'conceptualization of quiddities' (تصور الماهيات) and 'judgment about a future quiddity' (الحكم بأن الماهية الفلانية ستحصل غداً). The former requires current existence; the latter does not.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Reflect on why it resolves the objection
Consider how this distinction protects the original proof. The objection conflates two different cognitive acts. Once separated, the objection loses force.
Ask yourself: why would the original claim still hold?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثامن : وهو نقض هذا الدليل بالحوادث اليومية . فنقول : إن جمهور الفلاسفة ينكرون وجود هذا العلم المتغير الزماني ، فلا يكون هذا النقض وارداً عليهم . وأيضاً : فإذا سلمنا حصول هذا العلم إلا أنا نقول : الفرق ظاهر . وذلك لأن الذي ادعيناه ، هو أن تصور الماهيات مشروط بحصول تلك الماهيات ، فوجب من قدم العلم بهذه الماهيات ، قدم هذه الماهيات . فأما الحكم بأن الماهية الفلانية ستحصل غداً ، فإنه لا يتوقف على حصول تلك الماهية في الحال . وإلا لزم التناقض فظهر الفرق والله أعلم .
As for the eighth question: it is the refutation of this proof by daily events. We say: The majority of philosophers deny the existence of this changing temporal knowledge, so this refutation does not apply to them. Also, even if we concede the occurrence of this knowledge, we say: the difference is clear. That is because what we have claimed is that the conceptualization of quiddities (al-mahiyyat) is conditioned upon the existence of those quiddities, so from the pre-eternity of knowledge of these quiddities, the pre-eternity of these quiddities follows. As for the judgment that a certain quiddity will occur tomorrow, it does not depend on the existence of that quiddity at present. Otherwise a contradiction would follow. So the difference is apparent, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection: if knowledge of quiddities requires their pre-eternal existence, then our daily knowledge of future events would require those events to exist now. The author gives two responses. First, most philosophers deny the existence of such changing temporal knowledge altogether, so the objection is irrelevant. Second, even if we accept such knowledge, there is a clear difference: our claim was that conceptualization of quiddities (their essence) depends on their existence. But a judgment that a quiddity will occur tomorrow is a future contingent, not a conceptualization of an essence, so it does not require present existence. Otherwise a contradiction would arise, because nothing can be both existent and non-existent simultaneously. Thus the difference is clear.

Key Terms
العلم المتغير الزماني
الماهيات
التصور
الحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the author's second response to the objection from daily events?
A) He denies that such temporal knowledge exists.
B) He distinguishes conceptualization of quiddities from judgment about future contingents.
C) He concedes that quiddities must exist now.
D) He says the objection comes only from philosophers.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of future contingents does not require their present existence, unlike conceptualization of essences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan for tomorrow, do you subconsciously act as if your mental certainty about the future forces events into existence? How does the author's distinction between conceptualization and judgment correct that illusion?
?
In your own life, do you ever confuse your knowledge about what is true now with your expectations of what will happen? How can the lesson's sharp separation between these two cognitive acts change the way you trust God's knowledge without fatalism?
?
The objector assumed that all knowledge works the same way. Where in your faith or thinking have you made similar overgeneralizations about God's attributes? What would it cost you to accept the distinctions this lesson demands?

Why Creation from Nothing is Impossible in Our World
Razi argues that if creation from nothing were possible, the wise Creator would not choose a lesser method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third argument for the necessity of matter, which combines divine wisdom with empirical observation to rule out creation ex nihilo in the observed order.

Core Idea
If creation from nothing were possible, we would see it; we don't, so matter is necessary.

Razi presents his third argument by appealing to the attribute of divine wisdom. If God could create from absolute nothingness, that would be more perfect than working through matter. Since God always chooses the better, we would expect to see at least some things created from nothing. But we never do. This empirical fact proves that creation from nothing is impossible in the current order, thus every originated thing requires prior matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi uses a conditional argument and an analogy.
2
Identify the logical structure
Break down the argument into premises and conclusion. The passage contains: a conditional premise, a premise about divine wisdom, an observation about the world, and a conclusion.
The phrase 'لو كان التخليق من العدم المحض ممكناً' sets up the conditional.
3
Analyse the builder analogy
Razi gives an analogy of a builder who would not prepare materials if he could create instantly. Understand how this analogy supports the argument that we would observe creation from nothing if it were possible.
The analogy is not the argument itself but an illustration.
4
Connect to the overall proof
This is the third of three facets. See how it reinforces the previous arguments and leads to the conclusion that every originated thing must have a prior matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث في بيان الفتقار الحادث إلى المادة : هو أنه لو كان التخليق من العدم المحض والنفي الصرف : ممكناً . لكان هذا النوع من التخليق أكمل وأفضل من التخليق بواسطة تغير المادة في الأحوال والصفات . وترك الأفضل دائماً لا يليق بالحكيم العليم. فلو قلنا : إنه تعالى خلق جميع الأجسام ، لا عن مادة ، لوجب أن يحصل تكوين هذه الأشياء التي نشاهدها على هذا الوجه . وحيث لم توجد البتة ، علمنا أن التكوين عن العدم المحض : محال . والذي يقرره : أن من أراد بناء قصر أو دار ، فإنه لا يشتغل بإعداد اللبن والجذوع والمسامير ، لو كان يمكنه إيجاد القصر والدار دفعة واحدة . ولما لم نشاهد البتة تكوين شيء مما شاهدناه عن العدم المحض ، علمنا : أن ذلك في نفسه : محال ممتنع ، فيثبت بهذه الوجوه الثلاثة : أن كل محدث ، فلا بد له من مادة سابقة
The third facet in explaining the originated thing's need for matter: it is that if creation from pure nothingness and absolute negation were possible, then this type of creation would be more perfect and better than creation through the change of matter in states and attributes. And leaving the better always is not befitting for the Wise, All-Knowing. So if we say that He, exalted, created all bodies not from matter, then the formation of these things that we observe would have to occur in this way. And since they have never existed at all, we know that formation from pure nothingness is impossible. And what establishes it: that whoever wants to build a palace or a house would not occupy himself with preparing bricks, beams, and nails, if he could bring about the palace and house all at once. And since we have never observed the formation of anything that we have observed from pure nothingness, we know that that in itself is impossible and prohibited. Thus by these three facets it is established that every originated thing must have a prior matter.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument for the necessity of matter. Razi argues by considering the attribute of divine wisdom: if creation from absolute nothingness were possible, it would be more perfect than creation through the transformation of pre-existing matter. Since God is all-wise, He would always choose the better option. Therefore, if creation from nothing were possible, we would observe at least some things created that way. But we never observe anything coming into existence from absolute nothingness. This empirical absence proves that such creation is impossible in itself. Razi gives an analogy: a builder who could create a palace instantly would not bother gathering materials. The fact that everything we see is formed from something else shows that matter is a necessary precondition for origination. This argument from divine wisdom and empirical observation reinforces the previous two arguments, concluding that every originated thing requires a prior material cause.

Key Terms
العدم المحض (absolute nothingness)
التخليق (creation)
المادة (matter)
الحكيم العليم (the Wise, All-Knowing)
المحال (impossible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies God's power to create from nothing.
Correction: Razi does not claim God cannot create from nothing in principle; he argues that since we never observe it, it does not occur in the current order, implying a material substratum is necessary for all observed origination.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that creation from nothing is impossible in the observed world?
A) Because God is not wise.
B) Because we never observe anything created from absolute nothingness.
C) Because matter is eternal.
D) Because the builder analogy fails.

Key Takeaway
Razi's third proof uses divine wisdom and empirical observation to show that creation from nothing does not occur, therefore matter must exist prior to every originated thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that a wise person would not prepare materials if they could create the final product instantly. In your own life, where do you rely on shortcuts or expect results without putting in the needed groundwork? How does this argument expose the gap between your desires and the means God has appointed for their fulfillment?
?
The argument hinges on the principle that God does not leave the better for no reason. Consider a situation where you have chosen a lesser good when a greater was possible. What does that reveal about your priorities? How can you align your choices more with divine wisdom?
?
You witness every day that nothing appears from absolute nothingness—everything has a cause. Yet, do you sometimes live as if you can produce spiritual fruit without roots, or expect forgiveness without repentance? How does this argument call you to attend to the 'matter' of your soul?

Refuting the Third Objection: God's Free Will Does Not Imply Randomness
The author refutes the claim that God's free choice leads to purely accidental events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that God's free agency implies randomness is flawed, and how the author uses the concept of accidental events and natural regularity to refute it.

Core Idea
If God's choice were random, events would be irregular, but nature is regular.

The author addresses a third objection to his view: that God as a free agent could simply choose any possibility, making events pointless. He responds by showing that such a view would reduce events to accidental occurrences, which are neither necessary nor frequent. He then critiques the alternative explanation based on divine beneficence and points to the regular patterns in nature as evidence against pure randomness.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author first quotes the objection, then begins his response.
The passage is an excerpt; the full argument may continue.

Understand that the author is analyzing the claim that God is a preponderator who chooses one side without any cause.
3

The author argues that if the choice is without reason, the outcome is accidental. Accidental events are either impossible or not regular.
Note the two possibilities: impossible of occurrence, or possible but not perpetual or frequent.

The author dismisses the suggestion that God chooses what is most beneficial as extremely weak.
This is a brief dismissal; the author does not elaborate further here.

The author points to the regularity of natural phenomena (animals, plants, minerals) which depend on specific conditions. This regularity raises doubt about whether the cause is natural properties or the Agent.
The passage ends with this doubt; the author's full resolution is not present.

As for his statement in the third aspect: 'He, exalted, is a free agent (fā‘il mukhtār), He does what He wills and judges what He desires.' We say: As for the statement that He is a preponderator (murajjiḥ) of one side over the other, this would imply that the occurrence of one side is purely accidental (ittifāqī), and accidental matters are either impossible of occurrence, or if possible, they are neither perpetual nor frequent. And his statement: 'They are only because it is most beneficial for the servants' is extremely weak. Because the occurrence of animals, plants, and minerals, being dependent in the usual course on the states of matter, the four seasons, and the changes of celestial bodies, that has become a source of great doubt as to whether the effective agent in the occurrence of these things is natural properties (ṭabā’i‘) or the Agent (al-fā‘il).
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is the author's response to an objection that God's free agency implies He can simply choose one of two possibilities without any cause. The author argues that if God were merely a preponderator without a reason for His choice, then the occurrence of one possibility over the other would be purely accidental. But accidental things are either impossible or, if possible, they are not regular or frequent. The objection's claim that God chooses because it is most beneficial (aṣlaḥ) is dismissed as extremely weak. The author then points to the regular occurrence of natural phenomena like animals, plants, and minerals, which depend on specific conditions such as matter, seasons, and celestial changes. This regularity suggests that the effective cause might be natural properties (ṭabā’i‘) rather than a freely acting agent. The passage appears to be criticizing the idea that God's free will alone, without any underlying principle, can account for the regular patterns observed in nature. However, the argument is cut off, and the author's full resolution is not present in this window.

The Free Agent and the Intermediaries Argument
The author argues that the way of the free agent is more suitable and refutes explanation by created interests.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that attributing events directly to God's free choice is more suitable than alternative explanations involving intermediaries or created interests.

Core Idea
God's free agency is the direct cause of events, not intermediate natures.

In this passage, the author concludes his argument about divine action and natural causes. He presents a hypothetical to show that if events were not mediated by natural intermediaries, the intellect would still recognize God as the sole agent. He then reinforces this view by rejecting the idea that God's actions are based on created interests. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once. Pay attention to key terms like al-qadir al-mukhtar, al-taba'i', and al-fa'il al-mukhtar.
The passage is dense, so take your time.
2
Identify the hypothetical and its implication
Find the 'if' statement: 'if we assume that they occur without these intermediaries.' Understand what the intellect would judge about the connection of natures to events.
This is a key logical step in the argument.
3
Understand the refutation and conclusion
Notice the second part where the author dismisses explaining God's actions by the interests of servants. Reflect on why this strengthens the argument for God's free agency.
The refutation refers back to earlier chapters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القادر المختار . أما لو فرضنا أنها كانت تحدث من غير هذه الوسائط ، كانت العقول حاكمة . بأنه لا تعلق للطبائع بها البتة ، بل المؤثر فيها هو الفاعل المختار . فيثبت : أن هذا الطريق أصلح للمكلفين . وأيضاً : فتعليل أفعال الله تعالى ] (١) بمصالح العباد ، قد أبطلناه في الفصول السالفة . والله أعلم
The powerful, the free-choosing. But if we assume that they occur without these intermediaries, the intellects would judge that there is no connection of the natures to them at all, rather the effective agent is the free-choosing agent. So it is established that this way is more suitable for the responsible individuals. And also: the explanation of the actions of Allah, the Exalted, by the interests of the servants, we have refuted it in previous chapters. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is the concluding part of an argument concerning divine action and causality. It begins with a reference to God as 'the powerful, the free-choosing' (al-qadir al-mukhtar), establishing the theological premise that God is both omnipotent and acts freely. The author then presents a hypothetical: if we suppose that events occur without the mediation of natural causes (al-wasa'it), then the intellects would necessarily judge that natural natures (al-taba'i') have no connection whatsoever to those events. Instead, the only effective agent (al-mu'aththir) is the free-choosing agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar), i.e., God. From this, the author concludes that this way of understanding—attributing effects directly to God's free choice—is more suitable for those morally responsible (al-mukallafeen) because it avoids implying that God is compelled by natural causes. Then, the author adds a further point: the alternative explanation that accounts for God's actions based on the interests of the servants (masalih al-'ibad) has already been refuted in earlier chapters of the work. The passage ends with 'And Allah knows best' (wa-llahu a'lam), a typical closing formula.

Correction: It actually focuses on God's free agency and the role of secondary causes, not human free will.

Two Objectives of the Tenth Treatise
Introduces the tenth treatise and its two objectives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two sequential objectives of the tenth scientific treatise regarding the necessity of the world's attributes and its eternity.

Core Idea
The world's eternity is logically dependent on another necessary attribute.

The author opens the tenth scientific treatise by stating its two-part goal: first, to establish a necessary attribute of the world, and then to derive its eternity from that. This short introduction sets the stage for a longer argument. Pay attention to the conditional link between the two claims.

The Goodness and Wisdom Argument
Argues that God's goodness and wisdom entail the world's eternal preservation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from God's goodness and wisdom leads to the conclusion that the world remains eternally, and how the author defends it against two objections.

Core Idea
God's goodness and wisdom guarantee the world's eternal preservation.

This lesson presents a theological argument that draws on two divine attributes: goodness and wisdom. Since God is perfectly good and wise, His creation is perfectly made and will not be destroyed. The author then anticipates two objections and offers rebuttals. As you read, notice how the argument hinges on the nature of God versus contingent beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to get the overall flow. Note that the passage contains a main proof followed by two objections and answers.
The second answer is cut off in the source; we only have the beginning.
2
Identify the core argument
Find the premise that the world is good and well-made, and the conclusion that only an evil being would destroy it. Since God is not evil, He will not destroy it.
3
Examine the first objection and answer
The objection suggests God might create with one wisdom and later destroy with another. The answer distinguishes between beings whose actions depend on external conditions and God, who is the sole cause of all events.
Notice how the answer uses the concept of benefit turning into detriment.
4
Review the second objection and partial answer
The objection points to perishable individuals in the world. The answer begins by linking individuals' coming-into-existence to celestial motions. Consider how this might support the permanence of the celestial sphere.
The text cuts off, but the intended argument is that celestial motions themselves are not subject to conditional dependency.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider why this argument does not claim the world is eternal without beginning, but rather that once created, it remains forever due to God's attributes.
This addresses a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة في بيان أن العالم يجب أن يكون أبدياً : يقال : العالم حسن ، جيد الصنعة . وما كان لا يبطله ولا ينقضه إلا شرير ، وإله العالم ليس بشرير ، فلا ينقضه . وظاهر أن غيره لا يقدر على نقضه ، فوجب أن لا يبطل هذا العالم ، ولا ينتقض أبداً. فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه تعالى : صنعه الحكمة مخصوصة ، ثم إنه ينقضه ويبطله الحكمة أخرى . وأيضاً : فهذا ينتقض بأشخاص العالم . وأجاب عن الأول : بأن ما كان مصلحة ، قد ينقلب مفسدة في حق من كانت أفعاله موقوفة على حدوث شرائط ، لا يكون حدوثها من جهته. أما الباري تعالى لما كان حدوث كل الحوادث منه، وبإيجاده. فهذا التفاوت في حقه : محال . وأجاب عن السؤال الثاني : بأن حدوث الأشخاص في هذا العالم ، إنما كان بسبب أن حدوث هذه الحوادث السفلية ، مشروط بحدوث التغيرات الحاصلة ، بسبب اختلاف الحركات الفلكية . فلو قلنا مثل هذا المعنى في حدوث الحركات الفلكية ، لزم افتقار حدوث تلك الحوادث
The fourth proof for the necessity of the world being eternal: It is said: The world is good, well-made. And nothing destroys or nullifies it except an evil one, and the God of the world is not evil, so He will not nullify it. It is apparent that no other is able to nullify it. So it is necessary that this world does not perish and is not nullified ever. If they say: Why is it not permissible to say that He, exalted, made it with a particular wisdom, and then He nullifies and destroys it with another wisdom? Also, this is contradicted by the individuals of the world. He answered the first: That which is a benefit may turn into a detriment for one whose actions depend on the occurrence of conditions not from his side. As for the Creator, exalted, since the occurrence of all events is from Him and by His creation, this variation in His case is impossible. And he answered the second question: That the occurrence of individuals in this world is only because the occurrence of these lower events is conditional upon the occurrence of changes resulting from the differences of celestial motions. So if we said a similar meaning regarding the occurrence of celestial motions, it would necessitate the dependence of the occurrence of those events...
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage presents a theological argument from divine attributes. The world is good and well-made (ḥasan, jayyid al-ṣanʿa). Only an evil being would destroy such a perfect creation, but God is not evil, therefore He will not destroy it. Moreover, no other can destroy it. So the world must remain eternally. Two objections are raised: (1) God might create with one wisdom and later destroy with another wisdom. The answer: For beings whose actions depend on external conditions, a benefit may become a detriment. But God is the sole cause of all events; no external condition affects Him, so such variation is impossible for Him. (2) The individuals of the world (ashkhāṣ) perish, contradicting the claim of permanence. The answer: The coming into existence of individuals depends on changes in the celestial motions. Since those motions themselves are not subject to the same conditional dependency (the text cuts off), the argument suggests that the celestial sphere is permanent, and thus the world as a whole remains.

جيد الصنعة
شرير
حكمة
أشخاص
حركات فلكية
مصلحة
مفسدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims the world is absolutely eternal (qadīm) without beginning.
Correction: The argument does not assert that the world has no beginning; it argues for its permanence after creation, based on God's attributes of goodness and wisdom.

Checkpoint

A) Because God is good and only an evil being destroys good things.

D) Because destroying it would contradict His omnipotence.

Key Takeaway
God's perfect goodness and wisdom, combined with His exclusive causality, ensure that the world once created remains forever.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Sixth Argument: Cutting Off Beneficence
Enumeration of three defects that would cause cessation of beneficence, all impossible for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logic of the sixth argument for the impossibility of world annihilation, grounded in God's perfect beneficence.

Core Idea
Stopping a good act without reason would imply a flaw in God.

This lesson presents the sixth argument from a classical work, showing that the creation of the world is an act of beneficence to the needy. Ceasing that beneficence would imply a defect in God, which is impossible. Let's examine the logic closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the concise structure: claim, premise, conclusion.
The argument is very dense; each phrase carries weight.
2
Identify the three defects
The passage lists three possible reasons for cutting off beneficence. Find each one and understand why each is impossible for God.
Incapacity, ignorance, and stinginess are all imperfections.
3
Reflect on the premise
Consider why the argument assumes the world's creation is a beneficence to the needy. How does this connect to God's wisdom and generosity?
The argument builds on the idea that God's actions are always good and purposeful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : إن خلق هذا العالم : إحساناً إلى المحتاجين . وقطع الإحسان لا يكون إلا للعجز أو الجهل أو البخل . والكل على الله : محال .
The sixth proof: The creation of this world is a beneficence to the needy. And cutting off beneficence only occurs due to incapacity, ignorance, or stinginess. And all of these are impossible for God.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth argument (al-hujja al-sadisa). It states that the creation of the world is an act of beneficence (ihsan) towards those in need. Ceasing that beneficence (i.e., annihilating the world) can only be caused by one of three defects: incapacity (ajz), ignorance (jahl), or stinginess (bukhl). Since these are all impossible (muhal) for God, the annihilation of the world cannot occur. The argument relies on the premise that God's actions are wise and generous, and that stopping a good action without reason would be imperfect.

C) Because the world is eternal.
D) Because the needy would cease to exist.

Key Takeaway
The sixth argument shows that world annihilation would contradict God's perfect beneficence, as it would imply an imperfection in His nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The First Objection: God's Knowledge and Infinite Relations
Ibn Sina's objection from God's omniscience against the impossibility of infinite numbers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the first objection against the impossibility of infinite numbers, which argues that God's knowledge of infinite objects implies infinite relations in His essence.

Core Idea
Infinite objects of knowledge necessitate infinite relations, contradicting the denial of infinite numbers.

This lesson introduces a powerful objection (naqḍ) to the claim that infinite numbers are impossible. The objection leverages God's omniscience: if God knows infinitely many objects, each known object requires a distinct relation (iḍāfa) between God and that object. This seems to entail infinitely many relations in God's essence, which would be a concrete instance of an infinite number. Before we dive into the source text, consider how this objection challenges the very notion that all infinities are impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice how the author first concedes a point before launching the objection.
The passage has three parts: concession, objection, and response to a potential counter.
2
Identify the core argument
Find the key premise: God knows infinite objects. Then see how the author argues that each known object yields a distinct relation, leading to infinite relations.
The evidence for distinctness: one can know God knows X while doubting He knows Y.
3
Examine the counter-response
The author anticipates the reply that relations are merely mental. See how he pushes back: either they are real (then infinite numbers exist) or they aren't (then God doesn't actually know).
This is a classic dilemma argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا هو السؤال الذي عليه اعتماد الشيخ الرئيس [أبي] علي ابن سينا . سلمنا : أن ذلك المجموع يصح الحكم عليه بقبول الزيادة والنقصان فلم قلتم : إن كل ما كان كذلك ، فهو متناه ؟ ولم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن كل واحد منها يذهب إلى غير النهاية ، ولا ينتهي واحد منهما إلى الانقطاع ، مع أنه يكون أحدهما مشتملا على ما لم يحصل في الآخر ؟ وما الدليل على أنه لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر كذلك ؟ سلمنا : أن ما ذكرتموه ، يدل على أنه يمتنع وجود ما لا نهاية له ، إلا أنا نقول : ما ذكرتم ينتقض بأمور : الأول : إنكم تقولون : إنه تعالى عالم بما لا نهاية له من المعلومات. ولا شك أن الإضافة إلى أحد تلك المعلومات ، مغايرة إلى المعلوم الآخر ، لأن كون العالم عالم بذلك المعلوم المعين ، إضافة مخصوصة بين العالم وبين ذلك المعلوم . وكون ذلك العالم عالماً بمعلوم آخر إضافة أخرى بين ذلك العالم وبين المعلوم الآخر . والدليل على تغاير هذه الإضافات : أنه يصح أن يعلم كون العالم عالماً بهذا المعلوم ، مع الشك في كون ذلك العالم عالماً بالمعلوم الآخر . والمعلوم مغاير لغير المعلوم. فيثبت : أن كون العالم عالماً بهذا المعلوم : مغاير لكونه عالماً بذلك المعلوم الآخر . وإذا ثبت هذا فكونه تعالى عالماً بمعلومات لا نهاية لها : يقتضي حصول إضافات لا نهاية لها في ذاته وذلك نقض صريح على قولكم : إن أعداد لا نهاية لها : محال . لا يقال : هذه الإضافات أمور لا حصول لها وجود في الأعيان ، فزال الإشكال. لأنا نقول : نحن لا نريد بهذه الإضافة إلا كونه عالماً بهذا المعلوم ، وبذلك المعلوم. فإن زعمتم : أن إضاف علمه إلى هذا المعلوم ، وإلى ذلك المعلوم غير حاصل في نفس الأمر . فهذا نفي لكونه تعالى عالماً بهذه المعلومات . وإن سلمتم : أن تلك الإضافات حاصلة في نفس الأمر ، فقد لزم السؤال لا محالة
This is the question upon which the chief master [Abi] Ali Ibn Sina relied. We concede: that it is correct to judge that sum as susceptible to increase and decrease, so why do you say: everything that is such is finite? And why is it not permissible to say: each one of them goes to infinity, and neither of the two comes to an end, while one of them includes what is not attained in the other? And what is the evidence that it is not permissible for the matter to be thus? We concede: that what you have mentioned indicates that it is impossible for the infinite to exist, except that we say: what you have mentioned is refuted by things: The first: you say: He, exalted, knows infinite objects of knowledge. There is no doubt that the relation (al-iḍāfa) to one of those objects of knowledge is different from the other object of knowledge, because the knower's being knowing that particular object of knowledge is a specific relation between the knower and that object of knowledge. And that knower's being knowing another object of knowledge is another relation between that knower and the other object of knowledge. The evidence for the difference of these relations is: it is valid to know that the knower is knowing this object of knowledge while doubting that that knower is knowing the other object of knowledge. And the known is different from what is not known. Thus it is established: that the knower's being knowing this object of knowledge is different from his being knowing that other object of knowledge. And if this is established, then His, exalted, being knowing infinite objects of knowledge necessitates the occurrence of infinite relations in His essence, and that is a clear refutation of your statement: infinite numbers are impossible. Let it not be said: these relations are things that have no existence in external entities, so the problem is removed. For we say: we do not mean by this relation except His being knowing this object of knowledge, and that object of knowledge. If you claim that the addition of His knowledge to this object of knowledge and to that object of knowledge does not obtain in actuality, then this is a negation of His, exalted, being knowing these objects of knowledge. And if you concede that those relations obtain in actuality, then the question inevitably arises.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is an objection (naqḍ) against the claim that infinite numbers are impossible. The author first presents a concession from the opponent (likely Ibn Sina's argument about a sum being finite if susceptible to increase/decrease) and then gives the first counterexample: God's knowledge of infinite objects. The argument is: God knows infinitely many objects of knowledge. Each act of knowing a specific object is a distinct relation (iḍāfa) between God and that object. These relations are demonstrably different because one can know that God knows X while doubting He knows Y. Therefore, if God knows infinitely many objects, there must be infinitely many relations in His essence. This contradicts the claim that infinite numbers are impossible. The author then addresses a potential objection: that these relations are merely mental constructs without external existence. He replies that even if they are mental, they are still real in some sense; if the opponent denies their actuality, that would imply God does not actually know those objects (which is blasphemy), and if they concede their actuality, the infinite numbers problem remains.

Key Terms
إضافة (iḍāfa)
معلومات (objects of knowledge)
لا نهاية (infinity)
نقض (objection/refutation)
عالم (knower)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The relations (iḍāfāt) are merely mental concepts and thus pose no real problem for the impossibility of infinite numbers.
Correction: The author argues that if the relations are not real, then God's knowledge would not be actual; if they are real, then infinite numbers do exist in reality.

Checkpoint
What does the author use as evidence that each relation (iḍāfa) between God and an object of knowledge is distinct?
A) The objects are different in essence
B) One can know God knows X while doubting He knows Y
C) The relations are mental constructs
D) God's knowledge is unlimited

Key Takeaway
The first objection against the impossibility of infinite numbers argues that God's omniscience entails infinite relations in His essence, presenting a direct counterexample to the claim that all infinities are impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge of each of your actions requires a distinct relation with His essence, how does that change the way you think about even your smallest sin or good deed? Will you begin to see each moment as a direct, real connection to Allah?
?
The objection claims infinite relations must be real in God's essence. Does this make you feel more accountable before an all-knowing God whose essence is directly related to every one of your thoughts? How will you act differently today knowing that each thought is a distinct relation with your Creator?
?
The author argues that to deny the reality of those relations would be to deny God's actual knowledge of particulars. Do you truly believe that Allah knows every detail of your life? If so, why do you still act as if some things are hidden? What would it mean to live with the constant awareness of His knowledge?

The Infinite Regress Objection from Self-Knowledge
The author states the second objection involving infinite regress in divine self-knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second objection uses the infinite regress of God's self-knowledge to challenge the impossibility of infinite numbers.

Core Idea
God's self-knowledge multiplies objects infinitely, breaking the ban on infinite numbers.

In this passage, the author presents a powerful objection: if God knows something, He must also know that He knows it, and so on without end. This creates infinite levels of knowledge even for a single object. Since God knows infinitely many objects, the total becomes infinite times infinite — a clear contradiction to the claim that infinite numbers cannot exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the objection's structure come through naturally.
2
Trace the infinite regress
Notice how the argument proceeds: if God knows X, He must know that He knows X. This leads to an infinite chain for each known object.
3
Identify the contradiction
The conclusion: for each of the infinite objects, there are infinite levels of knowledge, leading to an infinite number of knowledge acts — contradicting the claim that infinite numbers cannot exist.
4
Connect to earlier doctrine
This objection directly challenges the earlier claim that infinite numbers are impossible in existence. Consider why this matters for the broader argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض الثاني : أن نقول : إنه تعالى لما كان عالماً بجميع المعلومات . فإذا علم شيئاً ، وجب أن يعلم كونه عالماً بذلك الشيء ، وأن يعلم كونه عالماً بكونه عالماً بذلك الشيء ، وهكذا إلى ما لا نهاية له . فقد حصل في هذا المعلوم الواحد مراتب لا نهاية لها من المعلومات ولما كانت معلومات الله تعالى غير متناهية ، وثبت : أن لله تعالى بحسب كل واحد من المعلومات علوم لا نهاية لها . فحينئذ يلزم أن يقال : إنه حصل لله تعالى علوم لا نهاية لها ، لا مرة واحدة ، بل مراراً غير متناهية وذلك يبطل قولكم : إن الأعداد التي لا نهاية لها ، يمتنع دخولها في الوجود. لا يقال : العلم بالعلم بالشيء ، نفس العلم بذلك الشيء . وأيضاً : فبتقدير التغاير ، إلا أن هذه المراتب التي لانهاية لها ، لا تحصل بالفعل بل بالقوة. لأنا نقول : أما الكلام الأول فضعيف . لأن المعلوم والعلم شيئان متغايران . فكان العلم بأحدهما مغايراً للآخر ، فكان العلم بالمعلوم مغايراً للعلم بذلك العلم . وأما الكلام الثاني فضعيف ، لأنه تعالى لو كان عالماً بتلك المراتب لا بالفعل بل بالقوة ، فحينئذ يلزم تجهيل الله تعالى منه . وأيضاً : يلزم كون الله تعالى محلا للحوادث لأن كل ما كان بالقوة ، فإنه لا يمتنع خروجه إلى الفعل
The second objection: we say: He, exalted, since He knows all objects of knowledge. So if He knows a thing, it is necessary that He knows that He knows that thing, and that He knows that He knows that He knows that thing, and so on to infinity. So there has occurred in this single object of knowledge infinite levels of objects of knowledge. And since the objects of knowledge of God, exalted, are infinite, and it is established that God, exalted, has, in respect of each individual object of knowledge, infinite acts of knowledge. Then it necessarily follows that it be said: there have occurred for God, exalted, infinite acts of knowledge, not once, but infinite times. And that invalidates your statement: infinite numbers are impossible to enter into existence. Let it not be said: the knowledge of the knowledge of the thing is the same as the knowledge of that thing. Also, even if we concede the difference, yet these infinite levels do not occur actually but potentially. For we say: the first statement is weak because the object of knowledge and the knowledge are two different things. So the knowledge of one is different from the other, so the knowledge of the object of knowledge is different from the knowledge of that knowledge. As for the second statement, it is weak because if He, exalted, were knowing those levels not actually but potentially, then that would entail ignorance (tajhīl) of God, exalted. Also, it would entail that God, exalted, is a locus of temporal events (maḥall li-l-ḥawādith) because everything that is potential does not prevent its coming into actuality.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is the second objection (naqḍ) against the impossibility of infinite numbers. The author argues that God's knowledge of each object implies an infinite regress: if God knows X, He must know that He knows X, and so on ad infinitum. Thus, for a single object, there are infinite levels of knowledge objects. Since God knows infinitely many primary objects, the total number of knowledge acts becomes infinite times infinite. This contradicts the claim that infinite numbers cannot exist. The author then refutes two counterarguments: (1) That knowledge of knowledge is the same as knowledge of the thing – he replies that knowledge and its object are distinct, so knowledge of X and knowledge of the knowledge of X are different. (2) That these levels are merely potential, not actual – he replies that if they were only potential, God would be ignorant of them (since potential knowledge is not actual knowledge), and also that potentiality implies the possibility of actualization, which would make God a subject of temporal events (ḥawādith), which is impossible.

Key Terms
مراتب
العلم بالعلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the second objection claim about the infinite levels of knowledge?
A) They prove God's knowledge is limited.
B) They show that infinite numbers can exist.
C) They contradict God's simplicity.
D) They are merely potential.

Key Takeaway
The infinite regress in God's self-knowledge creates an infinity of knowledge acts, undermining the claim that infinite numbers cannot be actual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge of you includes an infinite regress of 'knowing that He knows', does that make you feel more accountable for your private thoughts? Take a moment to reflect on one thought you would be embarrassed for others to know, and ask God for forgiveness.
?
The objection shows that God's knowledge is infinitely deep for every one of your actions. How does this change the way you view your daily choices, even the seemingly insignificant ones? Commit to living with greater mindfulness of God's ever-present, all-knowing gaze.
?
If every sin you commit is known by God in infinite detail, does that not make repentance more urgent? Consider a specific sin you have not yet repented from, and resolve to turn back to God now, before it becomes a habit.

Refuting the Counterarguments: Distinction and Potentiality
The author responds to two counterarguments against the infinite regress objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two counterarguments against the infinite regress objection and how the author refutes them.

Core Idea
Knowledge of knowledge is distinct from knowledge of thing, and potential knowledge is not actual knowledge.

After presenting the infinite regress objection, the author anticipates two possible rebuttals: that knowledge of knowledge is the same as knowledge of the thing, and that the infinite levels are merely potential. In this section, he systematically demolishes both, showing why they fail and what unacceptable consequences they entail.

Guided Path
1
Read the counterarguments and refutations
Read the Arabic and translation to see the two objections and the author's responses.
2
Analyze the first counterargument and its refutation
The first objection claims knowledge of knowledge is the same as knowledge of thing. The author refutes by noting that knowledge and its object are distinct, so knowledge of each is different.
3
Analyze the second counterargument and its refutation
The second objection says the infinite levels are potential, not actual. The author points out that potential knowledge would mean God is ignorant of those levels, and also that potentiality implies possible actualization, making God a locus of temporal events.

The second objection: we say: He, exalted, since He knows all objects of knowledge. So if He knows a thing, it is necessary that He knows that He knows that thing, and that He knows that He knows that He knows that thing, and so on to infinity. So there has occurred in this single object of knowledge infinite levels of objects of knowledge. And since the objects of knowledge of God, exalted, are infinite, and it is established that God, exalted, has, in respect of each individual object of knowledge, infinite acts of knowledge. Then it necessarily follows that it be said: there have occurred for God, exalted, infinite acts of knowledge, not once, but infinite times. And that invalidates your statement: infinite numbers are impossible to enter into existence. Let it not be said: the knowledge of the knowledge of the thing is the same as the knowledge of that thing. Also, even if we concede the difference, yet these infinite levels do not occur actually but potentially. For we say: the first statement is weak because the object of knowledge and the knowledge are two different things. So the knowledge of one is different from the other, so the knowledge of the object of knowledge is different from the knowledge of that knowledge. As for the second statement, it is weak because if He, exalted, were knowing those levels not actually but potentially, then that would entail ignorance (tajhīl) of God, exalted. Commentary
This passage is the second objection (naqḍ) against the impossibility of infinite numbers. The author argues that God's knowledge of each object implies an infinite regress: if God knows X, He must know that He knows X, and so on ad infinitum. The Third Objection: God's Power and Infinite Types of Possibles
Opening premise of the third objection: God's power to create all possibles and the infinity of their types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the premise of the third objection: that God's power over all possibles implies an infinite number of types, which challenges the impossibility of an infinite actual number.

Core Idea
If God can create all possibles, then infinite types must be real.

This lesson introduces the third objection against the claim that an infinite number is impossible. The objection begins with a simple premise: God is able to bring into existence all possible things, and the types of those possibles are infinite. We will examine this premise and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note that the passage is only the beginning of the objection; the full argument is missing due to a page break.
The text is incomplete, so focus on the stated premises.
2
Identify key terms
Identify the terms 'mumkināt' (possibles), 'anwā‘' (types), and 'ghayr mutanāhiya' (infinite). Understand their meanings in context.
3
Reflect on the argument
Consider why the infinity of types of possibles might be used as an objection. The full reasoning is cut off, but the intended challenge is likely that infinite types would entail an actual infinite in God's power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض الثالث: إنه تعالى متمكن من إيجاد جميع الممكنات. ولا شك أن أنواع الممكنات غير متناهية
The third objection: He, exalted, is able to bring into existence all possibles. And there is no doubt that the types of possibles are infinite.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the third objection. The author states that God is fully capable (mutamakkin) of creating all possible things. Since the types (anwāʿ) of possibles are infinite, this implies an infinite multitude of types that God can create. The argument is cut off, but the intent is to use this as another counterexample to the claim that infinite numbers are impossible. If there are infinite types of possibles, then either God creates them (making infinite actual entities) or He has power over them, which still entails an infinite number of objects of power.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This passage presents an objection, not a settled doctrine. Are you someone who runs away from difficult questions about God, or do you face them with trust? What question about God’s attributes have you been avoiding? Commit to studying it with humility.

Second Objection: Infinite Regress in Divine Knowledge
Second objection against finite divine knowledge, with response and refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the impossibility of infinite numbers, how it is argued from divine omniscience, and why the opponent's responses fail.

Core Idea
Infinite regress in God's knowledge challenges the impossibility of infinite numbers.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection against those who deny the existence of infinite numbers. The argument shows that God's omniscience leads to an infinite regress of levels of knowledge, which the opponent cannot dismiss without implying ignorance or change in God. We will walk through the objection, the two attempted rebuttals, and their refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the full objection
Carefully read the Arabic and English translation of the objection. Notice how it starts from God knowing a thing and then derives infinite levels of knowledge, leading to an impossibility according to the opponent's own principle.
The objection uses a reductio ad absurdum: if infinite numbers cannot exist, then this regress would be impossible.
2
Identify the two rebuttals
After the objection, the author presents two possible rebuttals: (1) that knowledge of knowledge is the same as knowledge of the thing, and (2) assuming distinction, that the levels exist only potentially, not actually.
The rebuttals are introduced with 'لا يقال' (It cannot be said).
3
Study the refutations
The author refutes each rebuttal in turn. The first refutation relies on the distinctness of knowledge and known. The second refutation argues that potential knowledge implies ignorance and makes God a locus for accidents.
Pay attention to the term 'محل للحوادث' – locus for accidents – which is a critical concept in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض الثاني: أن نقول: إنه تعالى لما كان عالماً بجميع المعلومات. فإذا علم شيئاً، وجب أن يعلم كونه عالماً بذلك الشيء، وأن يعلم كونه عالماً بكونه عالماً بذلك الشيء، وهكذا إلى ما لا نهاية له. فقد حصل في هذا المعلوم الواحد مراتب لا نهاية لها من المعلومات. ولما كانت معلومات الله تعالى غير متناهية، وثبت: أن لله تعالى بحسب كل واحد من المعلومات علوم لا نهاية لها. فحينئذ يلزم أن يقال: إنه حصل لله تعالى علوم لا نهاية لها، لا مرة واحدة، بل مراراً غير متناهية. وذلك يبطل قولكم: إن الأعداد التي لا نهاية لها، يمتنع دخولها في الوجود.
لا يقال: العلم بالعلم بالشيء، نفس العلم بذلك الشيء. وأيضاً: فبتقدير التغاير، إلا أن هذه المراتب التي لا نهاية لها، لا تحصل بالفعل بل بالقوة.
لأنا نقول: أما الكلام الأول فضعيف، لأن المعلوم والعلم شيئان متغايران. فكان العلم بأحدهما مغايراً للآخر، فكان العلم بالمعلوم مغايراً للعلم بذلك العلم. وأما الكلام الثاني فضعيف، لأنه تعالى لو كان عالماً بتلك المراتب لا بالفعل بل بالقوة، فحينئذ يلزم تجهيل الله تعالى منه. وأيضاً: يلزم كون الله تعالى محلاً للحوادث لأن كل ما كان بالقوة، فإنه لا يمتنع خروجه إلى الفعل.
The second objection: We say: He, exalted, since He is knower of all known things. So if He knows something, it is necessary that He knows that He is knower of that thing, and that He knows that He knows that He is knower of that thing, and so on ad infinitum. So there occurs in this single known thing infinite levels of known things. And since the known things of Allah, exalted, are infinite, and it is established that for Allah, exalted, with respect to each one of the known things there are infinite sciences. Then it necessarily follows that it must be said: There occurred for Allah, exalted, infinite sciences, not once but infinite times. And that invalidates your statement: It is impossible for infinite numbers to enter into existence.
It cannot be said: The knowledge of the knowledge of a thing is the same as the knowledge of that thing. Also: assuming the distinction, except that these infinite levels do not occur actually but potentially.
We say: As for the first statement, it is weak, because the known and the knowledge are two distinct things. So the knowledge of one is different from the knowledge of the other, so the knowledge of the known is different from the knowledge of that knowledge. And as for the second statement, it is weak, because if He, exalted, were knowing those levels not actually but potentially, then there would follow attributing ignorance to Allah, exalted, from it. And also it would follow that Allah, exalted, be a locus for accidents, because everything that is potential, then it is not impossible for it to come into actuality.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against the opponent's claim that infinite numbers cannot exist. The argument is: Since God knows all things, if He knows something, He must know that He knows it, and that He knows that He knows, and so on infinitely. Thus for each known thing there are infinite levels of knowledge. Since God's known things are infinite, there would be infinite sciences infinitely multiplied. This contradicts the opponent's claim that infinite numbers cannot occur. The opponent might respond that knowing that one knows is the same as the original knowledge, or that these levels exist only potentially. The author refutes both: knowledge and the known are distinct, so knowing the knowledge is different; and if it were only potential, it would imply ignorance in God and make Him a locus for accidents (since potential states can become actual). Thus the objection stands.

Key Terms
علم
معلوم
مراتب لا نهاية لها
تجهيل
محل للحوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that knowledge of knowledge is identical to knowledge of the object.
Correction: The author argues that knowledge and known are distinct, so knowing the knowledge is different from knowing the thing.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would follow if God's knowledge of the infinite levels were merely potential?
A) It would be consistent with divine perfection.
B) It would imply ignorance in God and make Him a locus for accidents.
C) It would show that infinite regress is impossible.
D) It would prove that knowledge is identical to known.

Key Takeaway
The objection shows that denying infinite numbers leads to logical difficulties in divine omniscience, and the opponent's counters fail because knowledge and known are distinct and potential knowledge is inconsistent with God's nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge of you were merely potential rather than actual, how would your relationship with Him change? What does it mean for your daily life that God actually knows every detail of your thoughts and actions, including your hidden intentions, without any potentiality?
?
The passage refutes the idea that knowledge of knowledge is identical to knowledge of the object. Are there areas in your own life where you confuse knowing about something (e.g., knowing that you know) with actually knowing the thing itself? How can you move from mere awareness to genuine, transformative knowledge of Allah?
?
Consider the statement that potentiality in God would make Him a locus for accidents. Do you ever treat your faith as something merely potential—something you know in theory but not in practice? What would it mean for you to make your faith actual, lived, and certain, so that you never attribute to Allah even the shadow of imperfection?

Third Objection: Infinite Capacities in Power
Third objection: infinite distinct possibilities in divine power

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third objection extends the infinite-regress argument from divine knowledge to divine power, and how the proof of distinct possibilities by differential knowability works.

Core Idea
Distinct knowability proves distinct possibilities, leading to infinite regress in power.

The third objection applies the same reasoning used against infinite divine knowledge to divine power. The author argues that since God can create all possible things, and the types of possibles are infinite, each type's possibility is distinct. This is proven by the fact that one can know one possibility without knowing another. Thus, the problem of infinite distinctions—and the same earlier objection—returns.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: two premises, a conclusion, and a proof. The author connects this objection back to the earlier one on knowledge.
The proof uses epistemic distinction: if we can know one possibility while ignorant of another, they must be really distinct.
2
Identify the two key premises
The passage states: (1) God is capable of all possible things, (2) the types of possibles are infinite. Recognize that these set up the argument for infinite distinct possibilities.
3
Understand the proof of distinctness
The author argues that the possibility of one type is distinct from another because we can know one without knowing the other. This is a direct application of the earlier argument about knowledge.
Think about why knowability implies real distinction in this context.
4
Connect to the earlier objection
The passage ends by stating that the same question from the discussion of knowledge returns. Recall what that question was (infinite regress of distinct attributes).
This shows the objection is part of a cumulative case against infinite attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض الثالث: إنه تعالى متمكن من إيجاد جميع الممكنات. ولا شك أن أنواع الممكنات غير متناهية. ولا شك أن إمكان إيجاد أحد تلك الأنواع مغاير لإمكان إيجاد النوع الآخر. بدليل: أنه يصح العلم بأحد الإمكانين مع الجهل بالإمكان الآخر، وحينئذ يعود السؤال المذكور في العلم.
The third objection: He, exalted, is capable of bringing into existence all possible things. And there is no doubt that the types of possible things are infinite. And there is no doubt that the possibility of bringing into existence one of those types is distinct from the possibility of bringing into existence the other type. The proof: it is valid to know one of the two possibilities while being ignorant of the other possibility, and then the question mentioned in connection with knowledge returns.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection, which extends the same infinite-regress argument to divine power (qudra). The author argues that since God can create all possible things, and the types of possibles are infinite, the possibility of creating each type is distinct from the possibility of creating another type. This is proven by the fact that one can know one possibility while being ignorant of another. Therefore, the same problem of infinite distinctions arises as with knowledge, and the earlier objection recurs.

Key Takeaway
The third objection argues that God's power to create infinite types of possibles requires infinite distinct possibilities, proven by differential knowability, renewing the infinite regress problem.

?

This lesson demands a choice: submit to the mystery or remain in intellectual pride.
?
The argument hinges on the fact that you can know one possibility while being ignorant of another. Consider your own knowledge of God's attributes: are you content with knowing just some aspects, or do you secretly wish to comprehend everything? How does this desire for complete knowledge become a form of arrogance that distances you from true servitude? Commit today to seeking knowledge for worship, not for control.
?
If the infinite regress argument is valid, it exposes the emptiness of trying to fully rationalize God. Will you let it push you to deeper reliance on God's own words, or will you cling to your intellectual constructs? Your answer reveals where your real trust lies.

The Fourth Objection: Only Possibles Are Objects of Power
Fourth objection: Only possibles are objects of power, while all three modes are objects of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection that distinguishes objects of divine knowledge from objects of divine power based on the threefold classification of existence.

Core Idea
Divine power is limited to possibles, while knowledge encompasses all three modes of existence.

In this passage, the author presents a fourth objection that challenges the scope of divine power. By classifying everything into three categories—necessary, impossible, and possible—the objection argues that only possibles fall under God's power, while all three fall under His knowledge. This distinction raises important questions about the relationship between God's knowledge and power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the three categories and the two claims about knowledge and power.
The passage is short but dense—take your time.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage classifies all matters into three modes: necessary, impossible, and possible. Note the Arabic terms: واجب الوجود, ممتنع الوجود, ممكن الوجود.
These are fundamental concepts in kalam.
3
Distinguish knowledge from power
The objection asserts that all three modes are objects of knowledge (داخلة في المعلومية), but only the possible is an object of power (المقدور فليس إلا الممكن). Reflect on why this is an objection.
The implication is that God's power is more limited than His knowledge.
4
Interpret the concluding phrase
The passage ends with 'لا نهاية لمعلومات الله ومقدوراته مع الثلاثة.' This is somewhat ambiguous; it likely means that both known things and possible things are infinite, but the ranks of power are fewer. Discuss what this means for the objection.
The phrase 'مع الثلاثة' may be incomplete—perhaps 'together with the three categories'.

النقض الرابع: كل أمر فهو إما واجب الوجود لذاته، أو ممتنع الوجود لذاته، أو ممكن الوجود لذاته. والأقسام الثلاثة داخلة في المعلومية، وأما المقدور فليس إلا الممكن. فمراتب المقدورات أقل من مراتب المعلومات، أنه لا نهاية لمعلومات الله ومقدوراته مع الثلاثة.
The fourth objection: Every matter is either necessary of existence by itself, or impossible of existence by itself, or possible of existence by itself. And the three divisions are included under knowability. As for the object of power, it is only the possible. So the ranks of objects of power are less than the ranks of objects of knowledge, that there is no end to the known things of Allah and His possible things with the three.

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection based on a classification of all things into three modes: necessarily existent, impossible, and possible. All three are knowable, but only the possible is an object of divine power (since God cannot create the impossible or change the necessary). Thus the range of objects of power is narrower than that of knowledge. The conclusion seems to be that both are infinite, but the objector may be raising a problem about the relationship between the two infinities.

The Infinity of Possibles and Distinction of Possibilities
Introduction to the problem of infinite possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that possible things are infinite and that the possibility of one type is distinct from another, based on the difference in knowledge.

Core Idea
Infinite possibles mean each possibility is distinct and knowable separately.

Before presenting the fourth refutation, the author sets up a key premise: possible things are infinite, and the possibility of creating one kind is different from another. This is proven by the fact that we can know one possibility without knowing another. This leads back to a prior question about divine knowledge, setting the stage for the refutation that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author states a claim and then gives evidence.
The evidence is based on the knowability of distinct possibilities.
2
Identify the main claim and evidence
The main claim is that possible things are infinite and the possibility of each kind is distinct. The evidence is that one can know one possibility while being ignorant of another.
3
Understand the implication
This distinction leads back to an earlier question about God's knowledge. Why does the author need to establish this? It sets up a problem that the refutation will address.
The question 'returned' is about how God knows infinite distinct possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الممكنات غير متناهية . ولا شك أن إمكان إيجاد أحد تلك الأنواع : مغاير لإمكان إيجاد النوع الآخر . بدليل : أنه يصح العلم بأحد الإمكانين مع الجهل بالإمكان الآخر ، وحينئذ يعود السؤال المذكور في العلم .
النقض الرابع : كل أمر فهو إما واجب الوجود لذاته ، أو ممتنع الوجود لذاته ، أو ممكن الوجود لذاته . والأقسام الثلاثة داخلة في المعلومية ، وأما المقدور فليس إلا الممكن . فمراتب المقدورات، أقل من مراتب المعلومات ، أنه لا نهاية المعلومات الله والمقدوراته مع
الثلاثة
Possible things are infinite. There is no doubt that the possibility of bringing into existence one of those kinds is different from the possibility of bringing into existence another kind. Evidence: that it is valid to know one of the two possibilities while being ignorant of the other possibility. Then the aforementioned question regarding knowledge returns.
The fourth refutation: Every thing is either necessary of existence in itself, or impossible of existence in itself, or possible of existence in itself. And the three divisions are included in knowability; as for what is within power, it is only the possible. So the ranks of things within power are less than the ranks of things known, that God's knowledge has no end, and His powers [also?] with the three.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage begins with an introductory statement about the infinity of possible things and the distinction between possibilities of different types, then presents the fourth refutation (نقض) against an opponent's view. The introduction argues that possibilities are infinite and that the possibility of creating one type is distinct from another, proven by the fact that one can know one possibility without knowing another. This leads back to a prior question about knowledge.

The fourth refutation itself classifies all things into three categories: necessary in itself, impossible in itself, and possible in itself. The author asserts that all three categories are objects of God's knowledge, but only the possible is within His power. Consequently, the range of things that God can do (the possible) is narrower than the range of things He knows (all three categories). The text then mentions that God's knowledge is infinite, and seems to suggest that His power is also infinite, but the sentence is cut off. The argument is a refutation likely aimed at those who claim that God's power is limited or that the objects of power and knowledge are coextensive.

إمكان (possibility)

A) They have different causes.

C) They are infinite in number.
D) God's power is limited.

Key Takeaway
Infinite possibilities are distinct in their possibility, and this difference is known through the knowability of each independent of the others.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God knows every distinct possibility—every potential creature, event, and world—does that make you feel more secure in His plan, or does it unsettle you to think He could have created an infinite number of alternatives? How does this truth affect your trust in the specific life He has given you?
?
The passage says it is valid to know one possibility while being ignorant of another. Are there possibilities in your own life—paths you could have taken—that you have ignored or refused to acknowledge? What would it mean for you to trust that God knows each of those distinct possibilities and chose this one for a reason?
?
If God's knowledge of possibilities is so vast that each one is distinct, how should that shape your attitude toward the unknown future? Are you living as if the future is a blur of vague possibilities, or are you confident that every detail is distinct and known to Him? What can you do today to align your heart with that reality?

Fourth Refutation: Three Categories of Existence
Fourth refutation: classification of existence into three categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of existence (necessary, impossible, possible) and how they relate to God's knowledge and power, as presented in the fourth refutation.

Core Idea
God knows all three categories but can only create the possible.

The author now presents the fourth refutation, which classifies every thing into three categories: necessary, impossible, and possible in themselves. While all three are known to God, only the possible lies within His power. This distinction shows that the objects of divine power are a subset of the objects of divine knowledge, countering an opposing view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage from 'النقض الرابع' to the end. Note the three categories stated at the beginning.
The text is cut off at the end; focus on the clear part.
2
Identify the three categories
The three categories are: necessary of existence in itself, impossible of existence in itself, and possible of existence in itself. Understand what each means.
These are fundamental ontological categories in Islamic theology.
3
Relate to knowledge and power
The passage says all three are known (included in knowability), but only the possible is within power. Reflect on why necessary and impossible are not within power.
Necessary exists by itself, impossible cannot exist, so neither is an object of power.
4
Consider the refutation's target
The refutation likely counters an opponent who claims that God's power and knowledge are coextensive. By showing that power is narrower, the author refutes that view.
The incomplete final line might have concluded that God's power is also infinite but with the three categories.

The Infinite Regress of Necessity
Second sub-argument demonstrating infinite regress from necessity attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second sub-argument against the opponent's conception of the attribute of necessity, and why it leads to infinite regress.

Core Idea
If necessity is an attribute, it cannot be possible—but then it requires another necessity, ad infinitum.

This passage presents the second sub-argument within the tenth refutation. It focuses on the attribute of necessity (wujūb) in the Necessary Being (wājib al-wujūd). Sanusi aims to show that if necessity is considered an attribute distinct from the essence, then it leads to an infinite regress of necessary attributes, thereby refuting the opponent's model.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully. Notice the logical flow: premise, conditional, conclusion, and the charge of infinite regress.
The passage is concise; each phrase carries logical weight.
2
Identify the core argument
The argument states that the necessity of the Necessary cannot be possible (otherwise the Necessary would not be necessary), so it must be necessary. But then it is an additional attribute, and we need another necessity for that attribute, leading to tasalsul.
Infinite regress is a standard reductio ad absurdum in kalam.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this refutes the opponent: if attributes are distinct and necessary, then each attribute itself requires necessity, leading to an endless chain. This shows the opponent's model is incoherent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض العاشر : الثاني : إن كون الواجب واجباً : صفة لذات الواجب . ثم ذلك الوجوب لا يعقل أن يكون ممكناً . وإلا لما بقي الواجب واجباً ، بل وجب كونه واجباً ، فيكون وجوبه زائداً عليه ، ولزم التسلسل
The tenth refutation: The second: Indeed, the fact that the Necessary (al-wājib) is necessary is an attribute of the essence of the Necessary. Then that necessity (al-wujūb) cannot be conceived as being possible; otherwise, the Necessary would not remain necessary. Rather, it must be necessary, so its necessity is additional to it, and infinite regress follows.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This is the second sub-argument within the tenth refutation. It deals with the attribute of necessity (wujūb) in the Necessary Being (wājib al-wujūd). The argument states that the fact that the Necessary is necessary is itself an attribute of its essence. This attribute (the necessity) cannot be merely possible; if it were possible, then the Necessary would not be necessary by itself. Therefore, that necessity must itself be necessary. But if it is necessary, then it is an additional attribute distinct from the essence, and that attribute itself would need to be necessary, leading to an infinite regress of necessary attributes. This shows that the opponent's way of conceiving attributes leads to absurdity.

Key Terms
واجب (wājib)
وجوب (wujūb)
صفة (ṣifa)
ذات (dhāt)
تسلسل (tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies that God has the attribute of necessity.
Correction: The argument does not deny the attribute; rather, it exposes a flawed metaphysical model that treats attributes as distinct necessary entities, leading to infinite regress.

Checkpoint
In the second sub-argument, what is the consequence if the necessity of the Necessary were possible?
A) It would make God possible.
B) The Necessary would cease to be necessary.
C) It would lead to an infinite regress immediately.
D) It would prove the existence of another necessary being.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's model of a distinct necessary attribute leads to an infinite regress, demonstrating its logical impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on God's attributes, do you ever unconsciously treat them as separate parts 'added' to His essence, like layers on a person's personality? How does this argument expose the error of that mental habit?
?
If God's necessity is not an extra attribute but simply what He is, what does that mean for how you approach Him in worship — do you seek a 'necessary' relationship or treat Him as optional?
?
Suppose you caught yourself thinking that God's attributes are like human qualities, only bigger. What specifically would you need to unlearn, and how will you replace that picture with the truth that His essence and attributes are one? What concrete step will you take today to deepen your tawḥīd?
