The Second Regress: Necessity Cannot Be Possible
Second regress on the necessity of the Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second regress argument that the attribute of necessity in the Necessary Being cannot be possible and must itself be necessary, leading to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
The necessity of the Necessary Being cannot be merely possible.

This lesson examines a subtle yet powerful argument about the nature of the attribute of necessity (wujūb) in the Necessary Being. The author presents a second regress that highlights a paradox: if necessity were possible, the Necessary Being would not be truly necessary. Understanding this argument deepens our appreciation of how classical kalam thinkers reasoned about divine attributes and essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the logical flow settle in your mind.
Notice that this is a very compressed argument.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage begins by stating that the necessity of the Necessary is an attribute of its essence. This is the starting point.
3
Examine the reason against possibility
Ask: Why can't this attribute be possible? The passage says if it were possible, the Necessary would not remain necessary.
Think about what 'possible' means here — contingent, could be otherwise.
4
Trace the regress
If necessity is necessary, it becomes an additional attribute, and then its own necessity would be another attribute, and so on infinitely.
This is the regress argument (tasalsul).
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The regress shows the difficulty of treating necessity as a real attribute separate from the essence. What might this imply about divine simplicity?
This connects to broader theological debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن كون الواجب واجباً : صفة لذات الواجب . ثم ذلك الوجوب لا يعقل أن يكون ممكناً . وإلا لما بقي الواجب واجباً ، بل وجب كونه واجباً ، فيكون وجوبه زائداً عليه ، ولزم التسلسل
The second: that the Necessary's being necessary (kawn al-wājib wājiban) is an attribute of the essence of the Necessary. Then that necessity (al-wujūb) is not intelligible as being possible, otherwise the Necessary would not remain necessary; rather, its being necessary is necessary, so its necessity is additional to it, and infinite regress follows.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents the second regress argument. It focuses on the attribute of necessity (wujūb) for the Necessary Being (al-wājib). The argument: the property 'being necessary' is an attribute of the essence of the Necessary. This attribute cannot be merely possible (because then the essence would be only possibly necessary, which contradicts being necessary). Therefore, the necessity itself must be necessary. That means the necessity is additional to the essence, and then the necessity of that necessity would be another attribute, leading to an infinite regress.

Key Terms
واجب
وجوب
صفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the attribute of necessity is identical to the essence and cannot be considered additional.
Correction: The passage explicitly argues that if necessity is necessary, then it becomes an attribute additional to the essence, leading to a regress. This shows that in this framework, necessity is conceptually separable.

Checkpoint
Why does the second regress argue that necessity cannot be possible?
A) Because the Necessary Being is self-sufficient
B) Because if necessity were possible, the Necessary might not be necessary
C) Because attributes are not real
D) Because possibility is not an attribute

Key Takeaway
The second regress reveals the logical tension in treating necessity as a real attribute separate from the essence, leading to an infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is necessary', do you ever imagine that His necessity is something added to Him, like a quality on top of His essence? How does this argument force you to re-examine that assumption? What will you do to ensure your understanding of Allah's necessity is pure and untainted by such a mistake?
?
The argument shows that any attribute distinct from God's essence leads to logical absurdity. Are there other attributes of Allah that you unknowingly treat as separate from His essence, making Him seem compound? Take a moment to name one and commit to reforming your understanding.
?
If the necessity of Allah is not a real attribute but is inherent to His very being, how should this affect the certainty of your reliance on Him? Does your daily trust reflect this deep, unbreakable necessity, or do you still act as if Allah's help is somehow contingent? What one change will you make today to shift your reliance?

Refuting the Objection on Divine Agency over Accidents and Aggregates
Author refutes objection about God being agent of each accident or their aggregate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical fallacy in the objection that God's agency over individual accidents does not imply agency over their aggregate, and how the author exposes its circular reasoning.

Core Idea
If God is the agent of each part, He is the agent of the whole—unless circularity sneaks in.

This passage tackles a sophisticated objection: if God creates each accident individually, does He also create the aggregate? The opponent tries to escape by claiming the aggregate is necessary when its parts occur. Sanusi dismantles this by exposing a tautology. Let's follow his razor-sharp logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: objection, author's counter, opponent's rejoinder, author's analysis.
Focus on the opponent's claim about the aggregate being necessary.
2
Identify the two claims
The opponent says God is either agent of each accident or agent of the aggregate. The author argues: if agent of each, then agent of aggregate. Then opponent says aggregate is necessary when its individuals occur.
3
Analyze the two interpretations
The author asks: do you mean the aggregate is necessary when each individual occurs, or when the aggregate occurs? The first is false (e.g., ten is not necessary when each unit appears). The second is circular.
This is the core logical move.
4
Understand the circularity
The second interpretation reduces to 'the aggregate is necessary when the aggregate occurs', which explains a thing by itself (تعليل الشيء بنفسه). This is logically impossible.
Circular reasoning invalidates the objection.
5
Conclude the lesson
Thus the opponent's objection fails. The author's argument stands: if God is agent of each accident, He is agent of the aggregate. The attempt to avoid this leads to absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثاني : وهو قولكم (۱) : ( إنه تعالى فاعل لكل واحد من تلك الحوادث ، أو فاعل لمجموعها ، فنقول : لما سلمتم كونه تعالى فاعلاً لكــل واحد من تلك الحوادث ، وجب كونه فاعلاً لمجموعها . قوله : ( المجموع واجب الحصول عند حصول آحاده قلنا تعنون به: أنه واجب الحصول عند كل واحد من آحاده ، أو تعنون به : أنه واجب الحصول عند مجموع أحاده ؟ والأول : ظاهر الفساد . لأن العشرة لا تكون واجبة الحصول عند حصول كل آحادها . والثاني أيضاً : ظاهر الفساد لأن العشرة ليست إلا جملة آحادها . فحينئذ يرجع حاصل الكلام : إلى أن المجموع واجب الحصول ، عند حصول المجموع ، وذلك يقتضي تعليل الشيء بنفسه . وإنه محال
As for the second question: it is your statement: 'He, exalted, is an agent for each one of those accidents, or an agent for their aggregate.' We say: since you have conceded that He, exalted, is an agent for each one of those accidents, then He must be an agent for their aggregate. His statement: 'The aggregate is necessarily obtained at the occurrence of its individuals.' We say: do you mean by it that it is necessarily obtained at each one of its individuals, or do you mean by it that it is necessarily obtained at the aggregate of its individuals? The first is clearly false, because ten is not necessarily obtained at the occurrence of each of its units. The second is also clearly false, because ten is nothing but the sum of its units. Then the conclusion of the statement reduces to: that the aggregate is necessarily obtained at the occurrence of the aggregate, and that requires the explanation of a thing by itself, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a response to the second question. The opponent claims that God is either the agent of each individual accident or the agent of their aggregate. The author argues that if God is agent of each, then He is agent of the aggregate. The opponent then says the aggregate is necessary when its individuals occur. The author analyzes this claim by asking for clarification: do you mean the aggregate is necessary at the occurrence of each individual (which is false because, e.g., ten is not necessary when each unit appears) or at the occurrence of the aggregate itself (which reduces to saying the aggregate is necessary when it occurs, which is circular—explaining a thing by itself)? Both options are absurd, so the objection fails.

Key Terms
فاعل
مجموع
آحاد
تعليل الشيء بنفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the aggregate's necessity is independent of its parts, allowing the opponent to evade the implication that God is agent of the aggregate.
Correction: The aggregate's necessity is nothing but the necessity of its parts collectively; claiming it is necessary when its individuals occur leads to circularity if interpreted consistently.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the opponent's statement that the aggregate is necessarily obtained at the occurrence of its individuals?
A) Because the aggregate is not a real entity.
B) Because it implies explaining a thing by itself.
C) Because God is not the agent of aggregates.
D) Because the individuals are not necessary.

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because it reduces to explaining a thing by itself, which is logically impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own beliefs about God's actions in the world, do you ever separate His agency over the whole from His agency over the parts? How might the lesson's logic reveal a weakness in that separation?
?
Think of a time you defended a belief with circular reasoning—where the justification assumed what it was trying to prove. How does this lesson's exposure of circularity challenge you to examine your own arguments more carefully?
?
If the aggregate is nothing but the sum of its parts, what does that imply about how you view God's involvement in every detail of your life? Are you tempted to think some details are too small for His agency?

The Necessity of Priority for the Free Agent
Author refutes claim that agent's priority over act is unnecessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the free agent must precede its act and how the author distinguishes between free and necessitating agency.

Core Idea
Free agency requires priority; necessitating agency does not.

The author directly refutes the objection that the agent's priority over the act is not necessary. He clarifies that this only applies to the necessitating agent, not the free agent. The key intuition is that a free agent acts by intention, and intending to bring about something that already exists is self-contradictory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the author's direct refutation at the start.
2
Identify the two types of agents
The author mentions two types: the free agent (الفاعل المختار) and the necessitating agent (الموجب بالذات). Understand how they differ in this context.
The distinction is central to the response.
3
Understand the intuitive impossibility argument
Reflect on why intending to bring about something that already exists is impossible by intuition. This is the core reason for priority.
Relate this to everyday examples of intention and action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثالث : وهو قوله : وتقدم الفاعل على الفعل : غير واجب ، فنقول : هذا باطل . لأن الفاعل المختار ، هو الذي يفعل بواسطة القصد (۱) والقصد إلى إيجاد الشيء حال بقائه : محال معلوم الامتناع بالبديهة وأما الوجوه التي ذكرتموها فنحملها على الموجب بالذات . وذلك غير ممتنع . إنما الممتنع حصول الفعل بالفاعل المختار ، مع كون ذلك الفعل قديماً .
As for the third question: it is his statement: 'The priority of the agent over the act is not necessary.' We say: This is false. Because the free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār) is the one who acts by means of intention (qaṣd). And the intention to bring about a thing while it still exists is impossible, known to be impossible by intuition. As for the aspects you mentioned, we apply them to the necessitating agent (al-mūjib bi-l-dhāt). That is not impossible. Only the occurrence of the act by the free agent while that act is eternal is impossible.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage responds to the third question. The opponent says that the agent's priority over the act is not necessary. The author refutes this by explaining that the free agent (الفاعل المختار) acts through intention, and intending to bring about something while it already exists is intuitively impossible. Therefore, the agent must precede the act. The author then clarifies that any arguments the opponent might bring apply to the necessitating agent (الموجب بالذات), not to the free agent. For the free agent, the impossible scenario is producing an eternal act; for the necessitating agent, priority is not required. Thus, the objection is based on confusing two types of agency.

Key Terms
فاعل مختار
قصد
موجب بالذات
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all agency requires priority of agent over act.
Correction: The author shows that priority is necessary only for the free agent, not for the necessitating agent.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the intention to bring about a thing while it still exists?
A) It is possible for the free agent.
B) It is impossible by intuition.
C) It is possible for the necessitating agent.
D) It is necessary for the act to be eternal.

Key Takeaway
The free agent must precede its act because its intention cannot be directed at an already existing thing, distinguishing it from the necessitating agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make plans, do you ever assume that God's actions are automatic or predictable? How does this passage remind you that God's priority over His acts means He is always intentional and not bound by necessity? How will you remember this when you feel like your dua is not being answered?
?

?
Think of a time you acted without intention, driven by habit or compulsion. How does that contrast with God's free agency? What would change in your life if you imitated God's intentionality more consciously?

Three Proofs for the Possibility of Change Between Non-Existents
Objector's three proofs for change between non-existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objector argues that two non-existent attributes can succeed each other, using three proofs involving impossibility, divine knowledge, and divine action.

Core Idea
Non-existents can change without real attributes.

This passage presents a counterargument to the claim that two non-existent states cannot replace each other. The objector offers three proofs, each showing that changes can occur between non-existents without implying real attributes. The first proof uses impossibility and possibility, the second divine knowledge, and the third divine action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the objector's argument flow naturally.
Note that the third proof is incomplete due to missing text.
2
Identify the three proofs
Locate each of the three proofs. Notice how each begins with 'The first:', 'The second:', 'The third:'.
The third proof cuts off mid-sentence.
3
Analyze the first proof
Understand the argument that impossibility and possibility cannot be existing attributes. Trace the reasoning that if they were existent, contradictions would follow.
This proof establishes that both are non-existent attributes that can change.
4
Analyze the second proof
See how the change in truth-value of 'God knows the world' does not imply a change in God's essence. This shows change between non-existents is possible.
The objector uses divine knowledge as an example.
5
Consider the third proof
Though incomplete, the third proof parallels the second using divine action (bringing into existence). Consider how it would likely conclude similarly.
The available text suggests a parallel argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الناس من قال: هذا الاحتمال غير باطل. ويدل عليه وجوه: الأول: وهو أن حدوث الحوادث في الأزل، كان ممتنعاً لعينه. ثم انقلب في لا يزال ممكناً لعينه. ثم ثبت بالدليل: أن الامتناع لا يعقل أن يكون صفة موجودة، وكذلك الإمكان لا يعقل كونه صفة موجودة. أما الامتناع فالدليل عليه: أنه لو كان صفة موجودة، لكان الموصوف بتلك الصفة أولى أن يكون شيئاً موجوداً، لشهادة صريح العقل بأن الصفة الموجودة يمتنع قيامها بالنفي المحض والعدم الصرف. فيلزم أن يكون ممتنع الوجود لذاته، واجب الوجود لذاته، وذلك محال. وأما الإمكان. فلانه لو كان موجوداً، لكان إما واجباً لذاته، وإما ممتنعاً لذاته، وإما ممكناً لذاته. والأول باطل. لأن الإمكان صفة، للممكن وصفة الشيء مفتقرة إلى الموصوف. والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته. والثاني باطل، لأن كل ما كان موجوداً، لم يكن ممتنع الوجود. والثالث باطل، وإلا لكان إمكان الإمكان مغايراً له، ولزم التسلسل، وهو محال. فيثبت: أن الامتناع قد تبدل بالإمكان، مع أن كل واحد منهما صفة عدمية. الثاني: إن العالم حين كان معدوماً، فإنه يصدق على البارى تعالى أنه ما كان عالماً بوجوده. وحين صار موجوداً، فإنه يصدق عليه أنه صار عالماً بوجوده. فعلى هذا التقدير: صدق أن البارى تعالى ما كان عالماً بوجود العالم، ثم صار عالماً بوجوده. فيلزم أن تحدث صفة العلم في ذات الله تعالى وذلك باطل. فعلمنا: أن صدق هذا النفي والإثبات، لا يدل على حدوث أمر، ولا على زواله. الثالث: إن العالم حين كان معدوماً، فإنه صدق على البارىء تعالى: بأنه ما أوجده. ولما دخل في الوجود، قد صدق عليه: أنه أوجده. فعل هذا التقدير: صدق على البارى تعالى: أنه ما كان موجوداً للعالم، ثم صار...
And among people there are those who say: This possibility is not invalid. And it is indicated by proofs: The first: That the occurrence of events in pre-eternity was impossible in itself. Then it changed to being possible in itself in 'lā yazālu' (eternity future). Then it was established by proof that impossibility cannot be understood as an existing attribute, and likewise possibility cannot be understood as an existing attribute. As for impossibility, the proof for it is: if it were an existing attribute, then the thing described by that attribute would more rightly be an existing thing, due to the testimony of clear reason that an existing attribute cannot subsist in pure negation and bare non-existence. So it would follow that it is necessarily existent in itself and necessary of existence in itself, and that is impossible. And as for possibility: because if it were existent, it would be either necessary in itself, or impossible in itself, or possible in itself. The first is false, because possibility is an attribute of the possible, and an attribute of a thing depends on the thing described, and what depends on another is possible in itself. The second is false, because everything that exists is not impossible of existence. The third is false, otherwise the possibility of possibility would be different from it, and infinite regress would follow, which is impossible. So it is established that impossibility changes into possibility, even though each one of them is a non-existent attribute. The second: That when the world was non-existent, it was true of the Exalted Creator that He was not knowing its existence. And when it came into existence, it is true of Him that He became knowing its existence. So on this estimation, it is true that the Exalted Creator was not knowing the existence of the world, then became knowing its existence. So it would follow that the attribute of knowledge occurs in the essence of God, and that is false. So we know that the truth of this negation and affirmation does not indicate the occurrence of something nor its removal. The third: That when the world was non-existent, it was true of the Exalted Creator that He did not bring it into existence. And when it entered existence, it is true of Him that He brought it into existence. So on this estimation, it is true of the Exalted Creator that He was not existent for the world, then became...
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage presents three counterarguments from those who reject the author's claim that two non-existents cannot replace each other. The first proof appeals to the case of impossibility and possibility in the context of the world's temporal origination. It argues that pre-eternal impossibility (the world being impossible) turned into possibility (the world becoming possible), and both impossibility and possibility are non-existent attributes (صفات عدمية) because they cannot be existing attributes. The proof shows that if they were existent, they would lead to contradictions (e.g., impossible becoming necessary). Therefore, change can occur between non-existent attributes. The second proof uses divine knowledge: before the world existed, God did not know it as existent; after it exists, He knows it as existent. This change in truth-value does not imply a change in God's essence (as that would be impossible). Therefore, change between non-existents is possible. The third proof (incomplete) parallels the second but with divine action (creating). The text cuts off, but the argument would likely conclude that such changes do not require real attributes. These proofs are the objector's challenge to the author's position.

Key Terms
امتناع
إمكان
صفة عدمية
العلم
الإيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the objector denies that attributes can change.
Correction: The objector is not denying that attributes can change; rather, they are arguing that non-existents can serve as the basis for change without real attributes.

Checkpoint
In the first proof, why is it argued that impossibility cannot be an existing attribute?
A) Because impossibility is not a thing
B) Because an existing attribute cannot subsist in pure non-existence
C) Because impossibility is eternal
D) Because possibility is a necessary attribute

Key Takeaway
The objector argues that changes between non-existent states are possible without requiring real attributes, as shown by impossibility, divine knowledge, and divine action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector argues that God's knowing the world after not knowing it does not imply change in God's essence. Think of a moment when you felt God was distant or unaware of your situation, and later felt His presence. Did you assume God changed? How does this argument challenge you to trust in God's unchanging nature while still believing He responds to your prayers? What practical step can you take today to rely on God's eternal knowledge rather than your changing feelings?
?
If possibility is a non-existent attribute, then your potential for good or bad deeds is not a 'thing' within you but a relationship to God's creation. How does that affect your view of free will and your responsibility to choose good? Will you live today as if your choices are real even if the 'possibility' is not an attribute? How can you use this understanding to increase your intention to obey God?
?
The objector uses logic to defend that change can happen without real attributes. But our hearts often feel that our spiritual states (like faith or doubt) are real things that come and go. If these states are non-existent attributes (relations or negations), does that make them less real to you? Or does it shift your reliance from your own fluctuating attributes to God's unchanging essence? Commit to one specific way you will remember this distinction in your worship today.

Divine Attributes
beginner
4 min
Refuting the Claim that God's Knowledge Changes
Sanusi refutes the claim that God's knowledge changes with the world's existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the apparent change in God's knowledge (when the world comes into existence) does not entail a change in God's essence.

Core Idea
Statements about God's knowledge can change without His knowledge changing.

Sanusi presents a second argument against those who claim that God's knowledge of the world must change when the world comes into existence. The opponent argues that before creation God was not knowing the world, and after creation He is knowing it. Sanusi refutes this by showing that such statements do not imply any actual change in God's attribute of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get its flow, then read the literal English translation. Pay attention to the structure: the opponent's claim and Sanusi's response.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
Find the two conditional statements: (1) when the world was non-existent, God was not knowing its existence; (2) when it became existent, He became knowing. Notice how the opponent concludes that this implies a change in God.
The opponent's reasoning is based on the apparent change in the truth value of statements about God's knowledge.
3
Understand Sanusi's refutation
Sanusi argues that the truth of such negative and positive statements does not indicate any actual occurrence or removal of an attribute in God's essence. The change is only in the known object, not in God's eternal knowledge.
This refutation is a standard kalam defense of divine immutability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن العالم حين كان معدوماً، فإنه يصدق على البارى تعالى أنه ما كان عالماً بوجوده. وحين صار موجوداً، فإنه يصدق عليه أنه صار عالماً بوجوده. فعلى هذا التقدير : صدق أن البارى تعالى ما كان عالماً بوجود العالم، ثم صار عالماً بوجوده. فيلزم أن تحدث صفة العلم في ذات الله تعالى وذلك باطل. فعلمنا : أن صدق هذا النفي والإثبات، لا يدل على حدوث أمر، ولا على زواله
Second: When the world was non-existent, it is true of God most high that He was not knowing of its existence. And when it became existent, it is true of Him that He became knowing of its existence. So on this supposition: it is true that God most high was not knowing of the existence of the world, then He became knowing of its existence. Thus it would follow that the attribute of knowledge arises anew in God's essence, and that is false. So we know: that the truth of this negation and affirmation does not indicate the occurrence of something, nor its removal.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument from an opponent and the author's refutation. The opponent's argument: if the world was non-existent, God was not knowing of its existence; when it became existent, He became knowing – this implies a change (huduth) in God's knowledge. The author refutes this by saying that the truth of such negative and positive statements does not entail any actual occurrence or removal of an attribute in God's essence. The knowledge of God is eternal and unchanging; the change is only in the relation between God's knowledge and the object known. The argument is a common one in kalam for defending divine immutability against objections based on apparent changes in God's knowledge of temporal events.

Key Terms
العلم (al-'ilm)
البارى تعالى (al-Bari ta'ala)
الحدوث (al-huduth)
النفي والإثبات (al-nafy wa al-ithbat)
ذات الله (dhat Allah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's knowledge changes when things come into being.
Correction: Kalam holds that God's knowledge is eternal and covers all things at once; the change is only in the known object, not in God's attribute.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does the author draw from the opponent's argument about God's knowledge?
A) That God's knowledge changes.
B) That the truth of negation and affirmation does not imply change in God.
C) That God was not knowing the world before its existence.
D) That knowledge is an eternal attribute.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is eternal and unchanging; apparent changes in statements about His knowledge merely reflect changes in the known objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you catch yourself thinking that God now knows a new situation because it just happened, what specific step will you take to remind yourself that His knowledge never changes? Write down the correction from Sanusi's argument and recite it.
?
Have you ever felt that God did not know your pain because it wasn't there before? How does the distinction between 'true statements' and 'actual attributes' in this lesson expose the error in that feeling? Commit to correcting that thought whenever it arises.
?
If you truly believe that God's knowledge does not 'become' or change, how will it transform the way you speak about Him in your daily supplications? Will you now avoid saying 'You now know' and instead say 'You always knew'? Write a short resolve.

Sanusi's Refutation of Change in God's Attribute of Creation
Sanusi refutes the claim that creation implies change in God's attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's argument against the claim that God's bringing the world into existence implies a change in His attribute of creation.

Core Idea
God's act of creation does not entail change in His essence.

This passage presents a third argument from Sanusi's theological work. The opponent suggests that the shift from 'God did not create the world' to 'God created the world' indicates a change in God. Sanusi refutes this by showing that if such a change were real, it would lead to an infinite regress or, if reduced to the world's existence, deny the Creator altogether. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Sanusi sets up the opponent's claim and then refutes it with two lines of argument.
The passage contains a conditional argument and an objection-response.
2
Identify the opponent's core claim
The opponent argues that because the statement about God changes from 'He did not create' to 'He created', this implies a change in God's attribute of creation.
This is a common anthropomorphic misunderstanding.
3
Trace Sanusi's first refutation
Sanusi argues that if this change implied an attribute occurring or vanishing in God's essence, then that new attribute would itself be created, leading to an infinite regress.
This highlights the impossibility of change in the immutable essence.
4
Understand the second refutation
Sanusi anticipates an objection that 'God's being existent for the world' is just the world's existence. He shows this reduces God's act to the effect, making the world self-existent, which denies the Creator.
This is a powerful refutation of reductionist views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن العالم حين كان معدوماً، فإنه صدق على البارىء تعالى : بأنه ما أوجده. ولما دخل في الوجود، قد صدق عليه : أنه أوجده. فعلى هذا التقدير : صدق على البارى تعالى : أنه ما كان موجوداً للعالم، ثم صار موجوداً له. فلو كان صدق هذا النفي والإثبات يوجب حدوث صفة في الذات، أو زوالها عنه. لزم حدوث الصفة في ذات الله تعالى، ثم إن حدوثها يكون بإحداث الله تعالى، وذلك يوجب التسلسل. فإن قالوا : لا معنى لكونه موجوداً للعالم، إلا نفس وجود العالم. فنقول : هذا يوجب نفي الصانع. لأنا لما قلنا : العالم إنما وجد، بإيجاد الله تعالى، ثم فسرنا إيجاد الله تعالى للعالم : بنفس وجود العالم : فحينئذ يلزم أن يقال : العالم إنما وجد لنفسه وصدق هذا الكلام يمنع من صدق قولنا : إنه إنما وجد بإيجاد الله تعالى، وذلك باطل
Third: When the world was non-existent, it was true of God most high that He did not bring it into existence. And when it entered into existence, it was true of Him that He brought it into existence. So on this supposition: it is true of God most high that He was not existent for the world, then He became existent for it. Now if the truth of this negation and affirmation necessitated the occurrence of an attribute in the essence, or its removal from it, then the occurrence of the attribute in God's essence would follow, and then its occurrence would be by God's bringing into existence, and that would necessitate an infinite regress. If they say: 'There is no meaning to His being existent for the world except the very existence of the world.' We say: This necessitates the denial of the Maker. Because when we said: 'The world only exists by God's bringing into existence,' and we interpreted God's bringing the world into existence as the very existence of the world, then it would necessarily be said: 'The world exists by itself' and the truth of this statement prevents the truth of our statement that it exists by God's bringing into existence, and that is false.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage presents a third argument and a refutation. The opponent suggests that the change in the statement 'God did not create the world' to 'God created the world' implies a change in God. The author argues that if this implied a change (huduth or removal), it would lead to an infinite regress because the new attribute would itself be created by God. Then the author anticipates an objection: the opponent might claim that 'God's being existent for the world' is just the world's existence itself. The author refutes this by showing that if we reduce God's creative act to the world's existence, then the world exists by itself, which denies the Creator. This is a classic refutation of occasionalist or reductionist views. The argument underscores that God's act of creation is a distinct attribute, not reducible to the created effect.

Key Terms
الإيجاد (al-ijad)
الصانع (al-Sani')
التسلسل (al-tasalsul)
وجود العالم (wujud al-'alam)
ذات الله (dhat Allah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's attribute of creation changes when He creates, as seen in the difference between 'He did not create' and 'He created'.
Correction: The change is only in the relation (the world's existence), not in God's essence or attribute. God's act of creating is eternal in His knowledge and will; the temporal effect does not imply change in Him.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if we interpret 'God's being existent for the world' as merely the world's existence?
A) It proves God's eternity.
B) It leads to an infinite regress.
C) It denies the existence of the Creator.
D) It makes God's attribute of creation temporal.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi shows that the change in statements about God's creation does not imply change in His essence; rather, it is the effect (the world) that changes, and any attempt to reduce God's act to the effect denies the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on God's act of creating the universe, do you find yourself subtly imagining a change in Him—from not creating to creating? How does Sanusi's argument against change in God's essence challenge that mental picture? What will you do to correct your thoughts about God's eternal attributes?
?
If you were to equate God's creative act with the mere existence of the world, you would risk denying the Creator Himself. In your daily life, do you ever act as if God's role is limited to the existence of things, forgetting His constant and eternal power? Spend today consciously acknowledging God as the ever-present Maker beyond what is seen.
?
Sanusi warns that attributing change to God leads to an infinite regress, which is absurd. Are there any beliefs or practices in your life that implicitly assume God changes—like expecting Him to react differently based on your actions, as if He acquires new attributes? Confess this and renew your tawheed by affirming that God's attributes are perfect and unchanging.

The Objection from God's Inability to Know Particulars
First horn of the dilemma on God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first part of the objection: if God does not know particulars, He cannot intend to create the world, so the world cannot be originated.

Core Idea
If God cannot know particulars, He cannot create at all.

The objector presents a dilemma: either God knows particulars or He does not. This lesson examines the first horn. If God lacks knowledge of particulars, then intention (qaṣd) to create is impossible, because intention requires knowledge of the thing's non-existence and future existence. Thus, the world cannot be originated, undermining the proof's assumption.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the conditional structure: 'If the truth is the second division...'
2
Identify the key claim
The objection states that if God does not know particulars, He cannot intend the world's existence. Intention (qaṣd) is conditioned on knowledge.
This is a direct challenge to the previous proof that the world is originated.
3
Reflect on the implication
If the world cannot be originated, then the proof's conclusion (that the eternal cannot cease) is not threatened. The objector is showing a flaw in the opponent's reasoning.
Think about how this objection fits into the larger debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: الكلام على هذه الحجة. من وجوه: الأول: أن نقول: البارى تعالى، إما أن يكون عالماً بالجزئيات، أو ليس كذلك. فإن كان الحق هو القسم الثاني، وهو أن العلم بالجزئيات والتغيرات على الله تعالى محال. فعلى هذا التقدير يمتنع كون العالم محدثاً، لأنه لو كان محدثاً، لما حدث إلا لأجل أنه تعالى قصد إلى إيجاده وتكوينه، لكن على تقدير بأن لا يكون عالماً بالجزئيات، فإنه يمتنع كونه قاصداً إلى إيجاد العالم، لأن القصد إلى إيجاد الشيء، مشروط بكونه عالماً، بأن العالم معدوم، وأنه سيوجد. فإذا كان العلم بالجزئيات محالاً، لكان هذا القصد محالاً. وإما إن كان الحق هو القسم الأول، وهو أنه تعالى عالم بالجزئيات، فنقول: فعلى هذا التقدير صدق على الله تعالى أنه ما كان عالماً في الأزل بوجود العالم، ثم صدق عليه: أنه صار عالماً بوجود العالم عند دخوله في الوجود، فصدق هذا النفي. والإثبات هل يقتضي زوال أمر كان، وحصول أمر حدث، أو لا يقتضي؟ فإن كان الأول. فنقول: ذلك الذي زال. إن كان قديماً فقد اعترفتم بأن القديم قد زال. وإن كان حادثاً فالكلام في ذلك الحادث، كالكلام في الأول، ولزم أن يكون كل علم مسبوقاً بعلم آخر إلى أول. وإذا جاز هذا، فقد بطل دليلكم في إفساد القول بحوادث لا أول لها. وأما إن قلنا: إن صدق قولنا ما كان عالماً بوجوده لا يقتضي حدوث ولا زوال شيء. فلم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن صدق قولنا بتحرك الجسم بعد أن كان ساكناً، لا يقتضي أيضاً زوال شيء، ولا حدوث شيء. وحينئذ يبطل الدليل المذكور؟ فهذا سؤال قاهر، قوي على هذا الدليل.
If it is said: The discussion on this proof [proceeds] from several aspects. The first: We say: The Creator, exalted is He, either knows particulars (juzʿiyyāt) or not. If the truth is the second division, that knowledge of particulars and changes is impossible for God, then on this assumption it is impossible for the world to be originated (muḥdath), because if it were originated, it would only occur because He intended its bringing into existence and formation. But on the assumption that He does not know particulars, it is impossible for Him to intend the bringing into existence of the world, because intention (qaṣd) to bring something into existence is conditioned by knowing that the world is non-existent and that it will exist. So if knowledge of particulars is impossible, this intention would be impossible. And if the truth is the first division, that He, exalted, knows particulars, we say: on this assumption, it is true of God that He was not knowing in eternity (azal) of the existence of the world, then it becomes true of Him that He became knowing of the existence of the world when it entered into existence. So this negation and affirmation [are both true]. Does this entail the passing away of something that was, and the occurrence of something that occurred, or not? If the first, we say: that which passed away—if it was eternal (qadīm), you have admitted that the eternal has passed away; and if it was originated (ḥādith), the discussion on that originated thing is like the discussion on the first, and it would be necessary that every knowledge is preceded by another knowledge ad infinitum. If this is allowed, your proof against the view of events without beginning (ḥawādith lā awwala lahā) is invalidated. And if we say: The truth of our statement 'He was not knowing of its existence' does not entail the occurrence or passing away of anything, then why is it not permissible to say that the truth of our statement 'the body moved after it was at rest' also does not entail the passing away of anything nor the occurrence of anything? Then the mentioned proof would be invalidated. This is an overwhelming and strong objection against this proof.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is an objection (or set of objections) to the previous proof that the eternal cannot cease. The objector presents two possibilities regarding God's knowledge of particulars. First, if God does not know particulars, then He cannot intend to create the world, so the world cannot be originated—thus the proof's assumption that the world is originated fails. Second, if God does know particulars, then His knowledge changes from not knowing the world's existence to knowing it after creation. The objector then asks whether this change entails the passing away of some previous state. If it does, and that previous state is eternal, then the eternal has ceased, contradicting the proof. If it is originated, an infinite regress of knowledges follows, which the objector claims the opponent rejects. If the change does not entail passing away, then by analogy the change from rest to motion does not entail passing away, thus invalidating the proof. The author acknowledges that this is a strong objection.

Key Terms
الجزئيات (juzʿiyyāt)
المحدث (al-muḥdath)
القصد (al-qaṣd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's lack of knowledge of particulars does not affect His ability to create.
Correction: The objection shows that intention (qaṣd) requires knowledge of the particular thing to be created; without such knowledge, intention is impossible.

Checkpoint
What does the objector claim is necessary for God to intend the creation of the world?
A) That God knows all universals.
B) That God knows that the world is non-existent and will exist.
C) That God knows His own essence.
D) That God knows the eternal decrees.

Key Takeaway
If God cannot know particulars, then intention to create is impossible, making the world unoriginated and the proof self-defeating.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that Allah knows every particular detail of your life, including your innermost thoughts and intentions, then why do you still act as if some of your actions are hidden from Him? What would change if you fully internalized that His knowledge of particulars is the very condition for your existence?
?
The argument shows that without knowledge of particulars, intention and creation are impossible. Think of a time you intended to do something good but failed because you lacked full knowledge. How does that human limitation reflect on the absolute necessity of Allah's perfect knowledge for His creative act? Does this make you appreciate His knowledge more? What will you do today to live in gratitude for His comprehensive knowledge?

The Objection from Change in God's Knowledge
Second horn of the dilemma on God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second part of the objection: if God does know particulars, His knowledge changes with creation, leading to problems of eternal attributes or infinite regress, which undermines the opponent's proof.

Core Idea
If God knows particulars, His knowledge changes — and that change is a problem.

The objector now considers the second horn: God does know particulars. Then His knowledge of the world's existence changes from non-knowledge to knowledge. This raises the question: does this change entail the passing away of a previous state? If so, that state either is eternal (contradicting the opponent's claim that the eternal cannot cease) or is originated (leading to infinite regress). If not, then the opponent's argument against motion (rest being eternal) is also invalidated. This is a powerful objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: after assuming God knows particulars, the objector presents two sub-cases.
Focus on the logical moves: the objector is turning the opponent's own reasoning against them.
2
Analyze the two sub-cases
First sub-case: if the change entails passing away of something, then either that thing is eternal (which the opponent denies can pass) or it is originated (leading to infinite regress). Second sub-case: if no passing away, then the opponent's proof about rest can be mirrored.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the conclusion
The objector ends by calling this a 'overwhelming and strong objection'. This shows the opponent must address it or abandon their proof.
The author (Sanusi) might not endorse the objection, but he presents it as a serious challenge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: الكلام على هذه الحجة. من وجوه: الأول: أن نقول: البارى تعالى، إما أن يكون عالماً بالجزئيات، أو ليس كذلك. فإن كان الحق هو القسم الثاني، وهو أن العلم بالجزئيات والتغيرات على الله تعالى محال. فعلى هذا التقدير يمتنع كون العالم محدثاً، لأنه لو كان محدثاً، لما حدث إلا لأجل أنه تعالى قصد إلى إيجاده وتكوينه، لكن على تقدير بأن لا يكون عالماً بالجزئيات، فإنه يمتنع كونه قاصداً إلى إيجاد العالم، لأن القصد إلى إيجاد الشيء، مشروط بكونه عالماً، بأن العالم معدوم، وأنه سيوجد. فإذا كان العلم بالجزئيات محالاً، لكان هذا القصد محالاً. وإما إن كان الحق هو القسم الأول، وهو أنه تعالى عالم بالجزئيات، فنقول: فعلى هذا التقدير صدق على الله تعالى أنه ما كان عالماً في الأزل بوجود العالم، ثم صدق عليه: أنه صار عالماً بوجود العالم عند دخوله في الوجود، فصدق هذا النفي. والإثبات هل يقتضي زوال أمر كان، وحصول أمر حدث، أو لا يقتضي؟ فإن كان الأول. فنقول: ذلك الذي زال. إن كان قديماً فقد اعترفتم بأن القديم قد زال. وإن كان حادثاً فالكلام في ذلك الحادث، كالكلام في الأول، ولزم أن يكون كل علم مسبوقاً بعلم آخر إلى أول. وإذا جاز هذا، فقد بطل دليلكم في إفساد القول بحوادث لا أول لها. وأما إن قلنا: إن صدق قولنا ما كان عالماً بوجوده لا يقتضي حدوث ولا زوال شيء. فلم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن صدق قولنا بتحرك الجسم بعد أن كان ساكناً، لا يقتضي أيضاً زوال شيء، ولا حدوث شيء. وحينئذ يبطل الدليل المذكور؟ فهذا سؤال قاهر، قوي على هذا الدليل.
If it is said: The discussion on this proof [proceeds] from several aspects. The first: We say: The Creator, exalted is He, either knows particulars (juzʿiyyāt) or not. If the truth is the second division, that knowledge of particulars and changes is impossible for God, then on this assumption it is impossible for the world to be originated (muḥdath), because if it were originated, it would only occur because He intended its bringing into existence and formation. But on the assumption that He does not know particulars, it is impossible for Him to intend the bringing into existence of the world, because intention (qaṣd) to bring something into existence is conditioned by knowing that the world is non-existent and that it will exist. So if knowledge of particulars is impossible, this intention would be impossible. And if the truth is the first division, that He, exalted, knows particulars, we say: on this assumption, it is true of God that He was not knowing in eternity (azal) of the existence of the world, then it becomes true of Him that He became knowing of the existence of the world when it entered into existence. So this negation and affirmation [are both true]. Does this entail the passing away of something that was, and the occurrence of something that occurred, or not? If the first, we say: that which passed away—if it was eternal (qadīm), you have admitted that the eternal has passed away; and if it was originated (ḥādith), the discussion on that originated thing is like the discussion on the first, and it would be necessary that every knowledge is preceded by another knowledge ad infinitum. If this is allowed, your proof against the view of events without beginning (ḥawādith lā awwala lahā) is invalidated. And if we say: The truth of our statement 'He was not knowing of its existence' does not entail the occurrence or passing away of anything, then why is it not permissible to say that the truth of our statement 'the body moved after it was at rest' also does not entail the passing away of anything nor the occurrence of anything? Then the mentioned proof would be invalidated. This is an overwhelming and strong objection against this proof.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is an objection (or set of objections) to the previous proof that the eternal cannot cease. The objector presents two possibilities regarding God's knowledge of particulars. First, if God does not know particulars, then He cannot intend to create the world, so the world cannot be originated—thus the proof's assumption that the world is originated fails. Second, if God does know particulars, then His knowledge changes from not knowing the world's existence to knowing it after creation. The objector then asks whether this change entails the passing away of some previous state. If it does, and that previous state is eternal, then the eternal has ceased, contradicting the proof. If it is originated, an infinite regress of knowledges follows, which the objector claims the opponent rejects. If the change does not entail passing away, then by analogy the change from rest to motion does not entail passing away, thus invalidating the proof. The author acknowledges that this is a strong objection.

Key Terms
العلم الأزلي (al-ʿilm al-azalī)
حوادث لا أول لها (ḥawādith lā awwala lahā)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's knowledge changing is unproblematic for the opponent.
Correction: The objection shows that any change in God's knowledge either contradicts the eternity thesis or leads to an infinite regress, both unacceptable to the opponent.

Checkpoint

A) The opponent's proof about rest and motion is strengthened.
B) It becomes permissible to say that a body moving after rest does not entail passing away, invalidating the proof.
C) An infinite regress of knowledges is required.

Key Takeaway
If God's knowledge changes, the opponent faces a dilemma: either the eternal passes away or infinite regress results, or the proof against motion collapses.

Second Objection: God's Power to Create and Its Eternality
Objection concerning God's power and the ceasing of an eternal attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the proof that the eternal cannot cease, focusing on God's power to create the world.

Core Idea
Is God's power to create the world eternal? The objection argues that it must cease after creation.

This lesson presents a challenging objection raised against the doctrine that eternal attributes cannot cease. The objector focuses on God's power (qudra) to create this world. By analyzing whether God was able to create from eternity and whether that ability remains after creation, the objection aims to show that an eternal attribute—specifically, the power to create this world—would have to perish. This argument forces us to refine our understanding of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the structure of the objection. Note the key terms.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The objector starts with a dichotomy: either God was able to create the world from eternity or not. Trace how each possibility is handled.
3
Understand the infinite regress argument
Why is the second possibility false? Because it would lead to an infinite regress of powers (tasalsul).
4
Follow the implication about the eternal attribute
The objector concludes that God's specific power to create this world must cease after creation, implying that an eternal attribute perishes.
The passage ends mid-response; the full reply will be covered later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني: أن نقول: البارى تعالى إما أن يقال: إنه كان في الأزل قادراً على إحداث هذا العلم، أو ما كان قادراً عليه. والثاني باطل. وإلا فتلك القادرية لا تحدث إلا لأجل قادر آخر. ولزم التسلسل وهو محال. فيثبت: أن الحق هو الأول. وهو أنه تعالى كان في الأزل قادراً على إحداث هذا العالم. وإذا أحدث هذا العالم، فبعد حدوثه. هل بقي قادراً على إحداثه، أو بقي كذلك؟ والأول باطل لأن القدرة على إحداث الشيء بعد وجوده محال. فيثبت: أنه تعالى كان قادراً على إحداث هذا العالم في الأزل. فيثبت أن بعد دخول هذا العالم في الوجود، ما بقي كونه قادراً على إحداث هذا العالم. فكونه قادراً على إحداث هذا العالم: صفة أزلية، وقد زالت وفنيت. وذلك قول بأن الأزلي قد زال. فإن قالوا: كونه تعالى قادراً على الإحداث والإيجاد: صفة أزلية. وأنها لا تزول ولا تبطل. فأما كونه قادراً على إيجاد هذا
The second question: We say: The Creator, exalted is He, either it is said that He was in eternity (azal) able (qādir) to bring about this world, or He was not able. The second is false; otherwise that ability would not occur except by another ability, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible. Thus it is established that the truth is the first: that He, exalted, was in eternity able to bring about this world. When He brings about this world, after its occurrence, is He still able to bring it about, or does He remain as such? The first is false because ability to bring about a thing after its existence is impossible. Thus it is established that He, exalted, was able to bring about this world in eternity. So it is established that after the entrance of this world into existence, He no longer remains able to bring about this world. Thus His being able to bring about this world is an eternal attribute (ṣifa azaliyya), and it has passed away and perished. That is to say that the eternal has passed away. If they say: His being able to bring about and create is an eternal attribute, and it does not pass away or perish. As for His being able to bring about this...
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection to the proof that the eternal cannot cease. The objector focuses on God's power (qudra). He argues that God was either able to create the world from eternity or not. The latter is false because it would require another power ad infinitum. So God was able from eternity. But after creating the world, is He still able to create it? No, because ability to create what already exists is impossible. Therefore, God's power to create that specific world must have ceased after creation. This means an eternal attribute (the power to create this world) has perished, implying that the eternal can cease. The objector then anticipates a response: the opponent might say that the power to create in general is eternal and does not cease. The objection cuts off here, but the intended answer would likely distinguish between the general power and the specific power. The lesson is incomplete because the page ends before the full response is given.

القدرة (al-qudra)
الصفة الأزلية (al-ṣifa al-azaliyya)
التسلسل (al-tasalsul)
الإحداث (al-iḥdāth)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's power is a single attribute that never changes and that all specific powers are eternal.
Correction: The objection distinguishes between the general power to create (which may be eternal) and the specific power to create a particular thing (which ceases after creation).

Checkpoint
Why does the objector consider the second possibility (that God was not able in eternity to create the world) false?
A) Because it would imply God is not all-powerful.
B) Because it would lead to an infinite regress of powers.
C) Because it contradicts the proof that the eternal cannot cease.
D) Because the world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The second objection challenges the eternality of attributes by arguing that God's specific power to create this world must cease after creation, implying that an eternal can perish.

Second Objection: Does God's Power Change After Creating?
Second objection and author's two-part rebuttal regarding God's eternal power and its alleged change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection to the author's argument for a temporal world, the opponent's distinction between essential attribute and relational addition, and the author's two-fold refutation showing consistency in dealing with relational eternals.

Core Idea
Eternal attributes can have non-eternal relations without contradiction.

This lesson addresses a sophisticated objection: if God's power to create the world is eternal, then after creation that specific power seems to cease, implying an eternal attribute perishes. The opponent tries to salvage the view by distinguishing between the eternal power itself and its temporal relation to the created world. The author counters with two arguments, showing that the opponent's own example of rest is similarly relational, and consistency demands accepting cessation of such relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing to analyze. Then read the translation. Let the argument's sequence sink in.
Focus on the logical flow: objection → opponent's excuse → first refutation → second refutation.
2
Identify the objection's structure
Notice how the objector sets up a dichotomy: either God was eternally able to create this world or not. The second leads to regress, so the first is true. Then after creation, does the power remain? The objector says no, because power to create what already exists is impossible.
This leads to the conclusion that an eternal attribute (the specific power to create this world) has ceased.
3
Understand the opponent's reply
The opponent distinguishes between God's eternal power (a real attribute that does not cease) and the specific relation (iḍāfa) of that power to this world. Only the relation ceases.
This is a common move in kalām: saving the eternal attribute by making the change merely relational.
4
Follow the first refutation
The author argues that the original proof (about infinite regress) applies to all eternal things, including relations. So if the opponent accepts the proof, it invalidates their own distinction.
5
Follow the second refutation
The author points out that the opponent's own concept of 'rest' (sukūn) is purely relational. If relations can cease in that case (as they do when a body moves), then consistency demands they can cease in the divine case too.
This exposes an inconsistency in the opponent's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : أن نقول : البارى تعالى إما أن يقال : إنه كان في الأزل قادراً على إحداث هذا العلم، أو ما كان قادراً عليه. والثاني باطل. وإلا فتلك القادرية لا تحدث [إلا] لأجل قادر آخر. ولزم التسلسل وهو محال. فيثبت : أن الحق هو الأول. وهو أنه تعالى كان في الأزل قادراً على إحداث هذا العالم . وإذا أحدث هذا العالم ، فبعد حدوثه. هل بقي قادراً على إحداثه ، أو بقي كذلك ؟ [والأول باطل] لأن القدرة على إحداث الشيء بعد وجوده محال . فيثبت : أنه تعالى كان قادراً على إحداث [هذا] العالم في الأزل . فيثبت أن بعد دخول هذا العالم في الوجود، ما بقي كونه قادراً على إحداث هذا العالم. فكونه قادراً على إحداث هذا العالم : صفة أزلية ، وقد زالت وفنيت . وذلك قول بأن الأزلي قد زال . فإن قالوا : كونه تعالى قادراً على الإحداث والإيجاد : صفة أزلية . وأنها لا تزول ولا تبطل . فأما كونه قادراً على إيجاد هذا العالم فلا معنى له إلا إضافة قدرته إلى هذا الايجاد المعين المخصوص ، فالزائل ليس إلا هذه الإضافة ، وذلك غير ممتنع . فنقول : هذا العذر مدفوع من وجهين : الأول : إن الدليل الذي ذكرتموه ، يتناول كل ما كان أزلياً ، سواء كان صفة حقيقية ، أو أمراً إضافياً . ولما كان الدليل عاماً ، وقد توجه النقض عليه ، فقد لزم الإشكال. • الثاني : وهو أن غاية كلامكم : أن هذا الذي زال ، ليس إلا مجرد إضافة ونسبة ، وكذلك السكون الذي زال ، لا حقيقة له إلا مجرد إضافة ونسبة . لانكم فسرتم السكون بمجرد كونه حاصلاً في الحيز المعين على سبيل الدوام . والحصول في الحيز المعين لا معنى له إلا إضافة مخصوصة ، ونسبة مخصوصة . عرضت فذات الجسم بالنسبة إلى ذلك الحيز. فيثبت : أن هذا السكون لا حقيقة له إلا محض الإضافة والنسبة . وتعلق قدرة الله [تعالى] بإيجاد هذا العالم أيضاً : نسبة مخصوصة ، وإضافة مخصوصة . فإن امتنع العدم على النسبة الأزلية ، والإضافة الأزلية ، وجب أن يكون الكل كذلك . وإن لم يمتنع ذلك في بعض الصور ، وجب أن يكون في الكل كذلك. فأما تجويزه في بعض الصور دون البعض ففاسد
The second question: We say: The Creator, exalted, is either said to have been eternally capable of bringing this world into existence, or not capable of it. The second is false; otherwise that capability would only arise from another capability, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. So it is established that the truth is the first: that He, exalted, was eternally capable of bringing this world into existence. And when He brought this world into existence, after its existence, did He remain capable of bringing it into existence, or did He remain as before? [The first is false] because the power to bring something into existence after its existence is impossible. So it is established that He, exalted, was capable of bringing [this] world into existence in eternity. So it is established that after this world entered existence, His being capable of bringing it into existence did not remain. So His being capable of bringing this world into existence is an eternal attribute, and it has ceased and perished. That is saying that the eternal has ceased. If they say: His being capable of bringing into existence and creating is an eternal attribute, and it does not cease nor become invalid. As for His being capable of bringing this world into existence, its meaning is nothing but the relation (iḍāfa) of His power to this specific, particular act of creation; what ceases is only this relation, and that is not impossible. We say: This excuse is refuted from two aspects: First: The proof you mentioned applies to everything that is eternal, whether it is a real attribute or a relational matter. Since the proof is general and a refutation has been directed against it, the difficulty necessarily arises. Second: The upshot of your statement is that what has ceased is nothing but a mere relation and attribution. Similarly, the rest that ceased has no reality except a mere relation and attribution. For you have interpreted rest as merely its being located in a specific locus continuously. Being in a specific locus means nothing but a particular relation and attribution that occurs to the essence of the body with respect to that locus. So it is established that this rest has no reality except pure attribution and relation. And the attachment of God's power [exalted] to bringing this world into existence is also a particular relation and a particular attribution. If non-existence is impossible for an eternal relation and eternal attribution, then all must be the same. And if that is not impossible in some cases, then it must be the same in all. As for permitting it in some cases but not others, that is invalid.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and the author's rebuttal in two parts. The objector argues that God's power to create this world is an eternal attribute, so if it ceases after creation, then an eternal attribute has ceased, which is impossible for the opponent. The opponent may respond by distinguishing between the essential attribute of power (which is eternal and does not cease) and the specific relation (iḍāfa) of that power to this particular world, which is not eternal and can cease. The author refutes this in two ways: First, the proof they originally used applies generally to all eternal things, whether real attributes or relational matters, so the objection does not invalidate the proof. Second, the opponent's own example of rest (sukūn) is also merely relational, so if they allow relational eternals to cease, then consistency demands they allow it in all cases, including the relation of God's power to the world. Otherwise, they are being arbitrary.

Key Terms
القدرة
الإحداث
الصفة الأزلية
الإضافة
النسبة
السكون
الإيجاد الذاتي
الإيجاد على سبيل الصحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the objector's argument forces the conclusion that God's eternal power changes or ceases.
Correction: The author shows that only the relation ceases, not the power itself, and that the opponent's own assumptions about rest lead to the same conclusion.

Checkpoint
What is the first way the author refutes the opponent's excuse that only the relation (iḍāfa) ceases, not the eternal attribute?
A) The relation is also eternal, so it cannot cease.
B) The original proof applies to everything eternal, including relations.
C) The attribute of power is not eternal.
D) The objector has no valid excuse.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the objection by showing that the opponent's own example of rest is relational, so if relational eternals can cease, then the relation of God's power to this world can also cease without affecting the eternal power itself.

Refutation of the Excuse That an Eternal Relation Can Cease
Refutes the excuse in two points.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that an eternal attribute can cease as a mere relation is inconsistent and logically flawed.

Core Idea
Eternal relations also cannot cease without contradiction.

This passage tackles a subtle excuse: that when God’s power to create a specific world ceases, it is only a relation that ends, not the attribute itself. The author refutes this with two arguments, showing that the excuse leads to an arbitrary and untenable position. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the excuse being refuted
Read the passage carefully and locate the excuse: that what ceases is only a relation (iḍāfa), not a real attribute, so no impossibility arises.
The excuse tries to preserve God's unchanging essence by denying change to the attribute itself.
2
Understand the first refutation
Notice the first point: the proof used to support the excuse is too general—it applies to all eternal things, including real attributes. Since the proof has been refuted, it cannot stand.
The author says the proof is 'general' (ʿāmm) and has been attacked (nuqqiḍa).
3
Grasp the second refutation and its conclusion
The second point draws an analogy between rest (sukūn) as a relation and God's power's attachment to creation. If one eternal relation can cease, why not all? The author demands consistency, dismissing selective allowance as invalid.
This refutation targets the core inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العالم فلا معنى له إلا إضافة قدرته إلى هذا الايجاد المعين المخصوص، فالزائل ليس إلا هذه الإضافة، وذلك غير ممتنع . فنقول : هذا العذر مدفوع من وجهين : الأول : إن الدليل الذي ذكرتموه، يتناول كل ما كان أزلياً، سواء كان صفة حقيقية، أو أمراً إضافياً . ولما كان الدليل عاماً، وقد توجه النقض عليه، فقد لزم الإشكال. • الثاني : وهو أن غاية كلامكم : أن هذا الذي زال، ليس إلا مجرد إضافة ونسبة، وكذلك السكون الذي زال، لا حقيقة له إلا مجرد إضافة ونسبة . لانكم فسرتم السكون بمجرد(١) كونه حاصلاً في الحيز المعين على سبيل الدوام . والحصول في الحيز المعين لا معنى له إلا إضافة مخصوصة، ونسبة مخصوصة . عرضت فذات الجسم بالنسبة إلى ذلك الحيز. فيثبت : أن هذا السكون لا حقيقة له إلا محض الإضافة والنسبة . وتعلق قدرة الله [ تعالى (٢) ] بإيجاد هذا العالم أيضاً : نسبة مخصوصة، وإضافة مخصوصة . فإن امتنع العدم على النسبة الأزلية، والإضافة الأزلية، وجب أن يكون الكل كذلك . وإن لم يمتنع ذلك في بعض الصور، وجب أن يكون في الكل كذلك. فأما تجويزه في بعض الصور دون البعض ففاسد
The world has no meaning except the addition of His power to this specific creation. What ceases is only this addition, and that is not impossible. We say: This excuse is repelled from two aspects: First: The proof you mentioned applies to everything that is eternal, whether it is a real attribute or an additive matter. Since the proof is general and a refutation has been directed against it, the problem necessarily arises. Second: The gist of your statement is that what has ceased is nothing but mere addition and relation, and likewise the rest (sukūn) that has ceased has no reality except mere addition and relation, because you interpreted rest as merely (1) its being located in a specific place perpetually. Being located in a specific place has no meaning except a specific addition and a specific relation that occurred to the essence of the body relative to that place. Thus it is established that this rest has no reality except pure addition and relation. The attachment of the power of God—exalted be He—(2) to the creation of this world is also a specific relation and a specific addition. If non-existence is impossible for the eternal relation and the eternal addition, then everything must be so. And if that is not impossible in some cases, then it must be so in all cases. As for allowing it in some cases and not others, that is invalid.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an excuse (ʿudhr) that claims that when an eternal attribute (like God's power to create a specific world) ceases, it is only a relation (iḍāfa) that ceases, not the attribute itself, so no impossibility arises. The author gives two objections: First, the proof used to support this excuse is too broad—it would apply to any eternal thing, including real attributes (ṣifa ḥaqīqiyya) and additive matters (amr iḍāfī), and since the proof has been refuted, it cannot stand. Second, the excuse defines rest (sukūn) as a mere relation (location in a place), so if rest's cessation is only a relation, then similarly the cessation of God's power's attachment to creation is also a relation. Consistency demands that if one eternal relation can cease, all can; if not, then none can. To permit it in some cases but not others is arbitrary and invalid.

Key Terms
إضافة (iḍāfa)
نسبة (nisba)
سكون (sukūn)
عذر (ʿudhr)
أزلي (azalī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An eternal attribute can cease as a mere relation without affecting God's essence or logical consistency.
Correction: The author shows that if relations are eternal, they cannot be singled out for cessation; either all eternal relations can cease or none, and the opponent's selective allowance is arbitrary.

Checkpoint
What are the two points by which the author refutes the excuse that an eternal relation can cease?
A) First: the proof is too general; Second: rest is also a relation, so the same logic applies.
B) First: the proof is too specific; Second: rest is a real attribute.
C) First: the world is temporal; Second: God's power is unchanging.
D) First: the proof contradicts scripture; Second: rest is not a relation.

Key Takeaway
To allow cessation for an eternal attribute disguised as a relation, but not for others, is arbitrary and logically invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas in your life where you hold inconsistent beliefs—accepting a principle in one situation but rejecting it in another? How does this lesson's demand for logical consistency challenge you to align your reasoning about God with reality?
?
When you think about God's power being attached to creation, do you ever subconsciously limit it based on your own preferences or logic? What would it mean for you to accept the full implications of God's eternal attributes without making comfortable exceptions?
?
This lesson exposes the arbitrariness of allowing rest to cease but not power attachment. Are you ever like the opponent, making convenient exceptions in your understanding of divine attributes to avoid uncomfortable consequences? What would change if you were truly consistent in your theology?

The Fourth Objection: Eternal Possibility Ceases
Objects that the eternal possibility of origination ceases after creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection to the creation of the world: that the eternal possibility of origination ceases after creation, implying change in the eternal.

Core Idea
An eternal attribute cannot cease—this objection challenges divine immutability.

This passage presents the fourth objection against the view that the world is originated. The objection argues that if the world is created, the possibility of its creation must be eternal, yet it ceases after creation. This raises a problem for divine immutability. We will explore the logic and how it challenges kalam theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms like 'ṣiḥḥa' (possibility) and 'ḥukm azalī' (eternal judgment).
2
Identify the logical structure
The objection has two premises: (1) the possibility of occurrence is continuous from eternity, (2) after occurrence, that possibility ceases. The conclusion is that an eternal judgment changes, which is contradictory.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Understand the contradiction
The key claim is that something eternal (the possibility) disappears, implying change in what is eternal. This challenges the principle of divine immutability.
4
Consider the implications
If this objection holds, then either the world is not originated, or the possibility is not an eternal attribute. Think about how a theologian might respond.
This objection is part of a larger dialectic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع : وهو أنه لو كان العالم حادثاً ، لكانت صحة حدوثه مستمرة من الأزل إلى وقت حدوثه. لما دللنا على أن تلك الصحة يمتنع [ أن يكون (٣) ] لها أول . ثم إذا حدث العالم ، لم تبق صحة حدوثه . لأنه الشيء بعد حدوثه لم يبق صحيح الحدوث، وإلا لزم أن يصدق على الموجود ، أنه يمكن أن يصير موجوداً ، وذلك باطل . فيثبت : أن تلك الصحة حكم أزلي ، مع أنها قد زالت
The fourth question: If the world were originated (ḥādith), then the possibility (ṣiḥḥa) of its occurrence would be continuous from eternity until the time of its occurrence, because we have demonstrated that that possibility cannot have a beginning (3). Then when the world occurs, its possibility of occurrence does not remain, because a thing after its occurrence is no longer possible of occurrence; otherwise it would follow that it is true of an existent that it can become existent, which is false. Thus it is established that that possibility is an eternal judgment (ḥukm azalī) while it has ceased.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection (as-suʾāl ar-rābiʿ). It argues that if the world is originated (ḥādith), then the possibility (ṣiḥḥa) of its occurrence must have been continuous from eternity until the moment of its occurrence, since that possibility cannot have a beginning. But after the world occurs, that possibility ceases to exist—an originated thing is no longer possible of occurrence. Therefore, we have an eternal judgment (ḥukm azalī) that ceases, which implies change in what is eternal, a contradiction. The objection thus challenges the view that the world is originated by highlighting the apparent change in an eternal attribute (the possibility).

Key Terms
حادث (ḥādith)
صحة حدوث (ṣiḥḥat ḥudūth)
أزلي (azalī)
حكم (ḥukm)
زوال (zawāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'possibility' is not a real attribute, so its cessation is not problematic.
Correction: The objection treats the possibility as an eternal judgment (ḥukm azalī) that must be real. Even if one denies its reality, the argument challenges the coherence of an eternal attribute ceasing, which is a problem for divine immutability.

Checkpoint
What contradiction does the fourth objection highlight?
A) The world is originated but its cause is eternal.
B) The possibility of the world's occurrence is eternal yet it ceases after creation.
C) God's power is limited by possibility.
D) The world's occurrence necessitates a change in God's will.

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection highlights a tension between the eternality of possibility and its cessation, challenging the coherence of creation from nothing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This objection forces you to confront whether your concept of divine immutability can accommodate the cessation of an eternal attribute. Are you willing to refine your understanding of God's eternity even if it challenges your previous beliefs?
?
Consider the possibility of creation—does it feel like a real attribute of God that must exist eternally? How does your heart grapple with the idea that something about God's relation to the world changes when He creates? Are you content with a simple answer, or do you feel the weight of this theological tension?
?
