The Dilemma of God's Will and the Timing of Creation
Poses a dilemma about God's will being eternal or temporal regarding creation's timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma posed by the fifth objection regarding God's eternal will and the specific timing of creation.

Core Idea
God's will cannot be both eternal and specifically timed without implicating change.

This passage presents a classic objection against the temporal origination of the world. The objection sets up a dilemma: if God willed creation at a specific time, was that will present from eternity or not? Either option seems to imply something problematic about God. Let's examine the reasoning and see how the objection unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Notice that the objection is about the timing of creation and God's will.
2
Identify the two alternatives
The passage presents two possibilities: either God's will to create at that time was eternal, or it was not. Find these in the text.
3
Consider the dilemma
Think about the implications of each alternative. If God's will is eternal, why is the world created at a specific time rather than another? If it is not eternal, then God acquired a new will, implying change.
The passage cuts off, but the dilemma is clear.
4
Reflect on the significance
This objection challenges how an eternal, unchanging God can will temporal events. It is a key issue in kalam arguments about the world's origination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الخامس : لو كان العالم حادثاً ، لكان حدوثه ، لأجل أنه تعالى أراد إحداثه في ذلك الوقت. فإما أن يقال : إنه تعالى كان في الأزل [ مريداً (1)] لأن يحدثه في ذلك الوقت المعين، أو ما كان موصوفاً بهذه الإرادة
The fifth question: If the world were originated, its occurrence would be because He—exalted be He—willed to bring it about at that time. Then either it is said that He—exalted be He—was in eternity willing (1) to bring it about at that specific time, or He was not described with this will.

The objection asks: was God in eternity willing to create it at that specific time, or was He not described with that will? The argument is cut off, but the dilemma suggests that either God's will is eternal and specific, implying change (since the will is realized later), or it is temporal, implying change in God. The objection aims to show a problem with temporal origination.

The Dilemma of an Eternal Will for a Temporal Event
Objection arguing that an eternal will for a temporal event leads to an absurdity whether it remains or ceases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that an eternal divine will for a temporal event leads to an impasse, and why it challenges the concept of an unchanging God.

Core Idea
An eternal will for a temporal event faces a logical dilemma.

This lesson unpacks a subtle objection about God's will. If God eternally willed creation at a specific time, what happens to that will after creation? The objector argues that either it remains (absurd) or ceases (contradicting eternity). This forces a critical examination of how divine attributes relate to temporal events.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the flow. Notice the conditional structure: 'If the world is originated...'.
The argument is built as a dilemma with two horns.
2
Identify the two alternatives
The objector presents two possibilities for God's will before creation: either God was eternally willing the specific time, or He was not. The second is rejected to avoid an infinite regress.
This establishes the first premise: God must have been eternally willing that specific time.
3
Unpack the temporal dilemma
After creation, does that eternal will remain or not? If it remains, God wills what already exists; if it ceases, an eternal attribute changes. Both are problematic.
This is the core logical tension.
4
Consider the opponent's reply
The opponent may say the will's essence remains but its relation to the specific time ceases. The objector dismisses this by referencing an earlier answer.
The objection is left unresolved in this passage, but it sets up a critical issue.
5
Reflect on the broader implications
Ask yourself: How does this objection challenge the classical Islamic view of God's will as eternal and unchanging?
This connects to deeper theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الخامس : لو كان العالم حادثاً ، لكان حدوثه ، لأجل أنه تعالى أراد إحداثه في ذلك الوقت. فإما أن يقال : إنه تعالى كان في الأزل مريداً لأن يحدثه في ذلك الوقت المعين، أو ما كان موصوفاً بهذه الإرادة في الأزل . والثاني باطل، وإلا لافتقر حدوث تلك الإرادة إلى إرادة أخرى وذلك باطل . فيثبت : أنه تعالى كان في الأزل مريداً لإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت ، وإذا جاء ذلك الوقت ، وأحدثه فيه . فهل بقي مريداً لإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت ، أو ما بقيت تلك الإرادة ؟ والأول باطل . وإلا لزم أن يقال : إنه بقي مريداً ، لإحداث الموجود في الوقت المتقدم . وذلك باطل . فيثبت : أن تلك الإرادة ما بقيت أنها كانت أزلية . فإن قالوا : ذات الإرادة باقية ، إلا أن ذلك التعلق المخصوص ، وتلك الإرادة المخصوصة ، قد بطل وزال . فنقول : الجواب ما ذكرناه في تقرير السؤال الثاني والله أعلم
The fifth question: If the world were originated, its occurrence would be because He, exalted, willed to bring it about at that time. Then either it is said that He, exalted, was in pre‑eternity willing to bring it about at that specific time, or He was not described with this will in pre‑eternity. The second is false; otherwise the occurrence of that will would need another will, and that is false. So it is established that He, exalted, was in pre‑eternity willing to bring about the world at that time. And when that time comes and He brings it about at that time, then does He remain willing to bring about the world at that time, or does that will not remain? The first is false; otherwise it would be necessary to say that He remained willing to bring about what already exists at a past time, and that is false. So it is established that that will did not remain, while it was eternal. If they say: The essence of the will remains, but that particular relation and that particular will have become null and ceased. Then we say: The answer is what we mentioned in the exposition of the second question. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is an objection (sual) about God's will. The objector argues: If the world is originated, its occurrence must be due to God's will at a specific time. Either God was willing that specific time from pre‑eternity or He was not. The latter is impossible because if the will itself originated, it would need another will, leading to infinite regress. So God must have been eternally willing the world at that specific time. Now, after that time arrives and the world is created, does God remain willing the world at that past time? If yes, it would mean He is willing the existence of something already existing at a past time, which is absurd. If no, then that eternal will has ceased, implying change in God. The opponent might respond that the essence of the will remains but the specific relation (tacalluq) ceases. The objector dismisses this by referring to an earlier answer (the second question). The objection aims to show that an eternal will for a temporal event leads to an impasse.

Key Terms
الإرادة (al-irāda)
أزلي (azalī)
تعلق (taʿalluq)
مريد (murīd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's eternal will can change its object without affecting its eternity.
Correction: The objection shows that if the specific object (the time) changes, either the will remains (absurd) or ceases (contradicting eternity).

Checkpoint
Why does the objector reject the possibility that God's will for creation at a specific time was not present in pre-eternity?
A) Because it would imply that God is not omnipotent
B) Because it would lead to an infinite regress of wills
C) Because it would mean the world is eternal
D) Because it contradicts God's goodness

Key Takeaway
The objection forces a dilemma: either God's will changes (impermissible) or it eternally wills a past event (absurd).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will is eternal and unchanging, does your own fluctuating will sometimes clash with His decree? How do you reconcile your changing desires with submission to a timeless will?
?
Imagine you prayed for something that did not happen at the time you expected. Does this lesson challenge you to trust that God's will was perfectly set in eternity, even if you cannot see why it was delayed?
?
When you reflect on your life, are there moments where you wanted to 'change God's mind'? How does the truth that God's will never changes reshape the way you should pray and hope?

Sixth Objection: Abrogation of an Eternal Command
Objection that abrogation implies cessation of the eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection against the Ash'arite claim that an eternal divine judgment can be abrogated.

Core Idea
Abrogating an eternal judgment implies its non-existence, which is contradictory.

This lesson examines a concise objection raised against the Ash'arite position on divine attributes. The objector highlights an apparent contradiction: if God's judgment is eternal, how can it be subject to abrogation? The passage defines abrogation and draws out the problematic consequence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice how the objector presents two claims and then draws a conclusion.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the two Ash'ari claims
The objector states that the Ash'arites claim God's judgment is eternal (qadīm) and that it is subject to abrogation (naskh). Which two attributes are being asserted?
These claims are the premises of the objection.
3
Understand the definition of naskh
According to the objector, abrogation means termination or cessation of the judgment. Why does this definition matter for the argument?
Alternative definitions might avoid the implication.
4
Grasp the logical consequence
If an eternal judgment ceases, that would mean the non-existence of something eternal. What is problematic about that?
Eternal entities are supposed to be unchanging and not subject to non-existence.
5
Reflect on the objection's force
Consider whether this objection is decisive or if a defense is possible. The passage presents a challenge to Ash'arite theology.
This prepares you for later responses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال السادس : زعمت الأشعرية : أن حكم الله قديم . ثم زعموا : ان أمر الله وحكمه قابل للنسخ . ولا معنى للنسخ : إلا انتهاء الحكم ، أو زواله . وعلى التقديرين : فذلك يقتضي عدم الأزلي
The sixth question: The Ashʿarites claimed that God's judgment (ḥukm) is eternal. Then they claimed that God's command and judgment are subject to abrogation (naskh). And abrogation means nothing but the termination of the judgment or its cessation. On both suppositions, that entails the non‑existence of the eternal.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a brief objection against the Ashʿarite position. They hold that God's judgment (ḥukm) is eternal (qadīm), yet they also claim that His command (amr) and ruling can be abrogated (naskh). The objector points out that abrogation means the end or removal of a judgment. If the judgment was eternal and then ceases, that would mean the non‑existence (ʿadam) of something eternal. This is problematic because eternal entities cannot cease to exist, since that would contradict their eternity. The objection challenges the coherence of an eternal attribute that can be abolished.

Key Terms
الحكم (al-ḥukm)
النسخ (al-naskh)
قديم (qadīm)
عدم الأزلي (ʿadam al-azalī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that abrogation only removes the obligation without affecting the eternal nature of the judgment itself.
Correction: The objector argues that abrogation terminates the judgment itself, implying its non-existence, which conflicts with its eternity.

Checkpoint

A) The transfer of a ruling to another time
B) The termination or cessation of the judgment
C) The confirmation of an eternal command
D) The modification of a ruling

Key Takeaway
The objection points out that abrogating an eternal judgment would entail its non-existence, which contradicts the concept of eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does your answer shape the way you approach difficult theological questions?
?
Consider the Ash'arite position that God's judgment is eternal yet abrogable. If you cannot fully reconcile this, are you willing to accept divine mystery, or does your intellect demand closure? How does your response affect your relationship with God?

The Sixth Objection: Abrogation of an Eternal Command
An objection to the Ash'ari claim that God's command is eternal yet abrogatable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection of abrogation challenges the Ash'ari claim that God's command is eternal, and why this is considered a logical contradiction.

Core Idea
An eternal command that can be abrogated is a contradiction in terms.

In this objection, the author presents a logical dilemma against the Ash'ari position on divine command. If God's judgment is eternal, how can it be subject to change? The passage defines abrogation and draws out its implication for the eternal nature of God's command. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author is presenting an objection, not an answer.
2
Identify the two Ash'ari claims
The passage begins by stating two Ash'ari claims: that God's judgment is eternal, and that it is subject to abrogation. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the meaning of abrogation
Abrogation is defined as either the termination or removal of the judgment. Reflect on why both definitions imply the judgment's end.
4
Grasp the logical problem
The objection concludes that on both suppositions, abrogation entails the non-existence of the eternal. Ask yourself: why is that a problem?
Think about the definition of 'eternal'—something that cannot cease to exist.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage presents the sixth objection against the Ash'ari position on the eternity of God's judgment. The author states the objection concisely: The Ash'aris assert that God's judgment is eternal, yet they also assert that it is subject to abrogation. But for the eternal, ceasing to exist is problematic, as it would mean the eternal becomes non-existent. Thus, the objection points out an inconsistency in the Ash'ari view: they claim the judgment is eternal, yet they allow its non-existence through abrogation. The author does not provide an answer here; this is a standalone objection.

The Logical Priority and Its Application to Temporal Attributes
Sanusi establishes the logical priority principle and applies it to temporal attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the logical priority of self-consideration over relational consideration proves that the possibility of the Essence acquiring temporal attributes is itself temporal.

Core Idea
What is true of a thing in itself precedes what is true of it in relation.

This lesson begins Sanusi's argument by establishing a foundational logical principle: the consideration of a thing in itself is prior to its relation with another. He applies this to temporal events to show that the possibility of the Divine Essence being described by temporal attributes is impossible in pre-eternity. Grasping this step is crucial for the subsequent proof of the body's temporalness.

Guided Path
1
Understand the priority principle
Read the first few lines: the consideration of a thing's state in itself is prior to its state with another. This is a logical axiom that underlies the entire argument.
The Arabic translates: 'consideration of the state of a thing in itself is prior to consideration of its state with another.'
2
Apply to temporal events
Notice how Sanusi then applies this: a temporal event (ḥādith) is impossible in pre-eternity, so its possibility of existing in itself is absent. Therefore, the possibility of something else (the Essence) being described by it is also absent in pre-eternity.
This establishes that the Essence's potential for temporal attributes is itself temporal.
3
Confirm the conclusion
The passage concludes that 'the possibility of the Essence being described by temporal attributes is a temporal matter, impossible in pre-eternity.' This is a direct result of the logical priority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اتصاف غيره به [ صفة عارضة (۱) ] له ، من حيث إنه هو . وإمكان وجوده في نفسه ، اعتبار حال ذاته من حيث إنها هي . ومن المعلوم : أن اعتبار حال الشيء في نفسه ، سابق على اعتبار حاله مع غيره . فيثبت : أن إمكان اتصاف غیره به ، فرع على إمكان وجوده في نفسه . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : الحادث ممتنع الوجود في الأزل ، وإذا كان إمكان وجوده في نفسه فائتاً في الأزل ، كان إمكان اتصاف غيره به أيضـاً فائتاً في الأزل . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن إمكان اتصاف الذات بالصفات الحادثة : أمر حادث ، ممتنع الحصول في الأزل. وأما بيان أن هذه القابلية من لوازم الذات : فلانا نقول : هذه القابلية ، إما أن تكون من الأمور اللازمة لماهية الجسم ، وإما أن تكون من العوارض المفارقة . فإن كان الأول فهو المطلوب ، وإن كان الثاني فنقول : لما كانت هذه القابلية ممكنة الحصول لتلك الذات ، كانت الذات قابلة لتلك القابلية . فقابلية تلك القابلية ، إن كانت من اللوازم فهو المطلوب ، وإن كانت من العوارض المفارقة ، كان الكلام فيها كالكلام في الأول . فيلزم التسلسل ، وهو محال . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن قابلية الجسم للصفات الحادثة : أمر حادث . وعند هذا نقول : إن كانت القابلية عين ذات الجسم ، لزم من حدوثها ، حدوث ذات الجسم ، وإن كانت مغايرة لذات الجسم ، وجب كونها من لوازم الذات ، وحينئذ يحصل لنا [ الجزم ] بأن الجسم لا ينفك عن الحوادث ، وقد سبق بيان أن ما لا ينفك عن الحادث فهو حادث . وحينئذ يلزم كون الجسم حادثاً . وهو المطلوب . وحينئذ يحصل لنا من هذا الدليل مطلوبان شريفان : الأول : إن الأجسام لما كانت قابلة للصفات الحادثة ، امتنع كونها قديمة . الثاني : إن الإله تعالى وتقدس ، لما كان قديماً، امتنع كونه قابلاً للصفات الحادثة
The description of other than Him by an accidental attribute (ṣifa ʿāriḍa) [belongs] to it, insofar as it is itself. And the possibility of its existence in itself is a consideration of the state of its essence insofar as it is itself. And it is known that consideration of the state of a thing in itself is prior to consideration of its state with another. So it is established that the possibility of describing other than Him by it is a branch of the possibility of its existence in itself. And if this is established, we say: The temporal (al-ḥādith) is impossible of existence in pre-eternity (al-azal). And if the possibility of its existence in itself is absent in pre-eternity, then the possibility of describing other than Him by it is also absent in pre-eternity. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that the possibility of the essence being described by temporal attributes (al-ṣifāt al-ḥāditha) is a temporal matter, impossible of occurrence in pre-eternity. As for the proof that this receptivity (al-qābiliyya) is among the necessary concomitants (lawāzim) of the essence: we say this receptivity is either among the necessary things for the quiddity (māhiyya) of the body, or among the separable accidents (al-ʿawāriḍ al-mufāriqa). If it is the first, that is the required. And if it is the second, we say: Since this receptivity is possible of occurrence for that essence, the essence is receptive to that receptivity. Then the receptivity of that receptivity, if it is from the necessary concomitants, that is the required, and if it is from the separable accidents, the discussion about it is like the discussion about the first. So an infinite regress (al-tasalsul) follows, which is impossible. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that the body's receptivity to temporal attributes is a temporal matter. And upon this we say: If the receptivity is identical to the body's essence, then from its temporalness the temporalness of the body's essence follows. And if it is other than the body's essence, it must be from the necessary concomitants of the essence. Then we obtain the certainty that the body is never free from temporal accidents (al-ḥawādith). And it has already been explained that whatever is never free from the temporal is itself temporal. Then the body's being temporal necessarily follows. And that is the required. Then from this proof we obtain two noble objectives (maṭlūbān sharīfān): First: Since bodies are receptive to temporal attributes, it is impossible for them to be eternal. Second: Since God (al-Ilāh), exalted and sanctified, is eternal, it is impossible for Him to be receptive to temporal attributes.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage is a multi-step proof (burhān) that the body's receptivity (qābiliyya) to temporal accidents is itself a temporal (ḥādith) attribute, not an eternal (qadīm) property of the body. The author begins by establishing a logical priority: the consideration of a thing in itself (e.g., its possibility of existence) is prior to its relation to something else (e.g., being described by an attribute). Therefore, the possibility of something else being described by an attribute depends on the possibility of that attribute existing in itself. Then he applies this to temporal events (ḥawādith): a temporal event is impossible in pre-eternity (al-azal), so its possibility of existing in itself is absent in pre-eternity; consequently, the possibility of the Divine Essence (al-dhāt) being described by such an attribute is also absent in pre-eternity. This proves that the possibility of the Essence acquiring temporal attributes is itself temporal and impossible in pre-eternity. Next, he turns to the body's receptivity (qābiliyya) to temporal accidents. He argues that this receptivity is either a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of the body's quiddity (māhiyya) or a separable accident (ʿāriḍ mufāriq). If it is a necessary concomitant, then the body is inherently temporal because it cannot exist without temporal accidents, which is the desired conclusion. If it is a separable accident, then an infinite regress (tasalsul) follows because the body would need to be receptive to that accident, and so on, which is impossible. Thus, the receptivity must be a necessary concomitant. Then, crucially, if the receptivity is identical to the body's essence, then the essence is temporal because the receptivity is temporal. If it is distinct, it is a necessary concomitant, implying the body is never free from temporal accidents, which is the definition of a temporal being (mā lā yanfakku ʿan al-ḥādith fa-huwa ḥādith). Therefore, the body is temporal. From this argument, two noble objectives (al-maṭlūbān al-sharīfān) are derived: (1) bodies cannot be eternal because they are receptive to temporal accidents; and (2) God, being eternal, cannot be receptive to temporal accidents. The passage is logically tight, employing the principle of impossibility of infinite regress and the argument from the inseparability of accidents.

Key Terms
اتصاف
إمكان
ذات
صفات حادثة
أزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the logical priority principle, what precedes consideration of a thing's state with another?
A) Consideration of the other's state in itself
B) Consideration of the thing's state in itself
C) The thing's actual existence
D) The other's description of the thing

Key Takeaway
The possibility of the Essence acquiring temporal attributes is itself a temporal impossibility in pre-eternity, due to the logical priority of self-consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's potential to be described by temporal events is itself temporal, how does that reshape your understanding of God's involvement in your daily life? Are you willing to let go of the idea that God's relationship with time is static?
?
You claim that God knows everything before it happens. How does this logical priority principle challenge you to think about God's knowledge of your future sins? Does it make you more conscious of your responsibility, knowing that God's knowledge is not a constraint on your free will?
?
Since the Essence cannot be described by temporal events in pre-eternity, what does that reveal about your own perception of God's 'closeness'? Will you strive to feel God's presence in the moment as a temporal, real encounter, rather than a distant, eternal abstraction?

Divine Attributes

The Receptivity Argument: Necessary Concomitant vs. Separable Accident
Sanusi proves that the body's receptivity to accidents is a necessary concomitant via infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi proves that the body's receptivity to temporal accidents is a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of its essence, using the impossibility of infinite regress.

Core Idea
If receptivity were accidental, an infinite regress would follow, proving it must be a necessary concomitant.

This lesson focuses on the second part of Sanusi's proof: establishing that the body's capacity to receive accidents (qābiliyya) is not a separable addition but a necessary concomitant of its essence. The argument uses a dilemma: either the receptivity is a necessary concomitant or a separable accident. If the latter, an infinite regress results, which is impossible. Therefore, it must be a necessary concomitant. This conclusion is crucial for linking the body's temporalness to its inherent receptivity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dilemma
Read the opening of this passage: the receptivity is either a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of the body's quiddity or a separable accident (ʿāriḍ mufāriq).
This is the classic 'division' (taqsīm) method.
2
Trace the first branch
If it is a necessary concomitant, the required conclusion is reached immediately: receptivity is intrinsic to the body.
3
Analyze the second branch
If it is a separable accident, then the body must be receptive to that accident, leading to another receptivity, and so on infinitely. This infinite regress (tasalsul) is impossible.
Each step repeats the same reasoning, creating a chain without end.
4
Conclude
Therefore, the receptivity cannot be a separable accident; it must be a necessary concomitant of the body's essence. This is then used to show that receptivity itself is temporal (as established earlier).
The passage ends with: 'So it is established that the body's receptivity to temporal attributes is a temporal matter.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a multi-step proof (burhān) that the body's receptivity (qābiliyya) to temporal accidents is itself a temporal (ḥādith) attribute, not an eternal (qadīm) property of the body. The author begins by establishing a logical priority: the consideration of a thing in itself (e.g., its possibility of existence) is prior to its relation to something else (e.g., being described by an attribute). Therefore, the possibility of something else being described by an attribute depends on the possibility of that attribute existing in itself. Then he applies this to temporal events (ḥawādith): a temporal event is impossible in pre-eternity (al-azal), so its possibility of existing in itself is absent in pre-eternity; consequently, the possibility of the Divine Essence (al-dhāt) being described by such an attribute is also absent in pre-eternity. This proves that the possibility of the Essence acquiring temporal attributes is itself temporal and impossible in pre-eternity. Next, he turns to the body's receptivity (qābiliyya) to temporal accidents. He argues that this receptivity is either a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of the body's quiddity (māhiyya) or a separable accident (ʿāriḍ mufāriq). If it is a necessary concomitant, then the body is inherently temporal because it cannot exist without temporal accidents, which is the desired conclusion. If it is a separable accident, then an infinite regress (tasalsul) follows because the body would need to be receptive to that accident, and so on, which is impossible. Thus, the receptivity must be a necessary concomitant. Then, crucially, if the receptivity is identical to the body's essence, then the essence is temporal because the receptivity is temporal. If it is distinct, it is a necessary concomitant, implying the body is never free from temporal accidents, which is the definition of a temporal being (mā lā yanfakku ʿan al-ḥādith fa-huwa ḥādith). Therefore, the body is temporal. From this argument, two noble objectives (al-maṭlūbān al-sharīfān) are derived: (1) bodies cannot be eternal because they are receptive to temporal accidents; and (2) God, being eternal, cannot be receptive to temporal accidents. The passage is logically tight, employing the principle of impossibility of infinite regress and the argument from the inseparability of accidents.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the body's receptivity to accidents is an accidental property that could be absent while the body remains eternal.
Correction: The argument shows that receptivity must be a necessary concomitant (lāzim) because the alternative leads to an infinite regress. Therefore, it is inseparable, and since it is temporal, the body must be temporal.

Checkpoint
What happens if the body's receptivity to accidents is assumed to be a separable accident?
A) The body becomes eternal.
B) The argument ends immediately.
C) An infinite regress of receptivities follows.
D) The receptivity is proven to be eternal.

Key Takeaway
The body's receptivity to temporal accidents must be a necessary concomitant of its essence, established by the impossibility of infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that your body's very capacity to experience change—every emotion, every thought—is not accidental but a necessary part of its existence, does that make you feel more or less responsible for how you use your body? Why?
?
The argument proves that infinite regress is impossible. If you look at your own life, do you ever act as if there is an infinite chain of causes that doesn't lead back to God? How does this logical truth confront that tendency?
?
Sanusi shows that rejecting a necessary concomitant leads to absurdity. Is there an area in your faith where you have been trying to treat an essential truth as if it were optional? What would it mean for you to accept it as necessary today?

The Conclusion and the Two Noble Objectives
Sanusi concludes the argument and extracts two theological implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi concludes that the body is temporal and derives two key theological principles: bodies cannot be eternal and God cannot receive temporal accidents.

Core Idea
The body's inseparable receptivity proves its temporalness, and the argument yields two profound truths.

This final lesson completes Sanusi's proof. Having established that the body's receptivity is a necessary concomitant and itself temporal, he now examines whether this receptivity is identical to the body's essence or distinct. In either case, the body's temporalness follows. He then articulates two noble objectives: bodies are not eternal, and God, being eternal, cannot be receptive to temporal accidents. This argument elegantly merges cosmology and theology.

Guided Path
1
Consider the identity of receptivity
Read the opening: if the receptivity is identical to the body's essence, then the essence is temporal because the receptivity is temporal. If it is distinct, it is a necessary concomitant, so the body always has it, meaning it never lacks temporal accidents.
This is a case analysis (taqsīm) similar to earlier.
2
Apply the principle that 'whatever is never free from the temporal is temporal'
Sanusi recalls a previously established principle: anything inseparable from temporal events is itself temporal. Since the body is never free from temporal accidents (due to the necessary concomitant), it must be temporal.
This principle is a standard kalām argument for the createdness of the world.
3
Derive the two noble objectives
First: bodies cannot be eternal because they are receptive to temporal attributes. Second: God, being eternal, cannot be receptive to temporal attributes. This protects divine transcendence.
The proof thus serves both cosmology (createdness of bodies) and theology (divine otherness).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اتصاف غيره به [ صفة عارضة (۱) ] له ، من حيث إنه هو . وإمكان وجوده في نفسه ، اعتبار حال ذاته من حيث إنها هي . ومن المعلوم : أن اعتبار حال الشيء في نفسه ، سابق على اعتبار حاله مع غيره . فيثبت : أن إمكان اتصاف غیره به ، فرع على إمكان وجوده في نفسه . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : الحادث ممتنع الوجود في الأزل ، وإذا كان إمكان وجوده في نفسه فائتاً في الأزل ، كان إمكان اتصاف غيره به أيضـاً فائتاً في الأزل . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن إمكان اتصاف الذات بالصفات الحادثة : أمر حادث ، ممتنع الحصول في الأزل. وأما بيان أن هذه القابلية من لوازم الذات : فلانا نقول : هذه القابلية ، إما أن تكون من الأمور اللازمة لماهية الجسم ، وإما أن تكون من العوارض المفارقة . فإن كان الأول فهو المطلوب ، وإن كان الثاني فنقول : لما كانت هذه القابلية ممكنة الحصول لتلك الذات ، كانت الذات قابلة لتلك القابلية . فقابلية تلك القابلية ، إن كانت من اللوازم فهو المطلوب ، وإن كانت من العوارض المفارقة ، كان الكلام فيها كالكلام في الأول . فيلزم التسلسل ، وهو محال . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن قابلية الجسم للصفات الحادثة : أمر حادث . وعند هذا نقول : إن كانت القابلية عين ذات الجسم ، لزم من حدوثها ، حدوث ذات الجسم ، وإن كانت مغايرة لذات الجسم ، وجب كونها من لوازم الذات ، وحينئذ يحصل لنا [ الجزم ] بأن الجسم لا ينفك عن الحوادث ، وقد سبق بيان أن ما لا ينفك عن الحادث فهو حادث . وحينئذ يلزم كون الجسم حادثاً . وهو المطلوب . وحينئذ يحصل لنا من هذا الدليل مطلوبان شريفان : الأول : إن الأجسام لما كانت قابلة للصفات الحادثة ، امتنع كونها قديمة . الثاني : إن الإله تعالى وتقدس ، لما كان قديماً، امتنع كونه قابلاً للصفات الحادثة

The author begins by establishing a logical priority: the consideration of a thing in itself (e.g., its possibility of existence) is prior to its relation to something else (e.g., being described by an attribute). Therefore, the possibility of something else being described by an attribute depends on the possibility of that attribute existing in itself. Then he applies this to temporal events (ḥawādith): a temporal event is impossible in pre-eternity (al-azal), so its possibility of existing in itself is absent in pre-eternity; consequently, the possibility of the Divine Essence (al-dhāt) being described by such an attribute is also absent in pre-eternity. This proves that the possibility of the Essence acquiring temporal attributes is itself temporal and impossible in pre-eternity. Next, he turns to the body's receptivity (qābiliyya) to temporal accidents. He argues that this receptivity is either a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of the body's quiddity (māhiyya) or a separable accident (ʿāriḍ mufāriq). If it is a necessary concomitant, then the body is inherently temporal because it cannot exist without temporal accidents, which is the desired conclusion. If it is a separable accident, then an infinite regress (tasalsul) follows because the body would need to be receptive to that accident, and so on, which is impossible. Thus, the receptivity must be a necessary concomitant. Then, crucially, if the receptivity is identical to the body's essence, then the essence is temporal because the receptivity is temporal. If it is distinct, it is a necessary concomitant, implying the body is never free from temporal accidents, which is the definition of a temporal being (mā lā yanfakku ʿan al-ḥādith fa-huwa ḥādith). Therefore, the body is temporal. From this argument, two noble objectives (al-maṭlūbān al-sharīfān) are derived: (1) bodies cannot be eternal because they are receptive to temporal accidents; and (2) God, being eternal, cannot be receptive to temporal accidents. The passage is logically tight, employing the principle of impossibility of infinite regress and the argument from the inseparability of accidents.

Key Terms
عين ذات
لوازم الذات
حوادث
قديم
حادث

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what follows if the body's receptivity to accidents is distinct from its essence?
A) The body is eternal.
B) The body is never free from temporal accidents.
C) The receptivity is not a necessary concomitant.
D) God can also have temporal attributes.

Key Takeaway
The body is temporal because it is inseparable from temporal accidents, yielding two truths: bodies cannot be eternal, and God cannot receive temporal attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that anything receptive to change cannot be eternal. Reflect on your own heart—your receptivity to change, to sin, to growth. Are you comfortable with your own temporalness, or do you cling to the illusion of permanence? What would it mean to live fully aware that you are a temporal being in need of the Eternal?
?
Sanusi concludes that God cannot receive temporal attributes. Consider: What attributes of God do you most need to remember in moments of anxiety or change? How does this truth free you from expecting God to change according to your circumstances?
?
This lesson forces you to choose: either the body's essence is identical to its receptivity or it is a necessary concomitant. Neither leaves room for the body to be eternal. Does your daily life reflect that you truly believe this—that you are not permanent? What would change if you lived with this conviction?

Eternity as a Real Attribute
Sanusi defends eternity as a real attribute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends eternity (qidam) as an established positive attribute and refutes the objection that it is merely a negation.

Core Idea
Eternity is not a mere negation but a real, positive attribute.

Before arguing that the world cannot be eternal, the author first clarifies what eternity means. He anticipates an objection: perhaps eternity is just the absence of prior non-existence. The response shows that such a negation, if taken as a positive attribute, leads to infinite regress, so eternity must be a real attribute. This foundation is essential for the subsequent proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author defines eternity and immediately addresses an objection.
The passage uses logical terms like 'negation of negation'.
2
Identify the objection and response
Find where the objector suggests eternity is just a negation. Then follow the author's rebuttal involving infinite regress.
3
Understand the conclusion on eternity
The author concludes that eternity is an established attribute because negation of non-existence is affirmation.
This sets up the next part of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الحادية عشر: لو كان الجسم قديماً، لكان مشاركاً لذات الله تعالى في القدم. فنقول: القدم صفة ثابتة. لأنه عبارة عن نفي العدم السابق، ونفي النفي ثبوت، فالقديم أمر ثابت. فإن قالوا: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إنه عبارة عن نفي مسبوقية هذا الوجود بالعدم، فنقول: هذه المسبوقية، إن كانت صفة وجودية، كانت أيضاً مسبوقة بالعدم، فيلزم أن تكون مسبوقية هذه المسبوقية زائدة عليها. ولزم التسلسل. فيثبت: أن هذه المسبوقية ليست صفة موجودة بل هي أمر عدمي، والقديم نقيضها ورافعها. وعدم العدم ثبوت. فيثبت: أن المفهوم من القدم ثابت. إذا ثبت هذا، فنقول: لو كان العالم قديماً، لكان مساوياً لذات الله تعالى، في صفة ثابتة، وهي القدم، فإما أن تحصل المخالفة بينهما باعتبار آخر، أو لا تحصل. فإن كان الأول فحينئذ تكون ذات الله تعالى وذات العالم مركبة من الأمر الذي حصلت به المشاركة، ومن الأمر الآخر الذي حصلت به المخالفة. وإذا كان كذلك، فنقول: كل واحد من هذين الاعتبارين، إما أن يكونا قديمين، أو محدثين. أو أحدهما قديم، والآخر محدث. فإن كانا قديمين، فهما أيضاً مشتركان في القدم ومختلفان باعتبار آخر، وإلا لامتنع أن يحكم على أحدهما بكونه مشتركاً فيه، وعلى الآخر بكونه غير مشترك فيه. فيلزم تركب كل واحد منهما عن أمرين آخرين. ويمر الكلام فيه إلى ما لا نهاية له، فيلزم كون كل واحد منها مركباً من أمور لا نهاية لها، لا مرة واحدة، بل مراراً لا نهاية لها. وهو محال. وأما إن قلنا: هذان الاعتباران محدثان، أو إن قلنا: إن أحدهما قديم والآخر محدث. فهذا محال. لأن المحدث لا يكون جزءاً من ماهية القديم. فيثبت: أن القول بالقديم، يفضي إلى أقسام باطلة، فكان القول به باطلاً. وأما إن لم يختلفا بإعتبار آخر، فالمساواة ظاهرة. وإنه محال. فوجب أن
The eleventh proof: If the body were eternal (qadīm), it would share in eternity (qidam) with the essence of God, exalted is He. We say: Eternity is an established attribute, for it is an expression of the negation of prior non-existence, and the negation of negation is affirmation, so the eternal is an established thing. If they say: Why is it not permissible to say that it is an expression of the negation of this existence being preceded by non-existence? We say: This being-preceded, if it were an existent attribute, would itself also be preceded by non-existence, so that would entail that the being-preceded of this being-preceded is additional to it, and an infinite regress would be entailed. Thus it is established that this being-preceded is not an existent attribute but rather a non-existent thing, and the eternal is its opposite and remover. And the negation of non-existence is affirmation. So it is established that what is understood from eternity is affirmed. If this is established, we say: If the world were eternal, it would be equal to the essence of God, exalted is He, in an established attribute, namely eternity. So either there occurs differentiation between them by another consideration, or it does not occur. If the first, then the essence of God and the essence of the world would be composed of the thing by which the sharing occurred and the other thing by which the differentiation occurred. And if that is so, we say: Each of these two considerations is either both eternal, or both temporally originated, or one eternal and the other temporally originated. If both are eternal, then they also share in eternity and differ by another consideration, otherwise it would be impossible to judge that one is shared and the other not shared. So it would entail that each of them is composed of two other things, and the discussion would continue indefinitely, leading to each of them being composed of infinite things, not once, but infinite times—and that is impossible. And if we say that these two considerations are temporally originated, or if we say that one is eternal and the other temporally originated, this is impossible, because the temporally originated cannot be a part of the essence of the eternal. Thus it is established that the assertion of eternity leads to false divisions, so the assertion of it is false. And if they do not differ by another consideration, then the equality is apparent, and that is impossible. So it is necessary that...
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage presents the eleventh proof against the eternity of the world. It begins by stating that if a body were eternal (qadīm), it would share the attribute of eternity (qidam) with God. The author first defends the nature of eternity as an established attribute: it is the negation of prior non-existence, and the negation of that negation is affirmation, so the eternal is something real. An objection is raised: could eternity be merely the negation of being preceded by non-existence? The author responds that if such 'being-preceded' were a positive attribute, it would itself be preceded, leading to an infinite regress; hence it is a non-existent thing, and the eternal is its opposite, confirming that eternity is a real attribute. Next, the main argument: if the world were eternal, it would be equal to God in the attribute of eternity. This equality must be accompanied by some difference, or they would be identical. If there is a difference, then both God and the world would be composed of the shared attribute (eternity) and the differentiating factor. Then each of these two components would itself require further composition, leading to an infinite regress—impossible. Alternatively, if the differentiating factor is temporal, then the temporal would be part of the eternal essence, also impossible. If there is no difference, then the world would be equal to God, which is impossible. The proof is cut off but clearly concludes that the world cannot be eternal.

Key Terms
قديم
قدم
صفة ثابتة
عدم
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the response to the objection, why can 'being-preceded by non-existence' not be a positive attribute?
A) Because then an infinite regress would follow.
B) Because it would make eternity temporal.
C) Because it contradicts the definition of eternity.
D) Because it would imply composition.

Key Takeaway
Eternity is an established positive attribute because it is the negation of non-existence, and the negation of non-existence is affirmation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat God's eternity as a real, positive attribute that gives you security, or do you think of it as merely 'not having a beginning' without affecting your daily life? What would change if you lived with constant awareness that God's existence is eternally fixed and certain?
?
When you face uncertainty or fear, do you naturally turn to the Eternal One as the only constant, or do you seek stability in temporary things? How can you train your heart to find rest in God's eternal nature?
?
The argument proves that eternity is more than a negation—it is a real quality of God. Is your relationship with God based on a positive knowledge of His attributes, or do you just negate limitations? What step can you take this week to deepen your understanding of one of His attributes?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Composition Leads to Infinite Regress
Sanusi argues that sharing eternity forces composition and infinite regress

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sharing the attribute of eternity between God and the world would entail composition and an impossible infinite regress, proving the world cannot be eternal.

Core Idea
If two beings share an attribute, they must differ in another, leading to endless composition.

Having established eternity as a real attribute, the author now builds the main proof. If the world were eternal, it would be equal to God in eternity. This equality must be accompanied by some difference, or they would be identical. That difference creates composition, and each component would itself need further composition, leading to an infinite chain of composites—an impossibility. This rigorous argument eliminates the possibility of an eternal world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Focus on the logical structure: equality, differentiation, composition, and the three possibilities for the components (both eternal, both temporal, etc.).
The passage is dense; read it slowly.
2
Trace the three possibilities
After establishing composition, the author examines whether the differentiating factor is eternal, temporal, or mixed. Each leads to contradiction.
3
Understand why infinite regress is impossible
If both components are eternal, they themselves would require further composition ad infinitum, which is absurd.
The other two cases are also impossible because the temporal cannot be part of the eternal.
4
Note the incomplete conclusion
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the intended conclusion is clear: the eternity of the world is impossible.
The argument is complete in essence.

The Negative Nature of Qidam (Eternity)
Sanusi defines qidam as negative and proves via infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why qidam (eternity) is a negative attribute and how the argument from infinite regress establishes it.

Core Idea
Eternity is not a positive quality but the negation of being preceded by non-existence.

This passage presents a crisp logical argument from Imam al-Sanusi that qidam (eternity) is not a positive attribute but a negation. By analyzing what it means to 'not be preceded by non-existence,' the author shows that any attempt to turn this into a positive property leads to an infinite regress. Understanding this distinction is foundational to Islamic theology's treatment of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the argument uses a conditional statement.
2
Follow the regress argument
The author hypothesizes that if 'non-precedence' were a positive attribute, it would itself need to be preceded by non-existence, leading to an infinite chain. Identify this step and see why it is impossible.
Infinite regress (tasalsul) is considered a logical impossibility in kalam.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since the alternative leads to absurdity, the non-precedence must be a negative thing. The negation of non-existence is existence, so the concept of qidam is established as a real negative notion.
This shows that negative attributes can be real without being positive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنه عبارة عن نفي مسبوقية هذا الوجود بالعدم، فنقول: هذه المسبوقية، إن كانت صفة وجودية، كانت أيضاً مسبوقة بالعدم، فيلزم أن تكون مسبوقية هذه المسبوقية زائدة عليها، ولزم التسلسل. فيثبت: أن هذه المسبوقية ليست صفة موجودة بل هي أمر عدمي، والقديم نقيضها ورافعها. وعدم العدم ثبوت. فيثبت: أن المفهوم من القدم ثابت.
It is the negation of the precedence of this existence by non-existence. We say: this precedence, if it were a positive attribute, would itself be preceded by non-existence, so it would necessitate that the precedence of this precedence be additional to it, and an infinite regress would follow. Thus it is established that this precedence is not an existent attribute but rather a negative thing, and the eternal (al-qadīm) is its opposite and remover. The negation of non-existence is affirmation. Thus it is established that the concept of eternity (al-qidam) is established.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of eternity (qidam) and proves its negative nature. The author begins by stating that qidam is the negation of being preceded by non-existence. He then argues that if this 'non-precedence' were a positive attribute, it would itself require prior non-existence, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). Therefore, it is not a positive attribute but a negative one (amr 'adami). The eternal being (al-qadīm) is the opposite of non-existence and its remover. The negation of non-existence is existence (thubūt), so the concept of qidam is established as a real negative notion.

Key Terms
qidam
tasalsul
amr 'adami
qadim

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qidam is a positive attribute like existence.
Correction: Qidam is the negation of being preceded by non-existence; it is a negative attribute.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject that the 'non-precedence' is a positive attribute?
A) Because it would lead to infinite regress.
B) Because it would make the world eternal.
C) Because the Quran denies it.
D) Because it contradicts divine unity.

Key Takeaway
Qidam is not a positive attribute but the negation of non-existence, and this is established by disproving the alternative via infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought of Allah's attributes as just 'extra' qualities added to His essence? How does the fact that qidam is a negation—not a positive addition—challenge that assumption? What does this mean for your worship: is Allah more than a collection of traits?
?
If you truly internalise that Allah's existence is not preceded by non-existence—that He is not contingent—would you fear anything else? Does your heart still tremble at created beings? What would it take for your reliance on Allah to reflect His absolute necessity?
?
In your daily life, you often think of yourself as having a beginning. How does meditating on Allah's eternal existence without beginning reshape your perspective on your own temporary existence? Are you living for the Eternal or the temporary?

Introduction to Article Three: God Acts by Choice
Announcement of the third article's topic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that this article is dedicated to proving that God acts by choice (فاعل بالاختيار) rather than by natural necessity.

Core Idea
God acts by choice, not by nature.

This brief passage marks the beginning of the third article of al-Maṭālib al-Āliyah. The author announces his intention to present proofs that the Creator of the universe is an agent who acts by choice (bi-al-ikhtiyār), in contrast to the philosophical view that He acts by natural necessity. As you read, note that this is merely an introduction; the actual arguments follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic heading
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the structure: the article number, the word 'fī' (on), and then the topic. The key phrase is 'fāʿil bi-al-ikhtiyār'.
2
Identify key terms
Find the two Arabic terms: 'fāʿil bi-al-ikhtiyār' (agent by choice) and 'mūjib bi-al-ghāth' (necessitating cause by nature). Understand their contrast.
The second term may be a variant of 'mūjib bi-al-dhāt'.
3
Understand the significance
This heading sets up a major theological dispute. The author is refuting the necessitarian view that God's action is determined by His essence, leaving no room for choice. Recognize that the entire article will defend the Ashʿarī position of voluntary agency.
4
Preview the upcoming arguments
While the passage itself contains no proof, it tells you what to expect. Think about what kinds of evidence the author might use to show that God acts by choice, such as the diversity in creation or the timing of events.
This prepares you for the following pages.

The Maker of the World Must Be a Free Agent
Opening assertion that the Creator is a free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Creator must be a free agent rather than a necessitating cause, and how this is derived from the principles of wisdom.

Core Idea
The Creator is a free agent, not a necessary cause.

This lesson introduces a foundational claim of Islamic theology: that the Maker of the world acts by free choice, not by necessity. The author draws on philosophical principles to establish this, setting the stage for further proofs. Let's examine this short but profound statement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

The Arabic is a single sentence, but packed with meaning.
2
Understand the key terms
Learn the difference between a free agent and a necessitating cause. A free agent acts by choice; a necessitating cause acts inevitably.
This distinction is central to the proof of God's will.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters: if God were a necessitating cause, creation would be involuntary; but we know God is perfect and acts with wisdom.
The article will build on this premise.

Divine Wisdom in the Sun's Creation: Planetary Motions as Evidence
A proof of divine wisdom through the sun's creation and planetary motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the precise motions of the planets around the Sun demonstrate divine wisdom according to the author.

Core Idea
The Sun's sovereignty over planetary motions reveals the Creator's wisdom.

In this passage, the author presents a theological argument for divine wisdom (ḥikma) by examining the intricate motions of the planets. He shows that the Sun's mean motion governs the epicycles of the upper and lower planets in a harmonious system, reflecting order and purpose. This is not merely astronomy; it is a reflection of the Creator's design.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then the translation. Notice how the author begins with a claim and then provides the first evidence.
The passage is dense with astronomical terms; focus on the overall argument.
2
Identify the three planetary groups
The author classifies planets into three groups: upper planets, lower planets, and the Moon. Note how their motions relate to the Sun's mean motion.
The upper planets have epicycles whose total motion equals the Sun's motion; lower planets' epicycle centers move at the Sun's pace; the Moon's center moves faster.
3
Understand the analogy
Read the part where the Sun is compared to a sovereign and planets to subjects. Reflect on how this supports the idea of divine wisdom.
The analogy extends to spheres, zodiac signs, degrees, minutes as regions, countries, localities, alleys.
4
Reflect on the theological purpose
Consider why the author includes this astronomical detail. It is to prove that the Creator's wisdom is manifest in the cosmos, a common theme in Kalām.
The Quranic verse at the end reinforces the idea of divine subjection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول
شرح منافع الشمس
نقول (۱) : إن آثار الحكمة في تخليق الشمس ظاهرة من وجوه :
الأول : إنه سبحانه وتعالى قدر تحركات الكواكب الثلاثة العلوية ، على محيطات تداويرها أن يكون مجموعها مع حركات مراكزها ، على محيطات حواملها ، مساوية لحركة الشمس الوسطى. فلا جرم صارت هذه الكواكب في ذرى تداويرها : مقارنة للشمس . وفي حضيض تداويرها مقابلة لها . وأما السفليات فجعلت حركة مركز تدويرها : مساوياً لحركة الشمس الوسطى . فلا جرم قد استوفت الحكمة البالغة : أقسام الحركة في مراكز التداوير التي عليها مدار الأدوار . فإن حركة مركز تدوير السفلين : مساوية لحركة جرم الشمس . وحركات مراكز التداوير الثلاثة العلوية : أنقص من حركة الشمس وحركة مركز تداوير القمر : أسرع من حركة الشمس. وبهذا الطريق يظهر أن الشمس بالنسبة إليها ، كالسلطان بالنسبة إلى العبيد . وسائر الكواكب يتحركون حولها على نسب مخصوصة ، موافقة للحكمة والمصلحة. بل نقول : إنه تعالى خلق الشمس في [؟] ، كالملك في العالم . فالكواكب كالجنود للملك ، والأفلاك كالاقاليم ، والبروج كالبلدان، والدرجات كالمحلات ، والدقائق كالأزقة . ولما كان الأمر
(۱) قال الله تعالى : ( وسخر لكم الشمس والقمر دأبين. وسخر لكم الليل والنهار )
Chapter One: Explanation of the Benefits of the Sun. We say (1): The traces of wisdom in the creation of the Sun are apparent from aspects: First: That He, glorified and exalted, ordained the motions of the three upper planets on the circumferences of their epicycles so that their sum, together with the motions of their centers on the circumferences of their deferents, is equal to the mean motion of the Sun. Hence these planets at the apogees of their epicycles are conjunct with the Sun, and at the perigees of their epicycles are opposite to it. As for the lower planets, He made the motion of the center of their epicycle equal to the mean motion of the Sun. Hence consummate wisdom has encompassed the kinds of motion in the centers of the epicycles upon which the cycles revolve. For the motion of the center of the epicycle of the two lower planets is equal to the motion of the Sun's body, and the motions of the centers of the epicycles of the three upper planets are slower than the motion of the Sun, and the motion of the center of the epicycle of the Moon is faster than the motion of the Sun. In this way it appears that the Sun in relation to them is like the sovereign in relation to slaves. The rest of the planets move around it in specific proportions, appropriate to wisdom and benefit. Rather, we say: He, exalted, created the Sun in [?], like the king in the world. So the planets are like soldiers to the king, the spheres like regions, the zodiacal signs like countries, the degrees like localities, the minutes like alleys. And when the matter... (1) Allah Almighty said: 'And He subjected for you the sun and the moon, both constant, and subjected for you the night and the day.'
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage presents a theological proof of divine wisdom (ḥikma) in the creation of the Sun, focusing on the orderly motions of the planets. The author begins by stating that traces of wisdom in the Sun's creation are evident from multiple aspects, then details the first aspect: the precise motions of the planets. He explains that the upper planets (Saturn, Jupiter, Mars) have epicyclic motions such that their combined motion equals the Sun's mean motion, causing them to be in conjunction or opposition at certain points. The lower planets (Venus, Mercury) have their epicycle centers moving equal to the Sun's mean motion. He emphasizes that wisdom (al-ḥikma al-bāligha) encompasses all kinds of motion in the epicycle centers that govern celestial cycles. The Moon's motion is faster. This arrangement shows that the Sun is like a sovereign and the planets like subjects moving around it. The author then extends the analogy: the Sun is like a king, planets like soldiers, spheres like regions, zodiac signs like countries, degrees like localities, and minutes like alleys. The passage ends with an incomplete sentence ('And when the matter...') and a Quranic verse (Surah Ibrahim: 33 or similar) as a footnote, supporting the claim that God subjected the sun and moon. The argument uses observational astronomy to demonstrate design and purpose, a common approach in Kalām to prove divine attributes like wisdom and power.

The Sun's Central Position and Proportional Motions
First proof of wisdom in the sun's creation, from its central position and proportional planetary motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the precise ordering of planetary motions relative to the sun demonstrates divine wisdom and why the sun's central position is analogous to a king in his kingdom.

Core Idea
The sun's central position and proportional planetary motions reveal a purposeful design.

This passage presents the first proof for the wisdom in creating the sun. By examining the movements of the upper and lower planets, the author shows how their epicyclic motions are perfectly calibrated to the sun's motion. This intricate harmony, coupled with the sun's central location, supports the conclusion that the universe is ordered with wisdom and purpose. In this lesson, we will explore the detailed arguments and the powerful analogy of the sun as king.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the technical terms like epicycles and mean sun.
2
Understand the rules for planetary motions
The author describes three categories: upper planets' motion sum equals sun's motion; lower planets' epicycle center equals sun's motion; moon's motion faster. This variety shows completeness of wisdom.
The exact numbers are not important; the idea of proportional design is key.
3
Reflect on the analogy of the king
The author compares the sun to a king, planets to soldiers, and spheres to provinces. This hierarchical analogy underscores the sun's sovereign role.
4
Analyze the structural argument about the sun's position
The author enumerates eleven spheres and places the sun in the middle, because that is where a king's palace should be. This is not merely physical but symbolic.
5
Synthesize the proof of wisdom
Ask yourself: How do these astronomical details and analogies together prove that the sun was created with wisdom?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إنه سبحانه وتعالى قدر تحركات الكواكب الثلاثة العلوية ، على محيطات تداويرها أن يكون مجموعها مع حركات مراكزها ، على محيطات حواملها ، مساوية لحركة الشمس الوسطى. فلا جرم صارت هذه الكواكب في ذرى تداويرها : مقارنة للشمس . وفي حضيض تداويرها مقابلة لها . وأما السفليات فجعلت حركة مركز تدويرها : مساوياً لحركة الشمس الوسطى . فلا جرم قد استوفت الحكمة البالغة : أقسام الحركة في مراكز التداوير التي عليها مدار الأدوار . فإن حركة مركز تدوير السفلين : مساوية لحركة جرم الشمس . وحركات مراكز التداوير الثلاثة العلوية : أنقص من حركة الشمس وحركة مركز تداوير القمر : أسرع من حركة الشمس. وبهذا الطريق يظهر أن الشمس بالنسبة إليها ، كالسلطان بالنسبة إلى العبيد . وسائر الكواكب يتحركون حولها على نسب مخصوصة ، موافقة للحكمة والمصلحة. بل نقول : إنه تعالى خلق الشمس في ، كالملك في العالم . فالكواكب كالجنود للملك ، والأفلاك كالاقاليم ، والبروج كالبلدان، والدرجات كالمحلات ، والدقائق كالأزقة . ولما كان الأمر كذلك ، لا جرم صار موضعها في الفلك المتوسط. كما أن دار الملك ، يجب أن تكون في وسط المملكة . وبيانه : أن جملة العالم إحدى عشرة كرة ، خمسة منها فلك الشمس وهي : فلك المريخ ، والمشتري، وزحل ، وفلك الثوابت ، والفلك الأعظم . وخمسة أخرى في داخل فلك الشمس . وهي : فلك الزهرة ، وعطارد ، والقمر ، ثم الكرة اللطيفة وهي النار والهواء ، والكرة الكثيفة وهي الماء والأرض . فلما كانت الشمس كالسلطان لعالم الأجسام ، لا جرم جعل مكانها في وسط كرات العالم .
The first: Indeed He, glorified and exalted, determined the movements of the three upper planets on the perimeters of their epicycles such that their sum with the movements of their centers on the perimeters of their carriers equals the movement of the mean sun. Hence inevitably these planets at the apogees of their epicycles are conjunct with the sun, and at the perigees of their epicycles are opposite to it. As for the lower planets, He made the movement of the center of their epicycle equal to the movement of the mean sun. Hence inevitably the perfect wisdom has encompassed the types of movement in the centers of the epicycles upon which the revolutions depend. For the movement of the center of the epicycle of the two lower planets is equal to the movement of the body of the sun, and the movements of the centers of the epicycles of the three upper planets are less than the movement of the sun, and the movement of the center of the epicycle of the moon is faster than the movement of the sun. In this way it appears that the sun in relation to them is like a king in relation to slaves; the rest of the planets move around it in specific proportions in accordance with wisdom and benefit. Rather we say: Indeed He, exalted, created the sun in [?], like a king in the world. So the planets are like soldiers for the king, the spheres like provinces, the zodiacs like cities, the degrees like neighborhoods, and the minutes like alleyways. Since the matter is thus, inevitably its place is in the middle sphere, just as the king's abode must be in the middle of the kingdom. Its explanation: The totality of the world consists of eleven spheres: five are above the sphere of the sun, namely the spheres of Mars, Jupiter, Saturn, the sphere of fixed stars, and the greatest sphere; and five others are inside the sphere of the sun, namely the spheres of Venus, Mercury, the moon, then the subtle sphere which is fire and air, and the dense sphere which is water and earth. Since the sun is like the king of the world of bodies, inevitably its place was made in the middle of the spheres of the world.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is the first proof (الوجه الأول) of the wisdom in the creation of the sun. It argues from the precise ordering of planetary motions relative to the sun. The author begins by describing how God determined the movements of the three upper planets (Mars, Jupiter, Saturn) on their epicycles so that their combined motion matches that of the mean sun. This ensures that at the apogees they are in conjunction with the sun and at perigees opposite. The lower planets (Venus, Mercury) have their epicycle center motion equal to the sun's motion. The moon's motion is faster. This variety of motions – some equal, some slower, some faster – shows that all possible types of motion are represented, manifesting complete wisdom. The sun is likened to a king, the planets to subjects moving around it in specific proportions. Then the author elaborates with a cosmic analogy: the sun is like a king in a kingdom, the spheres like provinces, the zodiacs like cities, etc. This leads to a structural argument: the sun occupies the middle sphere because a king's palace is in the center. The author enumerates eleven spheres: five above the sun's sphere (Mars, Jupiter, Saturn, fixed stars, outermost) and five inside (Venus, Mercury, moon, fire/air, water/earth). Since the sun is sovereign over the material world, its central position is fitting. This proof demonstrates divine wisdom through astronomical harmony and hierarchical order.

The Moon's Borrowed Light and the Wisdom of Eclipses
This passage presents the second proof: the moon's light is derived from the sun, demonstrated by eclipses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the astronomical argument that the moon's light is derived from the sun, as evidenced by lunar eclipses, and how this reveals divine wisdom in enabling celestial navigation.

Core Idea
The moon's eclipses prove its light is borrowed, unlocking the cosmos.

This passage presents the second proof of the sun's wisdom by focusing on the moon. It begins with an observable phenomenon—the moon's changing light—and argues that lunar eclipses demonstrate this light is not intrinsic. Moreover, eclipses serve a crucial purpose: they allow astronomers to determine the moon's true position and, by extension, the positions of other stars. Let's explore the logic behind this argument.

Guided Path
1
Observe the moon's light changes
Read the first part: the moon's light increases and decreases with distance from the sun. This suggests its light is not its own.
2
Distinguish essential vs. accidental light
The author introduces two types of light: essential (ذاتي) and accidental (عرضي). The moon's light is accidental, derived from the sun.
3
Consider the eclipse argument
If the moon's light were essential, it would not suffer eclipses. The fact that it does confirms its light is borrowed.
This is the key logical step.
4
Understand the practical benefit
Eclipses help determine the moon's true position, correcting for parallax. This is essential for mapping the heavens.
The author calls eclipses a 'key' to knowing celestial positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني : أن القمر يزداد نوره وينتقص ، بسبب قربه من الشمس ، وبعده عنها ، وكثيرون من الناس يزعمون : أن أنوار سائر الكواكب مقتبسة من الشمس، ولولا أن جرم القمر ليس نوره نوراً ذاتياً ، بل عرضياً مستفاداً من الشمس ، وإلا لما عرض له الخسوف . ولولا الخسوف لتعذر معرفة موضعه الحقيقي من الفلك ، بسبب ما يحصل فيه من اختلاف المنظر ، لأجل قربه من الأرض . ولو لم يعرف الموضع الحقيقي للقمر ، لتعذر معرفة المواضع الحقيقية لسائر الكواكب. فكان حصول الخسوف للقمر ، كالمفتاح المعرفة مواضع الأجرام النيرة الفلكية .
And the second aspect: That the moon increases and decreases in its light due to its proximity to the sun and its distance from it. Many people claim that the lights of the rest of the planets are borrowed from the sun. Were it not that the body of the moon does not have its light as essential light but rather accidental light derived from the sun, it would not be subject to eclipse. And were it not for the eclipse, it would be impossible to know its true position in the sphere due to the parallax that occurs in it because of its proximity to the earth. And if the true position of the moon were not known, the true positions of the rest of the planets could not be known. So the occurrence of the eclipse for the moon became like a key to knowing the positions of the luminous celestial bodies.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is the second proof (الوجه الثاني) of the sun's wisdom. It focuses on the moon. The argument begins with an observable fact: the moon's light waxes and wanes depending on its distance from the sun, indicating that its light is not intrinsic but borrowed. The author notes that many people already hold that the lights of all planets are borrowed from the sun. Then the proof takes a critical turn: if the moon's light were essential (not derived), it would not suffer eclipses. The fact that it does eclipse confirms its light is accidental. Moreover, lunar eclipses serve a crucial astronomical function: they allow astronomers to determine the moon's true position, correcting for parallax (the apparent shift due to the moon's nearness). Without eclipses, accurate positioning would be impossible. And since the moon's position is used to locate other stars, the eclipse becomes a key to the entire system. Thus the sun, via the moon's borrowed light and eclipses, enables humanity to map the heavens, showcasing divine wisdom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Lunar eclipses are random or merely natural phenomena with no purpose.
Correction: The author argues that lunar eclipses serve a specific purpose: they demonstrate the moon's light is borrowed and enable astronomers to determine the moon's true position, which is crucial for locating other celestial bodies.

Checkpoint
What does the author claim would be impossible without lunar eclipses?
A) To know the sun's position.
B) To know the moon's true position due to parallax.
C) To see the moon at night.
D) To determine the seasons.

Key Takeaway
Lunar eclipses are not just natural events but provide evidence that the moon's light is borrowed from the sun and serve as a key to understanding the positions of celestial bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you assume that what you see is the full truth, like assuming the moon has its own light? In what areas of your faith or life might you be mistaking a borrowed light for an intrinsic one? What step will you take today to uncover the true source?
?
If eclipses were not explained by science, would you still see them as part of God's plan? What does your answer reveal about your trust in God's wisdom even when you don't understand the mechanism? Will you commit to thanking Him for every natural event, even those you don't yet understand?
?
The moon's borrowed light enables knowledge of other stars. What 'borrowed' blessings in your life have enabled you to know or help others? Are you acknowledging the true Source of those blessings, or are you acting as if they are your own? How will you acknowledge Him today?
